The Four Topics of Divine Transcendence
The author lists the four topics of Chapter Two on divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four central topics that structure the discussion of tawhid and divine transcendence in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Divine transcendence is defended through four fundamental negations.

This passage serves as a chapter index, outlining the key investigations of Chapter Two. It establishes the core agenda for understanding tawhid not just as affirming God's oneness but also as actively negating any resemblance to creation. These four topics—negating corporeality, substance, accident, union, and temporal events—form the backbone of Islamic theological discourse on God's incomparability.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter index
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a list of four distinct investigations (mabāḥith).
The word 'mabāḥith' indicates these are topics to be studied in detail.
2
Identify the first topic
The first topic is 'on tawhid and transcendences'. Understand that tawhid here encompasses both affirming oneness and negating imperfections.
Tanzīh (transcendence) is a key concept alongside tawhid.
3
Identify the negation of corporeality
The second topic negates that God is a body, substance, or accident. This refutes anthropomorphism.
These three terms cover all created entities in classical physics.
4
Identify the negation of union and temporality
The third topic negates uniting with another, and the fourth negates being described by temporal events. These defend God's unique nature and timelessness.
Together, the four topics comprehensively safeguard divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني
وفيه مباحث :
الأول : في التوحيد
في التنزيهات
الثاني : الله تعالى ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض
الثالث : الله تعالى لا يتحد بغيره
الرابع : امتناع اتصاف الله تعالى بالحوادث
Chapter Two. And in it are investigations: First: On tawhid, on transcendences. Second: God Most High is not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident. Third: God Most High does not unite with another. Fourth: The impossibility of God Most High being described with temporal events.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a chapter index listing the four main topics of Chapter Two. The chapter is on tawhid (divine unity) and the transcendences (al-tanzīhāt), meaning the affirmation of God's incomparability. The first topic is on tawhid and the transcendences. The second topic affirms that God is not a body (jism), not a substance (jawhar), and not an accident (ʿaraḍ). This is a fundamental principle of Islamic theology that rejects anthropomorphism (tashbīh). The third topic states that God does not unite (lā yattaḥidu) with any other being, refuting concepts such as incarnation or union (ittiḥād). The fourth topic states the impossibility of God being described with temporal events (al-ḥawādith), meaning that He is not subject to change or temporality. Together, these four topics outline the core doctrine of divine transcendence in Islam.

Key Terms
التوحيد
التنزيهات
جسم
جوهر
عرض
اتحاد
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'tawhid' is only about God's oneness, excluding the negation of attributes.
Correction: Here tawhid explicitly includes transcendence (tanzih), which negates anthropomorphism and all imperfections.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is NOT a topic of Chapter Two?
A) God is not a body, substance, or accident
B) God does not unite with another
C) God is described with temporal events
D) On tawhid and transcendences

Key Takeaway
The four topics of Chapter Two systematically negate from God all forms of corporeality, composition, union, and temporality, thereby establishing His absolute transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God is not a body. When you think of God in your daily worship, do you ever unconsciously picture Him in your mind? What will you do when such thoughts arise?
?
The third topic says God does not unite with anything. Have you ever felt that God is 'inside' you or that you are 'one with God' in a mystical sense? How does this verse demand that you purify your belief?
?
The fourth topic says God is not described with temporal events—He does not change. When you pray, do you expect God to 'respond' in time as if He is affected by your actions? How does this lesson call you to have a deeper trust in God's eternal decree rather than seeking immediate changes?

Refuting Dualism: The Oneness of Good and Evil's Source
Al-Taftazani refutes dualist and Magian arguments for two principles of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the dualist argument that good and evil require two separate divine principles.

Core Idea
A single source of good and evil does not make God both good and evil.

This passage addresses a classic objection from Dualists and Magians: if one God created both good and evil, He would be both good and evil, which is impossible. Al-Taftazani shows how this entailment fails by carefully defining what 'good' and 'evil' mean when applied to God. The argument also considers the objection that an all-good God who allows evil must be powerless or evil Himself—a challenge answered by pointing to hidden wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dualist sects and their beliefs
Read the description of the Dualists (light/darkness) and Magians (Yazdan/Ahriman). Notice their shared assumption: good and evil need separate origins.
2
Analyze their central doubt (shubha)
The doubt is that a single source of both good and evil would be both good and evil, which is impossible. Understand why they think this is a contradiction.
This is the key premise al-Taftazani will dismantle.
3
Examine al-Taftazani's answer (al-jawab)
He prevents the entailment: 'good' and 'evil' can be used in a qualified sense (predominance) or a general sense (creator). The term 'evil' is not properly applied to God.
The answer distinguishes between moral evil and the creation of evil.
4
Consider the counter-objection
If God is all-good and all-powerful, why doesn't He stop evil? The passage acknowledges this objection and hints at an answer involving hidden wisdom.
The text cuts off, but the response likely references divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المشركون فمنهم الثنوية القائلون بأن للعالم إلهين نور هو مبدأ الخيرات، وظلمة هو مبدأ الشرور. ومنهم المجوس القائلون بأن مبدأ الخيرات هو (يزدان) ومبدأ الشرور هو (أهرمن) واختلفوا في أن (أهرمن) أيضاً قديم أو حادث من (يزدان) وشبهتهم أنه لو كان مبدأ الخير والشر واحد لزم كون الواحد خيراً وشريراً وهو محال . والجواب : منع اللزوم إن أريد بالخير من غلب خيره، وبالشر من غلب استحالة شره، ويمنع اللازم إن أريد خالق الخير وخالق الشر في الجملة . غاية الأمر أنه لا يصلح إطلاق الشرير لظهوره فيمن غلب شره . وعورض بأن الخير إن لم يقدر على دفع الشرير أو الشرور فعاجز، وإن قدر ولم يفعل فشرير، وإن جعل إبقاؤها خيراً لما فيه من الحكم والمصالح الخفية كما تزعم المعتزلة في خلق إبليس وذريته وأقداره وتمكينه من الإغواء، فلعل نفس
As for the polytheists, among them are the Dualists who say that the world has two gods: light, which is the principle of good things, and darkness, which is the principle of evil things. And among them are the Magians who say that the principle of good things is Yazdan and the principle of evil things is Ahriman, and they differed whether Ahriman is also eternal or originated from Yazdan. Their doubt is that if the principle of good and evil were one, then it would follow that the one is both good and evil, which is impossible. The answer: prevent the entailment if by 'good' one means that whose good prevails, and by 'evil' that whose evil prevails [or 'that whose evil is impossible'?]; and prevent the entailment if one means the creator of good and the creator of evil in general. The utmost is that the term 'evil' is not fit to be applied due to its being apparent in one whose evil prevails. And it was objected that if the good is not able to repel the evil or evils, then it is powerless, and if it is able but does not act, then it is evil; and if its preservation is deemed good because of the hidden wisdoms and benefits—as the Mu'tazila claim in the creation of Iblis and his progeny and his empowerment and enabling of misguidance—then perhaps the same...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualist and Magian polytheists. It begins by describing the Dualists (thanawiyya) who believe in two gods: light (source of good) and darkness (source of evil). The Magians (majus) believe in Yazdan as source of good and Ahriman as source of evil, with disagreement on whether Ahriman is eternal or originated from Yazdan. Their proof (shubha) is that if good and evil came from a single source, that source would have to be both good and evil, which is impossible. Al-Taftazani gives the answer (al-jawab): the entailment is denied if by 'good' one means that which predominantly brings good, and by 'evil' that which predominantly brings evil (or perhaps 'evil whose harm is impossible'? text unclear). The entailment is also blocked if one means simply the creator of good and creator of evil in general. The term 'evil' (sharrir) is not properly applied to God because it implies predominance of evil. Then a counter-objection (wu'rida) is raised: if the good (God) cannot repel evil, He is powerless; if He can but does not, He is evil. The answer would be (though the text cuts off) that the preservation of evil may be for hidden wisdom, as the Mu'tazila argue regarding the creation of Iblis. The passage ends abruptly with 'perhaps the same' (fal-'ala nafs), suggesting an unfinished argument.

Key Terms
الثنوية (al-thanawiyya)
المجوس (al-majus)
الخير (al-khayr)
الشر (al-sharr)
يزدان (Yazdan)
أهرمن (Ahriman)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That attributing both good and evil to God implies He is both good and evil in the same sense.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between the meaning of 'good' and 'evil' as predominant qualities or as creators; God is not morally evil even though He creates evil.

Checkpoint
What is the primary doubt (shubha) of the Dualists and Magians that this passage addresses?
A) That evil cannot exist if God is all-good.
B) That if one God created both good and evil, He would be both good and evil, which is impossible.
C) That God must have a partner to create evil.
D) That good and evil are both eternal principles.

Key Takeaway
A single source for both good and evil does not make God evil; the terms are applied differently based on predominance and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a painful trial or see evil in the world, do you ever catch yourself doubting that it comes from a single, wise, and good God? How does this lesson’s argument that the term 'evil' is not applied to God because it implies predominance of evil challenge you to reframe that doubt into trust?
?
Have you ever secretly assumed that because evil exists, there must be two separate sources—that God alone cannot be responsible? What would it cost you to fully embrace that He is the only source, and that every evil you experience serves a hidden purpose you may never see in this life?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that evil exists for hidden wisdom (al-hikam al-khafiyya). Is your faith in God’s justice and wisdom strong enough to accept that even the worst evils—like the creation of Iblis—have a good purpose? What would change in your daily reactions if you truly internalized this?

Refuting Claims of Divine Sonship and Shirk
Refutes claims of divine sonship and affirms God's uniqueness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic response to claims that prophets are sons of God, and the metaphorical interpretation of familial terms in scripture.

Core Idea
Words like 'father' and 'son' in scripture are metaphors, not biology.

This passage addresses two major claims from Jews and Christians about prophets being sons of God. The author provides a reasoned refutation based on scriptural interpretation and logical principles, showing that such language is metaphorical, not literal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
Read the passage to identify the specific groups (Jews and Christians) and the individuals they claim as sons of God ('Uzair and Jesus). Notice the reasons they give for these claims.
The Jews say 'Uzair is son of Allah because Allah revived him after death; Christians say Jesus is son because he was born without a father.
2
Understand the answer
Focus on the author's response: if the scriptural transmission is sound, the terms 'father' and 'son' are not literal. 'Father' means Lordship and origin; 'son' means complete devotion or an honorific title.
Note the Quranic grounding: the authority of the Gospel itself is used to argue that such language was also used for the community.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Realize that the negation of partnership in divinity (shirk) is established by both reason and revelation. Worship is due to God alone.
The passage ends with a Quranic quotation emphasizing God's oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على صورته وعظموه تشفعاً إلى الله تعالى وتوسلا (١)، ومنهم اليهود القائلون: إن عزيراً ابن الله (۲) ؛ لما أحياه الله تعالى بعد موته وكان يقرأ التوراة عن ظهر قلبه، ومنهم النصارى القائلون بأن المسيح ابن الله حيث ولد بلا أب، وورد في الإنجيل ذكرهما بلفظ الأب والابن. والجواب : أنه إن صح النقل من غير تحريف فمعنى الأبوة الربوبية، وكونه المبدأ والمرجع ومعنى النبوة التوجه إلى جناب الحق عز وجل بالكلية كابن السبيل أو قصد التشريف والكرامة، ولهذا نقل في الإنجيل مثل ذلك في حق الأمة أيضاً حيث قال : إني صاعد إلى أبي وأبيكم وإلهي وإلهكم . وبالجملة فنفي الشركة في الألوهية ثابت عقلاً وشرعاً وفي استحقاق العبادة شرعاً، وما أمروا إلا ليعبدوا إلهاً واحداً لا إله إلا هو سبحانه عما يشركون (۳)
upon his image and they magnified him seeking intercession and introduction to Allah Almighty (1). Among them are the Jews who say: 'Uzair is the son of Allah (2) because Allah brought him to life after his death and he used to recite the Torah from memory; and among them are the Christians who say that the Messiah is the son of Allah because he was born without a father, and their mention occurs in the Gospel with the words 'father' and 'son'. The answer: if the transmission is sound without distortion, then the meaning of 'fatherhood' is Lordship and His being the origin and reference, and the meaning of 'sonship' is complete orientation to the court of Truth Almighty like 'son of the road' (wayfarer) or an intention of honor and veneration. For this reason, similar language is transmitted in the Gospel concerning the community as well, where He said: 'I am ascending to my Father and your Father, and my God and your God.' In summary, the negation of partnership in divinity is established by reason and by law, and in the deservingness of worship by law; and they were commanded only to worship one God, there is no god but Him, exalted is He above what they associate (3).
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage refutes the claims of Jews and Christians that someone is a son of God. It begins by mentioning that some people set up images of righteous men seeking intercession, then it specifies the Jews who claimed 'Uzair is the son of Allah because Allah revived him after death and he could recite the Torah from memory, and the Christians who claimed the Messiah is the son of Allah because he was born without a father. The author then gives the answer: if the transmission is sound without distortion, the term 'father' means Lordship and being the origin and reference, and 'son' means total devotion to God (like a wayfarer) or an honorific. The author supports this by noting that the Gospel itself uses similar language for the community ('my Father and your Father'). The passage concludes that the negation of partnership in divinity is established by reason and revelation, and that worship is due to God alone, quoting the Quran (9:31).

Key Terms
شركة
ألوهية
توسل
تشفع
أبوة ربوبية
ابن الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The terms 'father' and 'son' in scripture imply a literal biological relationship between God and prophets.
Correction: In Islamic theology, these terms are interpreted metaphorically: 'father' refers to Lordship and origin, 'son' refers to complete devotion or an honorific title, as supported by the Gospel's own usage.

Checkpoint
What is the correct interpretation of 'son' when applied to a prophet in scripture according to the author?
A) A literal biological relationship
B) Complete devotion to God or an honorific
C) A mystical union with God
D) Adoption as a child

Key Takeaway
Scriptural terms 'father' and 'son' must be understood metaphorically, not literally, to maintain God's absolute oneness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, what or who do you rely on as your ultimate 'origin' or 'reference' besides God? How does the lesson's redefinition of 'fatherhood' challenge you to reorient that to God alone?
?
Have you ever used religious language carelessly, perhaps calling someone 'son of God' metaphorically, without realizing the theological weight? How will you guard your words to avoid even the appearance of shirk?
?
The Gospel verse says 'my Father and your Father'. If God is your 'Father' only in the sense of Lordship, does your obedience and devotion reflect that? In what specific situation today will you act as a true 'son of the road' — totally oriented toward Allah?

Philosophical Arguments for Divine Transcendence
Philosophers' proofs that God is not body, substance, or accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical arguments that God cannot be a body, substance, or accident because each entails dependence or composition.

Core Idea
Physical categories imply need; the Necessary Being needs nothing.

This lesson introduces the central thesis that God transcends all physical categories. The philosophers (al-Ḥukamāʾ) present concise arguments showing that describing God as a body, substance, or accident is impossible, as each category involves dependence on something else. Understanding these arguments is foundational for grasping divine transcendence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the thesis and philosophers' arguments
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice how the philosophers state their case succinctly.
The passage begins by stating what God is not, then gives reasons.
2
Identify the four arguments
The philosophers give four distinct arguments: against body, accident, substance, and place/direction. Identify each and note the key reason given.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Each argument points to some form of need or dependence. Ask yourself: why does need disqualify a thing from being God?
This connects to the concept of Necessary Being (wājib al-wujūd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ولا في مكان وجهة . فالحكماء (۱) : لأن الجسم محتاج إلى جزئه والعرض إلى محله والجوهر وجوده زائد على ماهيته والمكان والجهة من خواص الجسم . والمتكلمون : لأن الجوهر ينبيء لغة عما هو أصل الشيء والعرض: عما يمتنع بقاؤه، وإن دار مع القائم بنفسه والقائم بغيره والجسم حادث، لما سبق ومتحيز بالضرورة ومتصف ببعض الامتداد والاشكال لمخصص فتحتاج، ولو كان الواجب متحيزاً لزم قدم الحادث، أعني الحيز ، ولزم إمكان الواجب ووجوب المكان لأن التحيز محتاج إلى الحيز دون العكس ولكان إما في كل حيز فيخالط ما لا ينبغي مع لزوم التداخل، وإما في البعض بمخصص فيحتاج أولاً فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح . قال الواجب ليس بجسم (1) لأن كل جسم مركب من أجزاء عقلية هي الجنس والفصل ووجودية هي الهيولي والصورة أو الجواهر الفردة ومقدارية
The second investigation: that He, exalted, is not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident, nor in a place or direction. The philosophers (1): because the body is dependent on its part, the accident on its substrate, the substance has its existence in addition to its quiddity, and place and direction are properties of the body. The theologians: because the substance linguistically indicates that which is the origin of a thing, and the accident indicates that whose persistence is impossible, and they revolve around the self-subsistent and the non-self-subsistent; the body is originated, as previously mentioned, and it is necessarily occupying space, and it is characterized by some extension and shapes, so it needs a particularizer. If the Necessary were occupying space, then the originated — I mean space — would be eternal, and the Necessary would be contingent, and place would be necessary, because occupying space is dependent on space without the converse; and it would either be in every space, which would entail mixing with what is not appropriate and entail interpenetration, or in some spaces by a particularizer, which would first need something, thus necessitating preponderation without a preponderator. He said: the Necessary is not a body (1) because every body is composed of intellectual parts which are genus and differentia, and existential parts which are matter and form or individual atoms and quantity.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents proofs that God is not a body, substance, accident, nor in a place/direction. It begins by stating the thesis. Then it gives two sets of arguments: first from the philosophers: the body needs its part, the accident needs its substrate, substance has existence additional to its quiddity, and place/direction are properties of bodies. Second from the theologians: the substance linguistically means origin, accident means what cannot persist, and they revolve around self-subsistent and non-self-subsistent; the body is originated, occupies space, has extension and shapes, so it needs a particularizer. If the Necessary occupied space, then space would be eternal (contradiction), the Necessary would be contingent, place would be necessary; and either God would be in all places (mixing and interpenetration) or in some places by a particularizer, leading to an infinite regress or preponderance without cause. The passage concludes that the Necessary is not a body because every body is composed of parts (genus/differentia, matter/form, or atoms and quantity).

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
عرض
مكان
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can be described as a body or in a physical sense.
Correction: The philosophers show that bodies and physical categories are inherently dependent and composite, while God is absolutely independent and simple.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can God not be a body?
A) Because bodies are created
B) Because a body depends on its parts
C) Because bodies are made of atoms
D) Because bodies are in space

Key Takeaway
The philosophers demonstrate that describing God as a body, substance, accident, or in place/direction is impossible because each entails dependence or composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been reminded that Allah is not a body, nor in any place. Yet have you ever caught yourself thinking of Him as being 'up there' or 'in the sky'? What does that reveal about how your heart conceives of Him? Will you consciously replace that habit with the correct understanding of His transcendence, starting now?
?
If you truly believed that Allah is not dependent on anything, how would it change the way you ask from Him or the seriousness of your reliance on others? Can you honestly say your trust is in the One who needs nothing?
?
The substance has existence added to its quiddity, but for Allah existence is His very essence. This refutes any notion that Allah has an essence that could be defined separately. Are there times when you try to define or categorize Allah based on your limited mind? How will you guard against that intellectually?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
8 min
Theological Proofs: God is Not in Place and Not a Body
Theologians' proofs that God is not in place and not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theologians' arguments that God does not occupy space and is not composed of parts.

Core Idea
Spatial occupancy leads to absurdities; composition implies a need for a composer.

The theologians (al-Mutakallimūn) offer a more elaborate argument focusing on the implications of spatial occupancy and composition. They begin by defining substance and accident, then argue that the body is originated and requires a particularizer. They then show that if God were to occupy space, it would entail impossible consequences such as eternal originated things and the need for a particularizer. Finally, they argue that every body is composed of parts, so it cannot be the simple Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the theologians' arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: definitions, properties of body, reductio ad absurdum, and composition argument.
2
Analyze the reductio about space
The theologians imagine if God occupied space. Trace the steps: it would make space eternal, make God contingent, lead to particularization issues.
This is a powerful logical argument showing that spatiality is impossible for God.
3
Understand the composition argument
The passage concludes with a separate argument that the Necessary is not a body because every body is composed of parts (intellectual and existential).
This argument is common in kalām and falsafah.
4
Synthesize the lessons
Combine the philosophical and theological arguments to form a comprehensive understanding of divine transcendence.
Both traditions converge on God's incorporeality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ولا في مكان وجهة . فالحكماء (۱) : لأن الجسم محتاج إلى جزئه والعرض إلى محله والجوهر وجوده زائد على ماهيته والمكان والجهة من خواص الجسم . والمتكلمون : لأن الجوهر ينبيء لغة عما هو أصل الشيء والعرض: عما يمتنع بقاؤه، وإن دار مع القائم بنفسه والقائم بغيره والجسم حادث، لما سبق ومتحيز بالضرورة ومتصف ببعض الامتداد والاشكال لمخصص فتحتاج، ولو كان الواجب متحيزاً لزم قدم الحادث، أعني الحيز ، ولزم إمكان الواجب ووجوب المكان لأن التحيز محتاج إلى الحيز دون العكس ولكان إما في كل حيز فيخالط ما لا ينبغي مع لزوم التداخل، وإما في البعض بمخصص فيحتاج أولاً فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح . قال الواجب ليس بجسم (1) لأن كل جسم مركب من أجزاء عقلية هي الجنس والفصل ووجودية هي الهيولي والصورة أو الجواهر الفردة ومقدارية
The second investigation: that He, exalted, is not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident, nor in a place or direction. The philosophers (1): because the body is dependent on its part, the accident on its substrate, the substance has its existence in addition to its quiddity, and place and direction are properties of the body. The theologians: because the substance linguistically indicates that which is the origin of a thing, and the accident indicates that whose persistence is impossible, and they revolve around the self-subsistent and the non-self-subsistent; the body is originated, as previously mentioned, and it is necessarily occupying space, and it is characterized by some extension and shapes, so it needs a particularizer. If the Necessary were occupying space, then the originated — I mean space — would be eternal, and the Necessary would be contingent, and place would be necessary, because occupying space is dependent on space without the converse; and it would either be in every space, which would entail mixing with what is not appropriate and entail interpenetration, or in some spaces by a particularizer, which would first need something, thus necessitating preponderation without a preponderator. He said: the Necessary is not a body (1) because every body is composed of intellectual parts which are genus and differentia, and existential parts which are matter and form or individual atoms and quantity.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents proofs that God is not a body, substance, accident, nor in a place/direction. It begins by stating the thesis. Then it gives two sets of arguments: first from the philosophers: the body needs its part, the accident needs its substrate, substance has existence additional to its quiddity, and place/direction are properties of bodies. Second from the theologians: the substance linguistically means origin, accident means what cannot persist, and they revolve around self-subsistent and non-self-subsistent; the body is originated, occupies space, has extension and shapes, so it needs a particularizer. If the Necessary occupied space, then space would be eternal (contradiction), the Necessary would be contingent, place would be necessary; and either God would be in all places (mixing and interpenetration) or in some places by a particularizer, leading to an infinite regress or preponderance without cause. The passage concludes that the Necessary is not a body because every body is composed of parts (genus/differentia, matter/form, or atoms and quantity).

Correction: The theologians show that spatial location entails absurdities; God is not in any direction.

Philosophers' and Theologians' Proofs that God is Not a Body
The author negates divine corporeality and presents two sets of proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical and theological arguments that establish God's transcendence beyond body, substance, accident, place, and direction.

Core Idea
God is beyond all physical categories—body, substance, accident, place, and direction.

This passage presents the second section of the work, which negates corporeality and accidentality from God. The author first gives the philosophers' concise proof based on the nature of body and accident, then the theologians' more elaborate linguistic and conceptual argument. Both traditions converge on the same conclusion: God is absolutely transcendent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two distinct sections: philosophers (الحكماء) and theologians (المتكلمون).
2
Identify the philosophers' argument
The philosophers argue by analyzing the definition of body, accident, substance, and place. Find each element: body needs parts, accident needs locus, substance has existence added to essence, place/direction are properties of body.
This is a quick summary; we will unpack it in the commentary.
3
Analyze the theologians' argument
The theologians use linguistic definitions and logical consequences. They argue that body is originated and occupies space, requiring a determiner. If God occupied space, it would entail contradictions.
The argument has multiple steps: from necessary being to impossibility of spatial occupancy.
4
Ask: Why do both sets of arguments matter?
Consider how these arguments protect the concept of God from anthropomorphism and establish His absolute otherness.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني
أنه تعالى ليس بجسم ولا جوهر ولا عرض ولا في مكان وجهة . فالحكماء : لأن الجسم محتاج إلى جزئه والعرض إلى محله والجوهر وجوده زائد على ماهيته والمكان والجهة من خواص الجسم .
والمتكلمون : لأن الجوهر ينبيء لغة عما هو أصل الشيء والعرض: عما يمتنع بقاؤه، وإن دار مع القائم بنفسه والقائم بغيره والجسم حادث، لما سبق ومتحيز بالضرورة ومتصف ببعض الامتداد والاشكال لمخصص فتحتاج، ولو كان الواجب متحيزاً لزم قدم الحادث، أعني الحيز، ولزم إمكان الواجب ووجوب المكان لأن التحيز محتاج إلى الحيز دون العكس ولكان إما في كل حيز فيخالط ما لا ينبغي مع لزوم التداخل، وإما في البعض بمخصص فيحتاج أولاً فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح .
The second section: That He, exalted, is not a body, nor a substance, nor an accident, nor in a place or direction. The philosophers: because the body is in need of its part, and the accident is in need of its locus, and the substance whose existence is additional to its quiddity, and the place and direction are specific properties of the body. And the theologians: because 'substance' (al-jawhar) indicates linguistically what is the foundation of a thing, and 'accident' (al-'arad) indicates what cannot persist, although it revolves with the self-subsistent and the other-subsistent; and the body is originated, as previously mentioned, and is necessarily occupying space, and is characterized by some extension and shapes, so it requires a specific determiner; and if the Necessary were occupying space, it would necessitate the pre-eternity of the originated, namely space, and would necessitate the contingency of the Necessary and the necessity of place, because occupying space is in need of space but not vice versa; and it would either be in every space, which would entail mixing with what is not appropriate with the implication of interpenetration, or in some space due to a determiner, thus it would first need something, leading to preference without a preferrer.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the second section of the work, which asserts God's transcendence over body, substance, accident, place, and direction. It then presents two sets of arguments. First, the philosophers argue that God cannot be a body because a body is composed of parts (thus dependent), an accident requires a substrate, a substance has an existence that is additional to its essence, and place/direction are properties of bodies. Second, the theologians argue based on linguistic and conceptual grounds: 'substance' originally means the foundation of a thing, while 'accident' means what passes away; all bodies are originated and occupy space, requiring a determiner; if God were to occupy space, space would be pre-eternal (since God is eternal), but space is originated, so that is impossible; furthermore, occupying space would imply either God is in all spaces (leading to undesirable mixing and interpenetration) or in a particular space due to a determiner, which would create an infinite regress. The author follows the order of the arguments, first presenting the philosophers' concise proof, then the theologians' more elaborate one.

Correction: The passage argues that occupying space would entail originatedness and dependence, contradicting God's necessary existence.

Key Takeaway
God's absolute transcendence is established by both philosophical and theological reasoning, which independently prove that He cannot be a body, substance, accident, or located in space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God is not confined to any place or direction. Yet, when you sin, do you act as if you can hide from God because He is 'somewhere else'? Repent of this fallacy and live in full consciousness that He is with you everywhere.
?
The passage states that a body is in need of its parts. You are a body, dependent on your organs, health, and resources. Do you live with a constant sense of neediness towards God, or do you forget your contingency? Let this lesson humble you.
?
The theologians argued that if God were in a specific place, He would need a determiner, implying He is not the ultimate cause. Are there areas in your life where you limit God, thinking He cannot help you because of your circumstances? Renounce such limitations.

God is Not a Body
Excluding body from God using composition argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God cannot be a body because every body is composite and dependent on its parts.

Core Idea
God is not a body because every body needs its parts, but God needs nothing.

The author begins by excluding the possibility that God is a body. He defines a body as a composite of three kinds of parts, then argues that every composite depends on its parts, which cannot describe the Necessary Being. This foundational argument sets the stage for further exclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: definition, types of parts, then conclusion.
The passage is the first part of a longer argument.
2
Identify the three types of parts
The passage mentions intellectual parts (genus and differentia), existential parts (matter and form or atoms), and quantitative parts (dimensions). Try to list them.
These categories cover different aspects of composition.
3
Understand the logical implication
Anything composite is dependent on its parts for its existence and identity. Ask yourself: can something dependent be necessary?
Dependency contradicts the definition of the Necessary Being.
4
Apply to the Necessary Being
Since God is the Necessary Being, He must be absolutely independent. Therefore, He cannot be a body.
This is a rational proof, not just a scriptural statement.
5
Reflect on divine simplicity
Consider why simplicity (having no parts) is essential for the concept of God. This argument protects God from being composed.
Simplicity is a key attribute in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الواجب ليس بجسم لأن كل جسم مركب من أجزاء عقلية هي الجنس والفصل ووجودية هي الهيولي والصورة أو الجواهر الفردة ومقدارية هي الأبعاض له سوى هي الأبعاض، وكل مركب محتاج إلى جزئه ولا شيء من المحتاج بواجب، وليس بعرض لأن كل عرض محتاج إلى محل يقومه، إذ لا معنى ذلك ولا جوهر ؛ لأن معنى الجوهر ممكن يستغني عن المحل أو ماهية إذا وجدت كانت لا من موضوع فيكون وجوده زائداً عليه، والواجب ليس كذلك على ما سبق، وليس في مكان وجهه ؛ لأن المكان اسم للسطح الباطن من الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى أو للفراغ الذي يشغله الجسم والجهة اسم المنتهى مأخذ الإشارة ومقصد المتحرك فلا يكونان إلا للجسم والجسماني والواجب ليس كذلك .
He said: The Necessary is not a body, because every body is composed of intellectual parts which are genus and differentia, and existential parts which are matter and form or indivisible atoms, and quantitative parts which are the dimensions, [has other are the dimensions?], and every composite is in need of its part, and nothing that is in need is necessary. And it is not an accident, because every accident is in need of a substrate that sustains it, since there is no meaning to that. And it is not a substance, because the meaning of substance is a possible that is independent of a substrate, or a quiddity which, when it exists, is not in a subject, so its existence is additional to it; and the Necessary is not like that, as previously. And it is not in a place or direction, because place is the name for the inner surface of the container that touches the outer surface of the contained, or for the void that the body occupies; and direction is the name for the farthest point, the place of indication and the goal of movement; thus they exist only for a body or a corporeal, and the Necessary is not like that.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof that God (the Necessary Being) is not a body, accident, substance, or located in place or direction. The author logically excludes each category. The proof begins by defining a body as a composite of parts — intellectual (genus and differentia), existential (matter and form or atoms), and quantitative (dimensions) — and argues that every composite depends on its parts, so it cannot be necessary. Then it excludes accident, because every accident depends on a substrate. Then it excludes substance, because substance is defined as a possible entity that is independent of a substrate, and its existence is additional to its essence, whereas God's existence is necessary and identical to His essence. Finally, it excludes place and direction, as these are defined in relation to bodies: place is the containing surface or void, and direction is the endpoint of movement; both are properties of bodies and corporeals, so they cannot apply to God. The argument proceeds sequentially, each exclusion building on the previous one, emphasizing God's absolute simplicity and independence.

Key Takeaway

God is Not an Accident
Excluding accident from God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be an accident, as every accident depends on a substrate.

Core Idea
An accident needs a substance to exist; God needs nothing.

After excluding body, the author turns to accidents. An accident is defined as something that inheres in a substrate. Since God is absolutely self-subsistent, He cannot be an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. It is a very short argument.
The passage focuses on the definition of accident.
2
Understand 'accident' definition
An accident is something that exists only in a substrate (mahall). It has no independent existence.
Examples: color, temperature, knowledge.
3
Apply to God
God's existence is self-subsistent and not dependent on anything else. Therefore, He cannot be an accident.
This follows from the nature of necessity.

God is Not a Substance
Excluding substance from God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definition of substance and why it cannot apply to God.

Core Idea
A substance is a possible being with existence added to its essence; God's existence is necessary and identical to His essence.

The author now excludes substance. In kalam, 'substance' (jawhar) has a specific technical meaning: a self-subsistent possible entity whose existence is separate from its essence. Since God's existence is necessary and identical to His essence (as previously established), He cannot be a substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two definitions given for substance.
The passage builds on earlier conclusions.
2
Understand the definition of substance
Substance is defined as a possible entity independent of a substrate, or a quiddity whose existence is additional to its essence.
Both definitions imply contingency.
3
Identify the key phrase: 'existence is additional'
For a substance, existence is not part of its essence; it is an added attribute. This means it is possible (not necessary).
This is a crucial distinction.
4
Contrast with God
God's existence is necessary and identical to His essence, as argued earlier. Therefore, He is not a substance.
This preserves God's absolute necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الواجب ليس بجسم لأن كل جسم مركب من أجزاء عقلية هي الجنس والفصل ووجودية هي الهيولي والصورة أو الجواهر الفردة ومقدارية هي الأبعاض له سوى هي الأبعاض، وكل مركب محتاج إلى جزئه ولا شيء من المحتاج بواجب، وليس بعرض لأن كل عرض محتاج إلى محل يقومه، إذ لا معنى ذلك ولا جوهر ؛ لأن معنى الجوهر ممكن يستغني عن المحل أو ماهية إذا وجدت كانت لا من موضوع فيكون وجوده زائداً عليه، والواجب ليس كذلك على ما سبق، وليس في مكان وجهه ؛ لأن المكان اسم للسطح الباطن من الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى أو للفراغ الذي يشغله الجسم والجهة اسم المنتهى مأخذ الإشارة ومقصد المتحرك فلا يكونان إلا للجسم والجسماني والواجب ليس كذلك .

This passage presents a detailed proof that God (the Necessary Being) is not a body, accident, substance, or located in place or direction. The author logically excludes each category. The proof begins by defining a body as a composite of parts — intellectual (genus and differentia), existential (matter and form or atoms), and quantitative (dimensions) — and argues that every composite depends on its parts, so it cannot be necessary. Then it excludes accident, because every accident depends on a substrate. Finally, it excludes place and direction, as these are defined in relation to bodies: place is the containing surface or void, and direction is the endpoint of movement; both are properties of bodies and corporeals, so they cannot apply to God. The argument proceeds sequentially, each exclusion building on the previous one, emphasizing God's absolute simplicity and independence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think substance here means the Aristotelian category of substance as an independent entity, but in kalam it specifically means a possible being with existence added to essence.
Correction: The passage defines substance as a possible (mumkin) and emphasizes that its existence is additional (za'id) to its quiddity, which is not true of God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one of the definitions of substance?
A) An entity that occupies space
B) A possible that is independent of a substrate
C) A necessary being
D) A composite of matter and form

Key Takeaway
God is not a substance because He is not a possible being; His existence is necessary and identical to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does knowing that God is not a substance—that His existence is identical to His essence—challenge the way you mentally picture or relate to Him? Will you actively reject any mental image that tries to place Him in the category of existents?
?
The passage teaches that you, unlike God, are a possible being whose existence is added to your essence. When you look at your own life, does that contingency feel real to you? Or do you live as though you are self-sufficient? What is one practical step you will take today to remind yourself that you are utterly dependent on the Necessary Being for every breath?
?
If God's existence is identical to His essence, then He is not made up of parts or attributes added to His essence. How does this truth affect the way you call upon Him? For example, when you say 'Ya Qayyum' (O Self-Subsisting), does it now carry a deeper meaning? Pause for a moment and let that name transform your reliance on Him.

God is Not in Place or Direction
Excluding place and direction from God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God cannot be located in place or direction, as these are properties of bodies.

Core Idea
Place and direction depend on bodies; God is not a body, so He has no location.

The final exclusion addresses location. The author defines place and direction in terms of bodies and their movements. Since God is not a body, He cannot be in a place or have a direction. This completes the argument for divine transcendence over all physical categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the definitions of place and direction.
These definitions are technical.
2
Understand the definition of place
Place is defined as the inner surface of a container touching the contained, or the void occupied by a body.
Both definitions involve a body.
3
Understand the definition of direction
Direction is defined as the farthest point, the place of indication, and the goal of movement.
Direction only makes sense for moving bodies.
4
Apply to God
Since God is not a body (already proven), place and direction cannot apply to Him.
This refutes anthropomorphic ideas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الواجب ليس بجسم لأن كل جسم مركب من أجزاء عقلية هي الجنس والفصل ووجودية هي الهيولي والصورة أو الجواهر الفردة ومقدارية هي الأبعاض له سوى هي الأبعاض، وكل مركب محتاج إلى جزئه ولا شيء من المحتاج بواجب، وليس بعرض لأن كل عرض محتاج إلى محل يقومه، إذ لا معنى ذلك ولا جوهر ؛ لأن معنى الجوهر ممكن يستغني عن المحل أو ماهية إذا وجدت كانت لا من موضوع فيكون وجوده زائداً عليه، والواجب ليس كذلك على ما سبق، وليس في مكان وجهه ؛ لأن المكان اسم للسطح الباطن من الحاوي المماس للسطح الظاهر من المحوى أو للفراغ الذي يشغله الجسم والجهة اسم المنتهى مأخذ الإشارة ومقصد المتحرك فلا يكونان إلا للجسم والجسماني والواجب ليس كذلك .
He said: The Necessary is not a body, because every body is composed of intellectual parts which are genus and differentia, and existential parts which are matter and form or indivisible atoms, and quantitative parts which are the dimensions, [has other are the dimensions?], and every composite is in need of its part, and nothing that is in need is necessary. And it is not an accident, because every accident is in need of a substrate that sustains it, since there is no meaning to that. And it is not a substance, because the meaning of substance is a possible that is independent of a substrate, or a quiddity which, when it exists, is not in a subject, so its existence is additional to it; and the Necessary is not like that, as previously. And it is not in a place or direction, because place is the name for the inner surface of the container that touches the outer surface of the contained, or for the void that the body occupies; and direction is the name for the farthest point, the place of indication and the goal of movement; thus they exist only for a body or a corporeal, and the Necessary is not like that.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed proof that God (the Necessary Being) is not a body, accident, substance, or located in place or direction. The author logically excludes each category. The proof begins by defining a body as a composite of parts — intellectual (genus and differentia), existential (matter and form or atoms), and quantitative (dimensions) — and argues that every composite depends on its parts, so it cannot be necessary. Then it excludes accident, because every accident depends on a substrate. Then it excludes substance, because substance is defined as a possible entity that is independent of a substrate, and its existence is additional to its essence, whereas God's existence is necessary and identical to His essence. Finally, it excludes place and direction, as these are defined in relation to bodies: place is the containing surface or void, and direction is the endpoint of movement; both are properties of bodies and corporeals, so they cannot apply to God. The argument proceeds sequentially, each exclusion building on the previous one, emphasizing God's absolute simplicity and independence.

B) For a body or a corporeal
C) For all beings
D) For possible beings

Key Takeaway
God is not in a place or direction because these depend on being a body, which God is not.

Linguistic Basis for Negating Substance and Accident
The author explains the theologians' alternative approach based on linguistic derivation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early theologians used linguistic derivation as an alternative basis to negate substantiality and accidentally from God, distinct from logical definitions.

Core Idea
God is neither substance nor accident — the words themselves indicate composition and transience.

The early theologians developed a unique method to affirm God's transcendence. Instead of relying solely on logical categories, they turned to the Arabic language itself. By examining how the words 'jawhar' (substance) and 'arad' (accident) are actually used, they found a deeper basis to negate these attributes from Allah. In this passage, the author explains this alternative approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from the general claim to the linguistic definitions and examples.
The author uses a contrast between logical usage and linguistic origin.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find how the passage defines 'jawhar' and 'arad' from a linguistic perspective. Note that these definitions differ from the common logical ones.
Jawhar is defined as 'that from which a thing is composed', arad as 'what cannot persist'.
3
Examine the linguistic examples
Read the examples given for each term. For jawhar: 'so-and-so acts according to his noble origin', 'this garment is well-made'. For arad: 'this matter is accidental', 'a matter occurred'. Reflect on how these support the definitions.
The examples show that jawhar relates to origin and durability, while arad relates to temporariness.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Consider why this approach is used for negating these attributes from God. God is eternal and not composed, so He cannot be a jawhar (origin/composite) or arad (transient).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمتكلمين خصوصاً القدماء منهم في هذه التنزيهات مسلك آخر ففي نفي الجوهرية والعرضية أن الجوهر اسم لما يتركب منه الشيء، والعرض لما يستحيل بقاؤه وإن كان يصح في الشاهد أن كل جوهر قائم بنفسه وكل قائم بنفسه جوهر وكل عرض قائم بالغير وكل قائم بالغير عرض، إلا أن إطلاق اسم ليس من هذه الجهة بل من جهة ما ذكرنا بدلالة اللغة .
يقال : فلان يجري على جوهره الشريف أي أصله وهذا الثوب جوهري ، أي محكم الأصل جيد الصنعة وهذا الأمر عارض أي يزول، وعرض لفلان أمر.
And for the theologians, especially the early ones, there is another approach in these acts of transcendence: concerning the negation of substantiality and accidentally, that 'substance' (al-jawhar) is a name for that from which a thing is composed, and 'accident' (al-'arad) for what cannot persist; even if it is correct in the observable world that every substance is self-subsistent and every self-subsistent is a substance, and every accident is other-subsistent and every other-subsistent is an accident, nevertheless the application of the name is not from this aspect but from the aspect we mentioned, by the indication of language. It is said: So-and-so acts according to his noble substance (jawhar), meaning his origin; and this garment is substantial (jawhari), meaning firm in origin, good in workmanship; and this matter is accidental ('arid), meaning it disappears; and a matter occurred ('arada) to so-and-so.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents an alternative approach used by the early theologians to negate substantiality and accidentally from God. Rather than relying on logical definitions (substance as self-subsistent, accident as other-subsistent), they base their negation on linguistic usage. The word 'jawhar' in language means the original foundation or essence of a thing, as in 'so-and-so acts according to his noble origin' or 'this garment is well-made in its origin'. The word 'arad' means what is transitory and disappears, as in 'this matter is accidental' or 'a matter occurs'. Since God is eternal and not composed of parts, He cannot be a 'jawhar' (origin) in this sense, nor an 'arad' (transitory). The author notes that while in the observable world every self-subsistent is called a substance and every other-subsistent is called an accident, the actual basis for applying these names is linguistic, not logical. This reinforces the transcendence of God from these categories.

عرض
لغة
دلالة
جوهرية
عرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The linguistic approach contradicts or replaces the logical definitions of substance and accident.
Correction: The passage shows that the linguistic approach is an alternative basis for negation, not contradictory. The logical definitions remain valid in the observable world, but the primary basis for naming is linguistic.

Checkpoint

A) It is self-subsistent.
B) It is the origin or foundation from which a thing is composed.
C) It occupies space.
D) It has potentiality.

Key Takeaway
The early theologians used linguistic analysis to affirm that Allah is neither a composed origin nor transient, thus negating substantiality and accidentally from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Proof that God is Not a Substance or Accident
Taftazani argues God is neither substance nor accident using logical necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for why the Necessary Being cannot be a substance or an accident in classical Islamic theology.

Core Idea
God is neither substance nor accident because both entail dependency, contradicting necessity.

Taftazani presents a concise yet powerful argument that the Necessary Being cannot be a substance or an accident. He uses definitions from kalam metaphysics to show that both categories imply dependence on parts or a substrate, which is incompatible with necessary existence. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow: first, a statement about composition, then a denial of accident-hood, then a denial of substance-hood.
The first phrase is ambiguous; scholars interpret it as a denial of parts.
2
Identify the definitions
Identify how Taftazani defines 'substance' (jawhar) and 'accident' ('arad) in this passage. Note that a substance is a possible entity independent of a substrate, with existence superadded, while an accident needs a substrate.
These definitions are technical and differ from common usage.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Walk through the argument: The Necessary Being cannot be composite (needing parts), cannot be an accident (needing a substrate), and cannot be a substance (since substance is a possible with superadded existence, while the Necessary is self-necessary).
The conclusion that He is not in a place or direction sets up the next proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له سوى هي الأبعاض، وكل مركب محتاج إلى جزئه ولا شيء من المحتاج بواجب، وليس بعرض لأن كل عرض محتاج إلى محل يقومه، إذ لا معنى ذلك ولا جوهر ؛ لأن معنى الجوهر ممكن يستغني عن المحل أو ماهية إذا وجدت كانت لا من موضوع فيكون وجوده زائداً عليه، والواجب ليس كذلك على ما سبق، وليس في مكان وجهه
It has only parts? Every composite needs its component, and nothing needy is obligatory. And He is not an accident, because every accident needs a substrate to support it. So that meaning is not [applicable] nor is He a substance, because the meaning of substance is a possible that is independent of a substrate, or an essence which when it exists is not in a subject, so its existence is superadded to it. And the Necessary is not like that according to what preceded, and He is not in a place or direction.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a proof that God is neither a substance (jawhar) nor an accident ('arad). The author begins by noting that the Necessary Being cannot have parts (أبعاض) because anything composite depends on its parts, and what depends is not necessary. He then argues that God is not an accident because accidents require a substrate to inhere in. He is not a substance either, because a substance (in kalam terminology) is either a possible entity independent of a substrate or an essence that exists not in a subject, making its existence superadded to its essence. The Necessary Being, being self-sufficient and necessary, does not fit these descriptions. The passage concludes by stating that God is not in a place or direction, setting up subsequent proofs.

Key Terms

الجوهر
العرض
المركب
المحتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'substance' and 'accident' are merely logical categories with no real theological implications.
Correction: In kalam, these are technical terms: a substance is a self-subsisting possible entity, and an accident is an entity that inheres in a substrate. Denying them of God is a positive assertion of His self-sufficiency and independence.

Checkpoint

D) Because substances are physical bodies

Key Takeaway
God, as the Necessary Being, cannot be a substance or an accident because both entail dependency, while necessary existence is self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God is absolutely self-sufficient but I am utterly dependent on Him for every breath, do I truly live in constant awareness of my need—or do I pretend I can stand alone? What practical step can I take today to remind myself that my existence is a gift at every moment?

The passage teaches that God's existence is not 'superadded' to His essence—He is existence itself. When I think about my own life, do I treat my existence as something I own, or do I recognize that it is given to me anew each instant? How can I turn this realization into a moment of genuine gratitude right now?
?

How can I train my heart to see His attributes as a single, perfect reality without diminishing His oneness? What one thought pattern must I change to stop reducing Him?

The First Two Proofs: From Origination and Distinction
Taftazani states the first two proofs against divine corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two arguments used by Taftazani to prove that God is not a body: the argument from origination (ḥudūth) and the argument from distinction (tamyīz).

Core Idea
Every body is originated and occupies a place; God is neither.

Taftazani begins his case against divine corporeality with two straightforward proofs. The first relies on the established fact that every body is originated (ḥādith), while the second notes that every body is necessarily distinguished by occupying a specific location. Since God is eternal and not subject to spatial limitation, He cannot be a body. Let's examine these proofs in his own words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first two proofs
Read the Arabic excerpt containing the first and second proofs, then the translation. Notice how Taftazani references previous conclusions ('as previously [established]' and 'as will come').
2
Understand the first proof: origination
The first proof says every body is originated (ḥādith). Since God is eternal (qadīm), He cannot be a body. This relies on the earlier proof that all bodies are originated.
The term ḥudūth means origination in time.
3
Understand the second proof: distinction
The second proof says every body is necessarily distinguished (mutamayyiz) by occupying a specific location. God is not so, as will be shown later. This argument uses the concept of tamyīz, which means being distinct in place or position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نفي الجسمية وجوه : الأول : أن كل جسم حادث لما سبق . الثاني : أن كل جسم متميز بالضرورة . والواجب ليس كذلك لما سيأتي . الثالث : أن الواجب لو كان جسماً. فإما أن يتصف بجميع صفات الأجسام. فيلزم اجتماع الضدين كالحركة والسكون ونحوهما ، وإما ألا يتصف بشيء فيلزم انتفاء بعض لوازم الجسم ، مع أن الضدين قد يكونان بحيث يمتنع خلو الجسم عنهما ، وإما أن يتصف بالبعض دون البعض فيلزم احتياج الواجب في صفاته إن كان ذلك المخصص ويلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح ، إن كان لا لمخصص. الرابع : أنه لو كان جسماً لكان متناهياً لما مرَّ من تناهي الأبعاد، فيكون شكلاً، لأن الشكل عبارة عن هيئة إحاطة النهاية بالجسم ، وحينئذ إما أن يكون على جميع الأشكال أو لا لمخصص فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح . وهو محال ، أو على البعض دون البعض لمخصص فيلزم الاحتياج لا يقال هذا وارد في اتصاف الواجب بصفاته دون أضدادها لأنا نقول : صفاته صفات كمال يتصف بها لذاته، وأضدادها صفات نقص تنزه عنها لذاته بخلاف الأضداد المتواردة على الأجسام، فإنها قد تكون متساوية الأقدام،
And regarding the negation of bodiliness, there are proofs: First: that every body is originated (hadith) as previously [established]. Second: that every body is necessarily distinguished [by position] and the Necessary is not so, as will come. Third: that if the Necessary were a body, then either He would be qualified by all the attributes of bodies, leading to the conjunction of opposites like motion and rest and the like; or He would not be qualified by any, leading to the negation of some necessary concomitants of body, while opposites may be such that the body cannot be devoid of both; or He would be qualified by some but not others, leading to the need of the Necessary for a particularizer (mukhasis) in His attributes if there is one, or to preponderance without a preponderator if there is no particularizer. Fourth: that if He were a body, He would be finite, as [previously] established regarding the finitude of dimensions, and thus would have a shape, because shape is the configuration of the limit enclosing the body. Then either He would have all shapes or not — if without a particularizer, it leads to preponderance without preponderator, which is impossible; or some shapes over others by a particularizer, leading to need. One cannot object: 'This applies to God's being qualified by His attributes as opposed to their opposites,' for we say: His attributes are attributes of perfection with which He is qualified by His essence, and their opposites are attributes of deficiency from which He is transcendent by His essence, unlike the opposites that occur to bodies, for they may be equal in status.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage enumerates four proofs (وجوه) for negating that God is a body (jism). The first proof is that every body is originated (hadith), and God is eternal. The second is that every body is necessarily distinguished (i.e., occupies a specific location), and God is not so. The third proof is a trilemma: if God were a body, He would either have all bodily properties (leading to opposites like motion and rest), or none (negating necessary concomitants), or some (requiring a particularizer or preponderance without cause). The fourth proof argues that a body must be finite and possess a shape; if God had a shape, He would have to have a specific shape either by a particularizer or by chance, both impossible. The passage then addresses an objection: why don't these same arguments apply to God's attributes (e.g., He has knowledge but not ignorance)? The answer is that divine attributes are perfections essential to God, while their opposites are deficiencies from which He is essentially free. Bodily opposites (like motion/rest) are equal in claim, so lacking a particularizer would be arbitrary.

Key Terms
الجسم
الحدوث
التميز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God being 'in the heavens' implies spatial location.
Correction: The second proof argues that God is not subject to spatial distinction. Scriptural references to God being 'above' are interpreted metaphorically, not literally.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof against God being a body?
A) Every body is originated
B) Every body is distinguished
C) Every body has shape
D) Every body is composite

Key Takeaway
The first two proofs establish that God cannot be a body because bodies are originated and spatially determined, while God is eternal and beyond space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not a body, then He is not confined to any place. Where do you often 'place' God in your mind? Do you think of Him as being in the sky or in a particular direction? How does this proof challenge your unconscious assumptions? Will you today actively remind yourself that Allah is not limited by any space?
?
The proof says every body is originated—it came into being. Do you live as if your own body is temporary, or do you act as if it will last forever? How does remembering the origination of your body change your priorities today? What is one thing you can do today to prepare for the time when your body returns to its Creator?
?
God is not distinguished by position, meaning He has no location. In which 'position' of your life—your career, your relationships, your health—have you been acting as if God must fit into your plans? Are you submitting to the One who is beyond all positions, or are you trying to confine Him to one? What would it look like to surrender that area completely to Him today?

The Third Proof: The Trilemma of Bodily Attributes
Taftazani presents the third proof, a trilemma concerning bodily attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof against divine corporeality, which presents a trilemma: if God were a body, He would either have all bodily attributes, none, or some, each leading to an impossibility.

Core Idea
A body cannot avoid all its necessary attributes; God is free of need.

The third proof is more intricate. Taftazani presents a logical trilemma: if the Necessary Being were a body, He would have to be qualified by bodily attributes in one of three ways, each of which leads to an absurdity. This argument forces the conclusion that God cannot be a body, because the very concept of bodiliness entails attributes incompatible with divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof carefully
Read the Arabic text of the third proof and its translation. Notice the three exhaustive possibilities presented.
2
Analyze the three branches of the trilemma
The proof considers three scenarios: having all bodily attributes, having none, or having some. For each, Taftazani shows a problem: conjunction of opposites, negation of essential concomitants, or need for a particularizer.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since all three options are impossible, the original assumption (that God is a body) must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نفي الجسمية وجوه : الأول : أن كل جسم حادث لما سبق . الثاني : أن كل جسم متميز بالضرورة . والواجب ليس كذلك لما سيأتي . الثالث : أن الواجب لو كان جسماً. فإما أن يتصف بجميع صفات الأجسام. فيلزم اجتماع الضدين كالحركة والسكون ونحوهما ، وإما ألا يتصف بشيء فيلزم انتفاء بعض لوازم الجسم ، مع أن الضدين قد يكونان بحيث يمتنع خلو الجسم عنهما ، وإما أن يتصف بالبعض دون البعض فيلزم احتياج الواجب في صفاته إن كان ذلك المخصص ويلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح ، إن كان لا لمخصص. الرابع : أنه لو كان جسماً لكان متناهياً لما مرَّ من تناهي الأبعاد، فيكون شكلاً، لأن الشكل عبارة عن هيئة إحاطة النهاية بالجسم ، وحينئذ إما أن يكون على جميع الأشكال أو لا لمخصص فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح . وهو محال ، أو على البعض دون البعض لمخصص فيلزم الاحتياج لا يقال هذا وارد في اتصاف الواجب بصفاته دون أضدادها لأنا نقول : صفاته صفات كمال يتصف بها لذاته، وأضدادها صفات نقص تنزه عنها لذاته بخلاف الأضداد المتواردة على الأجسام، فإنها قد تكون متساوية الأقدام،
And regarding the negation of bodiliness, there are proofs: First: that every body is originated (hadith) as previously [established]. Second: that every body is necessarily distinguished [by position] and the Necessary is not so, as will come. Third: that if the Necessary were a body, then either He would be qualified by all the attributes of bodies, leading to the conjunction of opposites like motion and rest and the like; or He would not be qualified by any, leading to the negation of some necessary concomitants of body, while opposites may be such that the body cannot be devoid of both; or He would be qualified by some but not others, leading to the need of the Necessary for a particularizer (mukhasis) in His attributes if there is one, or to preponderance without a preponderator if there is no particularizer. Fourth: that if He were a body, He would be finite, as [previously] established regarding the finitude of dimensions, and thus would have a shape, because shape is the configuration of the limit enclosing the body. Then either He would have all shapes or not — if without a particularizer, it leads to preponderance without preponderator, which is impossible; or some shapes over others by a particularizer, leading to need. One cannot object: 'This applies to God's being qualified by His attributes as opposed to their opposites,' for we say: His attributes are attributes of perfection with which He is qualified by His essence, and their opposites are attributes of deficiency from which He is transcendent by His essence, unlike the opposites that occur to bodies, for they may be equal in status.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage enumerates four proofs (وجوه) for negating that God is a body (jism). The first proof is that every body is originated (hadith), and God is eternal. The second is that every body is necessarily distinguished (i.e., occupies a specific location), and God is not so. The third proof is a trilemma: if God were a body, He would either have all bodily properties (leading to opposites like motion and rest), or none (negating necessary concomitants), or some (requiring a particularizer or preponderance without cause). The fourth proof argues that a body must be finite and possess a shape; if God had a shape, He would have to have a specific shape either by a particularizer or by chance, both impossible. The passage then addresses an objection: why don't these same arguments apply to God's attributes (e.g., He has knowledge but not ignorance)? The answer is that divine attributes are perfections essential to God, while their opposites are deficiencies from which He is essentially free. Bodily opposites (like motion/rest) are equal in claim, so lacking a particularizer would be arbitrary.

Key Terms
الضدان
اللازمة
التخصيص
الترجيح بلا مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God has attributes like knowledge and power, that is similar to having bodily attributes.
Correction: This proof is specifically about bodily attributes that involve opposites like motion/rest; divine attributes are perfections, not neutral opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what problem arises if God were a body and had some bodily attributes but not others?
A) It leads to the conjunction of opposites
B) It implies God is finite
C) It requires a cause to specify which attributes God has
D) It denies God's eternity

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that the concept of bodiliness is incompatible with divine perfection by exhausting all logical possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were a body, He would need a particularizer. Do you ever treat God as if He were limited by time, space, or human attributes? In what ways do you unconsciously confine the Creator to creaturely categories? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized His infinite transcendence?
?
The trilemma shows that the very concept of bodily attributes leads to absurdity. Have you ever thought of God in a way that reduces Him to something manageable? Are there areas of your life where you try to fit God into your own expectations rather than submitting to His absolute otherness?
?
The proof relies on the principle of no preponderance without a cause. Do you live as if there is a cause for your existence beyond yourself? How does the impossibility of arbitrary preference in God apply to your own decisions and responsibilities?

The Fourth Proof and an Objection Answered
Taftazani presents the fourth proof about finitude and shape, then addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof against divine corporeality—that a body must be finite and have a specific shape, leading to preponderance without a preponderator—and the objection regarding divine attributes and its answer.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would have a shape, requiring a cause for that shape; but God is self-sufficient.

The fourth proof builds on the earlier finitude of dimensions. Taftazani argues that if God were a body, He would be finite and have a shape, and that shape would need a particularizer—otherwise it would be arbitrary. He then anticipates an objection: doesn't this same reasoning apply to God's attributes? In answering, he clarifies a crucial distinction between divine attributes of perfection and the neutral opposites found in bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth proof
Read the Arabic for the fourth proof and the translation. Note how it uses the premise that all bodies are finite (from earlier proof about dimensions) and that shape is the configuration of that finitude.
2
Understand the problem of shape
If God had a shape, He would either have all shapes (impossible), or a specific shape by a particularizer (implying need) or without (arbitrary preponderance).
3
Consider the objection and answer
The objection says this reasoning applies to divine attributes (e.g., why does God have knowledge and not ignorance?). The answer distinguishes: divine attributes are essential perfections, their opposites are deficiencies; bodily opposites are equally possible, so a particularizer is needed.
