First Proof: God Cannot Be in Space or Direction
Taftazani presents the first proof against divine spatiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalam argument against God being spatially located, based on the impossibility of a spatial entity coexisting with an eternal space.

Core Idea
If God were spatial, His space would be eternal, but only God is eternal.

This lesson unpacks the first of several proofs Taftazani offers for God's transcendence of space and direction. The argument is concise but powerful: if God occupied a location, that location would have to be eternal like God, yet everything other than God is originated. Through a simple syllogism, the impossibility of divine spatiality becomes clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the logical flow: 'if-were... then-necessarily... because... and the consequent is false...'. Try to identify the conditional statement and its implied conclusion.
The passage uses technical terms like 'الحيز' (space) and 'المتحيز' (spatial entity).
2
Understand the translation and key terms
Read the literal translation and match each Arabic term to its English equivalent. Pay special attention to 'القدم' (eternity) and 'الحدوث' (origination).
These terms are foundational in kalam for discussing God's attributes versus creation.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the argument into premises and conclusion. Use the guided breakdown below to see how the proof works step by step.
Notice that the proof relies on the principle that a spatial entity cannot exist without its space.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this proof matters for Islamic theology. It establishes that God is not confined by space, which supports His absolute transcendence (tanzih).
This counters anthropomorphic conceptions of God.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to ensure you grasp the core reasoning of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نفي الحيز والجهة وجوه . الأول : أنه لو كان الواجب متحيزاً للزم قدم الحيز ضرورة امتناع المتحيز بدون الحيز واللازم باطل لما مر من حدوث ما سوى الواجب وصفاته.
And regarding the negation of space and direction, there are proofs. First: that if the Necessary were spatial, then the space would necessarily be eternal because it is impossible for a spatial entity to be without space, and the consequent is false due to what has passed concerning the originatedness of everything other than the Necessary and His attributes.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for negating that God occupies a place (hayyiz) or direction (jiha). The author argues that if God were spatially located (mutahayyiz), the space He occupies would have to be eternal, since a spatial object cannot exist without its space. But everything other than God and His attributes is originated (hadith), as previously established. Therefore, the space would be originated, not eternal, contradicting the necessity of the space being coexistent with God. Hence, God cannot be in space or direction.

Key Terms
الحيز
الجهة
المتحيز
القدم
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's omnipresence means He is spatially present everywhere in creation.
Correction: Omnipresence in Islam means God's knowledge and power encompass everything, not that He occupies physical space. This proof shows that spatial location implies contingency and origination, which cannot apply to God.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that if God were spatial, the space would have to be eternal?
A) Because space is eternal by definition
B) Because a spatial entity cannot exist without its space
C) Because God is eternal and shared with space
D) Because the proof assumes space is divine

Key Takeaway
The first proof against divine spatiality shows that spatial location would require an eternal space, but everything other than God is originated, so God cannot be spatial.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining Allah as being 'up' in the sky or in a particular direction when you pray? After learning this proof, are you willing to let go of that mental image and worship a God who transcends all space? What changes in your heart when you truly conceive of Allah as non-spatial?

Think of a moment when you felt physically distant from Allah—perhaps during travel or in a crowded place. Does this lesson challenge the idea that distance is spatial? How can you cultivate closeness to a Being who is not confined to space? What practical steps can you take today to turn your heart toward Him without any thought of location?
?
The proof shows that spatiality implies contingency. Now examine your own relationship with places you consider 'holy' or 'spiritual' (e.g., the mosque, a prayer room). Are you tempted to think that Allah is more present there? How does this proof correct that notion? What would it mean for you to seek Allah's presence in every place and no place at once?

First Proof: Corporeality Entails Origination
First proof against corporeality: originatedness of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that every body is originated (hadith) implies that God cannot be a body.

Core Idea
A body cannot be eternal; hence God is not a body.

The first argument against attributing a body to God relies on a basic premise: every body is originated (hadith). Since God is eternal, He cannot be a body. This lesson unpacks that concise proof and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic together with its translation. Notice the concise style of al-Sanusi's proof.
2
Understand the key term 'ḥādith'
The term 'ḥādith' means originated (temporally created). In kalam, this is opposed to eternal (qadim).
3
Analyze the logical structure
The proof is a straightforward syllogism: (1) Every body is originated. (2) God is not originated (He is eternal). Therefore, God cannot be a body.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is foundational in Islamic theology for establishing tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نفي الجسمية وجوه : الأول : أن كل جسم حادث لما سبق . الثاني : أن كل جسم متميز بالضرورة . والواجب ليس كذلك لما سيأتي .
And concerning the negation of corporeality (jismiyya) there are aspects: The first: that every body is originated (hadith) as previously [established]. The second: that every body is necessarily distinguished (mutamayyiz). And the Necessary [Being] is not like that, as will come.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage introduces the first two arguments (wujuh) for denying that God is a body (jism). The first proof appeals to the established premise that every body is hadith (originated), while God is eternal. The second proof states that every body is necessarily mutamayyiz (spatially distinct) because bodies occupy space and have boundaries; the Necessary Being is not like that, as later arguments will show. These are concise pointers that are expanded elsewhere.

Key Terms
jism (body)
ḥādith (originated)
jismiyya (corporeality)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first premise used to deny that God is a body?
A) Every body is composed of parts
B) Every body is originated
C) Every body is perceptible
D) Every body is changeable

Key Takeaway
The first proof against corporeality: since every body is originated, the eternal God cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that every body is originated. Your own body will perish, but you worship the Eternal, who has no body. Does your daily routine reflect that your ultimate attachment is to the Eternal or to the temporary? What specific action can you take today to shift your focus?
?
If God were a body, He would be limited like you. Yet the proof shows He is beyond any form. When you pray, do you unconsciously imagine God in a shape? How can you train yourself to direct your worship to the Transcendent, without any mental image? Commit to a moment of silent awe after each prayer this week.

Second Proof: Corporeality Entails Spatial Distinction
Second proof against corporeality: spatial distinction of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that every body is spatially distinguished (mutamayyiz) implies that God cannot be a body.

Core Idea
A body necessarily occupies space; the Necessary Being does not.

The second argument against corporeality focuses on the necessary property of bodies: they are spatially distinguished (mutamayyiz), meaning they occupy space and have boundaries. Since the Necessary Being transcends spatial limitations, He cannot be a body. This lesson explains this crucial concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic and translation. Note the use of 'mutamayyiz'.
2
Understand 'mutamayyiz'
In this context, 'mutamayyiz' means spatially distinct — occupying a specific location and being separate from other things. It implies having dimensions and boundaries.
Some might mistakenly think it means 'distinguished' in a qualitative sense.
3
Connect to the Necessary Being
The Necessary Being (al-Wajib) cannot be spatially restricted because that would imply dependence on space and change. The author promises later arguments to prove this.
4
Compare with the first proof
Both proofs rely on essential properties of bodies (origination and spatial distinction) to show that God is beyond corporeality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And concerning the negation of corporeality (jismiyya) there are aspects: The first: that every body is originated (hadith) as previously [established]. The second: that every body is necessarily distinguished (mutamayyiz). And the Necessary [Being] is not like that, as will come.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage introduces the first two arguments (wujuh) for denying that God is a body (jism). The first proof appeals to the established premise that every body is hadith (originated), while God is eternal. The second proof states that every body is necessarily mutamayyiz (spatially distinct) because bodies occupy space and have boundaries; the Necessary Being is not like that, as later arguments will show. These are concise pointers that are expanded elsewhere.

Key Terms
jism (body)
mutamayyiz (spatially distinguished)
wājib (Necessary Being)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'mutamayyiz' means distinguished in excellence or essence rather than spatial distinction.
Correction: In this context, 'mutamayyiz' refers specifically to spatial differentiation (tamayyuz bi al-makan), as bodies occupy space and have boundaries.

The Third Proof Against Corporeality: A Trilemma and Its Defense
Third disjunctive proof with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the trilemma argument against God being a body and how the objection regarding divine attributes is answered.

Core Idea
God cannot be a body: three impossible options all lead to contradiction.

Sanusi presents a third, more refined proof against corporeality. It is a disjunctive argument that considers three possibilities if God were a body, each leading to an impossibility. He then anticipates an objection that might undermine the proof and provides a clear distinction between divine attributes and bodily opposites.

Guided Path
1
Understand the trilemma structure
Recognize that the argument considers three and only three possibilities if God were a body: having all bodily attributes, none, or some.
This is a classic disjunctive syllogism.
2
Examine the first horn (all attributes)
If God had all bodily attributes, opposites like motion and rest would coexist, which is impossible.
3
Examine the second horn (no attributes)
If God had no bodily attributes, some necessary concomitants would be negated, but a body cannot lack both opposites simultaneously.
E.g., a body must be either moving or at rest.
4
Examine the third horn (some attributes)
If God had some but not all bodily properties, a specifier would be needed; without it, preponderance without cause arises.
Both options are impossible for the Necessary Being.
5
Review the objection and answer
The objection claims the same reasoning could be used to deny God's actual attributes. The answer distinguishes essential perfections from contingent opposites.
Divine attributes are inherent perfections; their opposites are deficiencies God is free from.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن الواجب لو كان جسماً. فإما أن يتصف بجميع صفات الأجسام. فيلزم اجتماع الضدين كالحركة والسكون ونحوهما ، وإما ألا يتصف بشيء فيلزم انتفاء بعض لوازم الجسم ، مع أن الضدين قد يكونان بحيث يمتنع خلو الجسم عنهما ، وإما أن يتصف بالبعض دون البعض فيلزم احتياج الواجب في صفاته إن كان ذلك المخصص ويلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح ، إن كان لا لمخصص. لا يقال هذا وارد في اتصاف الواجب بصفاته دون أضدادها لأنا نقول : صفاته صفات كمال يتصف بها لذاته، وأضدادها صفات نقص تنزه عنها لذاته بخلاف الأضداد المتواردة على الأجسام، فإنها قد تكون متساوية الأقدام
The third: that if the Necessary [Being] were a body, then either He would be characterized by all the attributes of bodies, in which case the conjunction of opposites would follow, such as motion and rest and the like; or He would not be characterized by any of them, in which case the negation of some necessary concomitants of body would follow, even though opposites may be such that the body cannot be free of both of them; or He would be characterized by some but not others, in which case the Necessary would be in need of a specifier for His attributes if there is that specifier, and preponderance without a preponderator would follow if there is no specifier. It cannot be said: this objection applies to the characterization of the Necessary by His attributes without their opposites, for we say: His attributes are attributes of perfection with which He is characterized by His essence, and their opposites are attributes of deficiency from which He is exalted by His essence, unlike the opposites that occur to bodies, for they may be equal in standing.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This is the third proof against corporeality, structured as a trilemma. The author considers three possibilities if God were a body: (1) He would have all bodily properties, leading to the impossible conjunction of opposites (e.g., motion and rest simultaneously). (2) He would have none, but then some necessary concomitants of body (like being in a place) would be negated, yet bodies cannot lack both members of an opposite pair (e.g., a body must be either moving or at rest). (3) He would have some bodily properties but not others, which requires a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) for which properties He has, leading to either need (if the specifier exists) or preponderance without cause (if no specifier). The objection anticipates that this reasoning might also apply to God having attributes like knowledge but not ignorance, but the answer distinguishes: God's attributes are perfections inherent in His essence, while their opposites are deficiencies He is essentially free from, unlike bodily opposites which are equally applicable and require external specification.

Key Terms
ḍidd (opposite/antonym)
lāzima (necessary concomitant)
mukhaṣṣiṣ (specifier)
tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ (preponderance without preponderator)
ṣifāt kamāl (attributes of perfection)
ṣifāt nuqṣ (attributes of deficiency)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The same argument could be used to deny that God has attributes like knowledge (since they have opposites like ignorance).
Correction: God's attributes are perfections inherent in His essence; their opposites are deficiencies He is necessarily free from. This differs from bodily opposites, which are equally possible and require external specification.

Checkpoint
What logical problem arises in the third proof if God had some bodily attributes but not others?
A) Conjunction of opposites
B) Negation of all attributes
C) Need for a specifier or preponderance without cause
D) Equality of opposites

Key Takeaway
The trilemma shows that corporeality leads to logical absurdity, and the distinction between essential perfections and contingent opposites preserves God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument shows that God's attributes are essential perfections, not contingent like ours. Is there an attribute of God (e.g., His knowledge or power) that you have treated as something He could 'lose' or 'gain'? How will you realign your belief to see them as essential?
?
If God were subject to bodily limitations, He would need a specifier—but He is the Self-Sufficient. Have you ever acted as if God needed something from you, as if you could 'specify' His actions? What does this proof demand you change in your view of your own role before God?

Fourth Proof Against Corporeality: Finitude and Shape
Author presents fourth proof: if God were a body, He would be finite and have a shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth kalam argument that if God were a body, He would necessarily be finite and have a shape, leading to an impossible outcome, thus negating divine corporeality.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would have a shape—and a shape for the Necessary Being is impossible.

This lesson presents the fourth proof in the series against corporeality. The argument builds on the established finitude of all bodies: if God were a body, He would be bounded and have a distinct shape. The author then shows that any possible consequence of having a shape leads to an absurdity, thereby affirming God's transcendence beyond physical form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it deeply. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the two key implications of shape
The passage defines shape and then presents a dilemma. Identify the three possibilities: all shapes, no shape, or specific shape.
3
Trace the argument's logical branch
Follow the reasoning: if all shapes → impossible; if none → preponderance without preponderator; if specific → need for a specifier. All are impossible for God.
This step is crucial for understanding the proof's structure.
4
Reflect on why this proves God is not a body
Consider that the Necessary Being cannot have any of these impossible attributes. Therefore, He cannot be a body.
Connect to the broader theme of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لو كان جسماً لكان متناهياً لما مرَّ من تناهي الأبعاد، فيكون شكلاً، لأن الشكل عبارة عن هيئة إحاطة النهاية بالجسم ، وحينئذ. إما أن يكون الأشكال على جميع أو لا لمخصص فيلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح . وهو محال ، أو على البعض دون البعض لمخصص فيلزم الاحتياج
The fourth: that if He were a body, He would be finite, as has passed of the finitude of dimensions, and He would thus be a shape (shakl), because shape is the form of the enclosure of the boundary around the body. Then, either He would have all shapes or none without a specifier, in which case preponderance without a preponderator follows, and that is impossible; or He would have some but not others due to a specifier, in which case need follows.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
The fourth proof argues from the finitude of bodies: since every body has finite dimensions, it has a shape (shakl), defined as the enclosure of boundaries. If God were a body, He would have a shape. But then two possibilities: either He would have all shapes (impossible, as one body cannot have all shapes) or none (then a specific shape would arise without cause, which is preponderance without a preponderator), or He would have a specific shape due to a specifier (implying need). All options are impossible for the Necessary Being.

Key Terms
mutanāhi (finite)
shakl (shape)
nīhāya (boundary)
hay’a iḥāṭa (form of enclosure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'shakl' (shape) in this argument might be thought to mean form in a general sense, such as a Platonic Form.
Correction: In this context, 'shakl' specifically means a geometric shape or figure resulting from finite boundaries, not an abstract Form.

Checkpoint

A) Because all bodies are composed of elements
B) Because the definition of shape is the enclosure of a body's boundaries
C) Because shape is necessary for perception
D) Because bodies are divisible

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that attributing a body to God leads to the impossibility of shape, thus affirming His incorporeality.

First Two Proofs Against Place and Direction
Author presents first two proofs for negating place and direction from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs from kalam that demonstrate God's transcendence beyond occupying place or having direction.

Core Idea
God cannot be contained by space; place and direction are attributes of contingent things.

In this passage, the author presents two powerful proofs that establish God's transcendence over spatial categories. These proofs rely on the impossibility of an eternal place and the independence of a location from God. By examining these arguments, we deepen our understanding of why God is not subject to location or direction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the logical structure of each proof.
2
Identify the two proofs
Locate the first proof (if occupying place, place eternal) and the second proof (if in location, then need and contingency).
3
Examine the key ideas
Understand the concepts of mutahayyiz, makan, and the reasoning behind each proof.
Pay attention to the correction about 'al-makan' (the occupant) in the second proof.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these proofs uphold God's necessity and uniqueness.
Note the rejection of place as eternal or independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفى نفي الحيز والجهة وجوه . الأول : أنه لو كان الواجب متحيزاً للزم قدم الحيز ضرورة امتناع المتحيز بدون الحيز واللازم باطل لما مر من حدوث ما سوى الواجب وصفاته. الثاني : أنه لو كان في مكان لكان محتاجاً إليه ضرورة. والمحتاج إلى الغير ممكن فيلزم إمكان الواجب. ولكان المكان مستغنياً عنه لإمكان الخلاء والمستغني عن الواجب يكون مستغنياً عما سواه بطريق الأولى فيكون واجباً والمفروض أن الواجب هو المتمكن لا المكان ومعنى الوجهين على أن الحيز موجود لا متوهم.
And concerning the negation of place (hayyiz) and direction (jiha) there are aspects: The first: that if the Necessary were occupying place (mutahayyiz), then the place would necessarily be eternal, due to the impossibility of an occupant without place, and the consequent is false as previously [established] concerning the origination of everything other than the Necessary and His attributes. The second: that if He were in a location (makan), He would necessarily be in need of it. And that which is in need of another is possible (mumkin), so the possibility (imkan) of the Necessary would follow. And the location would be independent of Him due to the possibility of emptiness (khala'), and that which is independent of the Necessary would be independent of everything else a fortiori, thus it would be necessary (wajib), but the presupposed is that the Necessary is the occupant, not the location. And the meaning of the two aspects is that place is actual, not imaginary.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage gives the first two proofs that God does not occupy a place (hayyiz) or have a direction (jiha). The first proof assumes God is mutahayyiz (occupying space), which implies place is eternal, since an occupant cannot exist without place. However, everything other than God is originated (as previously argued), so place cannot be eternal; thus God cannot be mutahayyiz. The second proof argues from the concept of being in a location (makan): if God were in a location, He would need it, and need implies contingency (imkan), contradicting His necessity. Moreover, the location itself would be independent of God because emptiness (khala') is possible, meaning the location can exist without God. If independent of God, it would be independent of all else, making it necessary (wajib), but only God is necessary. The passage concludes that place is real (existing), not merely imaginary.

Key Terms
ḥayyiz
jiha
mutahayyiz
makan
imkān
khala‘
mumkin
wājib

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second proof relies on the impossibility of place (makan) rather than the contingency (imkan) of God.
Correction: The second proof actually argues from the independence of the location (makan) due to possible emptiness, not from God's contingency. The correct reading is 'al-makan' (the occupant) not 'al-imkan' (possibility).

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the first proof against God occupying place?
A) A place must be eternal if an occupant is eternal
B) An occupant cannot exist without place
C) Place is originated
D) God is not an occupant

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence over space is proven by the impossibility of an eternal place and the independence of a location from a necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God is not contained by any place, then your own existence is not a container for Him either. Where is He relative to your heart? Is your worship directed to a Being beyond all spatial concepts, or do you still imagine Him in a direction when you pray?

The proof says that need for location implies contingency. You depend on space and time every moment. How does your daily dependency on a physical location (home, work, mosque) shape your awareness of your own contingency before the Self-Sufficient?

The passage affirms that place is actual, not imaginary. But if God is not in that place, then nothing in creation can hold Him. Does this truth make you feel more awe—or more distant? And if more distant, what changes must you make to feel His closeness without confining Him?

The Third Proof Against Place and Direction
Author presents third disjunctive proof against divine location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third disjunctive proof that God cannot be in a place or direction, based on the impossibility of interpenetration, mixing, need, and preponderance without a preponderator.

Core Idea
God cannot be in any place; both options lead to absurdity.

The author continues the series of proofs establishing divine transcendence beyond space. This third argument uses a logical disjunction: if God were in a place, either He would occupy all places or only some. Both alternatives are rationally impossible. By working through these two branches, the proof decisively shows that attributing physical location to God is incoherent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the disjunction
Read the passage and identify the two possibilities presented: either God is in all places and directions, or He is in some but not others. These two options exhaust the logical possibilities.
Disjunctive proofs are common in kalam; they show that all options lead to contradiction.
2
Analyze the first branch
Consider the consequence if God were in all places. The text mentions interpenetration of occupants (tadākhul al-mutahayyizāt) and mixing with filth. Reflect on why these are impossible for God.
Interpenetration means multiple bodies occupying the same space, which is physically impossible.
3
Analyze the second branch
If God is in some places but not others, two sub-options arise: either there is a specifier for that place (implying need, iḥtiyāj) or no specifier (implying preponderance without cause, tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ). Both are impossible for the Necessary Being.
The concept of preponderance without a preponderator violates the principle of sufficient reason.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Since both branches lead to impossibility, the original assumption that God is in a place or direction must be false. This confirms God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان الواجب في حيز وجهة . فإما أن يكون في جميع الأحياز والجهات فيلزم تداخل المتحيزات ومخالطة الواجب بما لا ينبغي كالقاذورات. وإما أن يكون في البعض دون البعض، فإن كان المخصص لزم الاحتياج، وإلا لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح .
The third: if the Necessary were in a place and direction, then either He would be in all places and directions, in which case the interpenetration of occupants and the mixing of the Necessary with what is not appropriate, like filth, would follow; or He would be in some without others, and if there is a specifier, need would follow, otherwise preponderance without a preponderator would follow.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
The third proof against place and direction is a disjunction. If God existed in a physical place and direction, two possibilities arise. First, He could be in all places and directions simultaneously. This would entail tadakhul al-mutahayyizat (interpenetration of occupants), meaning multiple bodies would occupy the same space, which is impossible. It would also imply mukhalata (mixing) with inappropriate things like filth, which is unworthy of God. Second, He could be in some places but not others. In this case, either there is a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) determining which place, which implies need (ihtiyaj) in God, or no specifier, which would be preponderance without a preponderator (tarjih bila murajjih), also impossible. Thus, God cannot be in any place or direction.

Key Terms
tadākhul (interpenetration)
mukhālata (mixing)
qādurāt (filth)
iḥtiyāj (need)
tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ (preponderance without preponderator)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'interpenetration' means logical overlap, not physical co-occupation of space.
Correction: In kalam, tadākhul al-mutahayyizāt refers to two or more bodies occupying the same spatial location, which is physically impossible.

Checkpoint
What would follow if God were in some places but not others without a specifier?
A) Interpenetration
B) Mixing with filth
C) Preponderance without cause
D) Need

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses a disjunction to show that God's presence in any place leads to impossible consequences, reinforcing His absolute transcendence.

The Second Proof: God Cannot Be in a Place
Second proof that God cannot occupy a place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that God cannot be in a place, based on the absurd consequences of contingency and necessity.

Core Idea
If God were in a place, both God and that place would become impossible contradictions.

Sanusi now presents the second argument against God occupying a place. This proof is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming God is in a place leads to two impossible consequences. First, God would become contingent (dependent on that place). Second, the place would become necessary (independent of God). Both outcomes contradict the nature of the Necessary Being. This passage sharpens our understanding of divine transcendence by showing that even thinking of God spatially is rationally impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get the overall argument flow before analysis.
Notice the dense logical style of Sanusi.
2
Understand the reductio structure
Recognize that this proof assumes the opposite (God in a place) and derives contradictions. The argument has two branches, each ending in an absurdity.
Reductio arguments are common in kalam.
3
Identify the two contradictory consequences
First, God would need the place, making Him contingent. Second, the place would be independent of God, making it necessary. Both contradict the oneness and necessity of God.
These two aspects are explicitly mentioned in the passage.
4
Reflect on the final clarification
Note the author's remark that the argument assumes space (hayyiz) is real, not imaginary. This preempts a possible objection that space might be merely conceptual.
This shows the argument's grounding in realist ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه لو كان في مكان لكان محتاجاً إليه ضرورة. والمحتاج إلى الغير ممكن فيلزم إمكان الواجب. ولكان المكان مستغنياً عنه لإمكان الخلاء والمستغني عن الواجب يكون مستغنياً عما سواه بطريق الأولى فيكون واجباً والمفروض أن الواجب هو المتمكن لا الإمكان ومعنى الوجهين على أن الحيز موجود لا متوهم .
The second: If He were in a place, He would necessarily be in need of it. The one in need of something else is possible, so the possibility of the Necessary (Being) would follow. And the place would be independent of Him because of the possibility of emptiness, and the one independent of the Necessary (Being) is independent of everything else a fortiori, so it would be necessary, and it is assumed that the Necessary is the occupant not the possibility. And the meaning of the two aspects is that space is existent, not imaginary.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof that God (the Necessary Being) cannot be in a place. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum. First, if God were in a place, He would be dependent on that place, and that need would entail contingency (imkan), contradicting His necessity (wujub). Second, from the same assumption, the place would be independent of God (since it's conceivable without Him), and by the principle that whatever is independent of the Necessary is ultimately necessary, the place would become necessary—but the premise is that the Necessary is the one in the place, not the place itself. The author concludes by clarifying that these two aspects (the contingency of God and the necessity of the place) assume that space (hayyiz) is real and not merely imaginary.

Key Terms
مكان
محتاج
إمكان
واجب
متمكن
خلاء
حيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'need' in the argument is metaphorical or that dependence on a place does not imply contingency if the place is essential to existence.
Correction: In kalam, any external dependence—even on a location—makes a being contingent, because the Necessary Being is absolutely self-sufficient. The argument treats the need as real and literal.

Checkpoint

D) That neither God nor the place would be necessary.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that the assumption of God being in a place leads to both God's contingency and the place's necessity, which are impossible given God's unique necessity.

The Third Proof: God's Transcendence of Space
Third proof: dichotomy eliminates spatial location for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument against God being located in a specific direction, based on the impossibility of either universal or partial location.

Core Idea
God's transcendence of space is proven by a logical dichotomy that leads to absurdities.

This passage presents a crisp argument from contradiction: assuming God occupies a direction leads to two equally impossible outcomes. The author uses a standard kalam technique of dividing possibilities and eliminating each, showing that the assumption is rationally untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the argument's structure emerge naturally.
Notice the compact style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the two horns
The argument splits the assumption into two possibilities: God in all directions, or God in only some directions. Mark these in the text.
3
Understand the refutation of 'all directions'
If God were in all directions, overlap of extended things and contact with impurities would follow, which is impossible for God.
This uses the principle that God cannot be in impure places.
4
Understand the refutation of 'some directions'
If God is only in some directions, either there is a specific (causing dependence) or no specific (causing unjustified preference). Both are impossible.
The notion of preponderance without a preponderator is a key kalam principle.
5
Draw the conclusion
Since both possibilities lead to absurdity, the original assumption is false. God is not in any direction.
This establishes divine transcendence of space.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو كان الواجب في حيز وجهة . فإما أن يكون في جميع الأحياز والجهات فيلزم تداخل المتحيزات ومخالطة الواجب بما لا ينبغي كالقاذورات. وإما أن يكون في البعض دون البعض، فإن كان المخصص لزم الاحتياج، وإلا لزم الترجيح بلا مرجح .
The third: If the Necessary (Being) were in a region and direction, then either He would be in all regions and directions, which would entail overlapping of extended things and mixing of the Necessary with what is improper, like filth; or He would be in some (regions and directions) without others, and if there were a specific, then need would follow, and if not, then preponderance without a preponderator would follow.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument against God being located. It begins by assuming the Necessary Being (al-wajib) is in a specific region (hayyiz) and direction (jiha). The author then splits this assumption into two mutually exclusive possibilities: (1) He is in all regions and directions, or (2) He is in some but not others. The first option is rejected because it would place God in every place, including impure ones (kadhurat), and would cause overlapping of extended entities (tadakhul al-mutahayyizat), which is incompatible with divine majesty. The second option also fails because if there is a specific (mukhasis) that selects a particular location, then God would be dependent on that selector (ihtiyaj), entailing contingency; if there is no specific, then the choice of one location over another would be an unjustified preponderance (tarjih bila murajjih), which is rationally impossible. Thus, the assumption that God is spatially located leads to absurdity, proving His transcendence above place and direction.

Key Terms
حيز
جهة
متخصص
ترجیح
مرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could be in a good direction like above (al-‘uluw) or in a specific honorable place (like the Throne).
Correction: The argument shows that any location—whether high, low, east, west—is impossible for God, because either it implies He is in all places (including impure ones) or the choice of a particular place requires a cause, leading to dependence or an unjustified preference.

Checkpoint
What logical problem arises if God is in only some directions without a specific?
A) It would imply God needs something to prefer that direction.
B) It would mean God is in all directions.
C) There would be no problem; it is possible.
D) It would make God dependent on that direction.

Key Takeaway
God cannot be spatially located because assuming otherwise leads to logical impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you bow in sujood, do you unconsciously feel that you are approaching a physical direction? How does this logical proof challenge that instinct? Will you now train your heart to worship without any spatial imagination of God?
?
If God is not located anywhere, then He is equally near to every person without being contained. Does this truth make you feel a greater sense of accountability, knowing that you cannot hide from Him in any corner? What is one habit you will change because you are always in His presence, not just when you face a direction?

Prohibition of Applying 'Body' and 'Substance' to God
Identifies first opponent group and gives ruling plus philosophical nuance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author prohibits using the terms 'body' (jism) and 'substance' (jawhar) for God, even when they are reinterpreted to mean 'existent' or 'self-subsistent', and why this prohibition is both legal and precautionary.

Core Idea
Calling God 'body' or 'substance' is prohibited, even if redefined, to protect divine transcendence.

The author begins by identifying a group of opponents who, while not explicitly anthropomorphizing, still use terms like 'body' and 'substance' for God, reinterpreting them to mean simply 'existent' or 'self-subsistent'. This lesson examines the author's ruling that such usage is prohibited both by religious law and as a precaution, and explores the nuanced discussion of 'substance' among philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first group of opponents
Read the passage and locate the description of those who apply 'body' as 'existent' and 'substance' as 'self-subsistent'. Understand their reinterpretation.
2
Understand the author's ruling
The author declares the correct view as 'prohibition legally and as a precaution'. Reflect on the double emphasis: shar'an (legal) and ihtiyatan (precautionary).
The precautionary aspect suggests avoiding even potential confusion.
3
Examine the philosophical usage of 'jawhar'
The author notes that 'substance' meaning 'self-subsistent essence' is common among philosophers, leading even Ibn Karram to call God 'one in substance'. Yet the author still advises against it.
This shows the author's strictness in preserving tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخالفون في تنزيه الله تعالى قال : وأما المخالفون : فمنهم أطلق الجسم بمعنى الموجود والجوهر بمعنى القائم بنفسه، والحق المنع شرعاً واحتياطاً ومنهم المجسمة : القائلون بأنه جسم على صورة شاب أمرد أو شيخ أشمط أو شبيكة بيضاء تتلألأ . والمشبهة : القائلون : بأنه في جهة العلو وفوق العرش تماساً له أو محاذياً ببعد متناه أو غير متناه متمسكين بأن كل موجود جسم أو جسماني أو متحيز أو حال فيه ومتصل بالعالم أو منفصل وداخل العالم أو خارجه، وبظواهر النصوص المشعرة بالجهة والجسمية والجواب ظاهراً . أما المخالفون إجراء الجسم مجرى الموجود فمخالف للعرف واللغة. ولما اشتهر من الاصطلاحات، لكن إطلاق الجوهر بمعنى الموجود القائم بنفسه، الذات والحقيقة اصطلاح شائع فيما بين الحكماء. وبمعنى فمنها هنا يقع في كلام بعضهم إطلاق لفظ الجوهر على الواجب. وفي كلام ابن كرام أن الله احدى الذات احدى الجوهر، ومع هذا فلا ينبغي
The opponents concerning the transcendence of God Almighty. He said: As for the opponents: among them are those who apply 'body' in the sense of existent and 'substance' in the sense of self-subsisting; the correct view is prohibition legally and as a precaution. And among them are the Mujassima: those who say He is a body in the form of a beardless youth, or a grey-haired old man, or a white sparkling speck. And the Mushabbiha: those who say He is in the direction of above and above the Throne, in contact with it or parallel to it at a finite or infinite distance, holding that every existent is a body or corporeal or extended or inhering, and connected to the world or separate, inside the world or outside it, and (they cling to) the apparent meanings of texts that suggest direction and corporeality—and the answer is apparent. As for those opponents who treat 'body' as equivalent to 'existent', that contradicts custom and language and the well-known technical terms. However, applying 'substance' in the sense of the existent self-subsistent, essence and reality, is a common technical term among the philosophers. And by this meaning, some of them occasionally apply the word 'substance' to the Necessary (Being). And in the speech of Ibn Karram: that God is one in essence, one in substance—yet even this should not be done.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage classifies and refutes groups that violate the principle of divine transcendence (tanzih). The author lists three categories of opponents: First, those who loosely apply the terms 'body' (jism) and 'substance' (jawhar) to God, using them metaphorically to mean 'existent' or 'self-subsistent'. The author rules that this is prohibited both legally (shar'an) and as a precaution (ihtiyatan). Second, the Mujassima (anthropomorphists) who explicitly say God is a body in a specific human-like form. Third, the Mushabbiha (comparisonists) who assert that God is physically located above the Throne, either in contact or at a distance, basing their view on apparent meanings of scriptural texts. The author dismisses their position as obviously false (al-jawabu zahiran). Then he elaborates on the first group: treating 'body' as synonymous with 'existent' violates linguistic custom and technical terminology. However, he notes that using 'substance' (jawhar) to mean 'self-subsistent essence' is actually common among philosophers (hukama). Thus, some philosophers apply 'substance' to the Necessary Being. He also mentions Ibn Karram, who stated that God is one in essence and one in substance (i.e., a single substance). Even so, the author cautions that such usage should still be avoided (fa-la yanbaghi), implying that even metaphorically permitted terms may lead to confusion.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
قائم بنفسه
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that redefining 'body' and 'substance' to mean 'existent' and 'self-subsistent' makes them acceptable descriptions of God.
Correction: The author prohibits this even as a precaution, because these terms carry corporeal connotations that compromise divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the author's ruling on those who call God 'jawhar' (substance) meaning self-subsistent?
A) It is allowed because it is a common philosophical term.
B) It is prohibited absolutely.
C) It is prohibited legally and as a precaution.
D) It is permissible only if one clarifies the meaning.

Key Takeaway
Even reinterpreting 'body' and 'substance' to mean 'existent' and 'self-subsistent' does not make them safe to use for God; the author prohibits them legally and precautionarily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever casually described God using a word from everyday speech, thinking it harmless? How does this passage's warning about even reinterpreted terms challenge your willingness to guard your tongue about the Divine?
?
When you hear a respected scholar use a phrase about God that seems risky, do you instinctively follow them or do you pause to check if it aligns with the precautionary approach taught here?
?
What words or concepts about God do you use in private prayer or reflection that might be subtly influenced by human categories? Will you review them after reading this lesson, even if only for the sake of ihtiyat?

Refutation of Anthropomorphists: Mujassima and Mushabbiha
Describes and refutes explicit anthropomorphist sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific beliefs of the Mujassima (anthropomorphists) and the Mushabbiha (comparisonists) and why the author dismisses their claims as obviously false.

Core Idea
Denying God's transcendence by assigning bodily form or spatial location is self-refuting.

Having addressed those who misuse terms, the author now turns to groups that explicitly describe God with bodily attributes or spatial location. The Mujassima imagine God as a human-like body, while the Mushabbiha insist He is physically above the Throne, citing scriptural texts literally. This lesson presents their views and the author's brief but decisive refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the descriptions of Mujassima and Mushabbiha
Locate the two groups in the passage. Note the specific characteristics attributed to God by each: for the Mujassima, a body like a young man, old man, or a sparkling speck; for the Mushabbiha, location above the Throne with contact or distance.
2
Understand the reasoning of the Mushabbiha
The Mushabbiha base their view on the apparent meanings of scriptural texts that suggest direction and body. They also argue that every existent must be a body or extended.
The author considers this a flawed inference from created things.
3
Note the author's dismissal
The author says 'the answer is apparent' (al-jawabu zahiran), meaning the refutation is obvious from the principle of divine transcendence.

Scriptural Proofs of Anthropomorphists
Author presents scriptural proof texts used by those who affirm divine corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how anthropomorphists use Quranic verses and hadiths to argue for divine corporeality, and recognize that the author is presenting their case before refuting it.

Core Idea
Apparent scriptural literalism is the foundation of anthropomorphism, not orthodoxy.

In this passage, the author enters the discussion of the second category of anthropomorphist arguments: scriptural proofs. He lists several Quranic verses and hadiths that seem to describe God in spatial or bodily terms. It is crucial to note that the author himself does not endorse these literal interpretations; he is laying out the evidence that his opponents rely on, which he will later refute using figurative interpretation (ta'wil) or consignment of meaning (tafwid). As you read, observe how these texts, taken at face value, could lead to a corporeal conception of God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the passage
Notice that the passage begins with 'As for the second' and then presents Quranic verses followed by hadiths. Mark the transition.
The phrase 'As for the second' indicates this is part of a larger argument.
2
List the Quranic verses cited
Read through the verses: 'And your Lord comes', 'Do they await except that God should come to them?', 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne', etc. Note which attributes they suggest (hand, face, throne, etc.).
3
List the hadiths cited
Read the hadiths: the mute slave girl, God's descent, creation of Adam in God's image, the Foot in Hell, and God's laughter. Consider what actions or forms they attribute to God.
These hadiths are found in canonical collections but are interpreted figuratively by Sunni scholars.
4
Reflect on the author's purpose
Ask yourself: Why does the author list these texts without refuting them? The commentary explains that he is presenting the opponents' evidence before answering it.
The refutation follows in subsequent pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثاني : فلقوله تعالى : ( وجاء ربك ) هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم الله ( الرحمن على العرش استوى ) إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب ( ويبقى وجه ربك ) ( ويد الله فوق أيديهم ) ( ولتصنع على عيني ) ( خلقت بيدي ) ( والسموات مطويات بيمينه ) ( يا حسرتا على ما فرطت في جنب الله ) إلى غير ذلك وكقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام للجارية الخرساء: «أين الله فأشارت إلى السماء فلم ينكر عليها ، وحكم بإسلامها» . «إن الله تعالى ينزل إلى سماء الدنيا» الحديث. «إن الله خلق آدم على صورته» «إن الجبار يضع قدمه في النار» . «إنه يضحك إلى أوليائه حتى تبدو نواجذه» .
As for the second: such as His saying: 'And your Lord comes' (Q 89:22); 'Do they await except that God should come to them?' (Q 2:210); 'The Most Merciful established Himself upon the Throne' (Q 20:5); 'To Him ascends good speech' (Q 35:10); 'And there remains the Face of your Lord' (Q 55:27); 'And the hand of God is above their hands' (Q 48:10); 'And that you may be formed under My eye' (Q 20:39); 'I created with My hands' (Q 38:75); 'And the heavens are folded in His right hand' (Q 39:67); 'Alas for me over what I neglected in regard to God' (Q 39:56); and so on. And like his saying, peace be upon him, to the mute slave girl: 'Where is God?' She pointed to the sky, and he did not rebuke her and judged her to be a Muslim. [And his saying:] 'Indeed God descends to the heaven of the earth' — the hadith. 'Indeed God created Adam in His image.' 'Indeed the Compeller places His foot in the Fire.' 'Indeed He laughs at His saints until His molars appear.'

Commentary
This passage presents the second category of arguments used by the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima): scriptural proofs (al-thani). The author lists several Quranic verses that seem to attribute spatial location, body parts, or movement to God: e.g., 'Your Lord comes' (wa jā'a rabbuka), 'the Most Merciful established Himself on the Throne' (al-raḥmān ʿalā al-ʿarsh istawā), 'the hand of God' (yad Allāh), 'My hands' (yadayya), 'His right hand' (yamīnihi), 'the side of God' (janb Allāh). The author also cites hadiths: the story of the mute slave girl pointing to the sky, the hadith of God's descent to the lowest heaven, the creation of Adam in God's image, the placing of the Foot in Hell, and God's laughter at His saints. The author does not refute these texts here; he is simply stating the proofs that the anthropomorphists rely on. The listing serves as the objection that will be answered in subsequent discussion, likely by interpreting these texts figuratively (ta'wil) or consigning their meaning to God (tafwid).

Correction: The author is only presenting the opponents' evidence. The orthodox position interprets these texts in a way befitting God's transcendence, using figurative interpretation (ta'wil) or consignment of meaning (tafwid).

Scriptural Texts Suggesting Direction and the Rational Response
Lists anthropomorphic texts then gives two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash‘arī scholars resolve apparent anthropomorphic scriptural texts using two methods: tafwīḍ and ta’wīl.

Core Idea
Probable texts yield to certain reason; we either delegate meaning or interpret appropriately.

Muslim theologians encounter Quranic verses and hadiths that seem to attribute human-like qualities to Allah. This lesson presents the Ash‘arī approach to such texts. After listing several examples, the passage explains two mainstream responses: tafwīḍ (delegation) and ta’wīl (interpretation).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Observe the structure: first a list of texts, then the answer.
Notice the repeated phrase 'إلى غير ذلك' indicating more examples.
2
Identify the two responses
After the list, the passage presents 'الجواب' and then two methods: 'تفويض' (delegation) and 'تأويل' (interpretation). Underline these terms.
Each method is supported by a different reading of Quran 3:7.
3
Understand the key distinction
The passage calls the texts 'ظنيات سمعية' (probable transmitted matters) and contrasts with 'قطعيات عقلية' (certain rational proofs). Grasp why this hierarchy matters for tanzīh.
This distinction is foundational in Ash‘arī epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله الرحمن على العرش استوى إليه يصعد الكلم الطيب ويبقى وجه ربك ويد الله فوق أيديهم ولتصنع على عيني خلقت بيدي والسموات مطويات بيمينه يا حسرتا على ما فرطت في جنب الله إلى غير ذلك وكقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام للجارية الخرساء: «أين الله فأشارت إلى السماء فلم ينكر عليها، وحكم بإسلامها. «إن الله تعالى ينزل إلى سماء الدنيا» الحديث. إن الله خلق آدم على صورته إن الجبار يضع قدمه في النار. إنه يضحك إلى أوليائه حتى تبدو نواجذه. إن الصدقة تقع في كف الرحمن» إلى غير ذلك. والجواب: أنها ظنيات سمعية في معارضة قطعيات عقلية، فيقطع بأنها ليست على ظواهرها ويفوض العلم بمعانيها إلى الله تعالى اعتقاد حقيقيتها، جرياً على الطريق الأسلم الموافق للوقف على إلا الله في قوله تعالى: ﴿وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله﴾. أو تأول تأويلات مناسبة موافقة لما دلت عليه الأدلة العقلية على ما ذكر في كتب التفسير، وشروح الحديث سلوكاً للطريق الأحكم الموافق للعطف في إلا الله والراسخون في العلم
Allah the Merciful established Himself above the Throne; to Him ascends good speech; the Face of your Lord will remain; the Hand of Allah is above their hands; that you may be formed under My Eye; I created with My Hands; the heavens folded in His Right Hand; alas over what I neglected in regard to Allah; etc. And like his saying (peace be upon him) to the mute girl: 'Where is Allah?' So she pointed to the sky, and he did not disapprove of her, and judged her to be a Muslim. 'Indeed Allah descends to the heaven of the world' the hadith. 'Indeed Allah created Adam upon His image.' 'Indeed the Compeller will place His foot in the Fire.' 'Indeed He laughs at His friends until his molars appear.' 'Indeed charity falls into the Hand of the Rahman.' etc. The answer: They are probable (ẓanniyyāt) transmitted matters (sam‘iyya) in opposition to certain rational matters (qaṭ‘iyyāt ‘aqliyya), so it is judged that they are not upon their apparent meanings, and knowledge of their meanings is delegated (yufawwaḍ) to Allah, affirming their reality, following the safest path which agrees with stopping at 'except Allah' in His statement: 'And none knows its interpretation except Allah.' Or they are interpreted with appropriate interpretations consistent with what rational proofs indicate, as mentioned in books of exegesis and hadith commentaries, following the wiser path which agrees with the conjunction in 'except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge.'
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a response. First, it lists numerous Quranic verses and prophetic hadiths that, taken literally, seem to attribute spatial location, body parts, or direction to Allah. For example, verses about Allah's 'hand,' 'face,' 'eye,' and 'rising above the Throne,' and hadiths about Allah descending, creating Adam in His image, etc. These texts are cited by anthropomorphists to argue that Allah has physical attributes. Then comes the answer (al-jawāb): these texts are 'ẓanniyyāt sam‘iyya' – they are probable reports based on revelation, but they conflict with 'qaṭ‘iyyāt ‘aqliyya' – certain rational proofs (which establish that Allah is beyond space and direction). Since reason is certain, we know these texts cannot be literal. The response offers two mainstream Ash‘arī methods: (1) tafwīḍ – delegating the true meaning to Allah while affirming the reality of the attribute, following the verse 'And none knows its interpretation except Allah' (Q. 3:7) – this is the safer path. (2) ta’wīl – interpreting them in a way consistent with rational proofs, as done in tafsīr and hadith commentaries – this is the more judicious path, also supported by the reading of the verse that includes 'and those firmly rooted in knowledge.' The passage thus defends the transcendence of Allah (tanzīh) while upholding the authority of revelation.

Key Terms
ظنيات سمعية
قطعيات عقلية
تفويض
تأويل
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that tafwīḍ means we have no knowledge at all about the attributes, or that the Ash‘arī approach denies the reality of the attributes mentioned in scripture.
Correction: The passage clarifies that tafwīḍ involves affirming the reality of the attributes while delegating the modality (kayfiyya) to Allah. It is not denial; it is an acknowledgment that the true nature is known only to Allah, yet the attribute is real.

Checkpoint
What are the two methods mentioned to handle anthropomorphic scriptural texts?
A) Taṭṭīl and ta‘ṭīl
B) Tafwīḍ and ta’wīl
C) Tashbīh and tajsīm
D) Talāzum and taḥāfuẓ

Key Takeaway
When scripture apparently conflicts with certain rational proofs, the texts are handled either by delegating the meaning to Allah (tafwīḍ) or by interpreting them consistently with reason (ta’wīl), preserving divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'الرحمن على العرش استوى', do you ever catch yourself forming a mental image of Allah ‘sitting’? How does this lesson challenge you to guard your heart against such imaginations, even momentarily? What practical step will you take to train your mind to stop before forming images?
?
The passage offers two paths: tafwīḍ (surrender of meaning) and ta’wīl (interpretation). Which of these do you find more difficult to practice when you encounter a verse that unsettles you—and why? Be honest: does your discomfort reveal a deeper attachment to certainty or control over how Allah ‘must’ be?
?
You believe that Allah is beyond your senses, yet you often read His attributes without pausing to reaffirm tanzīh. Will you commit today to reciting 'subḥāna llāhi ‘ammā yasifūn' every time you encounter a potentially anthropomorphic verse, until it becomes a reflex that protects your reverence?

Why Scripture Uses Directional Language for God
Objection and answer about anthropomorphic expressions in scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the scriptures contain anthropomorphic expressions and how they reconcile with divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Scripture accommodates common understanding while hinting at God's true transcendence.

This passage presents a vital defense of Islamic scripture against a common objection: if God transcends direction, why do the Quran and hadith speak of Him as being 'above'? The author provides a theologically sophisticated answer that respects both the literal text and rational theology, explaining that such language is a pedagogical necessity for the common people while preserving deeper truths for the learned.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening lines of the passage where the objection is raised. Notice how it compares the lack of explicit denial of direction to the clear affirmations of God's existence and oneness.
The objection is from a hypothetical critic who expects consistency.
2
Identify the answer given
Find where the answer begins ('أجيب'). The author says that transcendence is beyond common minds, so scripture uses apparent anthropomorphism with subtle hints.
The answer has two parts: accommodation for common people and explanation of the prayer direction.
3
Understand the subtle hints
Look for the phrase 'تنبيهات دقيقة على التنزيه المطلق'. The scripture includes indirect references to absolute transcendence to guide the learned.
These hints prevent literal belief in a corporeal God.
4
Analyze the fiṭra argument
The author explains that raising hands to heaven is not because God is there, but because heaven is the qibla of prayer, the source of blessings.
This refutes the misconception that prayer direction implies location.
5
Reflect on the pedagogical principle
Consider how this balance between literal and rational understanding is a model for interpreting scripture in other contexts.
This shows the wisdom of Sharia in addressing all levels of intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا كان الدين الحق نفي الحيز والجهة فما بال الكتب السماوية والأحاديث النبوية مشعرة في مواضع لا تحصى بثبوت ذلك؟ من غير أن يقع في موضع منها تصريح بنقي ذلك، وتحقيق كما قررت الدلالة على وجود الصانع ووحدته وعلمه وقدرته، وحقية المعاد وحشر الأجساد في عدة مواضع، وأكدت غاية التأكيد، أن هذا أيضاً حقيق بغاية التأكيد، والتحقيق لما تقرر في فطرة العقلاء مع الأديان، والآراء من التوجه إلى العلو عند الدعاء ورفع الأيدي إلى السماء. أجيب : بأنه لما كان التنزيه عن الجهة مما تقصر عنه عقول العامة حتى يكاد يجزم بنفي ما ليس في الجهة كان الأنسب في خطاباتهم، والأقرب إلى اصطلاحهم، والأليق بدعوتهم إلى الحق ما يكون ظاهراً في التشبية وكون الصانع في أشرف الجهات مع تنبيهات دقيقة على التنزيه المطلق عما هو من سمات الحدوث، وتوجه العقلاء إلى السماء ليس من جهة اعتقادهم أنه في السماء، بل من جهة أن السماء قبلة الدعاء، إذ منها يتوقع الخيرات والبركات، وهبوط الأنوار ونزول الأمطار.
For it is said: If the true religion is the negation of place and direction, why are the heavenly books and prophetic traditions in countless places indicating the affirmation of that, without there occurring in any of them an explicit denial of its opposite, and confirmation just as you confirmed the indication of the existence of the Maker, His oneness, knowledge and power, and the reality of the resurrection and the gathering of bodies in several places, and they emphasized with utmost emphasis that this also deserves utmost emphasis and confirmation, given what is established in the innate nature (fiṭra) of rational people with religions and opinions of turning to highness at supplication and raising hands to heaven? The answer: because transcendence (tanzīh) from direction is something that the minds of the common people fall short of, to the point that they would almost be certain in negating something that is not in a direction, so the most appropriate in addressing them, the closest to their idiom, and the fittest for their invitation to truth was that which is apparent in anthropomorphism and that the Maker is in the noblest of directions, with subtle indications of absolute transcendence from what are marks of temporality (ḥudūth). And the turning of rational people to heaven is not from their belief that He is in heaven, but rather because heaven is the qibla of supplication, since from it are expected good things and blessings, the descent of lights and the falling of rain.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objection argues: if true religion demands the negation of place (ḥayyiz) and direction (jiha) from God, then why are the scriptures and hadiths filled with expressions that imply God has a direction (e.g., 'above', 'on the Throne'), without a single explicit statement denying that? Moreover, the objection notes that just as the scriptures emphasize proofs for God's existence, oneness, and resurrection, they should also have emphasized the denial of direction. Indeed, the innate disposition (fiṭra) of people leads them to look upward when praying and raise hands to heaven. The answer: God's transcendence (tanzīh) from direction is too subtle for the common people; if they were explicitly told that God is not in any direction, they might conclude He is nothing (non-existent). Therefore, the most appropriate way to address them is to use language that appears to affirm a direction (like 'above') while including subtle hints of absolute transcendence. The raising of hands to heaven is not because people believe God is located in heaven; rather, heaven is the qibla (direction) of supplication, because from it come blessings, rain, and divine lights. Thus, the common literal understanding is accommodated while preserving the rational truth for the learned.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That raising hands to heaven implies God is physically located in heaven.
Correction: The author clarifies that heaven is not God's location but the qibla of supplication, the source of blessings.

Key Takeaway
Scripture uses apparent anthropomorphism to accommodate common understanding while including subtle hints of God's absolute transcendence, and prayer direction to heaven is due to it being the qibla of supplication, not God's location.

The passage says the masses 'almost negate' what is not in a direction. Have you ever felt uneasy when you try to think of a non-spatial God? What does that discomfort reveal about your own need to deepen your tanzīh? Choose one hint from scripture that points to transcendence and sit with it until it touches your heart.
?
This lesson invites you to notice the 'subtle indications' embedded in apparently anthropomorphic verses. Are you someone who stops at the literal meaning, or do you search for the deeper pointers? Justifying Anthropomorphic Expressions in Scripture
Author defends anthropomorphic scripture as pedagogical adaptation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scripture uses anthropomorphic language and how it preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphic language adapts to human limits while preserving tanzih.

A common objection to scripture is that anthropomorphic verses seem to contradict God's transcendence. In this passage, the author provides a reasoned defense: such language is a pedagogical necessity for common people, who struggle with abstract transcendence. Subtle hints in the text, however, point to absolute tanzih. The author also clarifies that turning towards the sky in supplication is not because God is located there, but because the sky is the qibla of prayer, a source of blessings and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the structure: it is an answer to an implied objection about anthropomorphic expressions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main justification
Find the reason the author gives for anthropomorphic language. Why is it 'most appropriate' for the common people?
Look for the phrase 'لما كان التنزيه عن الجهة مما تقصر عنه عقول العامة'.
3
Examine the clarification about the qibla
The author addresses a potential misunderstanding: why do rational people turn towards the sky when praying? Read that part and understand the distinction.
Note the contrast between 'يعتقدون أنه في السماء' and 'قبلة الدعاء'.
4
Reflect on the balance between tashbih and tanzih
Consider how the author maintains both apparent anthropomorphism (tashbih) and absolute transcendence (tanzih). How do the 'subtle hints' function?
This balances two truths: the limits of human understanding and the reality of God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجيب : بأنه لما كان التنزيه عن الجهة مما تقصر عنه عقول العامة حتى يكاد يجزم بنفي ما ليس في الجهة كان الأنسب في خطاباتهم، والأقرب إلى اصطلاحهم، والأليق بدعوتهم إلى الحق ما يكون ظاهراً في التشبية وكون الصانع في أشرف الجهات مع تنبيهات دقيقة على التنزيه المطلق عما هو من سمات الحدوث، وتوجه العقلاء إلى السماء ليس من جهة اعتقادهم أنه في السماء، بل من جهة أن السماء قبلة الدعاء، إذ منها يتوقع الخيرات والبركات، وهبوط الأنوار ونزول الأمطار .

Therefore, the most effective approach in addressing them is to use language that appears anthropomorphic (tashbih), stating that the Creator is in the noblest of directions, while including subtle pointers to absolute transcendence from all marks of contingency (huduth). The author also clarifies that the turning of rational people towards the sky in supplication is not because they believe God is located there, but because the sky is the qibla of prayer, from which blessings and rains descend. This reconciles scriptural anthropomorphism with divine transcendence.

Critique of Excessive Transcendence: Calling God 'Thing'
Author critiques early scholars who avoided calling God 'thing'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some early scholars avoided calling God 'thing' and how the author refutes their claim that it implies similarity.

Core Idea
Shared names between God and creatures do not imply shared essence.

The principle of divine transcendence (tanzih) is crucial in Islamic theology, but some early scholars overextended it by refusing to apply terms like 'thing', 'knower', and 'powerful' to God, fearing it would imply similarity. This passage presents the author's critique of that position, clarifying that common names do not necessitate common essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the author's argument. Notice the key terms like تنزيه (transcendence) and مماثلة (similitude).
2
Identify the error of the early scholars
The early scholars avoided calling God 'thing' because they thought it would imply similarity. Understand their reasoning.
They were afraid that using the same name would make God like created things.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author says similarity only follows if the shared meaning is identical in both. Since it's not, the names are just homonyms.
This is a key principle: common names do not mean common essence.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this argument applies to other divine attributes like 'knower' and 'powerful'. This lesson guards against over-transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن بعض القدماء بالغوا في التنزيه حين امتنعوا عن إطلاق اسم الشيء بل العالم والقادر وغيرهما على الله تعالى زعماً منهم أنه يوجب إثبات المثل له، وليس ، إذ المماثلة إنما تلزم لو كان المعنى المشترك بينه وبين غيره فيهما على ذلك
And know that some of the early scholars went to extremes in transcendence when they refrained from applying the name 'thing', or rather 'knower', 'powerful', and others to God Almighty, claiming that it necessitates affirming similarity to Him, but it does not, for similarity only follows if the shared meaning between Him and others in them is [identical] in that way.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage critiques some early (qadama') scholars who overextended the principle of divine transcendence (tanzih) by avoiding the application of certain terms to God, such as 'thing' (shay'), 'knower' ('alim), 'powerful' (qadir), etc. They feared that using these terms would imply that God is similar (mithl) to created things. The author refutes this by stating that similitude (mumathala) only arises if the common meaning between God and His creatures is identical in both, which is not the case. The passage is incomplete but clearly argues that shared names do not entail shared essence.

Correction: The author clarifies that similarity only follows if the shared meaning is identical, which is not the case.

Key Takeaway
Shared names between God and creatures do not imply shared essence; over-transcendence can be as erroneous as anthropomorphism.
