God is Free from All Quantities and Qualities
Sanusi explains that God has no physical qualities and refutes the claim of intellectual pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being (Allah) cannot be described with any quantities or qualities, including the philosophers' claim of intellectual pleasure, and how this underscores divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God is beyond all physical and emotional attributes; even intellectual pleasure cannot be attributed without proof.

This lesson addresses a fundamental principle in Islamic theology: the absolute transcendence of God. Sanusi begins by stating that the Necessary Being has no quantities or qualities. He then lists examples of attributes that cannot apply to God. A key challenge comes from philosophers who claim that God experiences intellectual pleasure. The author refutes this by showing the argument is unproven and based on an analogy that may not hold for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the flow from assertion to examples to refutation.
Pay attention to the shift from discussing physical qualities to the more subtle issue of intellectual pleasure.
2
Understand the main claim
The central claim is that the Necessary Being (God) is not qualified by any quantities (like length, width) or qualities (like color, taste). This is a foundational principle of tawhid.
Quantities and qualities here refer to accidental attributes that imply corporeality.
3
Identify the denied attributes
The passage lists examples: length, width, form, color, taste, smell, joy, sorrow, anger, pleasure, pain. Note that even emotional states like joy and anger are denied.
These are all attributes associated with created, physical beings.
4
Examine the philosophers' claim
Philosophers argue that God experiences intellectual pleasure because He perceives His perfections and delights in them. This is a potential objection to God's transcendence.
The author takes this objection seriously and refutes it.
5
Follow the refutation
The refutation hinges on two interpretations: (1) if pleasure is the very perception of the agreeable, this is unproven; (2) if pleasure necessarily accompanies perception of the agreeable, it may only apply to humans, not God.
The key is that the analogy from human experience does not necessarily extend to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجب لا يتصف بالكميات ولا الكيفيات. قال تنبيه فلا يتصف بشيء من الكميات والكيفيات من الطول والعرض والصورة واللون والطعم والرائحة والفرح والغم والغضب واللذة والألم. وقول الحكماء باللذة العقلية لما أنه يدرك كمالاتها فيبتهج بها إنما يتم لو ثبت أن إدراك الملائم في الغائب لذة أو ملزوم لها كما في الشاهد. تنبيه لما ثبت أن الواجب ليس بجسم ظهر أنه لا يتصف بشيء من الكيفيات المحسوسة بالحواس الظاهرة أو الباطنة مثل الصورة واللون والطعم والرائحة واللذة والألم والفرح والغم والغضب ونحو ذلك إذ لا يعقل منها إلا ما يخص الأجسام وإن كان البعض منها مختصاً بذوات الأنفس ولأن للبعض منها تغيرات وانفعالات وهي على الله تعالى محال وأثبت الحكماء اللذة العقلية لأن كمالاته أمور ملائمة وهو مدرك لها فيبتهج بها. واعترض بأنه إن أريد أن الحاجة التي تسميها اللذة هي نفس إدراك الملائم فغير معلوم وإن أريد أنها حاصلة البتة عند إدراك الملائم فربما يختص ذلك بإدراكنا دون إدراكه فإنهما مختلفان قطعاً.
The Necessary is not qualified by quantities nor by qualities. He said: a notice: so He is not qualified by any of the quantities and qualities such as length, width, form, color, taste, smell, joy, sorrow, anger, pleasure, and pain. The saying of the philosophers about intellectual pleasure—because He perceives His perfections and delights in them—is only established if it is proven that the perception of the agreeable in the unseen is pleasure or its concomitant, as in the witnessed world. Notice: Since it is established that the Necessary is not a body, it appears that He is not qualified by any of the sensible qualities perceived by the external or internal senses, such as form, color, taste, smell, pleasure, pain, joy, sorrow, anger, and the like. For none of these are understood except as pertaining to bodies, even if some of them are specific to souled beings; and because some of them involve changes and affections, and these are impossible for God Most High. The philosophers affirmed intellectual pleasure because His perfections are agreeable things and He perceives them, so He delights in them. It was objected: if it is meant that the state that you call pleasure is the very perception of the agreeable, it is unknown; and if it is meant that it necessarily occurs upon perceiving the agreeable, then perhaps that is specific to our perception, not His perception, for they are certainly different.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a proof and explanation that the Necessary Being (Allah) is not described with any quantities or qualities. It begins by stating the rule, then gives examples of physical and emotional attributes that cannot apply to God. The author then addresses a potential objection: the philosophers' claim that God experiences intellectual pleasure. He argues that this would require proving that perceiving the agreeable in the unseen is always accompanied by pleasure, which is not established. Moreover, even if it were, it might apply only to human perception. Thus God is transcendent above all such attributes.

Key Terms
الواجب
الكميات
الكيفيات
اللذة العقلية
إدراك الملائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that attributing intellectual pleasure to God is permissible because it seems positive and non-physical.
Correction: The author shows that this attribution relies on an unproven analogy with human experience. In God, there is no need or change, so 'pleasure' cannot apply.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the philosophers' claim that God experiences intellectual pleasure?
A) Because pleasure is a bodily sensation
B) Because it would imply change in God
C) Because the perception of the agreeable in the unseen is not proven to be pleasure
D) Because God does not perceive anything

Key Takeaway
God's transcendence means He is free from all bodily and emotional attributes; the claim of intellectual pleasure is based on an unproven analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining God as having human-like emotions, even if only in your heart? What does this lesson reveal about the hidden danger of that tendency?
?
When you feel intense joy or sorrow in your worship, do you assume that God must feel something similar? How does the lesson’s warning about unproven analogy challenge your assumption?
?
The philosophers claimed God delights in His own perfections. Is there any part of you that wants God to 'feel' something because it makes Him more relatable? Can you accept a God who is utterly beyond such qualities, even if it feels distant?

Refuting Excessive Transcendence: Applying 'Thing' to God
Sanusi refutes those who avoid calling God 'thing' to avoid similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the name 'thing' can be applied to God without implying similarity to creation.

Core Idea
Avoiding the name 'thing' for God is an overcorrection; shared names do not imply equality.

Some early theologians, in their zeal to safeguard God's transcendence, avoided calling God by common names like 'thing' or even 'knower'. Sanusi argues this is unnecessary and rests on a misunderstanding of what similarity entails. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through before interpreting.
2
Identify the extreme position
Note which names the ancients refused to apply to God and why. What was their fear?
They avoided 'thing', 'knower', 'powerful' to avoid suggesting a likeness.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi explains that similarity (tamathul) only follows if the shared meaning is exactly equal in all respects. Since God's knowledge and will are nothing like our own, the objection fails.
4
Reflect on the principle
Think about how this principle applies to other divine names. Does calling God 'Merciful' imply His mercy is like ours?
The same logic applies: shared names do not force anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن بعض القدماء بالغوا في التنزيه حين امتنعوا عن إطلاق اسم الشيء بل العالم والقادر وغيرهما على الله تعالى زعماً منهم أنه يوجب إثبات المثل له وليس إذ المماثلة إنما تلزم لو كان المعنى المشترك بينه وبين غيره فيهما على جميع السواء ولا تساوي بين مشيئته ومشيئة غيره ولا بين علمه وعلم غيره وكذا الصفات.
Know that some of the ancients went to excess in transcendence when they refrained from applying the name 'thing', even 'knower' and 'powerful' and others, to God Most High, claiming that it necessitates affirming a likeness for Him. But it is not so, for similarity would only be necessitated if the meaning shared between Him and another were equal in every respect; but there is no equality between His will and the will of another, nor between His knowledge and the knowledge of another, and likewise for the attributes.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an extreme position in tanzih (divine transcendence). Some early theologians avoided calling God 'thing' or even 'knower' and 'powerful' because they thought it would imply similarity to created beings. The author argues that similarity (tamathul) would only follow if the common meaning were exactly identical in all respects. But God's knowledge and will are incomparable to those of creatures; thus there is no similarity. Hence it is permissible to apply these names to God.

Key Terms
القدماء
التنزيه
اسم الشيء
المماثلة
المعنى المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Applying the same name to God and creatures necessarily implies anthropomorphism.
Correction: The author clarifies that it does not unless the meanings are identical in every way, which they are not.

Checkpoint
Why did some ancients avoid calling God 'thing'?
A) Because 'thing' is a derogatory term
B) Because they believed it would imply God has a likeness
C) Because God is not a thing at all
D) Because the name 'thing' is not mentioned in revelation

Key Takeaway
The name 'thing' and other attributes can be applied to God because similarity is only entailed if the meanings were exactly equal, which they are not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you hear that God's will and knowledge are not equal to yours, does your heart feel distant or reassured? Commit this week to praising God with attributes like 'All-Knowing' and 'All-Powerful', reminding yourself that His attributes are beyond comparison.
?
Is there an area of your life where you quietly think God's understanding of you is limited because you imagine His knowledge is like yours? Starting today, correct that thought by asserting His perfect and distinct knowledge of your deepest needs.

Why Many Theologians Refrain from Quiddity
Sanusi explains the rejection of quiddity for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why many theologians avoid applying the concept of quiddity (mahiyyah) to God, and how it implies a genus which God transcends.

Core Idea
Quiddity asks 'what kind?' which implies genus; God is beyond all genera.

This lesson introduces a key theological precaution: the term 'quiddity' (mahiyyah) is often avoided for God because it suggests asking 'what kind of thing?'—a question that presupposes a genus. The passage explains the rationale behind this cautious approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about quiddity
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Note the clear statement that refraining from applying quiddity is the doctrine of many theologians.
2
Understand the meaning of quiddity
The passage defines quiddity as asking 'what is this thing?' regarding which genus it belongs to. Recognize that this implies categorization.
The term mahiyyah comes from 'ma huwa' (what is it).
3
Reflect on why it is problematic for God
Since God transcends all categories and genera, applying quiddity would inappropriately limit Him. This is why many theologians refrain.
This connects to the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih).
4
Connect to the broader theme
This passage sets the stage for al-Maturidi's answer, which explicitly says God is beyond likeness and genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الامتناع عن إطلاق الماهية فمذهب كثير من المتكلمين لأن معناها يقال ما هذا الشيء من أي جنس هو. قالوا وما روي أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله كان يقول إن الله تعالى مشيئة لا يعلمها إلا هو ليس بصحيح إذ لم يوجد في كتبه ولم ينقل عن أصحابه العارفين بمذهبه ولو ثبت فمعناه أنه يعلم نفسه بالمشاهدة لا بدليل أو خبر أو أن له اسماً لا يعلمه غيره فإن لفظة ما قد يقع سؤالاً عن الاسم. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله إن سألنا سائل عن الله تعالى ما هو قلنا إن أردت ما اسمه فالله الرحمن الرحيم وإن أردت ما صفته فسميع بصير وإن أردت ما فعله فخلق المخلوقات ووضع كل شيء موضعه وإن أردت ما ماهيته فهو متعال عن المثال والجنس.
As for refraining from applying quiddity (al-mahiyyah), it is the doctrine of many theologians, because its meaning is: one says 'what is this thing?' regarding which genus it belongs to. They said: and what has been narrated that Abu Hanifa, may God have mercy on him, used to say 'God Most High is a will (mashi'ah) known only to Him' is not authentic, for it is not found in his books, nor has it been transmitted from his companions who are knowledgeable of his school. And if it were established, its meaning would be that He knows Himself by direct vision without proof or report, or that He has a name that no one else knows, for the word 'ma' (what) can be a question about the name. Sheikh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: If a questioner asks us about God Most High: 'What is He?', we say: if you mean what is His name, then He is Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim; if you mean what is His attribute, then He is Hearing, Seeing; if you mean what is His action, then He creates creatures and places everything in its place; and if you mean what is His quiddity, then He is transcendent above likeness and genus.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage addresses three topics: (1) The rejection of applying 'quiddity' (mahiyyah) to God by many theologians, because quiddity asks 'what kind of thing is it?' implying genus, which God transcends. (2) The refutation of a reported saying of Abu Hanifa that 'God is a will known only to Him'—the author says it is inauthentic, and if authentic, it is interpreted as referring to God's knowledge of Himself or a hidden name. (3) The answer of Imam al-Maturidi to the question 'What is God?' He distinguishes four aspects: name, attribute, action, and quiddity, giving appropriate answers for each, emphasizing that God's quiddity is beyond likeness and genus.

Key Terms
الماهية
المتكلمين
الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think it is acceptable to ask 'What is God?' in the sense of quiddity, as if God belongs to a category.
Correction: The passage clarifies that quiddity implies genus, so it is avoided for God to preserve His transcendence.

Checkpoint
Why do many theologians avoid applying the term 'quiddity' to God?
A) Because it is not found in the Quran
B) Because it implies asking about the genus
C) Because it is a philosophical term
D) Because it was rejected by Abu Hanifa

Key Takeaway
Quiddity is avoided for God because it implies a genus, compromising divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever catch yourself trying to 'define' Him as if He fits into a category? How does this lesson expose that subtle idolatry in your heart? What will you do to repent of it today?
?
The truth that God is beyond any genus means He is utterly unique and incomprehensible. Does that humble you or make you feel distant? Examine your heart: do you really want a God you can fully grasp, or the true God who is beyond all categories?
?
How does your prayer change when you remember that you are addressing a Being who cannot be asked 'what is He?' because He has no genus? What will you change about your worship to reflect this awe?

Refuting the Report about Abu Hanifa
Sanusi refutes a report about Abu Hanifa and offers reinterpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the report that Abu Hanifa said 'God is a will known only to Him' is considered inauthentic, and how it may be reinterpreted if it were true.

Core Idea
A famous report about Abu Hanifa is rejected as unauthentic and re-interpreted to avoid theological error.

This lesson examines a controversial report attributed to Abu Hanifa regarding God's nature. The author argues that it is not found in his books nor transmitted by his reliable students, and offers possible reinterpretations to safeguard orthodox theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the report
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that the report claims Abu Hanifa said 'God is a will known only to Him'.
2
Note the reasons for inauthenticity
The author says the report is not found in Abu Hanifa's books nor transmitted by his knowledgeable companions. This is strong evidence against its authenticity.
This shows the importance of verifying reports through reliable sources.
3
Consider the possible reinterpretations
Even if the report were true, it could mean: (a) God knows Himself directly without proof, or (b) God has a name that no one else knows. The word 'ma' (what) can also ask about a name.
These interpretations avoid any compromise of God's transcendence.
4
Understand the lesson for theological method
This passage teaches us to critically examine reports and to give orthodox interpretations when necessary.

Al-Maturidi's Fourfold Answer to 'What is God?'
Al-Maturidi gives a fourfold answer to the question 'What is God?'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Maturidi's fourfold response to the question 'What is God?', distinguishing between name, attribute, action, and quiddity, and emphasizing God's transcendence beyond likeness and genus.

Core Idea
When asked 'What is God?', al-Maturidi gives four answers based on the intention: name, attribute, action, or quiddity—the last points to His transcendence.

This lesson presents the classic answer of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi to the fundamental question 'What is God?' He skillfully divides the question into four categories and provides appropriate responses, culminating in the affirmation that God's quiddity is beyond all comparison and category.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Maturidi's response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Al-Maturidi begins by considering the questioner's intention.
2
Identify the four categories
The response covers four possibilities: name, attribute, action, and quiddity. List them and their respective answers.
The answers are: (name) Allah, al-Rahman, al-Rahim; (attribute) Hearing, Seeing; (action) creating and placing; (quiddity) transcendent.
3
Understand the answer for quiddity
For quiddity, al-Maturidi says God is transcendent above likeness and genus. This echoes the earlier point about avoiding quiddity.
This is the most significant for theology.
4
Reflect on the overarching lesson
Al-Maturidi's answer shows that the question 'What is God?' can be answered in different ways depending on intent, and it safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text

Proofs Against Union and Indwelling in God
Al-Maturidi presents two proofs against union and indwelling in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments proving that the Necessary Being cannot unite with another or indwell in anything.

Core Idea
Union and indwelling imply dependence, contradicting divine necessity.

Al-Maturidi now turns to two key implications of divine transcendence: God does not unite with another being nor does He indwell in anything. These ideas are central to preserving God's absolute independence. The following passage presents two distinct proofs—one against union, two against indwelling—each grounded in the impossibility of dependence in the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once without over-analysing. Let the structure emerge: first union, then indwelling with two proofs and an objection-answer.
Notice the compact style; each proof relies on the concept of need (iftiqār).
2
Understand the argument against union
The union argument is brief: union of two things would make the Necessary become possible and vice versa, which is absurd. Reflect on why this is impossible by necessity.
This builds on earlier discussions about impossibility of union.
3
Analyze the first proof against indwelling
The first proof: whatever indwells depends on its locus. That dependence negates necessity. Then an objection about mixture (imtizāj) is answered: mixture is a bodily property involving division, so it reduces to body-in-place indwelling.
Note the universal scope: body, accident, form, attribute—all cases fall under the same dependency.
4
Analyze the second proof against indwelling
The second proof is a disjunction: if indwelling occurred, either it would be necessary or possible. If necessary, God would depend on the locus, implying His possibility and the locus's priority. If possible, He is independent—so indwelling is unnecessary. Either way, indwelling is impossible.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثالث : الواجب لا يتحد بغيره، ولا يحل فيه. أما الاتحاد فلما سبق من امتناع اتحاد الاثنين ولأنه يلزم كون الواجب هو الممكن والممكن هو الواجب وذلك محال بالضرورة وأما الحلول فلوجوه : 16 (۱۱۷) الأول : أن الحال في الشيء يفتقر إليه في الجملة. سواء كان حلول جسم في مكان ، أو عرض في جوهر أو صورة في مادة كما هو رأي الحكماء، أو صفة في (١١٧) ،موصوف كصفات المجردات والافتقار إلى الغير ينافي الوجوب . فإن قيل : قد يكون حلول امتزاج كالماء في الورد. قلنا : ذلك من خواص الأجسام ومفضي إلى الانقسام، وعائد إلى حلول الجسم في المكان. الثاني : أنه لو حل في محل فإما مع وجوب ذلك وحينئد يفتقر إلى المحل ويلزم إمكانه، وقدم المحل، بل وجوبه ، لأن ما يفتقر إليه الواجب أولى بأن يكون واجباً، وإما مع جوازه وحينئذ يكون غنياً عن الحل .
The third investigation: The Necessary Being does not unite with another nor indwell in it. As for union (ittiḥād), it is due to what preceded of the impossibility of two uniting, and because it would necessitate that the Necessary Being be the possible and the possible be the Necessary, and that is impossible by necessity. As for indwelling (ḥulūl), it is due to aspects: The first: That which indwells in a thing is in need of it in some respect, whether it be the indwelling of a body in a place, or an accident in a substance, or a form in matter as is the view of the philosophers, or an attribute in a described thing like the attributes of the immaterial beings. And need of another negates [divine] necessity (wujūb). If it is said: Indwelling may be of mixture like water in rose water. We say: That is a property of bodies and leads to division, and reverts to the indwelling of a body in a place. The second: If He were to indwell in a locus, then either it would be with necessity of that; then He would be in need of the locus and His possibility would follow, and the priority of the locus, rather its necessity, because that which the Necessary Being is in need of is more deserving of being necessary; or it would be with possibility; then He would be independent of indwelling.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Necessary Being (al-wājib) neither unites with another nor indwells in anything. It first addresses union (ittiḥād), repeating an earlier argument: union of two distinct things is impossible because it would make the Necessary (wājib) become possible (mumkin) and vice versa, which is absurd. Then it gives two proofs against indwelling (ḥulūl). The first proof: whatever indwells in something depends on it, whether it be a body in a place, an accident in a substance, a form in matter, or an attribute in a subject. This dependence negates divine necessity (wujūb). An objection about mixture (imtizāj) like water in rose water is answered: that is a bodily property involving division, so it reduces to body-in-place indwelling. The second proof: if God were to indwell in a locus, either it would be necessary, making Him dependent on the locus and implying His possibility, and the locus would be prior and even necessary; or it would be merely possible, meaning He is independent of indwelling. Either way, indwelling is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib)
اتحاد (ittiḥād)
حلول (ḥulūl)
محل (maḥall)
افتقار (iftiqār)
امتزاج (imtizāj)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's indwelling in creation (like being present everywhere) is similar to the indwelling discussed here, but the text shows that any form of indwelling implies dependence, which cannot apply to God.
Correction: The passage differentiates between God's omnipresence and the kind of indwelling (ḥulūl) that requires a locus and entails dependence. The latter is impossible for the Necessary Being.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a reason given against indwelling (ḥulūl) in this passage?
A) The indweller is dependent on the locus.
B) If indwelling were necessary, it would imply the possibility of the Necessary.
C) Indwelling of mixture is a bodily property that leads to division.
D) Indwelling would require two necessary beings.

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that God cannot unite with another or indwell in anything because both imply dependence, which contradicts His absolute necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if God needs your worship or good deeds, forgetting that He is al-Wājib (the Self-Sufficient)? How does this passage expose the emptiness of that assumption, and what change in your daily intentions does it demand?
?
Consider a time you prayed anxiously as if God needed to be coerced by your desperation. In what way does this lesson's proof against indwelling (that God needs nothing) free you from manipulating Him, and how will you approach prayer differently starting today?
?
The passage states that if God indwelt anything, that thing would have priority over Him. Where in your life have you given something more priority than God—making it a 'locus' of your trust? What concrete step can you take this week to realign that priority?

Argument Against Hulul: Self-Sufficiency and Dependence
Dialectical proof against hulul with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical argument against hulul (divine indwelling) based on the concepts of self-sufficiency and dependence, including the objections and responses.

Core Idea
The self-sufficient cannot become dependent; thus God cannot dwell in a locus.

This lesson presents a sophisticated theological argument that uses the logical relationship between a mode (ḥāl) and its locus (maḥall) to refute the possibility of God indwelling in creation. The argument proceeds through a series of objections and answers, each refining the proof. We will follow the dialectic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the back-and-forth structure of argument and objection.
The text is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the initial proof
The argument begins: a mode (ḥāl) necessarily depends on its locus, so if God were a mode, He would become dependent—contradicting self-sufficiency.
3
Examine the first objection
The objector says: the essence could necessitate the locus without needing it, much like an agent necessitates effects without need.
This challenges the direct inference to dependence.
4
Follow the refined argument
The proof is restated: self-sufficiency by essence entails possibility and pre-eternity of locus, which is impossible for God. The objection that lack of self-sufficiency does not imply essential need is countered by noting that mere absence of essential self-sufficiency entails possibility.
5
Understand the final response
The final reply clarifies that accidental need does not contradict essential self-sufficiency, but the proof only requires that God cannot be essentially dependent. Thus indwelling is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحال يجب افتقاره إلى المحل فيلزم انقلاب الغني عن الشيء محتاجاً إليه ، هكذا قرره الإمام رحمه ان الله . ثم اعترض بأنه على التقدير الأول لا يلزم الافتقار لجواز أن توجب ذاته ذلك المحل والمحل الحلول، أو توجب ذاته المحل، والحلول جميعاً، ووجوب اللوازم والآثار عند المؤثر لا يوجب احتياجه إليها . وعلى التقدير الثاني لا يلزم الانقلاب لان لا نسلم أن الحال في الشيء يكون محتاجاً إليه كالجسم المعين بحل في الحيز المعين مع عدم احتياجه في ذاته إليه . وقد تقرر بأنه إذا كان مستغنياً بالذات لزم إمكانه وقدم المحل وهو ظاهر. واعترض : بأن عدم الاستغناء بالذات لا يستلزم الاحتياج بالذات، ليلزم إمكانه في قدم المحل الجواز أن يكون كل من الغنى والاحتياج عارضاً بحسب أمر خارج . وأجيب بأن عدم مجرد الاستغناء بالذات يستلزم الإمكان لأن الواجب مستغني بالذات ضرورة ولا حاجة إلى توسيط الاحتياج بالذات، وقد يقرر بأنه إن كان محتاجاً بالذات لزم إمكانه ، وإلا امتنع حلوله . ورد : بأن عدم الاحتياج الذاتي لا ينافي عروض الاحتياج، فلا ينافي الحلول.
And the mode (ḥāl) necessarily depends on its locus (maḥall), so it necessitates the transformation of the self-sufficient (al-ghanī) from something to being in need of it. Thus the Imam (may God have mercy on him) established it. Then it was objected: on the first supposition, dependence is not necessitated, because it is possible that His essence necessitates that locus and the locus of indwelling, or His essence necessitates the locus and indwelling together, and the necessitation of concomitants and effects by the agent does not necessitate His need for them. On the second supposition, the transformation is not necessitated, because we do not concede that the mode (al-ḥāl) in a thing is in need of it, like a particular body that dwells in a particular space without being in need of it in its essence. And it has been established that if He is self-sufficient by essence, then His possibility and the pre-eternity of the locus are necessitated, and that is apparent. And it was objected: that the mere absence of self-sufficiency by essence does not entail neediness by essence, so as to necessitate His possibility in the pre-eternity of the locus; it is possible that both wealth and neediness are accidental according to an external matter. And it was answered: that the mere absence of self-sufficiency by essence entails possibility (because the Necessary is self-sufficient by essence necessarily, and there is no need to mediate neediness by essence). And it may be established that if He is needy by essence, then His possibility is necessitated; otherwise, His indwelling is impossible. And it was replied: that the absence of essential need does not contradict accidental need, so it does not contradict indwelling.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument against the notion that God could dwell in a body (ḥulūl). It begins with a proof: a mode (ḥāl) necessarily depends on its locus, so if God were a mode dwelling in something, this would turn the self-sufficient into a dependent. An objection is then raised: the self-sufficient's essence could necessitate the locus and indwelling without becoming dependent. A reformulated proof follows: self-sufficiency by essence entails possibility and the pre-eternity of the locus, which is impossible for God. Another objection counters that lack of self-sufficiency does not imply essential need. The final answer clarifies that the absence of essential need suffices for possibility, and accidental need does not contradict indwelling. The author follows the argumentative order: initial thesis, objection, refined thesis, second objection, final rebuttal.

Key Terms
الحال
المحل
الغني
الافتقار
الاستغناء بالذات
الحلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-sufficiency in God means He cannot have any relation or 'indwelling' even in a derived sense.
Correction: The argument does not deny all relations, but rather the logical implication that God would become dependent if He were a mode. Accidental relations are not the issue; the mode's essential dependence is.

Checkpoint
What consequence does the initial argument draw from the claim that a mode (ḥāl) necessarily depends on its locus?
A) God would be subject to change.
B) The self-sufficient would become dependent.
C) The locus would become eternal.
D) God would be divisible.

Key Takeaway
The argument against hulul hinges on the impossibility of the self-sufficient becoming dependent, and even objections based on accidental need do not undermine the proof.

?
If God is self-sufficient by essence, needing nothing and no one, then why do you pray? Do you truly believe your worship adds nothing to Him, or do you secretly think He needs your devotion? How will you let this truth reshape your intention in every act of worship today?
?
This passage refutes the idea that God can dwell in creation. Have you ever imagined God as 'inside' your heart or in a specific place in a way that reduces His majesty? Starting now, will you commit to always thinking of God as transcendent, yet knowing He is fully aware of you without any physical connection?
?
The argument shows that even accidental neediness is incompatible with God's essential self-sufficiency. Third Argument: Hulul as Perfection or Imperfection
Third argument: hulul is either a perfection or not; both options are impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that refutes the notion of divine indwelling (hulul) by showing it leads to either imperfection or dependence.

Core Idea
God cannot be perfected by another; therefore, hulul is impossible.

This lesson presents the third of several arguments against the concept of divine indwelling (hulul). The argument is a concise disjunctive syllogism that forces a choice between two unacceptable alternatives for God. By working through this reasoning, you will see why classical Islamic theology categorically rejects any notion of God in-dwelling creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise logical structure: a conditional with two branches.
2
Identify the two alternatives
The passage presents a disjunction: hulul is either an attribute of perfection or it is not. Find how each branch is handled.
The first branch negates hulul from God; the second leads to a contradiction.
3
Analyze the first alternative
If hulul is not a perfection, then it must be negated from the Necessary Being. Ask yourself: why does lacking a non-perfection require negation?
Because God must possess all perfections; a non-perfection cannot be attributed to Him.
4
Analyze the second alternative
If hulul is a perfection, then God would be perfected by something external. Reflect on why this is impossible for the self-sufficient Necessary Being.
This is the key: even if hulul were a perfection, it implies dependence, which is incompatible with God's nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أن الحلول في الغير إن لم يكن صفة كمال، وجب نفيه عن الواجب، وإن كان لزم كون الواجب مستكملاً بالغير وهو باطل وفاقاً.
Third: If indwelling (al-ḥulūl) in another is not an attribute of perfection, then its negation from the Necessary (al-wājib) is obligatory; but if it is, then it necessitates that the Necessary be perfected by another, which is invalid by consensus.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against divine indwelling. It is a disjunctive syllogism: either indwelling (ḥulūl) is a perfection or it is not. If it is not a perfection, then it cannot be attributed to God, who must possess all perfections. If it is a perfection, then God would require another being to perfect Him, making Him dependent. Both alternatives are impossible for the Necessary Being. The argument relies on the principle that God must be perfect in Himself and not derive perfection from anything external.

Key Terms
الحلول
صفة كمال
الواجب
المستكمل بالغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if hulul were a perfection, it would be acceptable for God.
Correction: However, even if it were a perfection, it would make God dependent on another for perfection, which is impossible for the self-sufficient Necessary Being.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what follows if hulul is considered an attribute of perfection?
A) It must be affirmed for God.
B) It would make God perfected by another.
C) It would make God divisible.
D) It would be a necessary attribute.

Key Takeaway
Indwelling (hulul) is impossible for God because it would either be a non-perfection or require God to be perfected by another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself seeking completion from people, achievements, or possessions? How does the truth that God alone is perfect and self-sufficient expose the emptiness of looking elsewhere for wholeness? What is one thing you can actively turn away from today to redirect that need to God alone?
?
The argument says that if God could be perfected by another, He would not be truly God. Do you live as though something else—your reputation, relationships, or success—is necessary for your sense of worth? What would it cost you to truly believe that only God can perfect you, and that He is already complete without you?
?
If indwelling were possible, it would imply God needs creation—and yet many people act as though God's favor depends on their deeds. Are you relying on your own actions to earn God's love, rather than acknowledging that God is already infinitely perfect and gives freely? How can you begin to trust His sufficiency instead of your own performance?

Fourth Argument: Hulul Implies Spatial Location
Sanusi presents the argument: hulul entails tahayyuz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of indwelling (hulul) is impossible for God because it entails spatial location (tahayyuz).

Core Idea
Indwelling necessarily implies occupying a place.

Sanusi presents the fourth rational argument against the idea that God could dwell in something. He begins by stating the entailment and then defines what indwelling means by rational consensus. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise style: a conditional and its consequence, then a definition of indwelling.
The term 'al-fard' here means a supervenient entity.
2
Understand the definition of indwelling
Sanusi states that indwelling, by rational consensus, means a supervenient (al-fard) occurring in a location (al-ḥayyiz) after the occurrence of a substance. This implies spatiality.
A substance (jawhar) is needed to occupy a location first.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
Since God is not a substance or body, He cannot be the subject of indwelling. Affirming hulul would imply spatial location, which is impossible for Allah.
This reinforces tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لوحل في شيء لزم تحيزه . لأن المعقول من الحلول باتفاق العقلاء هو حصول الفرض في الحيز تبعاً لحصول الجوهر. وأما صفات الباري عز وجل فالفلاسفة لا يقولون بها. والمتكلمون لا يقولون بكونها أعراضاً ولا بكونها حالة في الذات، بل قائمة بها بمعنى الاختصاص الناعت
Fourth: If He dwells in something, it would necessitate His being located (taḥayyuz). Because what is understood from indwelling by consensus of rational people is the occurrence of a supervenient (al-fard) in a location (al-ḥayyiz) following the occurrence of a substance. As for the attributes of the Exalted, the philosophers do not affirm them. And the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) do not say they are accidents nor that they are states (ḥāl) in the essence, but rather they are established with it in the sense of special description (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-nāʿit).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage contains two related points. First, the fourth argument against hulul: if God dwelt in something, He would have to occupy a spatial location (taḥayyuz). This is because indwelling, by rational consensus, means a supervenient entity (al-fard) occurs in a location following the occurrence of a substance (jawhar). Since God is not a body or substance, this is impossible. Second, the author clarifies that the divine attributes are not like physical properties: philosophers deny them entirely, while the mutakallimūn affirm them but not as accidents (aʿrāḍ) or states (aḥwāl) existing in God’s essence; rather, they are established with the essence in the sense of a special description (ikhtiṣāṣ nāʿit). This safeguards against the misconception that affirming attributes implies hulul.

Key Terms
التحيز
الحيز
الفرض
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, what does indwelling (ḥulūl) necessarily involve?
A) Division into parts.
B) Location in a spatial place.
C) Dependence on another.
D) Change over time.

Key Takeaway
Indwelling entails spatial location, making it impossible for the transcendent God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Divine Attributes: Neither Accidents nor States
Sanusi clarifies the position on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct theological position on divine attributes as established with the essence in a special descriptive sense, not as accidents or states.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are not physical properties.

Sanusi clarifies the status of divine attributes after refuting indwelling. He distinguishes the position of philosophers (who deny them) from the theologians who affirm them while rejecting any notion that they are accidents or states inhering in God. This lesson explains the concept of 'special description' (ikhtiṣāṣ nāʿit).

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that it addresses the nature of divine attributes separately from the argument against hulul.
This is an important aside to prevent misunderstanding.
2
Identify the two positions
The philosophers deny attributes altogether. The theologians affirm them but reject that they are accidents (aʿrāḍ) or states (aḥwāl) in the essence.
3
Understand the positive affirmation
Theologians say attributes are 'established with the essence in the sense of special description' (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-nāʿit). This means they are real descriptions that are not separable from the essence.
This avoids both anthropomorphism and negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لوحل في شيء لزم تحيزه . لأن المعقول من الحلول باتفاق العقلاء هو حصول الفرض في الحيز تبعاً لحصول الجوهر. وأما صفات الباري عز وجل فالفلاسفة لا يقولون بها. والمتكلمون لا يقولون بكونها أعراضاً ولا بكونها حالة في الذات، بل قائمة بها بمعنى الاختصاص الناعت
Fourth: If He dwells in something, it would necessitate His being located (taḥayyuz). Because what is understood from indwelling by consensus of rational people is the occurrence of a supervenient (al-fard) in a location (al-ḥayyiz) following the occurrence of a substance. As for the attributes of the Exalted, the philosophers do not affirm them. And the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) do not say they are accidents nor that they are states (ḥāl) in the essence, but rather they are established with it in the sense of special description (al-ikhtiṣāṣ al-nāʿit).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage contains two related points. First, the fourth argument against hulul: if God dwelt in something, He would have to occupy a spatial location (taḥayyuz). This is because indwelling, by rational consensus, means a supervenient entity (al-fard) occurs in a location following the occurrence of a substance (jawhar). Since God is not a body or substance, this is impossible. Second, the author clarifies that the divine attributes are not like physical properties: philosophers deny them entirely, while the mutakallimūn affirm them but not as accidents (aʿrāḍ) or states (aḥwāl) existing in God’s essence; rather, they are established with the essence in the sense of a special description (ikhtiṣāṣ nāʿit). This safeguards against the misconception that affirming attributes implies hulul.

Key Terms
الصفات
الأعراض

الاختصاص الناعت

Misconception: That affirming divine attributes implies they are accidents or states inhering in God.
Correction: The mutakallimūn affirm attributes without inherence; they are special descriptions of the essence.

Fifth Argument: Hulul Leads to Division or Minuteness
Fifth argument: Hulul in a body leads to division or minuteness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dichotomous argument that refutes the notion of God dwelling in a body by showing it leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
If God dwells in a body, He must be either divisible or the smallest.

This fifth argument presents a crisp logical dilemma. If God were to indwell a body, as some claim, then there are only two possibilities: He occupies all parts or one part. Both lead to impossible outcomes. Let's follow Sanusi's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once. Note the two alternatives.
2
Identify the dichotomy
Spot the two branches: dwelling in all parts or one part.
3
Understand the consequences
Dwelling in all parts necessitates division; dwelling in one part makes God the smallest.
4
Confirm the refutation
Both consequences are absurd by necessity and by opponent's admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أنه لو حل في الجسم على ما يزعم الخصم، فإما في جميع أجزائه فيلزم الانقسام أو في جزء منه فيكون أصغر الأشياء، وكلاهما باطل بالضرورة والاعتراف .
Fifth: If He dwells in a body, as the opponent claims, then either in all of its parts, which would necessitate division, or in a part of it, then He would be the smallest of things; both are invalid necessarily and by admission.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage contains a straightforward dichotomous argument. If God were to dwell in a body, the dwelling must be either in all parts of the body or in a single part. If in all parts, God would be divided, which is impossible for a non-corporeal being. If in a single part, then God would be smaller than that part, making Him the smallest of things. Both consequences are absurd by necessity and are acknowledged as such by the opponent. This argument assumes the opponent's own framework of bodies composed of parts.

Key Terms
الحلول
الجسم
الأجزاء
الانقسام
أصغر الأشياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That indwelling could be partial without implying limitation
Correction: The argument shows partial indwelling makes God confined to a part, rendering Him the smallest—an impossibility for the Creator.

Checkpoint
What necessary consequence does the fifth argument draw from the possibility of God dwelling in a single part of a body?
A) God would be infinitely large.
B) God would be the smallest of things.
C) God would be omnipresent.
D) God would be divisible.

Key Takeaway
Dwelling in a body leads to the absurdities of division or minuteness, proving God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God being 'with you' or 'close to you,' do you ever subconsciously imagine Him as present in a physical sense, like being inside your heart or beside you? How does the argument in this lesson expose that subtle anthropomorphism as a form of hulul, and how can you purify that feeling without losing intimacy?
?
This argument forces the conclusion: if God is in a place, He becomes limited and divisible. Have you ever, in a moment of desperation, begged God to 'come down' or 'enter' your situation as if He were absent? What does this passage say about that assumption, and how does it call you to trust in a God who is transcendent yet fully aware?
?
The opponent's claim stems from overfamiliarity with God, reducing Him to a thing that can be contained. In what areas of your life do you try to 'contain' or 'control' God—through rituals, concepts, or expectations of how He should act? How can you let go of that control and worship a God who is beyond all containment?

Sixth Argument: Hulul and Likeness of Bodies
Sixth argument: if bodies are alike, God could dwell in the vilest, leading to uncertainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument against hulul (divine indwelling) that uses the likeness of bodies to derive a absurd consequence.

Core Idea
If bodies are alike, hulul cannot be restricted—undermining divine transcendence.

The sixth argument targets the claim that God could dwell in a body. It assumes the opponent's view that all bodies are composed of identical indivisible atoms. If that is true, then dwelling in one body would imply the possibility of dwelling in any body, even the most contemptible. The argument forces a reductio ad absurdum: if hulul were possible, we could not deny that God dwells in a mosquito, which is clearly false.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...' leading to an impossible conclusion.
The text contains a small uncertainty: 'على ما بين الجاز' may be 'على ما بين الأجزاء' (according to what is between the parts). This does not affect the overall argument.
2
Identify the key premises
The opponent holds that bodies are composed of identical atoms (jawāhir farda). From this, if God dwells in a body, He could dwell in any body, including vile ones like a mosquito.
The similarity of bodies is the crucial premise.
3
Understand the reductio
The argument shows that accepting hulul leads to an absurd conclusion: we cannot be certain that God does not dwell in a mosquito. Since this is false (by consensus), the premise of hulul must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : لوحل في جسم والأجسام متماثلة لتركبها من الجواهر الفردة المتفقة الحقيقة على ما بين [الجاز؟] حلوله في أحقر الأجسام وأرذلها، فلا يحصل الجزم بعدم حلوله في مثل البعوض وهو باطل بلا نزاع .
Sixth: If He dwells in a body, and bodies are similar because they are composed of individual atoms (jawāhir farda) of identical essence, then according to what is between [the parts?] His dwelling in the vilest and most contemptible of bodies follows; then certainty of His not dwelling in something like a mosquito is not attained, and that is invalid without dispute.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument. It assumes the opponent's view that bodies are composed of identical indivisible atoms (jawāhir farda). If God dwells in one body, then by similarity, He could equally dwell in any body, even the most insignificant like a mosquito. This leads to an unacceptable consequence: we cannot be certain that God does not dwell in such vile things. The impossibility of this conclusion shows the premise of hulul to be false. The argument uses the principle of likeness (tamāthul) of bodies to force a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
الأجسام
المتماثلة
الجواهر الفردة
الحقيقة المتفقة
أحقر الأجسام
البعوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hulul could be restricted to a specific body, like a pure body, while avoiding vile ones.
Correction: If bodies are essentially alike (composed of identical atoms), then there is no basis to restrict indwelling. The argument shows that similarity makes restriction impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth argument, if bodies are composed of identical atoms, what follows from God dwelling in one body?
A) God must dwell in all bodies.
B) God could dwell in the vilest bodies like a mosquito.
C) God would be divisible.
D) Bodies would be eternal.

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument refutes hulul by showing that bodily similarity forces an absurd consequence: uncertainty about God dwelling in the lowliest of creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously assume that God's presence is confined to 'noble' things—like mosques, prayer, or sacred moments—while ignoring the lowly and despised in your life? How does this argument's use of a mosquito challenge you to rethink where you expect to encounter the Divine?
?
If you truly believe God transcends all bodies, then what does it mean for your daily life that He is not limited by your categories of worthy and unworthy? How can this certainty transform the way you treat the 'mosquitoes'—the people and situations you deem insignificant?
?
The argument leads to certainty: God does not dwell in a mosquito. Are you equally certain that God is far above every limitation you might place on Him? In what area of your faith are you hesitating to accept this transcendence, clinging instead to a comfortable, restricted view of God?

The Fifth Argument Against Hulul: Division or Smallest
The fifth proof: a dilemma leading to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof against hulul, which shows that divine indwelling in a body leads to absurd conclusions.

Core Idea
If God indwells a body, either He divides or becomes the smallest—both impossible.

The fifth argument against the doctrine of hulul (divine indwelling) uses a logical dilemma. The opponent claims God indwells a body; the argument shows that whether He indwells all parts or just one part, the conclusion is absurd. This forces the opponent to reject the premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: a conditional statement with two alternatives.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
The argument presents two possibilities: if God indwells all parts, then division occurs; if He indwells one part, then He becomes the smallest. Both are impossible for the divine.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since both outcomes are necessarily false and also admitted by the opponent to be false, the original claim of hulul must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أنه لو حل في الجسم على ما يزعم الخصم، فإما في جميع أجزائه فيلزم الانقسام أو في جزء منه فيكون أصغر الأشياء، وكلاهما باطل بالضرورة والاعتراف .
السادس : لوحل في جسم والأجسام متماثلة لتركبها من الجواهر الفردة المتفقة الحقيقة على ما بين الجاز حلوله في أحقر الأجسام وأرذلها، فلا يحصل الجزم بعدم حلوله في مثل البعوض وهو باطل بلا نزاع .
The fifth: That if it were to indwell the body as the opponent claims, then either in all its parts, so division would be necessary, or in one part of it, so it would be the smallest of things, and both are false necessarily and by admission. The sixth: If it were to indwell a body, and bodies are similar because they are composed of individual atoms of identical reality, then according to what is [unclear] it would be permissible for it to indwell in the most contemptible and lowliest of bodies, so certainty would not be obtained that it does not indwell in something like a mosquito, and that is false without dispute.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments against the doctrine of hulul (divine indwelling). The fifth argument: If God (or an attribute) were to indwell a body, either He indwells every part, which would entail division (impossible for the divine), or He indwells one part, making Him the smallest of things (absurd). Both conclusions are necessarily false. The sixth argument: If indwelling were possible, then because all bodies are composed of identical atoms, if God could indwell one body, He could equally indwell the most insignificant body, like a mosquito. But no one would admit that God indwells a mosquito; therefore, the original claim is false. The author uses hypothetical reasoning to show the impossibility.

Key Terms
حلول
الجسم
الانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument assumes God is a body.
Correction: The argument does not assume God is a body; it takes the opponent's claim that God indwells a body and shows it leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
What does the fifth argument conclude about indwelling in all parts?
A) It is possible if God is omnipresent
B) It leads to division, which is impossible for God
C) It is the only acceptable option
D) It makes God the smallest thing

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument uses a dilemma to show that divine indwelling in a body leads to impossible consequences, thus refuting the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you point to a belief you hold about God that, if logically examined, might lead to an absurd consequence like this?
?
If you were to defend God’s transcendence to someone who thinks He is 'near' in a physical sense, what part of this argument would you use to help them see the error?
?
Have you ever instinctively pictured God as being 'in' something (like the sky, a mosque, or your heart) in a way that this argument would force you to rethink? What will you change today?

The Sixth Argument Against Hulul: The Mosquito Analogy
The sixth proof: reductio ad absurdum using bodily similarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof against hulul, which uses the similarity of bodies to show that if indwelling were possible, it could occur in the lowliest body.

Core Idea
If indwelling were possible, God could dwell in a mosquito—which everyone denies.

The sixth argument against hulul builds on the idea that all bodies are composed of identical indivisible atoms. Therefore, if God can indwell one body, He can indwell any body, even the most contemptible. Since no one admits He indwells a mosquito, the original claim is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that there is an unclear word ('الجاز') but the overall meaning is clear.
The argument uses the idea that all bodies are similar in essence.
2
Understand the premise of bodily similarity
Bodies are composed of identical indivisible atoms ('jawahir fardah'), so they are similar in reality. If God can indwell one body, He can indwell any body.
3
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
If indwelling were possible in one body, it would be possible in the most contemptible body, like a mosquito. But no one accepts that God indwells a mosquito, so the original claim is false.
This is a clear example of a reductio ad absurdum.

Claims of Hulul and Ittihad and the Christian View
Exposition of claims of hulul/ittihad and critique of Christian Trinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different groups that claim divine union (ittihad) and the specific Christian doctrine of incarnation seen as a form of hulul/ittihad, and why this is rejected.

Core Idea
Only ignorance leads to claiming that God unites with creation.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid examines a sensitive area of creed: who claims that God becomes united with creation. The author lists Christians, extremist Shia, and some Sufis, then focuses on the Christian doctrine of the Trinity and incarnation. He critiques their logic and shows the impossibility of such union with God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the author's classification of groups who claim ittihad.
Pay attention to the terms hulul and ittihad.
2
Identify the eight possibilities
The author says opponents have eight possibilities regarding hulul and ittihad. Only the first is described. Consider why he might not elaborate further.
3
Analyze the Christian view
Focus on how the author describes Christian beliefs: one substance, three hypostases, and the union of the Word with Jesus. What does he criticize?
Note the terms: substance, hypostasis, Father, Son, Holy Spirit.
4
Reflect on the refutation
The author asserts that hulul/ittihad is impossible for both essence and attributes. How does this refute the Christian claim?
Connect to the impossibility of attributes being separated from essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : والاتحاد محكى عن النصارى في حق عيسى عليه السلام ، وعن بعض الغلاة في حق أئمتهم وعن بعض المتصوفة في حق كلمتهم ، وأما ما يدعي بعضهم من ارتفاع الكثرة عند الفناء في التوحيد، أو أنه لا كثرة في الوجود أصلاً فبحث آخر . والقول بالحلول يعني كما قامت الدلالة على امتناع الحلول والاتحاد على الذات فكذا على الصفات، بل أولى لاستحالة انتفاء الصفة عن الذات، والاحتمالات التي يذهب إليها أوهام المخالفين في هذا الأصل ثمانية . حلول ذات الواجب أو صفته في بدن الإنسان أو روحه، وكذا الاتحاد. والمخالفون منهم نصارى ومنهم منتمون إلى الإسلام. أما النصارى فقد ذهبوا إلى أن الله تعالى جوهر واحد، ثلاثة أقانيم ، هي الوجود والعلم والحياة المعبر عنها عندهم، بالآب والابن وروح القدس على ما يقولون : آباً ابناً روحاً قدساً، ويعنون بالجوهر القائم بنفسه، وبالأقنوم الصفة، وجعل الواحد ثلاثة جهالة، أو ميل إلى أن الصفات نفس الذات، واقتصارهم على العلم والحياة دون القدرة وغيره جهالة أخرى، وكأنهم يجعلون القدرة راجعة إلى الحياة والسمع والبصر إلى العلم ثم قالوا : إن الكلمة وهي أقنوم ، اتحدت بجسد المسيح وتدرعت بناسوته بطريق الامتزاج كالخمر بالماء عند العلم.
He said: And union (ittihad) is reported from the Christians regarding Jesus, peace be upon him, and from some extremists regarding their imams, and from some Sufis regarding their word. As for what some of them claim of the removal of multiplicity at annihilation (fana) in tawhid, or that there is no multiplicity in existence at all, that is another discussion. And the statement about indwelling (hulul) means: just as proof has been established for the impossibility of hulul and ittihad regarding the essence, so too regarding the attributes, rather more so, because it is impossible for an attribute to be absent from the essence. And the possibilities to which the fancies of the opponents go in this principle are eight: Indwelling of the essence of the Necessary or His attribute in the human body or spirit, and likewise union. The opponents include Christians and those affiliated with Islam. As for the Christians, they hold that God is one substance, three hypostases (aqanim), which are existence, knowledge, and life, expressed by them as Father, Son, and Holy Spirit, as they say: Father, Son, Holy Spirit. By 'substance' they mean that which subsists by itself, and by 'hypostasis' they mean attribute. Regarding the One as three is ignorance, or a tendency to say that attributes are the essence itself; and their restriction to knowledge and life to the exclusion of power and other attributes is another ignorance. It is as if they make power reducible to life, and hearing and sight to knowledge. Then they said that the Word, which is a hypostasis, united with the body of the Christ and clothed itself with his humanity by way of mixture, like wine with water.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage begins by stating the various groups that claim ittihad (union): Christians regarding Jesus, some Muslim extremists regarding their imams, and some Sufis regarding their 'word' (possibly the Logos or the Perfect Man). It then distinguishes this from the claim of fanatic monism that denies multiplicity altogether. The author asserts that just as hulul and ittihad are impossible for God's essence, they are even more impossible for His attributes, since attributes cannot be separated from the essence. Next, he enumerates eight possible positions that opponents might hold regarding hulul and ittihad, but he only explicitly mentions the first: indwelling of the divine essence or attribute in a human body or spirit, and similarly union. He then focuses on the Christian view as a major opposing group. He explains that Christians believe in one substance with three hypostases: existence (Father), knowledge (Son), and life (Holy Spirit). They equate hypostasis with attribute. The author criticizes this as ignorance, either for considering one as three or for making attributes identical to the essence. He notes their limitation to only three attributes (knowledge and life, with power subsumed under life, and hearing/sight under knowledge). Finally, he recounts the Christian claim that the Word (the second hypostasis) united with the body of Christ by mixture, like wine mixed with water. This passage serves to categorize and refute these beliefs.

Key Terms
الاتحاد (ittihad)
الحلول (hulul)
الأقانيم (aqanim/hypostases)
الجوهر (substance)
الكلمة (Word)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Christian doctrine of Trinity is not a form of hulul/ittihad but a separate mystery.
Correction: The author treats the Christian incarnation as a claim of union (ittihad) and refutes it as impossible, showing it falls under the categories of hulul/ittihad.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many possibilities do opponents hold regarding hulul and ittihad?
A) Three
B) Five
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The Christian view of incarnation is rejected by Islamic theology because it violates divine transcendence and the impossibility of union between Creator and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that the desire for union or indwelling (ittihad/hulul) stems from ignorance. Have you ever yearned for a spiritual experience that blurs the line between you and your Creator? In what ways are you tempted to 'become one' with God rather than submit to His absolute majesty and transcendence? This week, commit to a daily practice of reflecting on His greatness through His names, without seeking fusion.
?
The Christians restricted God's attributes to three. Do you, in your own concept of God, unconsciously limit His attributes to those that are comfortable or understandable to you? Are there attributes of God—like His overwhelming power, His detailed knowledge, or His justice—that you tend to downplay because they make you uncomfortable? Resolve to learn and affirm all of His names, not just the ones you like.
?
The passage warns that even those who affiliate with Islam can fall into errors of hulul and ittihad. Could there be any subtle beliefs or practices in your own life that imply that God dwells in certain places, objects, or people? How can you purify your tawhid so that you never, even in your heart, ascribe divine qualities to creation? Take a moment to repent from any such notion and renew your commitment to pure monotheism.

The Eight Possibilities of Hulul and Ittihad
Taftazani lists the eight possible forms of hulul and ittihad and their proponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight possible forms of hulul and ittihad as categorized by Taftazani, and why they are impossible for both the divine essence and attributes.

Core Idea
The eight possibilities of incarnation and union are all impossible for both God's essence and attributes.

Taftazani begins by reporting that doctrines of incarnation (hulul) and union (ittihad) are held by Christians and some Muslim extremists. He then establishes that these concepts are impossible for God's essence, and even more so for His attributes, because attributes cannot exist independently. He enumerates the eight possibilities entertained by opponents, categorizing them into groups of hulul and ittihad, each with four subtypes. This lesson unpacks those eight forms and identifies the proponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice how Taftazani first reports the doctrines, then states the impossibility, then lists the eight possibilities, and finally identifies the opponents.
Pay attention to the phrase 'والاحتمالات التي يذهب إليها أوهام المخالفين' which introduces the eight possibilities.
2
Identify the eight possibilities
The eight possibilities are four for hulul (incarnation) and four for ittihad (union). Each set consists of: essence or attribute in the human body or spirit. List them in your notes.
The text says 'حلول ذات الواجب أو صفته في بدن الإنسان أو روحه، وكذا الاتحاد' which implies the same four for ittihad.
3
Understand the impossibility extension
Taftazani argues that just as hulul/ittihad of the essence is impossible, so too for the attributes, and even more so because attributes cannot be separate from the essence. Reflect on why that strengthens the argument.
This addresses a potential misconception that only the essence is involved.
4
Recognize the proponents
The opponents are divided into two groups: Christians (regarding Jesus) and those who claim Islam (some ghulat and mutasawwifa). Note the reference to 'ghulat' and 'mutasawwifa' as groups that deviate.
The phrase 'وأما ما يدعي بعضهم من ارتفاع الكثرة عند الفناء' is set aside as a separate discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحلول والاتحاد محكى عن النصارى. قال: والاتحاد محكى عن النصارى في حق عيسى عليه السلام، وعن بعض الغلاة في حق أئمتهم وعن بعض المتصوفة في حق كلمتهم، وأما ما يدعي بعضهم من ارتفاع الكثرة عند الفناء في التوحيد، أو أنه لا كثرة في الوجود أصلاً فبحث آخر. والقول بالحلول يعني كما قامت الدلالة على امتناع الحلول والاتحاد على الذات فكذا على الصفات، بل أولى لاستحالة انتفاء الصفة عن الذات، والاحتمالات التي يذهب إليها أوهام المخالفين في هذا الأصل ثمانية. حلول ذات الواجب أو صفته في بدن الإنسان أو روحه، وكذا الاتحاد. والمخالفون منهم نصارى ومنهم منتمون إلى الإسلام.
Al-hulul (incarnation) and al-ittihad (union) are reported from the Christians. He said: al-ittihad is reported from the Christians regarding Jesus, peace be upon him, and from some ghulat (exaggerators) regarding their imams, and from some mutasawwifa (Sufis) regarding their word (kalam). As for what some claim of the removal of multiplicity at the time of annihilation (fana') in tawhid, or that there is no multiplicity in existence at all, that is another discussion. And the statement of hulul means, just as the proof has been established for the impossibility of hulul and ittihad of the Essence, so too regarding the attributes, rather it is more so, because the attribute cannot be separate from the Essence. The possibilities which the fantasies of the opponents in this principle lead to are eight: incarnation of the essence or its attribute in the human body or spirit, and likewise union. The opponents include Christians and some who claim belonging to Islam.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage defines the concepts of hulul and ittihad, and lists the eight possible forms. It begins by noting that the doctrine of incarnation and union is reported from Christians and some Muslim extremists. Taftazani then states that just as the impossibility of hulul and ittihad for the Essence of God is proven, it is likewise impossible for the attributes, and even more so because attributes cannot exist independently of the essence. The possibilities entertained by those who hold these views are enumerated as eight: four types of hulul (incarnation of the essence or attribute in the human body or spirit) and their corresponding four types of ittihad (union). Finally, he divides the opponents into two categories: Christians and those who claim to belong to Islam. This sets the stage for a detailed refutation of each group.

Key Terms

اتحاد
ذات
صفات
نصارى
غلاة

Misconception: Some think hulul and ittihad only apply to the divine essence, not to attributes.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that the impossibility applies to attributes as well, and even more strongly, because attributes cannot exist independently of the essence.

Key Takeaway
The eight possibilities of incarnation and union are all impossible for both God's essence and attributes, and the opponents include Christians and some who claim Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even unknowingly, imagined God as being confined to a particular place or person? Examine your heart: do you truly believe that God is utterly transcendent, or do you sometimes reduce Him to human-like presence? How can you purify your tawhid from any trace of incarnation?
?

?

How does this affect the way you think about God's attributes being manifest in creation? Will you guard your heart from even subtle forms of hulul?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
Correct Doctrine: Annihilation in Divine Unity (Fana')
Al-Taftazani distinguishes true spiritual annihilation from incarnation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct doctrine of spiritual annihilation (fana') in tawhid and how it differs from false doctrines of incarnation and union.

Core Idea
Spiritual annihilation is losing the illusion of otherness, not becoming God.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses two doctrines that might be mistaken for incarnation (hulul) or union (ittihad) but are actually orthodox. The first is the state of fana'—annihilation in divine unity. The seeker becomes so absorbed in contemplation of God that he loses awareness of his own existence and sees only God. This is a transformative spiritual experience, not a literal metaphysical change. Let's examine the text to understand this subtle but crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two schools
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the author mentions two schools that give the impression of incarnation and union. Focus on how he emphatically separates them from those false doctrines.
The passage only explains the first school; the second is mentioned but not detailed.
2
Understand the description of the first school
The first school describes the seeker's essence vanishing in God's essence and his attributes in God's attributes. The seeker becomes absent from everything else and sees only God. This is called 'annihilation in tawhid'.
The phrase 'vanishes in' (تضمحل) is key—it indicates a subjective state, not an actual ontological change.
3
Reflect on why this is not hulul or ittihad
The author explicitly says these schools 'give the impression of incarnation and union, but they are not part of it at all.' Why? Because fana' is about the seeker's perception, not a literal merging of essences.
The cutting off of the hadith suggests further scriptural support, but the point is clear: this is an orthodox spiritual state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وها هنا مذهبان آخران يوهمان بالحلول والاتحاد، وليسا منه في شيء. الأول: أن السالك إذا انتهى سلوكه إلى الله تعالى وفي الله يستغرق في بحر التوحيد والعرفان بحيث تضمحل ذاته في ذاته تعالى وصفاته في صفاته. ويغيب عن كل ما سواه، ولا يرى في الوجود إلا الله تعالى وهذا الذي يسمونه الفناء في التوحيد وإليه يشير الحديث الإلهي إن العبد لا يزال يتقرب إلي حتى...
And here there are two other schools that give the impression of incarnation and union, but they are not part of it at all. The first: that the seeker, when his spiritual wayfaring ends to God Most High and in God, he becomes immersed in the ocean of tawhid and gnosis such that his essence vanishes in His essence—exalted is He—and his attributes in His attributes. And he becomes absent from everything other than Him, and he sees nothing in existence except God Most High, and this is what they call annihilation (al-fana') in tawhid, and to it the divine hadith points: 'The servant continues to draw near to Me until...'
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage clarifies two correct doctrines that superficially resemble hulul and ittihad but are fundamentally different. The author names them as 'two other schools'. He then explains the first: it is the state of annihilation (fana') in tawhid. In this state, the seeker's self-awareness and awareness of created things vanish. He is so absorbed in contemplation of God's unity that he sees nothing but God. However, this is a subjective experience, not an actual ontological union. The author cites a divine hadith (hadith qudsi) to support this, though the quote is cut off. This doctrine is orthodox and does not imply incarnation.

التوحيد

الحلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fana' means actual union with God (ittihad) or incarnation (hulul).
Correction: Fana' is the annihilation of the perception of otherness, not literal merging; the seeker remains ontologically distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the seeker's essence and attributes in the state of fana'?
A) They become identical with God's essence and attributes
B) They vanish or become absent from his perception
C) They are transformed into divine attributes
D) They separate from the body

Key Takeaway
Fana' is a state of profound spiritual absorption where the seeker loses awareness of creation and sees only God, but without any metaphysical union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to love God above all else, can you honestly say that you would be content if your own identity—your desires, your achievements, your relationships—were to vanish from your awareness, leaving only Him? What does your hesitation reveal about your heart?
?
The seeker in this passage sees nothing but God. What is the one created thing you are most afraid to lose sight of? Will you ask God today to help you become absent from it, trusting that He alone is enough?
?
If the spiritual journey ends in fana’, then every attachment that survives is a competitor to His Oneness. What are you still clutching that prevents you from drowning in that ocean? How long will you keep it?
