Refutation of Divine Incarnation and Indwelling
Author rejects claims that God appears in human form or indwells mystics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claims of God appearing in human form or dwelling in a mystic are rationally and theologically unacceptable.

Core Idea
God's transcendence precludes any form of physical indwelling or union.

The author addresses two extreme claims: that God may appear in human form (analogous to angelic apparitions), and that a mystic may become a locus for divine indwelling, even uttering 'He is I.' These views violate divine transcendence, and the author dismisses them as self-evidently false. Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very dismissive tone at the end.
2
Identify the two erroneous views
The first view claims God can appear in human form, analogizing from angels. The second view claims God can dwell in or unite with a mystic. Find these in the text.
The first view is linked to Ali and his descendants; the second to certain Sufis.
3
Understand the author's dismissal
The author says 'the corruption of both views is clear without need for explanation.' Ask yourself why he considers them so obviously false.
Think about Islamic teachings on God's transcendence (tanzih).
4
Reflect on theological implications
Consider what this passage teaches about the nature of God in Islam. How does it protect divine transcendence?
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكبعض يمتنع ظهور الروحاني بالجسماني كجبريل في صورة دحية الكلبي الجن أو الشياطين في صورة الأناسى فلا يبعد أن يظهر الله تعالى في صورة بعض الكاملين. وأولى الناس بذلك علي وأولاده المخصوصون الذين هم خير البرية، والعلم وفي الكلمات العلمية والعملية فلهذا كان يصدر عنهم في العلوم والأعمال ما هو فوق الطاقة البشرية . ومنهم بعض المتصوفة القائلون بأن السالك إذا أمعن في السلوك، وخاصة لجهة الأصول، فربما يحل الله فيه تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علواً كبيراً ، وكالنار في الحجر، بحيث لا يتمايز أو يتحد به بحيث لا اثنينية ولا تغاير . وصح أن يقول : هو أنا ، وأنا هو . وحينئذ يرتفع الأمر والنهي، ويظهر من الغرائب والعجائب ما لا يتصور من البشر، وفساد الرأيين غني عن البيان.
And some [say] that it is not impossible for the spiritual to appear in the physical, like Jibril in the form of Dihyah al-Kalbi, and jinn or devils in the form of humans, so it is not far-fetched that God Almighty may appear in the form of some of the perfect ones. And the most deserving of people for that are Ali and his special descendants, who are the best of creation, and knowledge, and in the scientific and practical words; therefore, things beyond human capacity used to issue from them in sciences and actions. And among them are some Sufis who say that when the seeker proceeds deeply in the spiritual path, especially towards the fundamentals, perhaps God may dwell in him—exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation—like fire in stone, such that there is no distinction, or [He] unites with him such that there is no duality and no otherness. And it is considered valid for him to say: 'He is I, and I am He.' And then command and prohibition are lifted, and wonders and marvels beyond human imagination appear. The corruption of both views is clear without need for explanation.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage rejects two erroneous views. The first is the claim that it is possible for God to appear in human form, analogizing from angels and jinn taking physical form. The author mentions that some use this to elevate Ali and his descendants to superhuman status. The second is a Sufi claim that God may dwell in the seeker, leading to the statement 'He is I' and the lifting of divine commands. The author dismisses both as self-evidently false, emphasizing that their corruption needs no elaboration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the author rejects all Sufi practices or the veneration of Ali and his family.
Correction: The passage only targets extreme claims of hulul (indwelling) and ittihad (union), not moderate Sufi or Shia beliefs.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the corruption of the two views?
A) It requires extensive proof.
B) It is clearly evident.
C) It is debated among scholars.
D) It is acceptable in some contexts.

Key Takeaway
Claims of divine incarnation or indwelling are fundamentally incompatible with Islamic theology and are dismissed as self-evidently false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, in your desire for closeness to God, entertained the thought that He might dwell within you or be united with you? What does this passage teach you about the danger of such a belief, and how will you guard your heart against it?
?
When you hear claims of miraculous abilities from certain individuals, do you instinctively accept them because of their spiritual status? How does the author's dismissal of superhuman claims from Ali's descendants challenge your tendency to elevate humans, and what will you do to ensure your trust remains solely in God?
?
The author says the corruption of these views is 'clear without need for explanation.' Is it truly clear to you? If not, what does that reveal about your understanding of God's transcendence, and what concrete step will you take today to deepen that understanding (e.g., memorizing verses of tanzih, studying attributes)?

Understanding Fanā' and the Hadith of Divine Closeness
Author discusses Sufi fanā' and refutes literalist interpretations of a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proper interpretation of the hadith 'I become his hearing...' and why it does not imply pantheism or indwelling.

Core Idea
Divine closeness is experiential, not literal indwelling or union.

The hadith of divine closeness is often misunderstood. In this passage, the author explains that the language of such experiences is limited, and that fanā' is a path of direct vision, not rational proof. He also refutes the pantheistic notion that all existence is one absolute being. This lesson helps you navigate these subtle distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and author's caution
Start by reading the quoted part of the hadith and the author's note that expressions of indwelling or union arise from inadequate language, not reality.
Notice the author immediately distances himself from literal interpretations.
2
Understand fanā' as experiential
The author states that the path of fanā' is through direct vision (ʿiyān), not demonstration (burhān). Grasp the distinction between experiential knowledge and rational proof.
3
Refutation of absolute existence
The author presents and rejects the view that the Necessary Being is absolute existence without multiplicity. Understand why he considers this outside reason and revelation.
This part references an earlier discussion on existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحبه، فإذا أحببته كنت سمعه الذي به يسمع، وبصره الذي به يبصر (1) وحينئذ ربما يصدر عنه عبارات تشعر بالحلول أو الاتحاد لقصور العبارة عن بيان تلك الحال، وتعذر (2) الكشف عنها بالمقال. ونحن على سبيل التمني، نفترق من بحر التوحيد بقدر الإمكان، ونعترف بأن طريق الفناء فيه العيان دون البرهان والله الموفق . الثاني : أن الواجب هو الوجود المطلق ، وهو واحد لا كثرة فيه أصلاً، وإنما الكثرة في الإضافات والتعينات التي هي بمنزلة الخيال والسراب، إذ الكل في الحقيقة واحد يتكرر على مظاهر لا بطريق المخالطة، وبتكثره في النواظر، لا بطريق الانقسام، فلا حلول ها هنا، ولا اتحاد لعدم الاثنينية والغيرية وكلامهم في ذلك طويل خارج عن طريق العقل والشرح . وقد أشرنا في بحث الوجود إلى بطلانه ولكن من يضلل الله فما له من هاد. (1) . الحديث رواه الإمام البخاري في الرقاق 38 وأحمد بن حنبل جـ 6 : 256 ولفظه عند البخاري: إن الله قال : من عادى لي ولياً فقد آذنته بالحرب وما تقرب إلي عبدي بشيء أحب إلي مما افترضته عليه وما يزال عبدي يتقرب إلي بالنوافل حتى أحبه فإذا أحببته كنت سمعه الذي يسمع به وبصره الذي يبصر به ويده التي يبطش بها ورجله التي يمشي بها وإن سألني لاعطينه ولئن استعاذ بي عبدي لأعيذنه وما ترددت عن شيء أنا فاعله ترددي عن نفس المؤمن يكره الموت وأنا أكره مساءته». (2) في (ب) وبعد بدلاً من (وتعذر) .
I love him, and when I love him, I become his hearing by which he hears, and his sight by which he sees. (1) And then perhaps expressions issue from him that suggest indwelling (al-ḥulūl) or union (al-ittiḥād) due to the inadequacy of expression to describe that state, and the difficulty (2) of revealing it through speech. And we, by way of aspiration, separate ourselves from the sea of divine unity (al-tawḥīd) as much as possible, and we acknowledge that the path of annihilation (al-fanā') in it is through direct vision (al-ʿiyān) not demonstration (al-burhān). And Allah is the guide. Second: The Necessary Being is absolute existence (al-wujūd al-muṭlaq), and it is one with no multiplicity at all; multiplicity is only in relations and particularizations that are like illusion and mirage. For everything in reality is one, repeated in manifestations not by way of mixing, and multiplied in the eyes not by way of division. So there is no indwelling here, nor union, because there is no duality or otherness. Their discourse on this is lengthy, outside the way of reason and revelation. And we have already indicated its invalidity in the discussion of existence. But whoever Allah leads astray, there is no guide for him. (1) The hadith was narrated by Imam al-Bukhari in al-Riqāq (38) and Ahmad ibn Hanbal (vol. 6, p. 256). Its wording according to al-Bukhari: 'Allah said: Whoever is hostile to a friend of Mine, I declare war against him. My servant does not draw near to Me with anything more beloved to Me than what I have made obligatory upon him. My servant continues to draw near to Me with supererogatory works until I love him. Then I become his hearing with which he hears, his sight with which he sees, his hand with which he strikes, and his foot with which he walks. If he asks Me, I will surely give him; if he seeks refuge in Me, I will surely protect him. I do not hesitate about anything as much as I hesitate about taking the soul of My faithful servant: he hates death, and I hate to disappoint him.' (2) In (b) 'after' instead of 'and the difficulty'.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is an argument and commentary by the author on the famous hadith regarding divine closeness. The author begins by quoting part of the hadith: 'I love him, and when I love him, I become his hearing...' He then notes that such expressions may lead to statements implying indwelling (ḥulūl) or union (ittibād) due to the incapacity of language to fully describe the spiritual state. The author distances himself from such interpretations, emphasizing that the path of fanā' is experiential, not demonstrative. He then presents a second view: that the Necessary Being is absolute existence, without multiplicity, and that apparent multiplicity is illusory. The author rejects this as well, stating it is outside reason and revelation. He concludes the first part by referencing his earlier refutation. He then provides the full citation and text of the hadith from Bukhari. The overall purpose is to caution against literalist or pantheistic readings of the hadith.

Key Terms
فناء
حلول
اتحاد
توحيد
وجود مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith implies that Allah literally becomes the servant's physical senses.
Correction: The author explains that such expressions arise from the inadequacy of language to describe the spiritual state, not from actual indwelling or transformation.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why might statements suggesting indwelling or union be issued by people experiencing fanā'?
A) Because they have attained actual union with God.
B) Because language is inadequate to describe the experience.
C) Because they follow the Karramiyya sect.
D) Because they reject the hadith.

Key Takeaway
Fanā' is a state of direct experience, not rational proof, and the language of divine closeness in the hadith should not be taken literally as implying indwelling or pantheistic union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel intense closeness to Allah in your worship, do you ever catch yourself describing it in terms that subtly imply you have become part of Him or that He dwells within you? Will you consciously refuse to utter such words, even if they arise from the heart?
?
This lesson insists that the path of fanā' is through direct vision, not rational proof. Are you content to merely understand Allah intellectually, or are you actively seeking that direct, experiential knowledge? What one concrete change will you make today to transform your study into intimate encounter?
?
The author distances himself from literal indwelling with clear condemnation. Search your own heart: have you ever, even unintentionally, harbored a thought that Allah is 'inside' you or that you and He are not distinct? How will you purify your conception of tawḥīd from such subtle slips?

Defining the Dispute on Temporal Accidents
Al-Taftazani defines the scope of the dispute and refutes a preliminary argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'temporal accident' in this dispute and why relational attributes are not included.

Core Idea
Not all temporal associations are real accidents; only those entailing change are disputed.

Al-Taftazani opens by clarifying exactly what is under dispute. The Karramiyya claimed that God can be qualified by temporal accidents in the sense of things that come into existence after non-existence. But not all temporal relationships—like being a creator at a certain time—imply a change in God's essence. This lesson sets the boundaries of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining the dispute
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note how al-Taftazani distinguishes between real temporal accidents and relational or negative attributes.
The term 'al-ḥādith' here means 'temporal accident' as something that originates after non-existence.
2
Understand the Karramiyya position
The Karramiyya (a theological group) held that God can be described with temporal accidents. Al-Taftazani refutes this by limiting the scope.
3
Analyze the refutation of a false premise
Some argued that the corrector for qualification is the attribute itself, regardless of pre-eternity. Al-Taftazani shows this is invalid because pre-eternity might be a condition.
Notice the three possibilities he offers: the corrector is the pre-eternal attribute itself, or pre-eternity is a condition, or temporality is a preventer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
امتناع اتصاف الواجب بالحوادث
( في امتناع اتصافه بالحادث بمعنى الموجود بعد العدم خلافاً للكرامية، وأما الاتصاف بما له تعلقات حادثة أو بما يتجدد من السلوب والإضافات والأحوال فليس من المتنازع فلا يصلح تمسكاً لهم. والاستدلال بأن المصحح للاتصاف هو مطلق الصفة إذ لا عبرة بالقدم لكونه عدمياً فاسد لجواز أن يكون المصحح حقيقة الصفة القديمة أو يكون القدم شرطاً أو الحدوث مانعاً. لنا وجوه :
الأول : الإجماع على أن ما يصح عليه إن كان صفة كمال لم يخل عنه وإلا لم يتصف به
الثاني : أن الاتصاف بالحادث تغير، وهو عليه محال .
الثالث : أنه لو جاز لجاز في الأزل لاستحالة الانقلاب وهو يستلزم جواز وجود الحادث في الأزل لامتناع الاتصاف بالشيء بدونه .
الرابع : أنه لو جاز لزم عدم خلوه عن الحادث لاتصافه قبل ذلك الحادث بضده الحادث لزواله وبقابليته الحادث لما مر . واستضعف الأول بأن يجوز أن تكون الحوادث كمالات متلاحقة مشروطاً ابتداء الكل بانقضاء الآخر وفيه نظر. والثاني : بأن التغيير بمعنى تبدل في الصفات من غير تأثر عن الغير نفس المتنازع، والثالث بأن اللازم أزلية الجواز والمحال جواز الأزلية، والرابع : منع الملازمة )
The Fourth Section: The Impossibility of the Eternal Being Being Qualified by Temporal Accidents. (On the impossibility of His being qualified by the temporal accident in the sense of that which exists after non-existence, contrary to the Karramiyya. As for His being qualified by that which has temporal relationships, or by what is renewed of negations, relations, and states, that is not part of the disputed matter, so it cannot be used as a proof for them. And the argument that the corrector for qualification is the attribute absolutely, since there is no consideration for pre-eternity because it is non-existent, is invalid because it is possible that the corrector is the reality of the pre-eternal attribute, or that pre-eternity is a condition, or that temporality is a preventer. We have proofs: First: consensus that whatever is correct for [Him], if it is an attribute of perfection, He is not devoid of it; otherwise He is not qualified by it. Second: that being qualified by a temporal accident is change, and change is impossible for Him. Third: that if it were permissible, it would be permissible in pre-eternity because of the impossibility of transformation, and this necessitates the permissibility of the existence of a temporal accident in pre-eternity because being qualified by something without it is impossible. Fourth: that if it were permissible, it would necessitate that He is never free from a temporal accident, because before that temporal accident He would be qualified by its opposite, which is temporal, due to its passing away and His receptivity to the temporal accident, according to what has passed. And the first was weakened by saying that it is possible that temporal accidents are successive perfections, the beginning of all of them being conditioned by the end of the other, and there is consideration in this. And the second: that change in the sense of alteration in attributes without being affected by another is the very matter of dispute. And the third: that what is necessary is the pre-eternity of permissibility, but the impossibility is the impossibility of pre-eternity. And the fourth: denial of the necessary implication.)
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the main topic and four proofs for the impossibility of the necessary existent (al-wājib) being qualified by temporal accidents (al-ḥawādith). The author begins by clarifying that the dispute is specifically about real temporal accidents (existing after non-existence) as claimed by the Karramiyya, not about relational attributes or negative attributes which are permissible. Then he refutes an opposing argument that the corrector for qualification is the attribute absolutely, regardless of pre-eternity. The author then gives four proofs: First, consensus (ijmāʿ) that if the necessary existent can have a perfection attribute, He must have it eternally; otherwise He does not have it at all. Second, being qualified by a temporal accident implies change (taghayyur), which is impossible for the eternal. Third, if such qualification were possible, it would have been possible in pre-eternity (due to the impossibility of transformation from non-qualification to qualification) which leads to the absurdity of a temporal accident existing in pre-eternity. Fourth, if it were possible, then the necessary existent would never be free from temporal accidents, because before a given accident He would have its opposite (also temporal) and must be receptive to it. After the proofs, the author addresses and refutes potential objections to each proof, showing that the first objection (that temporal accidents could be successive perfections) is debatable, the second (that change is just the matter of dispute) is invalid, the third (that pre-eternity of permissibility is impossible) is denied, and the fourth (denial of necessary implication) is countered by affirming the implication.

الكرامية

القدم
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that any attribute that arises in time, like being the creator at a specific moment, implies a change in God's essence.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that such relational attributes are not real temporal accidents and are not part of the dispute.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the meaning of 'al-ḥādith' that is disputed?
A) That which exists after non-existence
B) Any attribute that changes over time
C) Relational attributes like 'Creator'
D) Negative attributes like 'not ignorant'

Key Takeaway
The dispute is specifically about real temporal accidents that come into existence after non-existence, not about relational or negative attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God 'changes His mind' or 'responds differently' based on your actions, as if time affects Him? How does this lesson force you to uproot that subtle anthropomorphism from your heart? What concrete steps will you take to consistently affirm God's transcendence over time and change?
?
This lesson distinguishes between real temporal accidents and relational or negative attributes. Do you truly believe that God's essence is unchanged even though He is now your 'Creator' and 'Provider'? If you are honest, does your worship sometimes assume God is affected by your worship? How will you cultivate a deeper awe of His perfection?
?
When Al-Taftazani presents the possibilities for the muṣaḥḥiḥ, he forces you to think about why God's attributes are what they are. Are you content to accept divine attributes without questioning? Or does your heart yearn to understand God's glory more deeply? What will you do to increase your knowledge of His names and attributes, so your love and fear of Him grow?

Proofs from Consensus and Impossibility of Change
Al-Taftazani presents the first two proofs: consensus and impossibility of change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two rational proofs for why the eternal being cannot be qualified by temporal accidents: consensus and the impossibility of change.

Core Idea
God cannot change, and consensus confirms He lacks temporal perfections.

After clarifying the scope, al-Taftazani presents his first two proofs. The first is based on consensus (ijmāʿ) that if something is compatible with God as a perfection, He must have it eternally; otherwise He does not have it at all. The second proof argues that being qualified by a temporal accident entails change, which is impossible for the necessary existent. These two proofs establish a foundational barrier against temporal accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof (consensus)
Read the Arabic for 'al-awwal: al-ijmāʿ...' and its translation. Understand the logical structure: if an attribute of perfection is possible for God, He must have it eternally; if not, He cannot have it at all.
This argument relies on the principle that God is perfect and lacks nothing potentially perfect.
2
Read the second proof (change)
The second proof: being qualified by a temporal accident is a change, and change is impossible for God. Consider why change is impossible for the necessary existent.
Change implies potentiality and dependence, which are incompatible with necessary existence.
3
Connect to the broader argument
These two proofs are the first in a series of four. They collectively establish why temporal accidents cannot inhere in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
امتناع اتصاف الواجب بالحوادث
( في امتناع اتصافه بالحادث بمعنى الموجود بعد العدم خلافاً للكرامية، وأما الاتصاف بما له تعلقات حادثة أو بما يتجدد من السلوب والإضافات والأحوال فليس من المتنازع فلا يصلح تمسكاً لهم. والاستدلال بأن المصحح للاتصاف هو مطلق الصفة إذ لا عبرة بالقدم لكونه عدمياً فاسد لجواز أن يكون المصحح حقيقة الصفة القديمة أو يكون القدم شرطاً أو الحدوث مانعاً. لنا وجوه :
الأول : الإجماع على أن ما يصح عليه إن كان صفة كمال لم يخل عنه وإلا لم يتصف به
الثاني : أن الاتصاف بالحادث تغير، وهو عليه محال .
الثالث : أنه لو جاز لجاز في الأزل لاستحالة الانقلاب وهو يستلزم جواز وجود الحادث في الأزل لامتناع الاتصاف بالشيء بدونه .
الرابع : أنه لو جاز لزم عدم خلوه عن الحادث لاتصافه قبل ذلك الحادث بضده الحادث لزواله وبقابليته الحادث لما مر . واستضعف الأول بأن يجوز أن تكون الحوادث كمالات متلاحقة مشروطاً ابتداء الكل بانقضاء الآخر وفيه نظر. والثاني : بأن التغيير بمعنى تبدل في الصفات من غير تأثر عن الغير نفس المتنازع، والثالث بأن اللازم أزلية الجواز والمحال جواز الأزلية، والرابع : منع الملازمة )
The Fourth Section: The Impossibility of the Eternal Being Being Qualified by Temporal Accidents. (On the impossibility of His being qualified by the temporal accident in the sense of that which exists after non-existence, contrary to the Karramiyya. As for His being qualified by that which has temporal relationships, or by what is renewed of negations, relations, and states, that is not part of the disputed matter, so it cannot be used as a proof for them. And the argument that the corrector for qualification is the attribute absolutely, since there is no consideration for pre-eternity because it is non-existent, is invalid because it is possible that the corrector is the reality of the pre-eternal attribute, or that pre-eternity is a condition, or that temporality is a preventer. We have proofs: First: consensus that whatever is correct for [Him], if it is an attribute of perfection, He is not devoid of it; otherwise He is not qualified by it. Second: that being qualified by a temporal accident is change, and change is impossible for Him. Third: that if it were permissible, it would be permissible in pre-eternity because of the impossibility of transformation, and this necessitates the permissibility of the existence of a temporal accident in pre-eternity because being qualified by something without it is impossible. Fourth: that if it were permissible, it would necessitate that He is never free from a temporal accident, because before that temporal accident He would be qualified by its opposite, which is temporal, due to its passing away and His receptivity to the temporal accident, according to what has passed. And the first was weakened by saying that it is possible that temporal accidents are successive perfections, the beginning of all of them being conditioned by the end of the other, and there is consideration in this. And the second: that change in the sense of alteration in attributes without being affected by another is the very matter of dispute. And the third: that what is necessary is the pre-eternity of permissibility, but the impossibility is the impossibility of pre-eternity. And the fourth: denial of the necessary implication.)
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the main topic and four proofs for the impossibility of the necessary existent (al-wājib) being qualified by temporal accidents (al-ḥawādith). The author begins by clarifying that the dispute is specifically about real temporal accidents (existing after non-existence) as claimed by the Karramiyya, not about relational attributes or negative attributes which are permissible. Then he refutes an opposing argument that the corrector for qualification is the attribute absolutely, regardless of pre-eternity. The author then gives four proofs: First, consensus (ijmāʿ) that if the necessary existent can have a perfection attribute, He must have it eternally; otherwise He does not have it at all. Second, being qualified by a temporal accident implies change (taghayyur), which is impossible for the eternal. Third, if such qualification were possible, it would have been possible in pre-eternity (due to the impossibility of transformation from non-qualification to qualification) which leads to the absurdity of a temporal accident existing in pre-eternity. Fourth, if it were possible, then the necessary existent would never be free from temporal accidents, because before a given accident He would have its opposite (also temporal) and must be receptive to it. After the proofs, the author addresses and refutes potential objections to each proof, showing that the first objection (that temporal accidents could be successive perfections) is debatable, the second (that change is just the matter of dispute) is invalid, the third (that pre-eternity of permissibility is impossible) is denied, and the fourth (denial of necessary implication) is countered by affirming the implication.

Key Terms
الإجماع
التغير
الكمال
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God can acquire new attributes over time, like becoming 'Creator' when creation occurs.
Correction: The first proof shows that if an attribute is a perfection, He must have it eternally; if not, He never has it. Temporal accidents are not eternal, so they cannot be attributed.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof (al-ijmāʿ), if an attribute is a perfection but not possible for God, what is the ruling?
A) He is qualified by it
B) He is not qualified by it
C) He is only qualified by it in this world
D) He may be qualified by it occasionally

Key Takeaway
The first two proofs establish that temporal accidents cannot be attributed to God because they are either not perfections or they entail change, which is impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what way have you subconsciously attributed change to God, perhaps by thinking He 'becomes' more pleased with you after good deeds? How does the second proof (that change is impossible for Him) correct your understanding and deepen your trust in His constant mercy?
?
The consensus of scholars is that God possesses all perfections and is devoid of imperfection. Have you ever doubted this principle when facing hardships? Reflect on a time when you questioned God's perfect attribute of justice or mercy. How does the first proof (consensus on perfection and imperfection) ground your trust in God's attributes even when your circumstances seem to contradict them?
?
If God cannot change, then His promises are absolutely reliable. Yet we often act as if we doubt His consistency. What specific area of your life reveals that you are not fully relying on God's unchanging nature? What step will you take today to align your actions with this truth?

The Third Proof: Impossibility of Transformation
Al-Taftazani presents the third proof, arguing from the impossibility of transformation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof: if a temporal accident were possible for God, it would have been possible in pre-eternity, leading to the absurdity of a temporal accident in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
If God could acquire a temporal accident now, He could have acquired it always, which is impossible.

The third proof is more subtle. It argues that if it were permissible for the necessary existent to be qualified by a temporal accident at some time, then it would have been permissible in pre-eternity as well. This is because the transition from not being qualified to being qualified is a transformation (inqilāb), which is impossible for the eternal being. Therefore, the only consistent possibility is that it was always permissible, but that would require a temporal accident to exist in pre-eternity, which is a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof in Arabic
Read the Arabic for 'al-thālith...' and the translation. Notice the logical steps: permissibility now implies permissibility in pre-eternity, which would entail existence of a temporal accident in pre-eternity.
Key concepts: 'jāza' (permissible), 'al-azal' (pre-eternity), 'inqilāb' (transformation).
2
Understand the impossibility of transformation
Why is transformation impossible? Because it would mean God changes from a state of not having an attribute to having it, which implies change.
The proof says 'li-stiḥālat al-inqilāb' (due to the impossibility of transformation).
3
Grasp the absurd conclusion
If permissible in pre-eternity, then a temporal accident would exist in pre-eternity, but temporal accidents cannot exist in pre-eternity by definition.
A temporal accident (al-ḥādith) is something that comes into existence after non-existence, so it cannot be eternal.

Source Text

Divine Transcendence

The Fourth Proof: Never Free from Temporal Accidents
Al-Taftazani presents the fourth proof, showing that permissibility leads to an infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof: if God could be qualified by a temporal accident, He would never be free from them, leading to an infinite regress or a contradiction.

Core Idea
If God could have one temporal accident, He would have to have an endless series of them, which is absurd.

The fourth proof builds on the same assumption. If it is permissible for God to be qualified by a temporal accident at one time, then before that accident He must have been qualified by its opposite (also temporal), because the opposite would have been present and then ceased. This would require an endless chain of temporal accidents, implying that God is never free from them. This contradicts the fact that temporal accidents are originated and dependent.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth proof
Read the Arabic for 'al-rābiʿ...' and the translation. Focus on the reasoning: if a temporal accident occurs, before it there must be its opposite, which is also temporal, leading to an infinite series.
Key phrase: 'lā yakhlu ʿan al-ḥādith' (never free from a temporal accident).
2
Understand the infinite regress
The proof shows that if one accident is accepted, a prior one must exist, and so on, resulting in an infinite chain.
This is a version of the argument that whatever can accept temporal accidents must always be subject to them.
3
See the absurdity
An infinite regress of temporal accidents is impossible because they are all contingent and would require a beginning.
The proof does not elaborate further; al-Taftazani simply states the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that even one temporal accident would lead to an endless chain of change in Allah. Reflect: Are there areas of your life where you secretly wish Allah would change—perhaps to fit your desires or to accommodate your excuses? How does knowing that Allah is free from all temporal accidents challenge you to submit to His perfect, unchanging attributes rather than asking Him to bend to your circumstances? Commit today to a specific prayer where you praise Him for His immutability.
?
Consider the infinite regress: each accident demands a previous one. Think of a small compromise you have allowed in your faith—a 'temporal accident' in your worship or character. Have you considered that such compromises, if left unchecked, can lead to an endless chain of spiritual decline? What is the 'first accident' you need to eliminate to stop the regress? Name it and resolve to remove it this week.

Refuting Objections to the Four Proofs
Al-Taftazani refutes objections to the four proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections raised against each of the four proofs and how al-Taftazani responds to them.

Core Idea
Each proof faces a specific objection, but al-Taftazani shows they are invalid.

After presenting the four proofs, al-Taftazani addresses potential criticisms. Each proof is challenged, and he provides a brief rebuttal. The first objection suggests temporal accidents could be successive perfections; the second claims change is just the matter of dispute; the third distinguishes between permissibility and actuality; the fourth denies the necessary implication. Understanding these responses deepens the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and responses
Read the Arabic from 'wa-stuḍʿifa' to the end. Notice the structure: each proof is weakened (istuḍʿifa) and then a response is implied or given.
The term 'istuḍʿifa' means 'was weakened' indicating an objection.
2
Analyze the first objection
To the consensus proof: Objectors say temporal accidents could be successive perfections. Al-Taftazani says 'fīhi naẓar' (there is consideration), implying the objection is dubious.
3
Analyze the second objection
To the change proof: Objectors say change in attributes without being affected is exactly the matter of dispute. Al-Taftazani retorts that this is just redefining the dispute, not a valid objection.
4
Analyze the third objection
To the third proof: Objectors say what is necessary is the pre-eternity of permissibility, but the impossible is the impossibility of pre-eternity. Al-Taftazani rebuts by denying the distinction.
5
Analyze the fourth objection
To the fourth proof: Objectors deny the necessary implication. Al-Taftazani implies the implication is valid.
The text only says 'manʿ al-mulāzama' but the context shows rejection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع
امتناع اتصاف الواجب بالحوادث
( في امتناع اتصافه بالحادث بمعنى الموجود بعد العدم خلافاً للكرامية، وأما الاتصاف بما له تعلقات حادثة أو بما يتجدد من السلوب والإضافات والأحوال فليس من المتنازع فلا يصلح تمسكاً لهم. والاستدلال بأن المصحح للاتصاف هو مطلق الصفة إذ لا عبرة بالقدم لكونه عدمياً فاسد لجواز أن يكون المصحح حقيقة الصفة القديمة أو يكون القدم شرطاً أو الحدوث مانعاً. لنا وجوه :
الأول : الإجماع على أن ما يصح عليه إن كان صفة كمال لم يخل عنه وإلا لم يتصف به
الثاني : أن الاتصاف بالحادث تغير، وهو عليه محال .
الثالث : أنه لو جاز لجاز في الأزل لاستحالة الانقلاب وهو يستلزم جواز وجود الحادث في الأزل لامتناع الاتصاف بالشيء بدونه .
الرابع : أنه لو جاز لزم عدم خلوه عن الحادث لاتصافه قبل ذلك الحادث بضده الحادث لزواله وبقابليته الحادث لما مر . واستضعف الأول بأن يجوز أن تكون الحوادث كمالات متلاحقة مشروطاً ابتداء الكل بانقضاء الآخر وفيه نظر. والثاني : بأن التغيير بمعنى تبدل في الصفات من غير تأثر عن الغير نفس المتنازع، والثالث بأن اللازم أزلية الجواز والمحال جواز الأزلية، والرابع : منع الملازمة )
The Fourth Section: The Impossibility of the Eternal Being Being Qualified by Temporal Accidents. (On the impossibility of His being qualified by the temporal accident in the sense of that which exists after non-existence, contrary to the Karramiyya. As for His being qualified by that which has temporal relationships, or by what is renewed of negations, relations, and states, that is not part of the disputed matter, so it cannot be used as a proof for them. And the argument that the corrector for qualification is the attribute absolutely, since there is no consideration for pre-eternity because it is non-existent, is invalid because it is possible that the corrector is the reality of the pre-eternal attribute, or that pre-eternity is a condition, or that temporality is a preventer. We have proofs: First: consensus that whatever is correct for [Him], if it is an attribute of perfection, He is not devoid of it; otherwise He is not qualified by it. Second: that being qualified by a temporal accident is change, and change is impossible for Him. Third: that if it were permissible, it would be permissible in pre-eternity because of the impossibility of transformation, and this necessitates the permissibility of the existence of a temporal accident in pre-eternity because being qualified by something without it is impossible. Fourth: that if it were permissible, it would necessitate that He is never free from a temporal accident, because before that temporal accident He would be qualified by its opposite, which is temporal, due to its passing away and His receptivity to the temporal accident, according to what has passed. And the first was weakened by saying that it is possible that temporal accidents are successive perfections, the beginning of all of them being conditioned by the end of the other, and there is consideration in this. And the second: that change in the sense of alteration in attributes without being affected by another is the very matter of dispute. And the third: that what is necessary is the pre-eternity of permissibility, but the impossibility is the impossibility of pre-eternity. And the fourth: denial of the necessary implication.)
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents the main topic and four proofs for the impossibility of the necessary existent (al-wājib) being qualified by temporal accidents (al-ḥawādith). The author begins by clarifying that the dispute is specifically about real temporal accidents (existing after non-existence) as claimed by the Karramiyya, not about relational attributes or negative attributes which are permissible. Then he refutes an opposing argument that the corrector for qualification is the attribute absolutely, regardless of pre-eternity. The author then gives four proofs: First, consensus (ijmāʿ) that if the necessary existent can have a perfection attribute, He must have it eternally; otherwise He does not have it at all. Second, being qualified by a temporal accident implies change (taghayyur), which is impossible for the eternal. Third, if such qualification were possible, it would have been possible in pre-eternity (due to the impossibility of transformation from non-qualification to qualification) which leads to the absurdity of a temporal accident existing in pre-eternity. Fourth, if it were possible, then the necessary existent would never be free from temporal accidents, because before a given accident He would have its opposite (also temporal) and must be receptive to it. After the proofs, the author addresses and refutes potential objections to each proof, showing that the first objection (that temporal accidents could be successive perfections) is debatable, the second (that change is just the matter of dispute) is invalid, the third (that pre-eternity of permissibility is impossible) is denied, and the fourth (denial of necessary implication) is countered by affirming the implication.

الاستضعاف
الملازمة
النظر
المتنازع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objections successfully refute the proofs.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows each objection is weak or question-begging, reaffirming the proofs.

Checkpoint
How does al-Taftazani respond to the second objection (about change being the matter of dispute)?
A) He accepts it
B) He says it is the very matter of dispute, implying it is an invalid objection
C) He provides a new proof
D) He ignores it

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani refutes each objection, showing they do not undermine the four proofs against temporal accidents.

The second objector redefined 'change' to avoid the proof. In your own life, are there areas where you redefine terms to avoid confronting uncomfortable truths about your faith or actions? What might it cost you to stop redefining and accept the matter as it is?
?
If you were to face a logical argument that seems to contradict what you know about God, would you trust that there is a refutation you haven't seen, or would your faith waver? How can you prepare your heart to stand firm in the face of such challenges?

Permissible Attributes and Refuting al-Razi's Objection
Al-Taftazani enumerates permissible divine attributes and refutes al-Razi's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which types of attributes are permissible for God according to the majority of Islamic theologians, and how this refutes al-Razi's objection that all sects are committed to temporal accidents in God's essence.

Core Idea
God's attributes can change in relation without changing His essence.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses a key theological tension: how can God be described with new attributes (like being the creator of a specific person) without implying change in His eternal essence? He clarifies that certain types of attributes—negations, relations, and real attributes with changing relations—are permissible because they do not affect God's essence. This understanding provides a direct response to Imam al-Razi's charge that all Islamic sects inevitably ascribe temporal accidents to God.

Guided Path
1
Understand the central prohibition
The passage begins by stating the majority view: God cannot be qualified by a temporal accident (something that begins to exist). The Karramiyya are the exception. This sets the boundary.
2
Identify the three permissible categories
Al-Taftazani explains three types of attributes that are permissible despite involving change on the creature's side: negations/relations, real attributes with changing relations, and states (aḥwāl) according to Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. Note that each involves a change in the correlate, not in God's essence.
3
See how this refutes al-Razi
With these categories established, al-Taftazani shows that the Ash'ari position does not entail God being a locus for temporal accidents. The new relations that arise when a creature is created are merely relational, not essential changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الرابع : الجمهور على أن الواجب يمتنع أن يتصف بالحادث أي الموجود بعد العدم خلافاً للكرامية . وأما اتصافه بالسلوب والإضافات الحاصلة بعد ما لم تكن ككونه غير رازق لزيد الميت، رازقاً لعمر المولود وبالصفات الحقيقية المتغيرة المتعلقات ككونه عالماً بهذا الحادث قادراً عليه فجائز، وكذا بالأحوال المتحققة بعد ما لم تكن كالعالميات المتجددة بتجدد المعلومات عند أبي الحسين البصري على ما سيجيء تحقيق ذلك، وبهذا يندفع ما ذكره الإمام الرازي من أن القول بكون الواجب محلاً للحوادث لازم على جميع الفرق الإسلامية وإن كانوا يتبرعون عنه. أما الأشاعرة : فلأن زيداً إذا وجد ، كان الواجب غير قادر على خلقه بعدما كان وفاعلاً له ؛ عالماً بأنه موجود مبصراً لصورته سامعاً لصوته، أمراً له بالصلاة بعد ما لم يكن كذلك
The Fourth Section: The majority (al-jumhūr) hold that the necessary existent (al-wājib) cannot be qualified by a temporal accident (al-ḥādith), i.e., that which exists after non-existence, contrary to the Karramiyya. As for His being qualified by negations and relations that occur after they were not, such as His being not a provider for the deceased Zayd and a provider for the newborn ʿAmr, and by real attributes whose relations change, such as His being knowledgeable of this temporal accident and powerful over it—that is permissible. Likewise, by states that come into existence after they were not, such as the states of being knower that are renewed with the renewal of objects of knowledge according to Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī, as will come later in the verification of that. And by this is refuted what Imam al-Razī mentioned: that the statement that the necessary existent is a locus for temporal accidents is necessary for all Islamic sects, even if they disavow it. As for the Ashʿaris: because when Zayd comes into existence, the necessary existent was not powerful over his creation after He was, and He is powerful over him; He knows him as existing, sees his form, hears his voice, and commands him to pray after He was not like that.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage clarifies the position of the majority (al-jumhūr) against the Karramiyya and responds to an objection by Imam al-Razi. The author first restates the fundamental principle: the necessary existent cannot be qualified by a temporal accident (something that exists after non-existence). However, He can be qualified by negations (salb) and relations (iḍāfāt) that arise when their subjects cease to exist or come into existence, such as 'not providing for the dead Zayd' and 'providing for the newborn Amr'. Similarly, real attributes (ṣifāt ḥaqīqiyya) whose relations (mutaʿalliqāt) change—like being knowledgeable of a particular event when it occurs—are permissible, because the attribute itself is eternal and only its relation to the temporal object changes. The same applies to states (aḥwāl) like the state of 'being knower' (ʿālimiyya) that renew with new objects of knowledge, as held by Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī. With these clarifications, the author refutes Imam al-Razi's claim that all Islamic sects are committed to the view that God is a locus for temporal accidents (maḥall li-l-ḥawādith). Specifically, against the Ashʿaris, al-Razi argued that when Zayd is created, God acquires new relations: He becomes powerful over his creation, knowledgeable of his existence, seeing his form, hearing his voice, etc., which seem to imply temporal accidents. But the author's point is that these are merely relational attributes (iḍāfāt) that do not affect God's essence and are thus permissible.

السلوب
الإضافات
الصفات الحقيقية
الأحوال
العالميات
أبو الحسين البصري
الإمام الرازي
الأشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any new description of God (like being the creator of a particular person) implies a change in God's essence, making Him subject to temporal accidents.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between real temporal accidents (which change the essence) and mere relational or negative attributes that arise from changes in creatures. The latter are permissible and do not affect God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, how does the Ashʿari position avoid being committed to temporal accidents in God's essence?
A) By denying that God has any attributes at all.
B) By stating that the attributes that change in relation (like knowledge of a new event) are merely relational (iḍāfī) and not real temporal accidents.
C) By claiming that God knows all things from eternity and never acquires new knowledge.
D) By rejecting the existence of temporal objects altogether.

Key Takeaway
God's essence does not change; new descriptions of Him are either negations, relations, or real attributes with changing relations, all of which are permissible.

Second Proof: The Change Argument and Its Answer
Presents the second proof and its answer distinguishing possibility in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against temporal attributes (the change argument) and the subtle distinction made in its answer between the eternity of the possibility of a temporal event and the possibility of the event itself being eternal.

Core Idea
The impossibility of change in God only rules out a temporal event being eternal, not the eternal possibility of such an event.

This lesson tackles the second and most central proof of those who deny that God can be described with temporal events (ḥawādith). The philosophers argue that such description would entail change, which is impossible for God. The answer, however, makes a subtle logical distinction that preserves both divine transcendence and the possibility of temporal attributes. Let us examine the proof and then carefully unpack the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof and answer
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the proof (1 sentence), then the answer, which contains a distinction and an analogy.
Focus on the words 'الثاني : وهو العمدة عند الحكماء' to see its importance.
2
Unpack the key distinction
The answer distinguishes between 'azaliyyat jawāz al-ḥādith' (eternity of the possibility) and 'jawāz azaliyyat al-ḥādith' (possibility of the event's eternity). Understand why only the former follows from the premises.
The impossible is the temporal event existing in pre-eternity, not the eternal possibility of its occurrence.
3
Grasp the analogy and conclusion
The analogy of divine power to create the world illustrates the point. God has the power in pre-eternity to create, but He cannot create the world in pre-eternity (since the world is temporal). Apply this to the case of temporal attributes.
This affirms that attributes do not imply change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : وهو العمدة عند الحكماء أن الاتصاف بالحادث تغير وهو على الله تعالى محال . وجوابه : أن اللازم من استحالة الانقلاب جواز الاتصاف في الأزل على أن يكون في الأزل قيداً للجواز، وهو لا يستلزم إلا أزلية جواز الحادث لا جواز الاتصاف في الأزل على أن يكون قيداً للاتصاف ليلزم جواز أزلية الحادث، ولا خفاء في أن المحال جواز أزلية الحادث، بمعنى إمكان أن يوجد في الأزل ، لا أزلية جوازه، بمعنى أن يمكن في الأزل وجوده في الجملة. وهذا كما يقال : إن قابلية الإله لايجاد العالم متحققة في الأزل بخلاف قابليته لايجاد العالم في الأزل أي يمكن في الأزل أن يوجده ، ولا يمكن أن يوجده في الأزل، ومبنى الكلام ، على أن يعتبر الحادث بشرط الحدوث، وإلا فلا خفاء في إمكان وجوده في الأزل.
The second proof, which is the mainstay with the philosophers, is that description with a temporal event is change, and change is impossible with respect to God—exalted is He. Its answer: That which follows from the impossibility of alteration is the possibility of description in pre-eternity, with the condition that pre-eternity be a qualifier for the possibility, and this entails only the eternity of the possibility of the temporal event, not the possibility of description in pre-eternity as a qualifier for the description such that the eternity of the temporal event would follow. And it is obvious that what is impossible is the possibility of the eternity of the temporal event, meaning the possibility of its existence in pre-eternity, not the eternity of its possibility, meaning that its existence at some time could be possible in pre-eternity. This is like saying that the divine capacity to create the world is realized in pre-eternity, as opposed to His capacity to create the world in pre-eternity, i.e., He can create it in pre-eternity, but He cannot create it in pre-eternity. The basis of the discussion is that the temporal event is considered with the condition of temporality; otherwise, there is no doubt about the possibility of its existence in pre-eternity.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against temporal attributes, which is central to the philosophers: describing God with a temporal event would entail change, which is impossible for God. The answer makes a subtle distinction: from the impossibility of change, we infer that description in pre-eternity is possible only if pre-eternity qualifies the possibility itself, not the description. This yields the eternity of the possibility of the temporal event, not the possibility of the event itself in pre-eternity. The impossible thing is the existence of a temporal event in pre-eternity, not the eternal possibility of its occurring. The analogy of divine power to create the world illustrates this: God has the power in pre-eternity to create the world, but that does not mean He can create the world in pre-eternity, because a temporal event by definition cannot exist in pre-eternity. Therefore, temporal attributes do not necessarily imply change in God.

Key Terms
التغير
الانقلاب
الأزل
الجواز
قابلية الإله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God can be described with a temporal event, then that event would be eternal (in pre-eternity), implying change.
Correction: The answer clarifies that what is eternal is only the possibility of the event, not the event itself. The event remains temporal, so no change occurs in God.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in the answer to the second proof?
A) Between eternal and temporal attributes.
B) Between the eternity of the possibility of a temporal event and the possibility of its eternity.
C) Between change and alteration.
D) Between philosophers and theologians.

Key Takeaway
The change argument fails because it confuses the eternal possibility of a temporal event with the event itself being eternal; temporal attributes do not imply change in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously imagine God changing His mind or reacting to your deeds as if He is affected by time? How does the lesson's distinction between eternal possibility and temporal actuality expose that as a mistake? Will you consciously affirm God's immutability in your next prayer?

When you face hardship, do you assume that God's power to help you is limited to the moment? How does the source's teaching that God's capacity is eternally realized yet His act is in time strengthen your trust? What must you change in your reliance on Him?
?
The philosophers argued that temporal attributes entail change in God, which would be impossible. But the answer shows that temporal attributes do not imply change in God's essence. How does this affect your view of God's actions in history? Are you willing to accept that God can create temporal events without changing Himself?

The Fourth Argument: Why God Cannot Be Described as Originated
Al-Taftazani presents the fourth argument and its two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against describing God with originated attributes and its two supporting proofs.

Core Idea
The fourth argument claims that if God had originated attributes, He would be originated.

This lesson presents the fourth kalām argument that God cannot possess originated attributes. The argument is based on the implication that if God were described with something originated, He would not be free from it, making Him originated as well. The passage then lays out two proofs for this implication, which we will examine carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
The argument is presented from the opponent's perspective.
2
Identify the main claim and two proofs
The main claim is that God cannot be described with originated attributes. The two proofs (أحدهما, ثانيهما) support the implication that if He were, He would be originated.
3
Focus on key terms
Pay attention to الحادث, القديم, الضد, القابلية, الأزلية. These are essential for the argument.
The proof about potentiality (قابلية) introduces a technical concept.
4
Understand the phrase 'to assist the opponent'
The author includes this argument to show that even if the opponent's premises are accepted, the conclusion is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لو جاز اتصافه بالحادث، لزم عدم حلوله عن الحادث فيكون حادثاً لما سبق في حدوث العالم ولمساعدة الخصم على ذلك.
وأما الملازمة فلوجهين :
أحدهما: أن المتصف بالحادث لا يخلو عنه، وعن ضده. وضد الحادث حادث، لأنه منقطع إلى الحادث، ولا شيء من القديم كذلك، لما تقرر أن ما يثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه. وثانيهما : أنه لا يخلو عنه وعن قابليته وهي حادثة لما مر من أن أزلية القابلية تستلزم جواز أزلية المقبول فيلزم جواز أزلية الحادث وهو محال ، وكلا الوجهين ضعيف .
أما الأول : فلأنه إن أريد بالضد ما هو المتعارف فلا نسلم أن لكل صفة ضد أو أن الموصوف لا يخلو عن الضدين، وإن أريد أن مجرد ما ينافيه وجودياً كان أو عدمياً حتى إن عدم كل شيء ضد له ويستحيل الخلو عنهما ، فلا نسلم أن ضد الحادث حادث فإن القدم والحدوث إن جعلا من صفات الموجود خاصة، فعدم الحادث قبل وجوده ليس بقديم ولا حادث وإن أطلقا على المعدوم أيضاً باعتبار كونه غير مسبوق بالوجود أو مسبوقاً به فهو قديم ، وامتناع زوال القديم إنما هو في الموجود لظهور زوال العدم الأزلي لكل حادث .
وأما الثاني : فلأن القابلية اعتبار عقلي معناه إمكان الاتصاف ولو سلم فأزليتهما إنما تقتضي أزلية جواز المقبول أي إمكانه لا جواز أزليته ليلزم المحال. وقد عرف الفرق .
The fourth: that if it were permissible for Him to be described with the originated (al-ḥādith), then His not [being] free from the originated would be necessary, so He would be originated, due to what preceded concerning the origination of the world and to assist the opponent regarding that. As for the implication (al-mulāzama), it is by two aspects: One of them: that the one described by the originated is not free from it nor from its opposite (ḍidd). And the opposite of the originated is originated, because it is attached [i.e., terminates] to the originated; and nothing of the eternal (al-qadīm) is like that, because it is established that what is proven eternal, its non-existence is impossible. And the second: that he is not free from it nor from its potentiality (qābiliyya), which is originated, because it has passed that the pre-eternity (azaliyya) of potentiality entails the permissibility (jawāz) of the pre-eternity of the potential recipient (al-maqbūl), so the permissibility of the pre-eternity of the originated would follow, which is impossible. And both aspects are weak. As for the first: because if by 'opposite' is meant what is conventional (al-mutʿārif), then we do not grant that every attribute has an opposite or that the described is not free from the two opposites; and if what is meant is simply that which contradicts it, whether existent or non-existent, so that the non-existence of every thing is its opposite and it is impossible to be free from both, then we do not grant that the opposite of the originated is originated. For 'eternity' (al-qidam) and 'origination' (al-ḥudūth) if made among the attributes of existent (al-mawjūd) specifically, then the non-existence of the originated before its existence is neither eternal nor originated; and if they are also applied to the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) by virtue of its being not preceded by existence or preceded by it, then it is eternal. And the impossibility of the removal of the eternal is only concerning the existent, due to the manifest removal of the pre-eternal non-existence for every originated thing. As for the second: because potentiality is a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqlī), its meaning is the possibility (imkān) of description. And even if it were granted, their pre-eternity only entails the pre-eternity of the permissibility (jawāz) of the potential recipient, i.e., its possibility, not the permissibility of its pre-eternity, so as to necessitate the impossible. And the distinction is known.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage contains the fourth argument (al-rābiʿ) against the view that God can be described with originated (ḥādith) attributes, followed by a refutation of its two supporting proofs. The argument states: if it were permissible for God to be described with an originated thing, then He would not be free of it (i.e., He would have to be characterized by it), and consequently He would be originated—because being characterized by something that changes or is temporary implies that the subject itself is subject to change and thus originated. The two proofs for this implication (mulāzama) are then presented. The first proof: the one described by an originated cannot be free from it or from its opposite; but the opposite of an originated is also originated (since it terminates in the originated); and nothing eternal has opposites that are originated; moreover, what is proven eternal cannot cease to exist. The second proof: the described cannot be free from the originated nor from its potentiality (qābiliyya), which is originated; the pre-eternity (azaliyya) of that potentiality would imply the permissibility of the pre-eternity of the potential recipient (maqbūl)—i.e., the originated thing would be pre-eternally possible, which is absurd. The author then refutes both proofs, calling them weak (ḍaʿīf). For the first: the conventional understanding of 'opposite' (ḍidd) is not accepted—not every attribute has an opposite, nor is it necessary that the subject be free from two opposites. If 'opposite' is defined more broadly as anything that contradicts it (whether existent or non-existent), then the opposite of an originated is not necessarily originated; rather, 'eternity' (qidam) and 'origination' (ḥudūth) are attributes of existents specifically. The non-existence of an originated thing before its existence is neither eternal nor originated (since non-existence has no temporal status). If they are applied to non-existents by virtue of being preceded by existence or not, then that non-existence would be eternal—but the impossibility of ceasing applies only to existent things, because pre-eternal non-existence can cease for every originated thing. For the second: potentiality is a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqlī) meaning the possibility (imkān) of being described. Even if its pre-eternity were granted, it would only entail the pre-eternity of the permissibility (jawāz) of the potential recipient—i.e., its mere possibility—not the permissibility of its actual pre-eternity (which would entail the impossible). The author concludes that the distinction (between possibility and actual pre-eternity) is well known.

Key Terms
الحادث (al-ḥādith)
القديم (al-qadīm)
الضد (al-ḍidd)
القابلية (al-qābiliyya)
الأزلية (al-azaliyya)
الجواز (al-jawāz)
المقبول (al-maqbūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first proof (opposite) claim about the opposite of an originated attribute?
A) It is eternal.
B) It is also originated.
C) It is non-existent.
D) It is mental.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses the necessity of being free from opposites and potentiality to argue that God cannot have originated attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is free from all originated attributes, how does that change the way you rely on Him in moments of crisis? Do you ever subconsciously think of God as changing His mind or being affected by your actions? Repent and reaffirm your belief in His eternal perfection.
?
The argument shows that any description of God with temporality leads to absurdity. Are there any beliefs or statements about God that you have heard or held that might imply temporality? Take a moment to examine your own speech about God and seek knowledge to correct any inadvertent anthropomorphism.
?
The concept of 'opposite' (ḍidd) is used here to show that God cannot be associated with originated attributes. Can you identify areas in your life where you confuse God's attributes with created qualities? What changes must you make in your worship to reflect the understanding that God is utterly unlike creation?

Refuting the Fourth Argument: Responding to Its Two Proofs
Al-Taftazani refutes the two proofs of the fourth argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the two proofs of the fourth argument, clarifying the concepts of 'opposite' and 'potentiality'.

Core Idea
The refutation shows that the concepts of opposite and potentiality do not entail origination.

In this lesson, we examine al-Taftazani's refutation of the fourth argument. He dismisses both proofs as weak, providing detailed criticisms: for the first, he clarifies the meaning of 'opposite'; for the second, he explains that potentiality is a mental consideration. We will study these refutations carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author declares both proofs weak (ḍaʿīf).
2
Understand the first refutation
The author distinguishes between conventional and broad definitions of 'opposite'. For each, he denies that the opposite of an originated must be originated.
Key point: non-existence is not a temporal attribute.
3
Understand the second refutation
The author argues that potentiality is a mental consideration (iʿtibār ʿaqlī) meaning possibility. Its pre-eternity only entails the pre-eternity of possibility, not actual pre-eternity.
4
Reflect on the significance
These refutations show that the arguments rely on equivocal or misinterpreted terms. The author's critique is based on precise kalām terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه لو جاز اتصافه بالحادث، لزم عدم حلوله عن الحادث فيكون حادثاً لما سبق في حدوث العالم ولمساعدة الخصم على ذلك.
وأما الملازمة فلوجهين :
أحدهما: أن المتصف بالحادث لا يخلو عنه، وعن ضده. وضد الحادث حادث، لأنه منقطع إلى الحادث، ولا شيء من القديم كذلك، لما تقرر أن ما يثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه. وثانيهما : أنه لا يخلو عنه وعن قابليته وهي حادثة لما مر من أن أزلية القابلية تستلزم جواز أزلية المقبول فيلزم جواز أزلية الحادث وهو محال ، وكلا الوجهين ضعيف .
أما الأول : فلأنه إن أريد بالضد ما هو المتعارف فلا نسلم أن لكل صفة ضد أو أن الموصوف لا يخلو عن الضدين، وإن أريد أن مجرد ما ينافيه وجودياً كان أو عدمياً حتى إن عدم كل شيء ضد له ويستحيل الخلو عنهما ، فلا نسلم أن ضد الحادث حادث فإن القدم والحدوث إن جعلا من صفات الموجود خاصة، فعدم الحادث قبل وجوده ليس بقديم ولا حادث وإن أطلقا على المعدوم أيضاً باعتبار كونه غير مسبوق بالوجود أو مسبوقاً به فهو قديم ، وامتناع زوال القديم إنما هو في الموجود لظهور زوال العدم الأزلي لكل حادث .
وأما الثاني : فلأن القابلية اعتبار عقلي معناه إمكان الاتصاف ولو سلم فأزليتهما إنما تقتضي أزلية جواز المقبول أي إمكانه لا جواز أزليته ليلزم المحال. وقد عرف الفرق .

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the opposite of an originated thing must also be originated.
Correction: The refutation clarifies that non-existence (the opposite in a broad sense) is neither eternal nor originated, and that potentiality is merely a mental consideration, not a real temporal property.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is potentiality (qābiliyya)?
A) A real accident.
B) A mental consideration meaning possibility.
C) An eternal attribute.
D) A type of opposite.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani shows that the fourth argument's proofs rely on ambiguous terms and fail to establish that God must be originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's eternity, do you unconsciously treat non-existence as if it were a temporal stage that can be 'before' or 'after'? How does this lesson's correction about non-existence challenge that assumption in your own heart?
?
The refutation shows that potentiality is merely a mental consideration, not a real external quality. Have you ever treated a possibility in your mind as a concrete reality, leading you to false conclusions about God's attributes? Will you consciously discipline your thinking to distinguish mental concepts from external truth?
?
Al-Taftazani exposes the weakness of relying on ambiguous terms like 'opposite' to argue about God. Are there any ambiguous or borrowed concepts you have been using to defend your understanding of tawḥīd without careful definition? Will you commit to seeking precise, scripturally-grounded language instead?

Defending Divine Power Over Evil: Refuting Magians and al-Nazzam
Taftazani presents and refutes objections from Magians and al-Nazzam on God's power over evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the objections of the Magians and al-Nazzam against God's power over evil by distinguishing between power and actual creation.

Core Idea
God's power over evil does not require its actual occurrence; wisdom prevents it.

Taftazani addresses two groups who deny that God has power over evil: the Magians, who posit a second creator for evil, and al-Nazzam, who argue that if God could create evil, He would, leading to absurdities. The response clarifies that power does not necessitate action, and God's wisdom ensures evil does not actually proceed from Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two objections
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the two groups mentioned: the Magians and al-Nazzam. What is their common claim?
2
Understand the Magian objection and its refutation
The Magians deny God's power over evil to avoid attributing both good and evil to Him. Taftazani refutes this by noting that God's actions in His dominion are not subject to human categories of evil.
3
Analyze al-Nazzam's objection and Taftazani's response
Al-Nazzam argues that if God could create evil, He would, leading to foolishness or ignorance. Taftazani responds that power does not entail actuality; God's wisdom prevents evil due to absence of motive or presence of obstacles.
4
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider the difference between having power over something and actually doing it. This is crucial: God has power over all things, but actual creation depends on wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخالف المجوس المنكرون لشمول قدرة الله تعالى. طوائف منهم المجوس القائلون بأنه لا يقدر على الشرور حتى خلق الأجسام المؤذية . وإنما القادر على ذلك فاعل آخر عندهم (أهرمن) لئلا يلزم كون الواحد خيراً شريراً، وقد عرفت ذلك . ومنهم النظام وأتباعه القائلون بأنه لا يقدر على خلق الجهل والكذب والظلم ، وسائر القبائح ، إذ لو كان خلقها مقدوراً له لجاز صدورها عنه ، واللازم باطل لإفضائه إلى السفه إن كان عالماً بقبح ذلك، وباستغنائه عنه وإلى الجهل إن لم يكن عالماً. والجواب : لا نسلم قبح الشيء بالنسبة إليه كيف وهو تصرف في ملكه؟ . ولو سلم : فالقدرة عليه لا تنافي امتناع صدوره عنه نظراً إلى وجود المصارف وعدم الداعي، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه.
And those who deny the comprehensiveness of God's power – including the Magians and al-Nazzam – have disagreed. Among the groups are the Magians, who say that He is not able to do evil, even the creation of harmful bodies, rather the one able to do that is another agent according to them (Ahriman), to avoid the consequence that the One is both good and evil – and you already know that. And among them is al-Nazzam and his followers, who say that He is not able to create ignorance, lies, injustice, and all base things, because if their creation were within His power, it would be possible for them to proceed from Him, and the consequent is false because it would lead to foolishness if He knew their evil and to ignorance if He did not. The answer is: we do not concede that a thing is evil relative to Him, for it is an action in His own dominion. And even if we concede, power over it does not preclude its non-occurrence due to the existence of obstacles or absence of motive, even if it is possible in itself.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents objections from the Magians and al-Nazzam regarding God's power over evil, followed by Taftazani's response. The Magians deny God's power over evil to avoid attributing both good and evil to Him, positing a second creator (Ahriman). Al-Nazzam argues that if God could create evil, He would, but since He doesn't, it would imply foolishness or ignorance. The answer refutes both: God's actions in His dominion are not subject to human categories of evil; power does not necessitate actual creation. Even if evil were possible, God's wisdom prevents its occurrence due to lack of motive or presence of obstacles. Thus, God has power over all things, but actual creation depends on wisdom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God has power over evil, then He must create evil.
Correction: Power over a thing does not necessitate its occurrence; God's wisdom can prevent evil even if it is possible.

Checkpoint
According to the response given by al-Taftazani, why is God not obligated to refrain from having power over evil?
A) Because evil is impossible for God.
B) Because God is not described as having power over evil.
C) Because power over a thing does not entail its actual occurrence, and God’s wisdom prevents it.
D) Because God only has power over good.

Key Takeaway
God has power over all things, including evil, but His wisdom prevents the actual occurrence of evil due to absence of motive or presence of obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God was unjust when something bad happened to you? How does Taftazani's argument that no action is evil relative to God challenge that feeling, and what does it demand of you in terms of trust?
?
Al-Nazzam assumed that if God can do evil, He must do it. In your own life, do you sometimes assume that if something is possible, it must happen – especially when doubting God's wisdom? How does Taftazani's point about obstacles and absence of motive change that assumption?
?
If you truly believe God has power over all evils but withholds them out of wisdom, what does that imply for how you should respond when you face trials? What action will you take this week to surrender to His wisdom rather than question His power?
