Al-Ka'bi's Objection on Divine and Human Acts and Taftazani's Response
Taftazani presents al-Ka'bi's objection that divine and human acts cannot be similar due to differing attributes and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's argument that God cannot create acts similar to human acts due to differences in attributes, and how Taftazani refutes it by denying the exhaustive categorization of human acts.

Core Idea
God's power is not limited by the moral categories we apply to human acts.

This passage introduces a challenge from the Mu'tazili scholar al-Ka'bi: if God is transcendent, can He produce acts that look like human acts? Al-Ka'bi argues that human acts are always characterized as futile, foolish, or humble, which cannot apply to God. Taftazani counters by showing the classification is incomplete, leaving room for divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation to understand al-Ka'bi's claim and the categories he uses to distinguish human acts from divine acts.
Notice that the objection is based on the assumption that human acts always fall into these negative categories.
2
Note variations in other works
Observe how al-Muhassal and al-Mawaqif differ in listing the categories. This shows the objection was not uniformly presented.
The variation indicates that the categorization is not fixed, which weakens the argument.
3
Understand the response
Analyze Taftazani's two-part response: first, the categories are not exhaustive (man' al-hasr), and second, even if an act involves obedience or humility, it entails compliance with another, which does not apply to God.
The response shows that the objection fails because it overgeneralizes human acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم أبو القاسم البلخي المعروف بالكعبي، وأتباعه القائلون بأنه لا يقدر على مثل مقدور العبد حتى لو حرك جوهراً إلى حيز وحركه العبد إلى ذلك الحيز لم تتماثل الحركتان، وذلك لأن فعل العبد إما عبث أو سفه أو تواضع بخلاف فعل الرب. وفي عبارة المحصل بدل الطاعة وعبارة المواقف إما طاعة أو معصية، أو سفه، وليست على ما التواضع لأن السفه، وإن جاز أن يجعل شاملاً للعبث فلا خفاء في شمولية المعصية ينبغي أيضاً. والجواب منع الحصر ككثير من المصالح الدنيوية. فإن قيل : المشتمل على المصلحة المحضة، أو الراجحة طاعة وتواضع . قلنا ممنوع بل إذا كان فيه امتثال وتعظيم للغير، وهذا لا يتصف به فعل الرب، وإن اشتمل على المصلحة
And among them is Abu al-Qasim al-Balkhi known as al-Ka'bi, and his followers, who say that He is not able to do the like of what is within a servant's power, such that if He moves an atom to a location and the servant moves it to that location, the two motions would not be similar, because the servant's act is either futility or foolishness or humility, unlike the Lord's act. In al-Muhassal, it says 'obedience' instead, and in al-Mawaqif, it says either obedience or disobedience or foolishness, and it is not upon humility because foolishness, even if it can be taken to include futility, there is no doubt that disobedience should also be included. The answer is: the exclusive enumeration is denied, as in many worldly benefits. If it is said: what consists of pure or preponderant benefit is obedience or humility, we say: it is denied; rather, if it involves compliance and glorification of another, that does not characterize the Lord's act, even if it contains benefit.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents al-Ka'bi's objection that God cannot create acts identical to those of servants because the servant's acts are characterized as futile, foolish, or humble, unlike God's acts. Variations in other sources include 'obedience' or 'disobedience'. Taftazani's response is twofold: first, the categorization is not exhaustive—many human acts have worldly benefits that are not inherently base. Second, even if an act involves obedience or humility, it entails compliance with another, which does not apply to God. Thus, there is no barrier to God having power over similar acts; the difference in attribution does not negate capability.

Key Terms
البلخي
الكعبي
العبث
السفه
التواضع
الطاعة
المعصية
الحصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All human acts can be categorized as base or evil, such as futility, foolishness, or disobedience.
Correction: Many human acts are neutral or beneficial (worldly benefits) and do not fall into these categories, so the objection's premise is false.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of al-Taftazani's response to al-Ka'bi?
A) God and the servant have different capacities.
B) The categories of acts (futility, foolishness, humility) are not exhaustive, so they do not prove a difference.
C) Human acts are always base.
D) God cannot create similar acts.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes al-Ka'bi by arguing that the categories of human acts are not exhaustive, so they cannot prove that God cannot perform similar acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider an action you did today that you consider trivial or even futile. Do you believe that God could have created that very action with the same physical properties? If not, what does that imply about your understanding of God's power? How does Taftazani's argument force you to reconsider?
?
You often categorize your acts as 'obedient' or 'disobedient' towards God. Does this lesson challenge you to see that those labels are relational, not essential to the act itself? How does that affect your view of God's sovereignty over every aspect of your life, including your wrongdoings?
?
If God's power is not limited by the moral categories of human acts, then what does that say about your tendency to judge others' actions? Are you, like al-Ka'bi, limiting God by your own categories? How can you align your understanding with Taftazani's refutation?

Divine Power and the Problem of Evil: An Objection Answered
al-Taftazani answers the objection that God doing evil would entail foolishness or ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians defend God's transcendent majesty against the objection that He could do evil, by distinguishing between power and actual occurrence.

Core Idea
God's power over evil does not imply He does it; wisdom prevents it.

A common objection against divine omnipotence is that if God can do anything, He could also do evil. This would imply either foolishness or ignorance in God. In this passage, al-Taftazani responds by redefining the framework: ugliness is relative to a sovereign owner, and power does not necessitate action. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the compressed dialectical style.
The objector uses two disjuncts: either God knows the ugliness (leading to foolishness) or does not know (leading to ignorance).
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
The objector forces a choice: if God knows the act is ugly and does it, that's foolish; if He doesn't know, that's ignorance. Both are impossible for God.
The objection is based on the assumption that certain acts are inherently ugly (qabih).
3
Follow the answer's first line of defense
The answer denies the premise: ugliness is not absolute; for God, as owner of the entire dominion, all His acts are just. The concept of ugliness does not apply to the sovereign.
This is a theological voluntarist position.
4
Examine the concession and final argument
Even if one concedes possibility, power over an act does not imply its occurrence. There may be preventive causes (masarif) and no motive (da'i).
This distinguishes between ability and actual performance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لإفضائه إلى السفه إن كان عالماً بقبح ذلك، وباستغنائه عنه وإلى الجهل إن لم يكن عالماً. والجواب : لا نسلم قبح الشيء بالنسبة إليه كيف وهو تصرف في ملكه؟ . ولو سلم : فالقدرة عليه لا تنافي امتناع صدوره عنه نظراً إلى وجود المصارف وعدم الداعي، وإن كان ممكناً في نفسه.
Because it leads to foolishness if He knows the ugliness of that, and to ignorance if He does not know. The answer: We do not concede the ugliness of a thing relative to Him, how [can it be ugliness] while it is an act in His dominion? And if conceded: then power over it does not negate the impossibility of its occurring from Him, considering the existence of [preventive] causes and the absence of motive, even if it is possible in itself.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that if God were to do something He knows to be evil, it would lead to foolishness (safah) or ignorance (jahl). The author (al-Taftazani) answers by denying the premise that such actions are 'ugly' (qabih) in relation to God, because God is the absolute owner of His dominion and can act as He wills. Even if one concedes that something is possible, God's power over it does not entail its occurrence, because there may be preventive causes (masarif) and lack of motive (da'i). Thus, the objection fails.

Key Terms
السفه
الجهل
قبح
تصرف في الملك
المصارف
الداعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's inability to do evil limits His omnipotence.
Correction: Evil is not something God would do, not because He lacks power, but because His wisdom and perfection prevent it. Power over evil does not mean it occurs.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does God not do evil acts?
A) Because He lacks power over them.
B) Because He is not aware of their ugliness.
C) Because of preventive causes and absence of motive, not lack of power.
D) Because evil acts are impossible in themselves.

Key Takeaway
God's power over evil does not imply its occurrence; ugliness is relative and God's wisdom prevents any evil action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you confront suffering or injustice in your own life, do you instinctively question God's wisdom or power? How does al-Taftazani's argument challenge you to realign your perspective, and what concrete step will you take to submit your judgment to His sovereignty?
?
If ugliness is relative to God's dominion, then your sense of what is 'fair' or 'unfair' may be limited. In what area of your life are you most prone to judge God's actions as unjust? Will you today actively surrender that judgment in prayer?
?
God's power over evil does not mean He does evil—but you often have the ability to do wrong and yet choose not to. What evil action are you capable of but refrain from? How can you strengthen your resolve by remembering that wisdom and motive, not just power, should guide your choices?

Textual Evidence that Allah is the Sole Creator
Cites Quranic verses as evidence for God as sole creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Quranic verses serve as direct textual evidence that Allah alone is the creator of all things.

Core Idea
The Quran’s own words establish Allah as the sole creator.

This lesson introduces the first proof used by orthodox theologians: direct textual evidence from the Quran. We will examine two key verses that explicitly state that Allah is the Creator of everything, both in general and in specific terms. The passage shows how these texts form a foundational argument for God’s exclusive creative power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source text and its translation carefully, noting the general and specific verses. Observe how the author introduces the proof as 'texts' (النصوص).
2
Identify the two Quranic verses
Focus on the first verse (general) and the second (specific). Understand their explicit claims: Allah is Creator of all things; He created heavens, earth, darkness, light, etc.
The first verse is from Surah al-An'am, verse 102; the second from verse 1.
3
Understand the argumentative structure
See how the author uses these texts to support the claim that no other creator exists. The argument is straightforward: since the Quran is the word of God, its explicit statements are sufficient proof.
This proof is often the first presented in theological works to ground the discussion in revelation.
4
Reflect on the approach
Consider why textual evidence is used as the first proof and how it complements rational proofs. Think about why the author distinguishes general and specific indications.
This helps to show that the Quran covers both broad and detailed creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : النصوص الدالة إجمالاً على أنه خالق الكل لا خالق سواه وتفصيلاً على أنه خالق السموات والأرض ، والظلمات والنور، والموت والحياة، وغير ذلك من الجواهر والأعراض. قال تعالى: ﴿ذلكم الله ربكم لا إله إلا هو خالق كل شيء فاعبدوه﴾ الأنعام آية ١٠٢. وقال تعالى: ﴿الحمد لله الذي خلق السموات والأرض وجعل الظلمات والنور﴾ الأنعام آية ١.
First: the texts that indicate generally that He is the Creator of everything, no creator besides Him, and in detail that He is the Creator of the heavens and the earth, the darkness and the light, death and life, and other than that of substances and accidents. Almighty said: 'That is Allah, your Lord; there is no deity except Him, the Creator of all things, so worship Him.' (Surat al-An'am, verse 102). And Almighty said: 'All praise is due to Allah, who created the heavens and the earth and made the darkness and the light.' (Surat al-An'am, verse 1).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the position of Ahl al-Ḥaqq that God is the sole creator without any real agent besides Him. The proof is textual: verses from the Quran that explicitly state that God is the Creator of everything. The author distinguishes between general and specific indications: generally, the verse 'there is no deity except Him, the Creator of all things' and specifically, the verse that He created the heavens, earth, etc. The proof argues that if God is the Creator of everything, then nothing can be created by another agent independently. This is a straightforward appeal to revelation as the foundation of the argument.

Key Terms
النصوص
خالق كل شيء
جواهر
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that these Quranic verses are merely proof texts that require no interpretation and are sufficient on their own without rational arguments.
Correction: While these verses are clear and foundational, theologians like Sanusi often combine textual proof with rational proof. The verses are taken at face value, but they are part of a larger system of evidence.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse is cited as a general proof that God is the Creator of all things?
A) 'All praise is due to Allah, who created the heavens and the earth.'
B) 'That is Allah, your Lord; there is no deity except Him, the Creator of all things.'
C) 'He is the Creator of the heavens and the earth.'
D) 'He created death and life.'

Key Takeaway
The Quran explicitly states that Allah is the Creator of everything, providing foundational textual evidence for the doctrine of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Allah is the Creator of everything including you. Is there any part of your life where you feel you are the master of your own destiny? How would your daily decisions change if you truly internalized that every breath and every moment of success is from Him alone?
?
The verse commands worship because He is the Creator. Do you worship Him as your sole Creator, or do you often seek validation and security from people, money, or status? What would it mean for you to redirect all worship—including your hopes and fears—exclusively to Allah?
?
If Allah created both the darkness and the light, then He controls both your trials and your joys. When you face a difficulty, do you still see His creative hand, or do you attribute your suffering to bad luck or others? How would seeing Him as the sole Creator change your response to hardship starting today?

The Objection that Life Implies Corporeality and Its Answer
Taftazani refutes the objection that God's life would entail a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the objection that God's life would entail a body by distinguishing between accompaniment and conditioning in the witnessed world.

Core Idea
What is true for bodies is not necessarily true for God.

In the previous lesson, we saw an objection based on God's attributes implying commonality with contingent beings. Here, another objection argues that life, hearing, and sight in the witnessed world always belong to bodies, so if God has these attributes, He must be a body. Taftazani's answer is a masterful move: he denies that the witnessed-world definition or conditions must apply to the Unseen. Let's examine this objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Identify the objector's claim and the reasoning behind it. Notice the implication that life requires a body.
The objector relies on the definition of life as temperament in the physical world.
2
Understand the response
Notice how Taftazani rejects the premise by saying the attributes are not defined or conditioned by bodily properties; they merely accompany them in the witnessed world.
The key move is denying necessity of conditioning.
3
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider why this principle is important for Islamic theology: it protects God's transcendence from being limited by worldly categories.
This is a recurring theme in kalam about the Unseen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبهة أخرى وهي أنه لو كان حياً سميعاً بصيراً لكان جسماً، واللازم باطل. وجه اللزوم أن الحياة اعتدال نوعي للمزاج الحيواني على ما سبق، أو صفة تتبعها مقتضية للحس والحركة الإرادية وقد عرفت أن المزاج من الكيفيات الجسمية، وأن السمع والبصر وسائر الإحساسات تأثر للحواس عن المحسوسات، أو حالة إدراكية تتبعه وليست الحواس إلا وهي جسمانية. والجواب: أنا لا نسلم كون الحياة والسمع والبصر عبارة عما ذكرتم أو مشروطة به في الشاهد، فضلاً عن الغائب. غاية الأمر أنها في الشاهد تقارن ما ذكرتم، ولا حجة على الاشتراط، وقد تكلمنا على ذلك فيما سبق.
Another doubt: that if He were living, hearing, seeing, He would be a body, and the consequent is false. The aspect of implication is that life is a specific equilibrium of the animal temperament according to what preceded, or an attribute that follows it and necessitates sensation and voluntary motion. And you have known that temperament is among bodily qualities, and that hearing, sight, and all sensations are affections of the senses from the sensed, or a perceptual state that follows it, and the senses are nothing but physical. The answer: We do not accept that life, hearing, and sight are what you mentioned or conditioned by that in the witnessed world, let alone in the Unseen. At most, they accompany what you mentioned in the witnessed world, and there is no proof of conditioning. We have spoken about that previously.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer. The objector argues that in the witnessed world, life requires a body (due to temperament), so if God is living, He would be a body. The answer (jawab) rejects the premise: we do not accept that life, hearing, and sight are defined by or conditioned on bodily properties like temperament. They may accompany these in the witnessed world, but that does not mean they are conditioned by them. Thus the objection fails. The author refers to earlier discussions for further details.

Key Terms
الحياة
المزاج
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of life from the physical world must apply to God.
Correction: The author argues that the witnessed-world definition is not necessary for God; it only accompanies bodily life, not conditions it.

Checkpoint
What is the answer to the objection that life implies a body?
A) God has a spiritual body
B) The definition of life from the witnessed world is not necessary for God
C) Life is not an attribute of God
D) God's life is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
Taftazani refutes the objection by denying that the conditions of life in the witnessed world are necessary for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objection and Answer on Specifying Adam and 'With Our Eyes'
Objection and answer about the dual and plural forms in Qur'anic expressions for Adam and the ark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam defense against apparent anthropomorphic expressions in the Qur'an regarding the creation of Adam and the phrase 'with Our eyes'.

Core Idea
The dual and plural forms in Qur'an are rhetorical, not literal.

In this passage, an objector raises a problem: if all things are created by God's power, why specify Adam with the dual form (بأيدينا) and use the plural 'بأعيننا'? The answer clarifies that these expressions are for honor and emphasis on perfect power and comprehensive care, not literal multiplicity. This lesson unpacks the theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage (فإن قيل...) to understand the question. Notice the two issues: specifying Adam and the plural form.
2
Study the answer
Read the answer (أجيب...) and note how it addresses each point: the dual indicates completeness of power, and the plural indicates comprehensive protection.
The idiom 'بمرأى الملك ومسمع' is used to explain the meaning.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Consider why this answer is important: it defends divine transcendence (tanzih) while affirming the Qur'anic text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : جملة المكونات مخلوقة بقدرة الله تعالى فما وجه تخصيص خلق آدم عليه الصلاة والسلام سيما بلفظ المثنى ، وما وجه الجمع في قوله : بأعيننا ..؟ أجيب بأنه أريد كمال القدرة، وتخصيص آدم تشريف له وتكريم ومعنى تجري بأعيننا، أنها تجري بالمكان المحوط بالكلاءة والحفظ والرعاية يقال : فلان بمرأى الملك من ومسمع إذا كان بحيث تحوطه عنايته وتكتنفه رعايته
If it is said: All created things are created by the power of God Almighty, so what is the reason for specifying the creation of Adam (peace and blessings upon him) particularly with the dual form, and what is the reason for the plural in His saying: 'with Our eyes'? The answer: He intended the perfection of power, and specifying Adam is an honor and veneration for him. And the meaning of 'sailing with Our eyes' is that it sails in a place surrounded by protection, safeguarding, and care. It is said: So-and-so is in the sight and hearing of the king if he is such that his care encompasses him and his protection surrounds him.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (فإن قيل... أجيب). The objector notes that all things are created by God's power, so why specify Adam with the dual form ('بأيدينا' implied) and why use the plural 'بأعيننا'? The answer explains: the dual form indicates completeness of God's power (since a dual action can imply two agents, but here it emphasizes perfect power), and specifying Adam is a mark of honor. Regarding 'بأعيننا', the plural is not literal but idiomatic: it means that the ark sailed under God's comprehensive protection, as if in a place surrounded by His care and safeguarding. The author gives an Arabic idiom: someone is 'in the sight and hearing of the king' meaning they are under his full care. Thus, the passage guards against anthropomorphism by attributing multiple eyes to God, explaining it as a metaphor for divine care.

Key Terms

بأعيننا
المثنى
الجمع
الكلاءة
الحفظ
الرعاية
مرأى
مسمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dual and plural forms indicate multiple agents or literal eyes.
Correction: The dual form is rhetorical for completeness of power; the plural is idiomatic for comprehensive protection.

Checkpoint

A) That God created Adam with His two hands
B) The perfection and completeness of God's power
C) That Adam was created by a specific angel
D) That God's hands are literal

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic expressions 'with Our hands' and 'with Our eyes' are understood figuratively to denote divine power and care, not literal body parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says Adam was honored with the dual form to show 'perfection of power'. Are you truly trusting in a power that is complete, or are you still leaning on your own limited strength?

Rejected Interpretation and Metaphorical Principle
Rejection of literal 'eyes' and statement of metaphorical method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari scholars interpret Quranic terms like 'hand' and 'eye' as metaphors and how this safeguards divine transcendence from anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Metaphorical interpretation quickly negates any illusion of God resembling creation.

This passage addresses a specific far-fetched interpretation of 'eyes' in the Quran and then articulates the broader method used by scholars of eloquence: terms like 'hand', 'eye', and 'istiwa' are metaphors for divine attributes. The goal is to prevent any suggestion of corporeality. As you read, note how the author distinguishes the quick negation of anthropomorphism from the deeper recognition that these are merely representations for human understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow from rejecting an interpretation to stating the principle.
2
Identify the rejected interpretation
The passage begins by dismissing a view that 'eyes' refers to springs. Note why it is called 'far-fetched' (بعيد).
3
Understand the metaphorical method
The verifiers explain that terms like 'istiwa', 'hand', 'right hand', and 'eye' are metaphors for domination, power, and sight respectively. See how each metaphor corresponds to a divine attribute.
4
Grasp the purpose of metaphor
The primary purpose is to quickly negate any illusion of resemblance (تشبه) or corporeality (تجسم). Recognize that this is a pedagogical tool.
5
Reflect on the representations
The passage notes that these are mere representations (تمثيلات) of intellectual meanings in sensory forms. Think about why such representations are used for human understanding.
The author references his commentary on al-Talkhis for more detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : المراد الأعين التي انفجرت من الأرض وهو بعيد ، وفي كلام المحققين من علماء البيان أن قولنا الاستواء مجاز عن الاستيلاء، واليد واليمين عن القدرة ، والعين عن البصر، ونحو ذلك، إنما هو لنفي وهم التشبه والتجسم بسرعة، وإلا فهي تمثيلات وتصويرات للمعاني العقلية بإبرازها في الصور الحسية، وقد بينا ذلك في شرح التلخيص.
And it was said: What is meant are the springs that burst from the earth, and it is far-fetched. And in the speech of the verifiers among the scholars of eloquence, our saying 'istiwa' is a metaphor for domination, and 'hand' and 'right hand' for power, and 'eye' for sight, and the like, is only to quickly negate the illusion of resemblance and corporeality. Otherwise, they are representations and depictions of intellectual meanings by presenting them in sensory images. And we have explained that in the commentary on al-Talkhis.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage first dismisses an alternative interpretation that 'أعين' (eyes) refers to springs that burst from the earth, calling it 'far-fetched' (بعيد). Then it presents the established view of the verifiers (المحققين) among eloquence scholars: terms like 'istiwa' (establishing), 'hand', 'right hand', and 'eye' in the Quran are metaphorical expressions. Specifically, 'istiwa' is metaphor for domination or control, 'hand' and 'right hand' are metaphors for power, and 'eye' is a metaphor for sight. The purpose of this metaphorical interpretation is to quickly negate any illusion of resemblance (تشبه) or corporeality (تجسم) in God. If taken otherwise, they are mere representations (تمثيلات) and depictions (تصويرات) that present intelligible meanings in sensory forms for human understanding. The author notes that he has already explained this in his commentary on al-Talkhis (a famous work on rhetoric). This passage reinforces the Ash'ari principle of ta'wil (interpretation) to safeguard divine transcendence.

Key Terms
الأعين
الاستواء
اليد
اليمين
العين
مجاز
الاستيلاء
القدرة
البصر
التشبه
التجسم
تمثيلات
صور حسية
شرح التلخيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Quranic expressions like 'hand' and 'eye' are literal attributes of God, implying He has a physical body.
Correction: The passage explains that these are metaphors intended to quickly negate any illusion of resemblance or corporeality. They are representations of intellectual meanings in sensory forms, not literal descriptions.

Checkpoint

A) To make the verses more poetic
B) To quickly negate any illusion of resemblance and corporeality
C) Because the literal meaning is impossible to understand
D) To restrict God's attributes to just power and sight

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari scholars interpret Quranic expressions like 'hand' and 'eye' metaphorically to swiftly dispel any notion of God having a body or resembling creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about God's 'hand' or 'eye', do you catch yourself forming a mental image? What will you do the next time that happens to immediately reject that illusion and affirm His transcendence?
?
The passage says the metaphors are to 'quickly negate the illusion of resemblance'. How quickly does your mind resist anthropomorphic thoughts? If you find yourself lingering on a sensory image of God, what concrete step will you take to redirect your understanding to the intellectual meaning?
?
Are you content to accept that God's true nature is beyond your sensory experience, or do you feel a pull to imagine Him in familiar forms? How will you train yourself to think of His attributes as realities beyond your comprehension, trusting the scholars' method of ta'wil?

The Beatific Vision: Sunni Doctrine and the Point of Dispute
Sanusi states the Sunni doctrine on seeing God and clarifies the actual point of dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the actual point of dispute regarding the vision of God in the afterlife between Ahl al-Sunnah and other sects, and how Sanusi clarifies it.

Core Idea
The real dispute is about a perceptual state like vision without direction, not about knowledge or physical mechanisms.

Sanusi begins by stating the Sunni doctrine that believers will see God in Paradise without physical constraints. He then identifies groups who disagree and clarifies what is not in dispute. Finally, he pinpoints the exact issue: whether a state analogous to vision, which in this world requires direction and facing, can apply to Allah, who is transcendent beyond direction and place.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Sanusi first states the Sunni position before addressing disagreements.
The passage uses technical terms like الرؤية (vision) and الجهة (direction).
2
Identify the three parts of the passage
The passage has three logical parts: 1) the Sunni position and opponents, 2) what is not in dispute, 3) the actual point of dispute illustrated by an analogy.
This will help you see the argumentative structure.
3
Analyze the analogy of the sun
Sanusi uses the example of knowing the sun, seeing it with eyes closed, and seeing it with eyes open. Understand how each level differs and why the highest level is called vision.
This analogy is key to grasping the precise point of disagreement.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
After studying the passage, answer the multiple-choice question to confirm your understanding of the point of dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب أهل السنة إلى أن الله تعالى يجوز أن يرى وأن المؤمنين في الجنة يرونه منزهاً عن المقابلة والجهة والمكان وخالفهم في ذلك جميع الفرق، فإن المشبهة والكرامية إنما يقولون برؤيته في الجهة والمكان لكونه عندهم جسماً تعالى عن ذلك، ولا نزاع للمخالفين في جواز الانكشاف التام العلمي ولا لنا في امتناع ارتسام صورة من المرئي في العين أو اتصال الشعاع الخارج من العين بالمرئي، أو حالة إدراكية مستلزمة لذلك. وإنما محل النزاع أنا إذا عرفنا الشمس مثلاً بحد أو رسم كان نوعاً من المعرفة ثم إذا أبصرناها وغمضنا العين كان نوعاً آخر فوق الأول، ثم أنا إذا فتحنا العين حصل نوع آخر من الإدراك فوق الأولين نسميها الرؤية ولا تتعلق في الدنيا إلا بما هو في جهة ومكان فمثل هذه الحالة الإدراكية هل تصح أن تقع بدون المقابلة والجهة وأن تتعلق بذات الله تعالى منزهاً عن الجهة والمكان؟
The People of the Sunnah held that Allah the Exalted may be seen and that the believers in Paradise will see Him, transcendent above facing, direction, and place. All the sects disagreed with them in that. Indeed, the Anthropomorphists and the Karramiyyah only affirm seeing Him in direction and place because He is, in their view, a body—exalted is He above that. There is no dispute with the opponents regarding the permissibility of complete intellectual disclosure, nor with us regarding the impossibility of the imprinting of an image from the seen object in the eye, or the connection of a ray emitted from the eye to the seen object, or a perceptual state entailing that. Rather, the point of dispute is that when we know the sun, for example, by a definition or description, that is a type of knowledge; then when we see it and close the eye, that is another type above the first; then when we open the eye, another type of perception occurs above the first two, which we call vision, and in this world it does not relate except to what is in direction and place. So can such a perceptual state occur without facing and direction? And can it relate to the essence of Allah the Exalted, transcendent above direction and place?
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines the Sunni position on the beatific vision and clarifies the actual point of dispute. The author begins by stating the Sunni doctrine: Allah may be seen, and the believers will see Him in Paradise, but their vision is transcendent above facing, direction, and place. He notes that all other sects disagreed, particularly the Anthropomorphists (al-Mushabbiha) and the Karramiyyah, who affirm seeing in direction and place because they consider God a body—which is impossible for Him. The author then removes two issues from the dispute: first, the opponents do not deny the possibility of complete intellectual knowledge of God; second, the Sunnis do not affirm the physical mechanisms of vision like image imprinting or ray emission. The actual point of dispute is defined by an analogy: knowing something by definition is one type of knowledge; seeing it with eyes closed is a higher type; seeing with eyes open is a still higher type called vision, which in this world requires direction and place. The question is whether that same perceptual state can occur without direction and place, and whether it can relate to God's essence, which is transcendent. Thus, the dispute is about a specific perceptual state, not about all perception.

Correction: Sanusi clarifies that intellectual knowledge is not disputed, and physical mechanisms are not part of the Sunni position. The dispute is specifically about a perceptual state analogous to vision without direction.

Two Proofs from Musa's Request for Vision
Author presents two aspects of the proof from the verse for the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two textual proofs that the Sunni tradition uses to argue for the possibility and actual occurrence of seeing God in the afterlife, derived from Mūsā's request in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Musa's request for vision proves that seeing God is possible, not impossible.

In this passage, the author tackles a classic theological question: Can believers see God in the afterlife? By examining the story of Mūsā (Moses) in the Qur'an (7:143), he extracts two decisive proofs that establish the possibility of ru'yat Allāh. Both proofs rely on transmitted revelation, but they are so strong that they leave no room for doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the language, then read the translation. Note that the author acknowledges the proofs are based on transmitted text, not reason.
2
Understand the first proof
Focus on the first proof: if vision were impossible, Mūsā would not have asked. The author explains the implication: Mūsā was either knowledgeable or ignorant, both incompatible with prophethood.
This proof relies on the established fact that Mūsā asked for vision.
3
Understand the second proof
Consider the second proof: the conditional statement linking vision to the mountain's stability. Since the condition is possible, the conditioned is also possible. This establishes that vision is not inherently impossible.
The author uses the principle that what is conditioned on the possible is itself possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام طلب الرؤية، وأن الرؤية علقت على استقرار الجبل، إنما يثبت بالنقل دون العقل. قلنا : نعم لكنه قطعي لا نزاع في إمكانه بل وقوعه لنا من المنقول قوله تعالى حكاية رب أرني أنظر إليك (١) الآية والاستدلال فيها من وجهين : أحدهما : أنه لو لم تجز الرؤية لم يطلبها موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام. واللازم باطل بالنص والإجماع والتواتر وتسليم الخصم. وجه اللزوم أنه إن كان عالماً بالله تعالى، وما يجوز عليه، وما لا يجوز ، كان طلبه الرؤية عبثاً وإجراء لا يليق بالأنبياء . وإن كان جاهلاً لم يصلح أن يكون نبياً كلياً. وثانيهما : أنه علق الرؤية على استقرار الجبل وهو ممكن في نفسه ضرورة والمعلق على الممكن ممكن لأن معنى التعليق أن المعلق يقع على تقدير المعلق (٢) عليه، والمحال لا يقع على شيء من التقادير.
That Musa, peace be upon him, asked for vision, and that vision was conditioned on the stability of the mountain, is only established by transmission, not reason. We say: Yes, but it is definitive, there is no dispute about its possibility, rather its occurrence for us from the transmitted is His statement, exalted, relating: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You' (7:143) — the verse. And the inference from it is in two aspects: One of them: that if vision were not permissible, Musa, peace be upon him, would not have requested it. And the consequent is false by the text, consensus, tawatur, and the opponent's admission. The aspect of the implication is that if he was knowledgeable of Allah, and what is permissible for Him and what is not, then his request for vision would be futile and improper, unbefitting prophets. And if he was ignorant, he would not be fit to be a universal prophet. And the second: that he conditioned vision on the stability of the mountain, which is possible in itself necessarily, and what is conditioned on the possible is possible, because the meaning of conditioning is that the conditioned occurs on the estimation of the conditioner, and the impossible does not occur under any estimation.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs (wajhān) for the possibility of ru'yat Allāh (seeing God), derived from the story of Mūsā (Moses) in the Qur'ān (7:143). The author first notes that the historical fact that Mūsā asked to see God and that God conditioned vision on the mountain's stability are matters established solely by transmitted revelation (naql), not reason. Nevertheless, the transmitted proof is decisive (qaṭ'ī), leaving no room for dispute about the possibility or even actual occurrence of vision for believers. The first proof is based on Mūsā's request: if vision were impossible, a prophet of Mūsā's stature would not have requested it. The implication (luzūm) is explained: either Mūsā knew God's attributes and what is permissible for Him, in which case requesting the impossible would be futile and unbefitting a prophet; or he was ignorant, which would disqualify him from prophethood. Both alternatives are unacceptable. The second proof uses the conditional statement: God said, 'You will not see Me, but look at the mountain; if it remains in its place, then you will see Me.' The stability of the mountain is possible, and what is conditioned on something possible is itself possible. The impossible cannot be attached to any condition.

الرؤية
استقرار الجبل
التعليق
الممكن
النبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the proof relies solely on reason, but the author clarifies it is based on transmitted text.
Correction: The proofs are grounded in the Qur'an and consensus, not unaided reason.

Checkpoint

B) Because Mūsā knew God's attributes and would not ask for the futile
C) Because the mountain did not actually stand
D) Because the Mu'tazila denied the request

Key Takeaway
The two proofs from Mūsā's story conclusively show that seeing God is possible and will occur for believers.

?
Musa’s request was humble: 'My Lord, show me.' When was the last time you asked Allah for something with that level of humility and sincerity? If you knew for certain that your request would be answered, what would you ask for right now — not for worldly gain, but for a deeper knowledge of Him?

Refutation of the First Two Mu'tazilite Objections
Author refutes two Mu'tazilite claims about Musa's request and the verse's meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite reinterpretations of Musa's request are invalid and how the author defends the apparent meaning of the verse.

Core Idea
Mu'tazilite reinterpretations contradict the explicit wording and context of the Quranic verse.

The Mu'tazila raised several objections to the Ash'ari proof for the possibility of seeing Allah based on Musa's request. The first two objections attempt to reinterpret the verse away from its apparent meaning. In this passage, the author firmly refutes these claims by showing they violate the text, conflict with God's response, and are logically inconsistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the two objections and the author's refutation.
The author uses strong language like 'fāsid' (invalid) to dismiss the objections.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the two Mu'tazilite claims: (1) Musa asked for necessary knowledge, not vision; (2) there is an ellipsis meaning 'show me a sign'. Understand each claim.
Both objections try to avoid the literal meaning of requesting vision of Allah.
3
Analyze the refutation
The author gives multiple reasons: contradiction with apparent meaning, mismatch with God's reply 'lan tarānī', and internal inconsistencies. List the reasons.
The author also points out that the verb 'to look' with 'ilā' is explicit for vision.
4
Reflect on the method
Notice how the author uses textual evidence (ẓāhir), consensus (ijmā' al-Mu'tazila), and logical reasoning to refute the objections.
This is a classic kalam argumentative style.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترضت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول: أن موسی عليه الصلاة والسلام لم يطلب الرؤية، بل عبر بهـ لازمها الذي هو العلم الضروري. الثاني : : أنه على حذف المضاف، والمعنى أرني آية من آياتك، أنظر إلى آياتك، وكلاهما فاسد لمخالفته الظاهر بلا ضرورة ولعدم مطابقته الجواب أعني قوله : ولن تراني لأنه نفي لرؤية الله تعالى بإجماع المعتزلة لا للعلم الضروري، ولا لرؤية الآية، والعلامة . كيف وموسى عليه الصلاة والسلام عالم بربه سمع كلامه، وجعل يناجيه ويخاطبه، واختص من عنده بآيات كثيرة؟ فما معنى طلب العلم الضروري، واندكاك الجبل أعظم آية من آياته فكيف يستقيم نفي رؤية الآية، وأيضاً الآية إنما هي عند اندكاك الجبل لا استقراره فكيف يصح تعليق رؤيتها بالاستقرار؟ وأيضاً الرؤية المقرونة بالنظر الموصول (بإلى نص في الرؤية كذا في الإرشاد لإمام الحرمين). وما وقع في المواقف من أن الرؤية وإن استعملت للعلم لكنه بعيد جداً إذا وصلت بإلى سهوا ، ومؤول بأن النظر بمعنى الرؤية فوصله وصلها وإلا فليس في الآية وصل الرؤية بإلى .
And the Mu'tazila objected with several aspects: The first: that Musa, peace be upon him, did not request vision, but rather expressed its necessary concomitant, which is necessary knowledge. The second: that there is an ellipsis of the genitive, and the meaning is 'Show me a sign of Your signs, that I may look at Your signs.' And both are invalid because they contradict the apparent meaning without necessity, and because they do not match the answer, namely His statement: 'You will never see Me,' for it is a negation of seeing Allah by consensus of the Mu'tazila, not a negation of necessary knowledge, nor of seeing a sign. How? And Musa, peace be upon him, is knowledgeable of his Lord, heard His speech, used to converse with Him, and was specially favored with many signs? So what is the meaning of requesting necessary knowledge? And the crushing of the mountain is a greater sign of His signs, so how is the negation of seeing a sign appropriate? Also, the sign only occurs at the time of the mountain's crushing, not its stability, so how is the conditioning of its vision on stability correct? Also, vision coupled with 'to' (ilā) is text regarding vision, as in al-Irshād of Imām al-Ḥaramayn. And what occurred in al-Mawāqif that vision, although used for knowledge, is very far-fetched when connected with 'to' (ilā) inadvertently, and it is interpreted that 'looking' (naẓar) is in the sense of vision, so its connection is its connection; otherwise there is no connection of vision with 'to' in the verse.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents the first two objections raised by the Mu'tazila against the proof from Mūsā's request, along with the author's refutation. The first objection claims that Mūsā did not request actual vision (ru'ya) but rather its necessary concomitant (lāzim), which is necessary knowledge (al-‘ilm al-ḍarūrī) of God. The second objection posits an ellipsis (ḥadhf al-muḍāf) in the verse, interpreting 'show me' (arinī) as 'show me a sign' and 'look at You' (anẓuru ilayka) as 'look at Your signs'. The author declares both invalid because they violate the apparent (ẓāhir) meaning without necessity, and they do not match God's response 'lan tarānī' ('you will never see Me'), which by Mu'tazilite consensus negates seeing God, not necessary knowledge or seeing a sign. The author further argues: if Mūsā already knew God intimately through speech and signs, why would he need necessary knowledge? The crushing of the mountain (indikāk al-jabal) is itself a greater sign, and it is mentioned as a condition for vision, not for a sign. Moreover, the phrase 'look at you' (anẓuru ilayka) with the preposition 'ilā' is explicitly about vision, as stated in al-Irshād. The passage also clarifies a point from al-Mawāqif: that 'ru'ya' is sometimes used for knowledge, but that is far-fetched when connected with 'ilā'; the proper interpretation is that 'naẓar' here means vision, so the connection is of vision itself.

Key Terms

العلم الضروري
حذف المضاف
الظاهر
نفي الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Mūsā's request can be reinterpreted as asking for knowledge or a sign, thus avoiding the implication that seeing Allah is possible.
Correction: The author shows that such reinterpretations violate the explicit wording, the context, and God's response, and are logically inconsistent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the Mu'tazilite claim that 'arinī anẓur ilayka' means 'show me a sign'?
A) Because Mūsā already saw many signs
B) Because God's answer 'lan tarānī' negates seeing God, not a sign
C) Because the mountain's stability is a sign
D) Because the Mu'tazila themselves deny seeing signs

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite claim that Mūsā asked for necessary knowledge or a sign is refuted by the explicit wording of the Quranic text and the logical context of the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a clear verse from the Quran that makes you uncomfortable (like 'lan tarānī' used as a proof), do you try to reinterpret it to fit your preconceptions, or do you submit to its apparent meaning? Which Mu'tazilite attitude do you see in yourself?

Musa (as) already knew Allah intimately, yet he longed to see Him. How deep is your longing to meet Allah? Are you satisfied with mere knowledge, or do you yearn for direct encounter? What one change will you make this week to increase that yearning?
?

Refutation of Jahiz's Objection on Musa's Request
The author refutes al-Jāhiz's claim that Mūsā's vision request was for his people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that Mūsā's request for vision was only for his people is invalid, based on three arguments from the author.

Core Idea
Prophets do not confirm falsehood, even for a pedagogical purpose.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle objection from al-Jāhiz and his followers regarding Mūsā's request to see Allah. They argued that Mūsā only asked on behalf of his people, not for himself. The author refutes this interpretation with three cogent arguments, showing that a prophet would never delay refuting falsehood or engage in teaching through apparent error.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the source passage carefully to grasp al-Jāhiz's claim: Mūsā asked for vision only for his people, using a fortiori argument.
Note the key phrase 'al-ṭarīq al-ūlā'.
2
Identify the three refutations
The author gives three reasons (amā awwalan, amā thāniyan, amā thālithan). List them in your own words.
These are the core of the argument.
3
Examine the first refutation
Since deeming vision permissible is false and even kufr, a prophet would not delay refutation. Compare Mūsā's immediate response to the request for an idol.
4
Examine the second refutation
Mūsā only informed of non-occurrence, not impossibility. The people intended obstinacy, not genuine request.
5
Examine the third refutation
If the people believed Mūsā, his statement that vision is impossible would suffice. There was no need to request the impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث للجاحظ وأتباعه . أن موسى عليه السلام إنما سأل الرؤية لأجل قومه حين قالوا : أرنا الله جهرة (٣) وقالوا : لن نؤمن لك، حتى نرى الله جهرة (٤) وأضاف السؤال إلى نفسه (٥) ليمنع فيعلم امتناعها بالنسبة إلى القوم بالطريق الأولى. ولهذا قال أتهلكنا بما فعل السفهاء منا وهذا مع مخالفته الظاهر حيث لم يقل أرهم ينظروا إليك فاسد . أما أولاً : فلأن تجويز الرؤية باطل بل كفر عند أكثر المعتزلة، فلا يجوز لموسى عليه الصلاة والسلام تأخير الرد وتقرير الباطل. ألا ترى أنهم لما قالوا اجعل لنا إلهاً كما لهم آلهة (٦) () ردّ عليهم من ساعته بقوله إنكم قوم تجهلون (٨). وأما ثانياً : فلأنه لم يبين الامتناع لهم بل غايته الإخبار بعدم الوقوع، وإنما لقصدهم التعنت، والإلزام على موسى عليه السلام لا لطلبهم الباطل. وأما ثالثاً فلأنهم إن كانوا مؤمنين بموسى مصدقين لكلامه، كفاهـم إخباره بامتناع الرؤية من غير طلب للمحال، ومشاهدة لما جرت من الأحوال والأهوال
The third [objection] is of al-Jāhiz and his followers: that Mūsā, peace be upon him, only asked for vision for the sake of his people when they said: 'Show us Allah openly' (4:153) and said: 'We will not believe you until we see Allah openly' (2:55). And he attributed the request to himself to prevent, so that the impossibility [of vision] might be known with respect to the people by a fortiori argument. For this reason he said: 'Will You destroy us for what the foolish among us have done?' (7:155). This, despite contradicting the apparent meaning, since he did not say 'Show them that they may look at You,' is invalid. As for the first [point]: because deeming vision permissible is false, rather disbelief according to most Mu'tazila, so it is not permissible for Mūsā, peace be upon him, to delay the refutation and confirm falsehood. Do you not see that when they said: 'Make for us a god as they have gods' (7:138), he refuted them immediately with his words: 'Indeed, you are a people who act ignorantly' (7:138). And as for the second: because he did not explain the impossibility to them; rather his utmost was informing of non-occurrence. And they intended obstinacy and binding Mūsā, not a request for falsehood. And as for the third: because if they were believers in Mūsā, accepting his speech, his informing them of the impossibility of vision would suffice without requesting the impossible and witnessing the states and terrors that occurred.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third Mu'tazilite objection, attributed to al-Jāhiz (head of the Jāhiziyya sect) and his followers. Their claim is that Mūsā did not actually request vision for himself; rather, he asked on behalf of his people who had demanded to see God openly (Qur'ān 4:153, 2:55). Mūsā attributed the request to himself to teach them the impossibility of vision by a fortiori argument (al-ṭarīq al-ūlā). They cite Mūsā's later statement: 'Will You destroy us for what the foolish among us have done?' (7:155) as evidence. The author declares this interpretation invalid (fāsid) for three reasons: First, since deeming vision permissible is false (bāṭil) and even kufr according to most Mu'tazila, a prophet like Mūsā would not delay refuting error by posing such a request. He would have immediately corrected them, as he did when they asked for an idol (7:138). Second, Mūsā's response only informed of non-occurrence (al-ikhbār bi-'adam al-wuqū'), not impossibility. The people intended obstinacy (ta'annut), not a genuine request. Third, if the people already believed Mūsā, his mere statement that vision is impossible would suffice; there was no need to request the impossible or for them to witness terrifying events.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الجاحظ
الطريق الأولى
الامتناع
التعنت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A prophet may feign a request to teach a lesson, even if it appears to confirm falsehood.
Correction: The author argues that this would be tantamount to confirming falsehood, which is impermissible for a prophet. Mūsā's conduct must be consistent with truth at all times.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why would Mūsā not have delayed refuting the people's request for vision if it were impossible?
A) Because he wanted to show them a miracle
B) Because deeming vision permissible is kufr, so he would have immediately corrected them
C) Because his people did not actually believe in him
D) Because the mountain's stability was a sign

Key Takeaway
The author demonstrates that a prophet's conduct must be consistent with truth; he cannot engage in actions that appear to confirm falsehood, even for a higher pedagogical aim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a small compromise in truthfulness—a white lie, a delayed correction, an ambiguous phrase—for what you thought was a higher good? How does Musa's immediate refutation of his people's request for a god (7:138) confront that justification?
?
When have you remained silent in the face of falsehood, telling yourself it was for the sake of peace or because correcting it would be too costly? What would it cost you to, like Musa, refute even a well-intentioned error without delay?
?
Is there a falsehood you are currently 'attributing to yourself'—a pretense or a role you play—for the sake of others, convincing yourself it is pedagogical or merciful? What would it mean to abandon that pretense and stand only in truth, even if it makes things harder for those you are trying to help?

Al-Jahiz's Argument and Initial Refutation
Presents al-Jahiz's argument and the author's initial refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jahiz's claim that Musa asked for ru'ya on behalf of his people and the author's initial rejection that this contradicts the apparent meaning of the verse.

Core Idea
Musa's request was personal, not on behalf of his people.

Al-Jahiz and his followers attempted to explain away Musa's request for vision by claiming he sought it on behalf of his people. This lesson presents that argument and the author's immediate critique that it contradicts the Quranic text.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the passage where al-Jahiz presents his argument: Musa asked for ru'ya for his people and added the request to himself to demonstrate impossibility by priority.
Notice the phrase 'بالطريق الأولى' – by priority.
2
Note the author's rebuttal
The author declares the argument invalid because Musa did not say 'Show them looking at You', contradicting the apparent meaning.
This is a direct textual criticism.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the author's rebuttal is sufficient to dismiss the argument without further analysis at this stage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث للجاحظ وأتباعه. أن موسى عليه السلام إنما سأل الرؤية لأجل قومه حين قالوا: أرنا الله جهرة وقالوا: لن نؤمن لك، حتى نرى الله جهرة وأضاف السؤال إلى نفسه ليمنع فيعلم امتناعها بالنسبة إلى القوم بالطريق الأولى. ولهذا قال أتهلكنا بما فعل السفهاء منا وهذا مع مخالفته الظاهر حيث لم يقل أرهم ينظروا إليك فاسد. أما أولاً: فلأن تجويز الرؤية باطل بل كفر عند أكثر المعتزلة، فلا يجوز لموسى عليه الصلاة والسلام تأخير الرد وتقرير الباطل. ألا ترى أنهم لما قالوا اجعل لنا إلهاً كما لهم آلهة رد عليهم من ساعته بقوله إنكم قوم تجهلون. وأما ثانياً: فلأنه لم يبين الامتناع لهم بل غايته الإخبار بعدم الوقوع، وإنما لقصدهم التعنت، والإلزام على موسى عليه السلام لا لطلبهم الباطل. وأما ثالثاً فلأنهم إن كانوا مؤمنين بموسى مصدقين لكلامه، كفاهم إخباره بامتناع الرؤية من غير طلب للمحال، ومشاهدة لما جرت من الأحوال والأهوال وإلا لم يفد الطلب والجواب لأنهم وإن سمعوا الجواب فهو المخبر بأنه كلام الله تعالى. والمعتزلة تحيروا في هذا المقام، فزعموا تارة أنهم كانوا مؤمنين، لكن لم يعلموا مسألة الرؤية، وظنوا جوازها عند سماع الكلام واختار موسى عليه السلام في الرد عليهم طريق السؤال والجواب من الله ليكون أوثق عندهم وأهدى إلى الحق، وتارة أنهم لم يكونوا مؤمنين حق الإيمان ولا كافرين بل مستدلين، أو فاسقين أو مقلدين. فاقترحوا ما اقترحوا وأجيبوا بما أجيبوا، وأضاف موسى عليه السلام الرؤية إلى نفسه دونهم لئلا يبقى لهم عذر، ولا يقولوا لو سألها لنفسه لرآه لعلو قدره عند الله تعالى، وكل ذلك خبط، لأن السائلين القائلين: لن نؤمن لك حتى نرى الله جهرة، لم يكونوا مؤمنين ولا حاضرين عند سؤال الرؤية ليسمعوا جواب الله تعالى، وإنما الحاضرون هم السبعون المختارون ولا يتصور منهم عدم تصديق موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام في الاخبار بامتناع الرؤية، ولا فائدة للسؤال بحضرتهم على تقدير امتناع الرؤية، إلا أن يطلعوا فيخبروا السائلين ولا شك أنهم إذا لم يقبلوه من موسى مع تأيده بالمعجزات فمن السبعين أولى.
The third (argument) is by al-Jahiz and his followers: That Musa, peace be upon him, only asked for ru'ya for the sake of his people when they said: 'Show us God openly' and said: 'We will not believe you until we see God openly'. And he added the question to himself to prevent, so that its impossibility would be known with respect to the people by priority. Hence he said: 'Would you destroy us for what the fools among us have done?' And this, despite its apparent contradiction, since he did not say 'Show them looking at You' is invalid. As for firstly: Because permitting ru'ya is false, rather disbelief according to most Mu'tazila, so it is not permissible for Musa, peace and prayer be upon him, to delay the refutation and confirm falsehood. Do you not see that when they said: 'Make for us a god as they have gods', he refuted them immediately by saying: 'Indeed you are a people who are ignorant'. And as for secondly: Because he did not show impossibility to them, rather his purpose was only to inform of non-occurrence, and they intended obstinacy and blaming Musa, not their false request. And as for thirdly: Because if they were believers in Musa, accepting his speech, his informing them of the impossibility of ru'ya would suffice them without asking for the impossible, and witnessing what happened of conditions and terrors. Otherwise, the request and answer would not be useful, because even if they heard the answer, it would inform that it is the speech of God Almighty. And the Mu'tazila were confused in this position, so they claimed sometimes that they were believers, but they did not know the issue of ru'ya, and thought its permissibility when hearing the speech, and Musa chose in refuting them the way of asking and answering from God to be more authoritative for them and more guiding to the truth; and sometimes that they were not believers in true faith nor disbelievers, but seekers of proof, or sinners, or blind followers. So they proposed what they proposed and were answered what they were answered, and Musa added the ru'ya to himself excluding them so that no excuse remains for them, and they would not say: If he asked it for himself, he would have seen Him due to his high rank with God. And all of that is confusion, because the questioners who said: 'We will not believe you until we see God openly' were not believers nor present at the time of asking for ru'ya to hear God's answer. Rather, the present ones were the seventy chosen ones, and it is inconceivable that they would disbelieve Musa in informing of the impossibility of ru'ya. And there is no benefit in asking in their presence on the assumption of ru'ya's impossibility, except that they might learn and inform the questioners, and no doubt that if they did not accept it from Musa supported by miracles, then from the seventy it is even more unlikely.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third argument of al-Jahiz and his followers regarding Prophet Musa's request to see God. The argument claims that Musa only asked for ru'ya on behalf of his people, who had demanded to see God. Musa added the request to himself to demonstrate impossibility by priority, and said 'Would you destroy us for what the fools among us have done?' The author then refutes this with three points: First, permitting ru'ya is false and even kufr according to most Mu'tazila, so Musa would not delay refutation. Second, Musa's response indicated non-occurrence, not impossibility. Third, if his people truly believed him, they would have accepted his report without demanding ru'ya. The author then discusses the Mu'tazila's confusion, who attempted various excuses—claiming the people were believers but ignorant of ru'ya, or that they were neither believers nor disbelievers, etc. The author dismisses all as confusion, because the actual questioners were not present when Musa asked for ru'ya; only the seventy chosen elders were present, and they would have accepted Musa's word. Therefore, the argument fails.

Key Terms
رؤية
جاحظ
موسى
سؤال
امتناع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Musa asked for ru'ya only for his people.
Correction: The verse shows Musa asked personally, not on behalf of others.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jahiz claim about Musa's request?
A) Musa asked for himself.
B) Musa asked for his people.
C) Musa did not ask.
D) Musa asked for angels.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jahiz's argument that Musa asked for his people is invalid because the Quranic text shows he asked personally, not on their behalf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the story of Musa asking to see Allah, do you ever feel that you understand better than the prophet himself? This lesson shows that even a great scholar like al-Jahiz can misinterpret the Quran. How do you guard against reading your own desires or preconceptions into the verses? What practical step can you take today to approach the Quran with more humility?
?
If you were in Musa's position, would you have asked on behalf of the people or only for yourself? What does this lesson teach you about the sincerity and personal nature of your own supplications? Are your prayers more about what you want for yourself or what you want for others? How can you strike a better balance?
?
The author dismisses al-Jahiz's argument because it contradicts the apparent meaning of the verse. Are you willing to accept that your own interpretations of Islamic texts might be invalid if they conflict with the clear wording? Think of one belief or practice you hold that you have not verified from the primary sources. Will you commit to checking it today?

Three Refutations of Al-Jahiz's Argument
Enumerates three refutations of al-Jahiz's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three detailed refutations against al-Jahiz's argument: that permitting ru'ya is disbelief, that Musa only informed of non-occurrence, and that believers would have accepted his report.

Core Idea
Each refutation demolishes a different assumption in al-Jahiz's claim.

After declaring al-Jahiz's argument invalid, the author provides three specific reasons. This lesson examines each reason and shows how they collectively refute the notion that Musa's request was pedagogically motivated.

Guided Path
1
Read the three refutations
Read the passage that enumerates the three points: first, second, and third.
Notice the structure: أما أولاً, أما ثانياً, أما ثالثاً.
2
Understand the first refutation
The first refutation argues that permitting ru'ya is kufr, so Musa would not delay refutation, as proven by his immediate rebuke regarding the calf.
This shows consistency in Musa's prophetic behavior.
3
Understand the second refutation
The second refutation points out that Musa only informed of non-occurrence, not impossibility, so it was not a demonstration of impossibility.
This distinguishes between reporting and proving.
4
Understand the third refutation
The third refutation says that if the people truly believed, they would have accepted his word without asking; if not, the request and answer would not benefit them.
This undermines the purpose of the supposed pedagogical request.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third argument of al-Jahiz and his followers regarding Prophet Musa's request to see God. What is the first refutation against al-Jahiz?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Allah's command is clear to you, do you still seek a visible sign before obeying? Consider that the people of Musa, despite witnessing miracles, demanded to see Allah. What does your demand for proof before obedience reveal about your trust in Allah's word? Commit today to obey one command you have been hesitant about without requiring a sign.
?
The first refutation emphasizes that allowing the request to stand without rebuke would confirm falsehood. Think of a time when you remained silent about a false belief or practice among your peers to avoid conflict. How can you, like Musa, speak truth immediately even when it is uncomfortable? Who is someone you need to correct with wisdom and courage this week?
?
Musa's people witnessed terrifying conditions but still demanded ru'ya. Are there repeated blessings or trials in your life that should make Allah's power evident, yet you still feel distant from Him? What specific event today will you take as a direct communication from Allah, without demanding further proof?

Refutation of the Mu'tazila's Confusion
Discusses and refutes Mu'tazila's attempts to excuse al-Jahiz's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's various attempts to defend al-Jahiz's position and the author's ultimate refutation that the questioners were not present.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's excuses collapse because the actual questioners were absent during Musa's request.

Al-Jahiz's followers, the Mu'tazila, offered several convoluted explanations to salvage his argument. This lesson presents their confusion and the author's decisive rebuttal based on the historical context of the event.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's claims
Read the passage where the Mu'tazila propose that the questioners were believers but ignorant, or not fully believers, etc.
Notice their attempts to reconcile the argument with prophetic integrity.
2
Understand the author's refutation
The author dismisses all as confusion because the actual questioners were not present; only the seventy elders were there, who already believed.
This is a historical and logical refutation.
3
Consider the conclusion
The request was pointless since the present elders would have believed without it, and the absent questioners would not benefit.
This undercuts any remaining defense of al-Jahiz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث للجاحظ وأتباعه. أن موسى عليه السلام إنما سأل الرؤية لأجل قومه حين قالوا: أرنا الله جهرة وقالوا: لن نؤمن لك، حتى نرى الله جهرة وأضاف السؤال إلى نفسه ليمنع فيعلم امتناعها بالنسبة إلى القوم بالطريق الأولى. ولهذا قال أتهلكنا بما فعل السفهاء منا وهذا مع مخالفته الظاهر حيث لم يقل أرهم ينظروا إليك فاسد. أما أولاً: فلأن تجويز الرؤية باطل بل كفر عند أكثر المعتزلة، فلا يجوز لموسى عليه الصلاة والسلام تأخير الرد وتقرير الباطل. ألا ترى أنهم لما قالوا اجعل لنا إلهاً كما لهم آلهة رد عليهم من ساعته بقوله إنكم قوم تجهلون. وأما ثانياً: فلأنه لم يبين الامتناع لهم بل غايته الإخبار بعدم الوقوع، وإنما لقصدهم التعنت، والإلزام على موسى عليه السلام لا لطلبهم الباطل. وأما ثالثاً فلأنهم إن كانوا مؤمنين بموسى مصدقين لكلامه، كفاهم إخباره بامتناع الرؤية من غير طلب للمحال، ومشاهدة لما جرت من الأحوال والأهوال وإلا لم يفد الطلب والجواب لأنهم وإن سمعوا الجواب فهو المخبر بأنه كلام الله تعالى. والمعتزلة تحيروا في هذا المقام، فزعموا تارة أنهم كانوا مؤمنين، لكن لم يعلموا مسألة الرؤية، وظنوا جوازها عند سماع الكلام واختار موسى عليه السلام في الرد عليهم طريق السؤال والجواب من الله ليكون أوثق عندهم وأهدى إلى الحق، وتارة أنهم لم يكونوا مؤمنين حق الإيمان ولا كافرين بل مستدلين، أو فاسقين أو مقلدين. فاقترحوا ما اقترحوا وأجيبوا بما أجيبوا، وأضاف موسى عليه السلام الرؤية إلى نفسه دونهم لئلا يبقى لهم عذر، ولا يقولوا لو سألها لنفسه لرآه لعلو قدره عند الله تعالى، وكل ذلك خبط، لأن السائلين القائلين: لن نؤمن لك حتى نرى الله جهرة، لم يكونوا مؤمنين ولا حاضرين عند سؤال الرؤية ليسمعوا جواب الله تعالى، وإنما الحاضرون هم السبعون المختارون ولا يتصور منهم عدم تصديق موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام في الاخبار بامتناع الرؤية، ولا فائدة للسؤال بحضرتهم على تقدير امتناع الرؤية، إلا أن يطلعوا فيخبروا السائلين ولا شك أنهم إذا لم يقبلوه من موسى مع تأيده بالمعجزات فمن السبعين أولى.
The third (argument) is by al-Jahiz and his followers: That Musa, peace be upon him, only asked for ru'ya for the sake of his people when they said: 'Show us God openly' and said: 'We will not believe you until we see God openly'. And he added the question to himself to prevent, so that its impossibility would be known with respect to the people by priority. Hence he said: 'Would you destroy us for what the fools among us have done?' And this, despite its apparent contradiction, since he did not say 'Show them looking at You' is invalid. As for firstly: Because permitting ru'ya is false, rather disbelief according to most Mu'tazila, so it is not permissible for Musa, peace and prayer be upon him, to delay the refutation and confirm falsehood. Do you not see that when they said: 'Make for us a god as they have gods', he refuted them immediately by saying: 'Indeed you are a people who are ignorant'. And as for secondly: Because he did not show impossibility to them, rather his purpose was only to inform of non-occurrence, and they intended obstinacy and blaming Musa, not their false request. And as for thirdly: Because if they were believers in Musa, accepting his speech, his informing them of the impossibility of ru'ya would suffice them without asking for the impossible, and witnessing what happened of conditions and terrors. Otherwise, the request and answer would not be useful, because even if they heard the answer, it would inform that it is the speech of God Almighty. And the Mu'tazila were confused in this position, so they claimed sometimes that they were believers, but they did not know the issue of ru'ya, and thought its permissibility when hearing the speech, and Musa chose in refuting them the way of asking and answering from God to be more authoritative for them and more guiding to the truth; and sometimes that they were not believers in true faith nor disbelievers, but seekers of proof, or sinners, or blind followers. So they proposed what they proposed and were answered what they were answered, and Musa added the ru'ya to himself excluding them so that no excuse remains for them, and they would not say: If he asked it for himself, he would have seen Him due to his high rank with God. And all of that is confusion, because the questioners who said: 'We will not believe you until we see God openly' were not believers nor present at the time of asking for ru'ya to hear God's answer. Rather, the present ones were the seventy chosen ones, and it is inconceivable that they would disbelieve Musa in informing of the impossibility of ru'ya. And there is no benefit in asking in their presence on the assumption of ru'ya's impossibility, except that they might learn and inform the questioners, and no doubt that if they did not accept it from Musa supported by miracles, then from the seventy it is even more unlikely.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third argument of al-Jahiz and his followers regarding Prophet Musa's request to see God. The argument claims that Musa only asked for ru'ya on behalf of his people, who had demanded to see God. Musa added the request to himself to demonstrate impossibility by priority, and said 'Would you destroy us for what the fools among us have done?' The author then refutes this with three points: First, permitting ru'ya is false and even kufr according to most Mu'tazila, so Musa would not delay refutation. Second, Musa's response indicated non-occurrence, not impossibility. Third, if his people truly believed him, they would have accepted his report without demanding ru'ya. The author then discusses the Mu'tazila's confusion, who attempted various excuses—claiming the people were believers but ignorant of ru'ya, or that they were neither believers nor disbelievers, etc. The author dismisses all as confusion, because the actual questioners were not present when Musa asked for ru'ya; only the seventy chosen elders were present, and they would have accepted Musa's word. Therefore, the argument fails.

Correction: The author shows their claims are baseless because the questioners were absent.

A) Because the Mu'tazila are unorthodox.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's confusion is refuted by the historical fact that the demanding questioners were not present during Musa's request.

?
When you encounter a proof that contradicts your previous understanding, do you react like the Mu'tazila—searching for excuses—or do you submit to the truth even if it humbles you? Which path will you choose today?

Fourth Argument: Seeking Ru'ya for Increased Certainty
Refutes the claim that Musa asked for ru'ya for increased tranquility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the comparison between Musa's request for ru'ya and Ibrahim's request to see how God revives the dead is invalid.

Core Idea
Asking for the impossible, even with good intentions, implies ignorance.

This lesson examines the fourth argument proposed by those who claim Musa asked for ru'ya despite knowing its impossibility, supposedly to gain greater certainty. The argument draws a parallel with Prophet Ibrahim's request. However, the refutation shows a critical flaw: the analogy fails because requesting something impossible implies ignorance, a point even basic Mu'tazila understood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully and then its literal translation. Notice the structure: the argument is presented, then refuted.
The passage is a single sentence but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the analogy
The argument compares Musa's request to Ibrahim's request. Understand the similarity being claimed: both sought increased certainty through combined evidence.
3
Grasp the refutation
The refutation points out that asking for the impossible implies ignorance. Reflect on why this is a powerful counter to the analogy.
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: أنه سأل الرؤية مع علمه بامتناعها لزيادة الطمأنينة بتعاضد دليل العقل والسمع كما في طلب ابراهيم عليه السلام أنه يريه كيفية إحياء الموتى ورد بأن هذا لا ينبغي أن يكون بطريق طلب المحال الموهم الجهل بما يعرفه آحاد المعتزلة.
The fourth: That he asked for ru'ya while knowing its impossibility for increased tranquility through the corroboration of intellect and revelation, as in the request of Ibrahim, peace be upon him, that He show him how He revives the dead, and it is refuted that this should not be through the path of asking for the impossible which implies ignorance of what is known by individual Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument that Prophet Musa asked for ru'ya despite knowing its impossibility, in order to gain greater certainty by combining rational and revealed evidence, similar to Prophet Ibrahim's request to see how God revives the dead. The refutation states that this comparison is invalid because asking for something known to be impossible implies ignorance of basic theological facts that even ordinary Mu'tazila are aware of. Therefore, the analogy does not hold, and the argument is rejected.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Musa's request for ru'ya is similar to Ibrahim's request to see how God revives the dead.
Correction: Ibrahim asked about a possible event (resurrection), while ru'ya is considered impossible for Allah in this life. Thus the analogy is invalid.

Checkpoint

B) By saying asking for the impossible implies ignorance.
C) By saying the Quran forbids it.
D) By saying the Mu'tazila refuted it.

Key Takeaway
The comparison between Musa's request and Ibrahim's request fails because asking for the impossible, unlike asking for a possible demonstration, implies ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you feel your faith wavering, do you seek certainty through signs that Allah has already declared impossible, or through the evidence He has already given you? What is one concrete step you will take to rely on the proofs He has provided instead of demanding the impossible?
