The Fourth Objection: Musa's Request for Ru'yah
The author presents and refutes the fourth objection regarding Musa's request for ru'yah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the argument that Musa asked for ru'yah to increase reassurance, and why it is invalid.

Core Idea
Asking for the impossible implies ignorance unbecoming of a prophet.

In this passage, the author addresses the fourth objection raised by those who claim that Prophet Musa (peace be upon him) asked to see Allah, knowing it was impossible, merely to strengthen his certainty by combining rational and scriptural evidence. The author refutes this by pointing out the grave implication that such a request would attribute ignorance to a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the two parts: the objection and the refutation.
Notice the compact style of the author.
2
Identify the parallel with Ibrahim
The objection compares Musa's request to Ibrahim's request to see how Allah revives the dead. Understand the difference between the two requests.
Ibrahim's request was for a possible event; Musa's was for the impossible.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author argues that asking for the impossible is like claiming ignorance of what even the lowest Mu'tazilite knows. Why is this a strong refutation?
It shows that such a request is beneath prophetic dignity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه سأل الرؤية مع علمه بامتناعها لزيادة الطمأنينة بتعاضد دليل العقل والسمع كما في طلب ابراهيم عليه السلام أنه يريه كيفية إحياء الموتى ورد بأن هذا لا ينبغي أن يكون بطريق طلب المحال الموهم الجهله بما يعرفه آحاد المعتزلة .
Fourth: That he asked for the vision while knowing its impossibility to increase reassurance by the mutual reinforcement of the evidence of reason and revelation, as in the request of Ibrahim, peace be upon him, that He show him how He revives the dead. And it was replied that this ought not to be by way of asking for the impossible, which implies ignorance of what the lowest of the Mu'tazila know.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection made against the story of Musa asking for ru'yah. The objection claims that Musa, knowing the impossibility of seeing Allah, asked for it only to increase his heart's reassurance, similar to Ibrahim's request to see how Allah revives the dead. The author then gives the refutation: this is inappropriate because it would imply that Musa was ignorant of what even the least knowledgeable of the Mu'tazila know—that asking for the impossible out of ignorance is not befitting a prophet. The refutation asserts that the analogy with Ibrahim is invalid because Ibrahim asked for a demonstration of a possible event (reviving the dead) while Musa asked for an inherently impossible vision.

Correction: The passage shows that such a request would imply ignorance of the impossibility, which is beneath the status of prophets.

D) Because the comparison with Ibrahim is exact

Key Takeaway
Musa's request for ru'yah cannot be excused as seeking reassurance because it would imply ignorance of the impossibility, which is beneath a prophet's status.

The Fifth Objection: Musa's Ignorance of the Ru'yah Issue
The author presents and refutes the fifth objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth objection against the possibility of ru'yah and why the author rejects it as an insult to the prophet's knowledge.

Core Idea
Excusing Musa's ignorance is an insult to prophethood.

This passage tackles a crucial theological objection: could Prophet Musa have been ignorant of the ru'yah issue? The objectors propose excuses, but the author exposes them as a thinly veiled insult to the prophet's station. Let's examine the objection and the author's sharp refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then read the translation. Notice the tone of the author's response.
The author uses strong language at the end.
2
Identify the objection's two scenarios
The objector offers two possibilities to explain Musa's request: either he was too busy to consider the issue, or he considered it and sought knowledge. Find these in the passage.
Both scenarios assume Musa did not know the ruling.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author calls this a 'softening of expression' and says it reduces the prophet to the level of Mu'tazilite dregs. Ask yourself why this is so offensive.
The author equates the objection to accusing the prophet of crass ignorance.
4
Reflect on the key takeaway
Consider why the author ends with a prayer seeking refuge. This shows the gravity of the accusation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: إن معرفة الله تعالى لا تتوقف على العلم بمسألة الرؤية، فيجوز أن يكون لاشتغاله بسائر العلوم والوظائف الشرعية لم تخطر بباله هذه المسألة حين سألوها منه ، فطلب العلم ثم تاب عن تركه طريقة الاستدلال أو خطر بباله، وكان ناظراً فيها طالباً للحق ، فاجترؤوا على السؤال ليتبين له جلية الحال، وهذا تعبيرا وتلطيف للعبارة في التعبير عن جهل كليم الله بما يجوز عليه، وما لا يجوز، وقصروه في المعرفة عن حثالة المعتزلة . نعوذ بالله من الغباوة والغواية .
Fifth: That knowledge of Allah, exalted, is not dependent on knowledge of the issue of ru'yah. So it is possible that due to his preoccupation with other sciences and religious duties, this issue did not occur to him when they asked him about it. Then he sought knowledge, and then repented for abandoning the method of inference, or it occurred to him and he was examining it, seeking the truth. So they dared to ask so that the reality of the matter would become clear to him. And this is a euphemism and softening of expression in describing the ignorance of the interlocutor of Allah (Kaleem Allah) regarding what is permissible for Him and what is not, and they reduced him in knowledge to the dregs of the Mu'tazila. We seek refuge in Allah from stupidity and misguidance.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, which attempts to excuse Musa's request by suggesting that knowledge of Allah does not depend on knowing the ru'yah issue. The objectors propose that Musa might have been so busy with other religious sciences that he never considered this issue until the Israelites asked, and then he sought knowledge. Or he might have repented from his earlier neglect of inference and then engaged in inquiry. The author criticizes this as a polite expression for what is actually an accusation of ignorance against Musa, implying he was less knowledgeable than the lowliest Mu'tazilite. The author ends with a prayer seeking refuge from such stupidity.

Correction: The author argues that claiming Musa was ignorant is an insult to his prophethood and reduces him below the Mu'tazilites.

A) As a reasonable defense
B) As a polite way of accusing the prophet of crass ignorance
C) As a valid theological point
D) As a position held by the Ash'aris

Key Takeaway
The objection that Musa was ignorant of ru'yah is a euphemistic insult to prophethood, which the author refutes by highlighting its offensive implication.

Objection and Answer on Stability During Movement
First objection and answer on stability during movement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a kalam scholar defends the possibility of the mountain's stability during the revelation by distinguishing essential possibility from simultaneous occurrence.

Core Idea
Stability during movement is possible because essential possibility allows one state to replace another.

The objector argues that the mountain's stability is impossible at the moment of looking because it is trembling. The defender responds by clarifying that stability can occur during movement—not simultaneously, but by stillness replacing movement. This hinges on the concept of essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati), which remains constant. Let's read the exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim: 'We do not concede that He conditioned the vision upon the stability of the mountain absolutely... rather it is after the looking... a state of trembling and crumbling.'
Note the use of the particle 'fa' to indicate sequence.
2
Identify the key point
The objector says stability at that moment is impossible. Recognize that they reject the possibility of stillness replacing movement.
3
Read the answer
The defender states: stability during movement is possible by obtaining stillness instead of movement, because essential possibility does not cease.
Essential possibility means the thing could be otherwise.
4
Understand the distinction
The impossibility is only the simultaneity of movement and stillness, not their sequence. The analogy of Zayd standing while sitting illustrates this.
One state can follow another; only concurrent opposites are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاعتراض على الوجه الثاني من طريق الاستدلال فمن وجوه : :أحدها: أنا لا نسلم أنه علق الرؤية على استقرار الجبل مطلقاً أو حالة السكون ليكون ممكناً بل عقيب النظر بدلالة الفاء وهو حالة تزلزل واندكاك، ولا نسلم إمكان الاستقرار حينئذ . والجواب : أن الاستقرار حالة الحركة أيضاً ممكن بأن يحصل بدل الحركة السكون لأن الإمكان الذاتي لا يزول ولهذا صح جعله دكاً. فإنه لا يقال جعله دكاً إلا فيما يجوز أن لا يكون كذا، وإنما المحال هو اجتماع الحركة والسكون، وهذا كما أن قيام زید حال قعوده يمكن وبالعكس واجتماعهما محال . وما يقال : إن الاستقرار مع الحركة محال إن أريد به الاجتماع فمسلم ، لكن ليس هو المعلق عليه، وإن أريد المقيد بالمعية فممنوع. فإن قيل : قد جعلتم الأعم وهو الإمكان الذاتي مستلزماً للأخص وهو الاستقبالي. قلنا العموم والخصوص بينهما إنما هو بحسب المفهوم دون الوجود، لأن الممكن الذاتي ممكن أبداً، وقد يقال في الجواب إنه علقها على استقرار الجبل من حيث هو من غير قيد، وهو ممكن في نفسه ويرد عليه أنه واقع في الدنيا فيلزم وقوع الرؤية فيها ،
As for the objection to the second way of argumentation, it is from several aspects: One of them: We do not concede that He conditioned the vision upon the stability of the mountain absolutely or upon a state of rest such that it would be possible; rather it is after the looking indicated by the particle 'fa', and that is a state of trembling and crumbling, and we do not concede the possibility of stability at that time. The answer: That stability during movement is also possible, because by obtaining stillness instead of movement, since essential possibility does not cease; therefore it was made a crumbled thing. For it is not said 'He made it crumbled' except regarding something that can be otherwise. Rather the impossible is the simultaneity of movement and stillness. And this is like the standing of Zayd during his sitting: it is possible, and the opposite, and their simultaneity is impossible. And what is said: That stability with movement is impossible: if what is meant is simultaneity, it is conceded, but that is not what is conditioned; if what is meant is that it is restricted to companionship, then it is denied. Then it is said: You have made the more general, which is essential possibility, entail the more specific, which is stability. We say: The generality and specificity between them is only according to concept, not existence, because the essentially possible is possible eternally. And it may be said in answer that He conditioned it upon the stability of the mountain in itself without qualification, and it is possible in itself, but it is objected that it occurs in this world, so the occurrence of the vision would follow.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage contains a detailed objection and counter-response regarding the interpretation of the verse in which Allah says 'look at the mountain; if it stays in its place, then you will see Me' (Quran 7:143). The objector argues that the condition of stability (istiqrar) is linked to the mountain's state after looking (bi dalalat al-fa'), which is a state of trembling, so stability is impossible at that moment. The answer explains that stability during movement is possible because the mountain could become still instead of moving; essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) remains. The impossibility is only the simultaneous presence of movement and stillness, not one after the other. Then a further objection is raised: the objector accuses the defender of making the general (essential possibility) entail the specific (stability). The defender responds that the relationship is conceptual, not existential. Lastly, a possible answer is proposed: that the condition was unqualified, so it is possible, but it is then objected that if stability occurs in this world, then the vision would also occur in this world, which is not the case. The passage ends with that objection, implying that further discussion is needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that stability and movement cannot coexist at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they can exist consecutively, but not simultaneously. Stability during movement is possible by sequential replacement.

Checkpoint

A) By denying that the mountain was moving

C) By saying that the vision was not actually conditioned on stability

Key Takeaway
Essential possibility remains even if a state is not actual, so stability can occur during movement as long as it is not simultaneous.

Further Objections: Simultaneity and Generality
Second and third objections and answers on simultaneity and generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the next stage of the debate: the distinction between simultaneity and companionship, and how essential possibility relates to specific possibilities.

Core Idea
Generality in concept does not entail generality in existence.

After the first response, the objector raises a new point: stability with movement might be impossible if 'with' means companionship. Then a further objection challenges the defender for making the general (essential possibility) entail the specific (stability). The defender clarifies that the relationship is conceptual, not existential. Finally, an alternative answer is proposed and then refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
The objector says: 'If 'with' means simultaneity, conceded; if companionship, denied.'
The objector is refining the meaning of 'with' to avoid the earlier response.
2
Understand the distinction
The defender had used the possibility of stillness replacing movement; the objector now distinguishes that from the condition being fixed to companionship.
3
Read the third objection
Then it is said: 'You have made the general (essential possibility) entail the specific (stability).'
This accuses the defender of a logical error.
4
See the answer
The defender responds: 'Generality and specificity between them is only according to concept, not existence, because the essentially possible is possible eternally.'
Essential possibility is a conceptual category, not a guarantee of occurrence.
5
Consider the proposed answer and refutation
Another answer is proposed: the condition was unqualified, so it is possible. But then it is refuted: if the mountain were stable in this world, the vision would occur, which is false.
This shows the debate continues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاعتراض على الوجه الثاني من طريق الاستدلال فمن وجوه : :أحدها: أنا لا نسلم أنه علق الرؤية على استقرار الجبل مطلقاً أو حالة السكون ليكون ممكناً بل عقيب النظر بدلالة الفاء وهو حالة تزلزل واندكاك، ولا نسلم إمكان الاستقرار حينئذ . والجواب : أن الاستقرار حالة الحركة أيضاً ممكن بأن يحصل بدل الحركة السكون لأن الإمكان الذاتي لا يزول ولهذا صح جعله دكاً. فإنه لا يقال جعله دكاً إلا فيما يجوز أن لا يكون كذا، وإنما المحال هو اجتماع الحركة والسكون، وهذا كما أن قيام زید حال قعوده يمكن وبالعكس واجتماعهما محال . وما يقال : إن الاستقرار مع الحركة محال إن أريد به الاجتماع فمسلم ، لكن ليس هو المعلق عليه، وإن أريد المقيد بالمعية فممنوع. فإن قيل : قد جعلتم الأعم وهو الإمكان الذاتي مستلزماً للأخص وهو الاستقبالي. قلنا العموم والخصوص بينهما إنما هو بحسب المفهوم دون الوجود، لأن الممكن الذاتي ممكن أبداً، وقد يقال في الجواب إنه علقها على استقرار الجبل من حيث هو من غير قيد، وهو ممكن في نفسه ويرد عليه أنه واقع في الدنيا فيلزم وقوع الرؤية فيها ،
As for the objection to the second way of argumentation, it is from several aspects: One of them: We do not concede that He conditioned the vision upon the stability of the mountain absolutely or upon a state of rest such that it would be possible; rather it is after the looking indicated by the particle 'fa', and that is a state of trembling and crumbling, and we do not concede the possibility of stability at that time. The answer: That stability during movement is also possible, because by obtaining stillness instead of movement, since essential possibility does not cease; therefore it was made a crumbled thing. For it is not said 'He made it crumbled' except regarding something that can be otherwise. Rather the impossible is the simultaneity of movement and stillness. And this is like the standing of Zayd during his sitting: it is possible, and the opposite, and their simultaneity is impossible. And what is said: That stability with movement is impossible: if what is meant is simultaneity, it is conceded, but that is not what is conditioned; if what is meant is that it is restricted to companionship, then it is denied. Then it is said: You have made the more general, which is essential possibility, entail the more specific, which is stability. We say: The generality and specificity between them is only according to concept, not existence, because the essentially possible is possible eternally. And it may be said in answer that He conditioned it upon the stability of the mountain in itself without qualification, and it is possible in itself, but it is objected that it occurs in this world, so the occurrence of the vision would follow.

Commentary
This passage contains a detailed objection and counter-response regarding the interpretation of the verse in which Allah says 'look at the mountain; if it stays in its place, then you will see Me' (Quran 7:143). The objector argues that the condition of stability (istiqrar) is linked to the mountain's state after looking (bi dalalat al-fa'), which is a state of trembling, so stability is impossible at that moment. The answer explains that stability during movement is possible because the mountain could become still instead of moving; essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) remains. The impossibility is only the simultaneous presence of movement and stillness, not one after the other. Then a further objection is raised: the objector accuses the defender of making the general (essential possibility) entail the specific (stability). The defender responds that the relationship is conceptual, not existential. Lastly, a possible answer is proposed: that the condition was unqualified, so it is possible, but it is then objected that if stability occurs in this world, then the vision would also occur in this world, which is not the case. The passage ends with that objection, implying that further discussion is needed.

The Rational Proof for the Possibility of Seeing God
Al-Taftazani presents the early Sunni rational proof for the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early Sunni rational proof for the possibility of seeing God, based on the common cause of existence.

Core Idea
If existence makes vision possible for creatures, then it makes vision of God possible too.

This lesson presents the rational proof used by early Sunni theologians to demonstrate that seeing God is rationally possible. The proof builds on our everyday experience of seeing substances and accidents, and argues that the common cause of vision—existence—is shared by God. By following the logical steps, you will see how kalam reasoning moves from observed facts to divine truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense logical style of Taftazani.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify that we perceive substances and accidents by necessity, and that correct vision depends on the existence of the object. These are the starting points.
3
Follow the reasoning from cause to conclusion
Trace how the proof argues that a single effect (correct vision of both substances and accidents) requires a single common cause, which must be existence rather than origination.
Pay attention to why origination is eliminated.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that seeing God is possible (imkan), not that it actually occurs. Understand why this is a proof of possibility, not actuality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : تمسك المتقدمون من أهل السنة في إمكان الرؤية بدليل عقلي . تقريره أنا نرى الجواهر والأعراض بحكم الضرورة كالأجسام والأضواء والألوان والأكوان، وباتفاق الخصوم. وإن زعم البعض منهم في بعض الأعراض أنها أجسام وفي الطول الذي هو جواهر ممتدة أنه عرضه ورد بأنه يدرك الطول بمجرد تأليف عدة من الجواهر في سمت وإن لم يخطر بالبال شيء من الأعراض. وقد يستدل على رؤية القبيلين بأنا نميز بالبصر بين نوع ونوع من الأجسام كالشجر والحجر، ونوع ونوع من الألوان كالسواد والبياض من غير أن يقوم شيء منها بآلة الإبصار. وبالجملة لما صحت رؤيتهما وصحة الرؤية أمر يتحقق عند الوجوب، وينتفي عند العدم لزم أن يكون لها علة لامتناع الترجح بلا مرجح ، وأن يكون تلك العلة مشتركة بين الجوهر والعرض لما مر من امتناع تعليل الواحد بعلتين وهي إما الوجود وإما الحدوث، إذ لا ثالث يصح للعلية والحدوث أيضا غير صالح لأنه بالعدم وهو اعتباري محض أو عن الوجود بعد العدم. ولا مدخل للعدم فتعين الوجود، وهو مما يشترك فيه الواجب لما مر في مبحث الوجود، فلزم صحة رؤيته وهو المطلوب .
And the second: The early ones from Ahl al-Sunnah adhered to the possibility of vision (al-ru'ya) with a rational proof. Its explanation: We perceive substances and accidents by necessity, such as bodies, lights, colors, and locations, and by agreement of the opponents. And even if some of them claim that some accidents are bodies, and that length, which is extended substances, is an accident, it is refuted by the fact that length is perceived merely by composing several substances in a line even if no accident comes to mind. And one may infer the seeing of both kinds from the fact that we distinguish by sight between types of bodies like tree and stone, and between types of colors like black and white, without any of them arising in the organ of vision. In sum, when their vision is correct and correctness of vision is something that is realized when there is existence and negated when there is non-existence, it follows that there is a cause for it, because there is no preferment without a preferrer, and that this cause is common between substance and accident, based on the impossibility of a single thing being caused by two causes. And that cause is either existence or origination (al-huduth), since there is no third that is valid for causality, and origination is also not valid because it is by non-existence, which is purely relational, or by existence after non-existence. Non-existence has no part, so existence is determined. And existence is something in which the Necessary shares, as has passed in the discussion of existence. So the possibility of seeing Him follows conclusion, and that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the rational proof used by early Sunni theologians for the possibility (imkan) of seeing God (ru'yat Allah). The proof begins by establishing that we do, in fact, see substances and accidents by necessity—such as bodies, lights, colors, and spatial locations—and this is agreed upon even by opponents. Some might object that certain perceived things like length are not pure accidents but extended substances; however, the proof counters that length can be perceived by composing several substances in alignment without any accident being mentally noted. Another argument is that we discriminate between types of bodies and colors visually without any of those qualities inhering in the eye. The core reasoning follows: since we see both substances and accidents (the two main categories of created things), and since the correctness of vision is something that obtains when the object exists and is negated when it is nonexistent, there must be a cause for this correctness to avoid an arbitrary preference. This cause must be common to both substance and accident, because a single effect (correct vision of both) cannot have two different causes unless they coincide—but here the effect is a single type of correctness. The only candidates are existence (al-wujud) or origination (al-huduth), and origination is invalid because it depends on nonexistence (being an accident) or on existence after nonexistence, both of which are either purely relational or secondary. Nonexistence itself has no causal role. Therefore, the common cause is existence. And existence is shared by the Necessary Being (God), as established earlier. Thus, seeing God is possible. This is a classic kalam proof relying on the principle of a common cause (jami') to infer the possibility of seeing an object that shares the relevant property (existence) with objects we already see.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الجوهر
العرض
الوجود
الحدوث
العلة
إمكان الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof intends to prove that we will actually see God in this world or the afterlife.
Correction: The proof only establishes rational possibility (imkan), not actual occurrence. It shows that seeing God is not impossible, but does not prove that it will happen.

Checkpoint
What is the common cause that, according to this proof, makes seeing both substances and accidents possible?
A) Origination (al-huduth)
B) Existence (al-wujud)
C) Being an object of the senses
D) Contingency (al-imkan)

Key Takeaway
The possibility of seeing God is rationally established by the common cause of existence shared by all visible objects and God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is what makes you visible to others, and God's existence makes Him 'visible' in principle, does the thought that you will one day see Him fill you with longing or fear? What would it take for you to long for that encounter starting today?
?
This proof relies on reason alone. When you encounter doubts about unseen realities like the vision of God, do you tend to disregard reasoned proofs or embrace them? How can you make the intellectual pursuit of tawhid a regular practice in your life?
?
The argument establishes possibility, not actuality. Are you living as though the vision of God is possible but not guaranteed? What would change in your daily priorities if you truly believed you will see your Lord?

The Objection of Direction and Facing in Seeing God
Sanusi presents and refutes the objection of direction/facing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the vision of God requires direction and facing, and the author's initial refutation denying this necessity.

Core Idea
Direction and facing are not necessary for vision; Allah creates perception as He wills.

The objection to seeing God often hinges on the assumption that vision requires spatial direction and facing. Sanusi presents this objection and immediately refutes it by denying the necessity of such conditions. This lesson examines the objection and the key point of the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing the objection: if God is seen with something else, direction is necessary; if alone, hidden things become visible. Understand the logic.
The objection assumes vision always requires physical conditions.
2
Identify the author's response
Find where the author says 'The answer: That the necessity of facing and direction is denied.' This is the core refutation.
The author denies the premise, not the conclusion.
3
Consider the claim of necessity
The author argues that the claim of necessity is not accepted because many rational beings have disputed it. Reflect on why this is significant in kalam.
Kalam arguments rely on universal acceptance of rational judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البعض، ولكان رؤيته إما مع رؤية شيء آخر مما في الجنة فيكون على جهة منه ضرورة. إن رؤية الشيئين دفعة لا تعقل إلا كذلك، وإما لا معها فيكون ما هو باطن في الدارين مرئياً وما هو ظاهر غير مرئي مع شرائط الرؤية، وحديث غلبة شعاع أحد المرئيين إنما يصح في الأجسام. والجواب : أن لزوم المقابلة والجهة ممنوع، وإنما الرؤية نوع من الإدراك يخلقه الله تعالى متى شاء، ولأي شيء شاء، ودعوى الضرورة فما نازع فيه الجم الغفير من العقلاء غير مسموع. ولو سلم في الشاهد، فلا يلزم في الغائب لأن الرؤيتين مختلفتان، إما بالماهية، وإما بالهوية لا محالة، فيجوز اختلافهما في الشروط واللوازم وهذا هو المراد بالرؤية بلا كيف بمعنى خلوها عن الشرائط، والكيفيات المعتبرة في رؤية الأجسام والأعراض، لا بمعنى خلو الرؤية أو الرائي أو المرئي عن جميع الحالات والصفات على ما يفهمه أرباب الجهالات، فيعترضون بأن الرؤية فعل من أفعال العبد، أو كسب من أكسابه فبالضرورة يكون واقعاً بصفة من الصفات، وكذا المرئي بحاسة العين، لا بد أن يكون له كيفية من الكيفيات. نعم يتوجه أن يقال : نزاعنا إنما هو في هذا النوع من الرؤية، لا في الرؤية المخالفة لها بالحقيقة المسماة عندكم بالانكشاف التام، وعندنا بالعلم الضروري.
... some, and His vision would either be with the vision of something else in Paradise, so it would necessarily be in a direction relative to it. Because seeing two things at once is not conceivable except in that way, or it would be without that, so that which is hidden in the two abodes would be seen and that which is apparent would not be seen despite the conditions of vision, and the talk of one of the two visible things overcoming the ray is only valid for bodies. The answer: That the necessity of facing and direction is denied. Rather, vision is a type of perception that Allah creates whenever He wills and for whatever He wills, and the claim of necessity in something that a vast multitude of rational beings have disputed is not accepted. And even if it is conceded in the witnessed realm, it does not apply to the unseen because the two visions are different, either in essence or in identity inevitably, so it is permissible for them to differ in conditions and concomitants. And this is what is meant by 'vision without modality' (ru'ya bi-la kayf) in the sense of being free from the conditions and modalities considered in the vision of bodies and accidents, not in the sense of the vision, the seer, or the seen being devoid of all states and attributes as understood by the ignorant. Thus they object that vision is an act of the servant or an acquisition of his, so necessarily it occurs with some attribute, and likewise the seen by the eye must have some modality. Yes, it can be said: our dispute is only about this type of vision, not about the vision that differs in reality, which you call complete disclosure (inkishaf tam) and we call necessary knowledge ('ilm daruri).
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the vision of God based on the necessity of direction and facing, and the author refutes it. The objection argues that if God is seen in Paradise, He must be seen either together with some other thing, requiring a direction relative to that thing, or alone, which would imply that hidden things become visible while apparent ones are not, which is absurd. The author responds by denying the necessity of facing and direction, stating that vision is a perception Allah creates freely. The claim of necessity is disputed by many rational thinkers. Even if conceded in the physical world, it does not apply to the unseen because the two types of vision differ in essence or identity. The author clarifies that 'vision without modality' means freedom from the conditions of physical vision, not that vision itself has no qualities. The dispute is specifically about this type of vision, which the opponents call complete disclosure and the author calls necessary knowledge.

Key Terms
رؤية
مقابل
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all vision, including of God, requires spatial direction and facing.
Correction: Vision is a perception created by Allah as He wills; it does not necessarily require direction or facing.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main response to the objection that vision of God requires direction and facing?
A) He agrees but says it is a special case.
B) He denies the necessity of facing and direction.
C) He says vision only occurs with direction in the afterlife.
D) He argues that vision is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Direction and facing are not necessary for vision; Allah creates perception freely, and disputed claims of necessity are invalid.

Vision Without Modality and the Two Types of Vision
Sanusi explains 'vision without modality' and distinguishes two types of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what 'vision without modality' means and the distinction between two types of vision: complete disclosure and necessary knowledge.

Core Idea
Vision without modality means freedom from physical conditions, not from all attributes.

After refuting the necessity of direction, Sanusi clarifies the meaning of 'vision without modality' (ru'ya bi-la kayf). He explains that it refers to being free from the conditions of physical vision, not that vision has no attributes. He further distinguishes between two types of vision: the one under dispute (which he calls necessary knowledge) and another called complete disclosure.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification on 'bi-la kayf'
Read from 'ولو سلم في الشاهد' to the end. Focus on the definition of 'vision without modality'.
The author emphasizes that it means absence of physical conditions, not absence of all qualities.
2
Identify the two types of vision
Find where the author distinguishes the disputed vision (necessary knowledge) from complete disclosure. Understand the difference.
The opponents call it complete disclosure; the author calls it necessary knowledge.
3
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this distinction matters for the debate. The author argues that the two differ in essence or identity, so conditions from one don't apply to the other.
This allows for different rules for different types of vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البعض، ولكان رؤيته إما مع رؤية شيء آخر مما في الجنة فيكون على جهة منه ضرورة. إن رؤية الشيئين دفعة لا تعقل إلا كذلك، وإما لا معها فيكون ما هو باطن في الدارين مرئياً وما هو ظاهر غير مرئي مع شرائط الرؤية، وحديث غلبة شعاع أحد المرئيين إنما يصح في الأجسام. والجواب : أن لزوم المقابلة والجهة ممنوع، وإنما الرؤية نوع من الإدراك يخلقه الله تعالى متى شاء، ولأي شيء شاء، ودعوى الضرورة فما نازع فيه الجم الغفير من العقلاء غير مسموع. ولو سلم في الشاهد، فلا يلزم في الغائب لأن الرؤيتين مختلفتان، إما بالماهية، وإما بالهوية لا محالة، فيجوز اختلافهما في الشروط واللوازم وهذا هو المراد بالرؤية بلا كيف بمعنى خلوها عن الشرائط، والكيفيات المعتبرة في رؤية الأجسام والأعراض، لا بمعنى خلو الرؤية أو الرائي أو المرئي عن جميع الحالات والصفات على ما يفهمه أرباب الجهالات، فيعترضون بأن الرؤية فعل من أفعال العبد، أو كسب من أكسابه فبالضرورة يكون واقعاً بصفة من الصفات، وكذا المرئي بحاسة العين، لا بد أن يكون له كيفية من الكيفيات. نعم يتوجه أن يقال : نزاعنا إنما هو في هذا النوع من الرؤية، لا في الرؤية المخالفة لها بالحقيقة المسماة عندكم بالانكشاف التام، وعندنا بالعلم الضروري.
... some, and His vision would either be with the vision of something else in Paradise, so it would necessarily be in a direction relative to it. Because seeing two things at once is not conceivable except in that way, or it would be without that, so that which is hidden in the two abodes would be seen and that which is apparent would not be seen despite the conditions of vision, and the talk of one of the two visible things overcoming the ray is only valid for bodies. The answer: That the necessity of facing and direction is denied. Rather, vision is a type of perception that Allah creates whenever He wills and for whatever He wills, and the claim of necessity in something that a vast multitude of rational beings have disputed is not accepted. And even if it is conceded in the witnessed realm, it does not apply to the unseen because the two visions are different, either in essence or in identity inevitably, so it is permissible for them to differ in conditions and concomitants. And this is what is meant by 'vision without modality' (ru'ya bi-la kayf) in the sense of being free from the conditions and modalities considered in the vision of bodies and accidents, not in the sense of the vision, the seer, or the seen being devoid of all states and attributes as understood by the ignorant. Thus they object that vision is an act of the servant or an acquisition of his, so necessarily it occurs with some attribute, and likewise the seen by the eye must have some modality. Yes, it can be said: our dispute is only about this type of vision, not about the vision that differs in reality, which you call complete disclosure (inkishaf tam) and we call necessary knowledge ('ilm daruri).
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the vision of God based on the necessity of direction and facing, and the author refutes it. The objection argues that if God is seen in Paradise, He must be seen either together with some other thing, requiring a direction relative to that thing, or alone, which would imply that hidden things become visible while apparent ones are not, which is absurd. The author responds by denying the necessity of facing and direction, stating that vision is a perception Allah creates freely. The claim of necessity is disputed by many rational thinkers. Even if conceded in the physical world, it does not apply to the unseen because the two types of vision differ in essence or identity. The author clarifies that 'vision without modality' means freedom from the conditions of physical vision, not that vision itself has no qualities. The dispute is specifically about this type of vision, which the opponents call complete disclosure and the author calls necessary knowledge.

انكشاف تام
علم ضروري

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think 'vision without modality' means the vision of God has no qualities at all.
Correction: It means the vision is free from physical conditions like direction, not that it is devoid of all attributes.

Checkpoint

A) The vision has no attributes whatsoever.
B) The vision is free from the conditions of seeing physical bodies.
C) The seer must be without any state.
D) The seen object has no modality.

Key Takeaway
'Vision without modality' means freedom from physical conditions, and the disputed vision is a specific type called 'necessary knowledge'.

Refuting the Suspicion of Emanation and Impression
The author presents and refutes the suspicion of emanation and impression.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the suspicion based on emanation and impression of physical vision and its refutation, which distinguishes between physical sight and the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
Physical sight theories do not apply to the divine vision of God.

This lesson presents the second suspicion against the possibility of seeing Allah, based on the classical theories of how physical vision works. The author refutes it by showing that these theories are disputed and, even if accepted, do not apply to the unseen realm.

Guided Path
1
Read the suspicion passage
Read the passage where the opponent argues that vision requires emanation or impression, both impossible for God.
Note the two theories: emission of ray and impression of image.
2
Read the author's response
Read the reply: the theories are disputed, and even if true, apply only to physical sight.
Notice the distinction between witnessed (shahid) and unseen (gha'ib).
3
Reflect on the logic
Understand why the refutation works: either the two visions differ in essence or they agree but can occur in different ways.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة المخالف على عدم الرؤية

قوله : الثاني : الشبهة الثانية شبهة الشعاع والانطباع وهي أن الرؤية إما باتصال شعاع العين بالمرئي، وإما بانطباع الشبح من المرئي في حدقة الرائي على اختلاف المذهبين وكلاهما في حق الباري تعالى ظاهر الامتناع فتمتنع رؤيته .
والجواب : أن هذا مما نازع فيه الفلاسفة فضلاً عن المتكلمين على ما سبق في بحث القوى . ولو سلم فإنما هو في الشاهد دون الغائب. إما على تقدير اختلاف الرؤيتين بالماهية فظاهر وإما على تقدير اتفاقها فلجواز أن يقع أفراد الماهية الواحدة بطريق مختلفة .
The opponent's evidence for the impossibility of vision. He said: The second suspicion: the suspicion of emanation and impression, which is that vision is either by the connection of the eye's ray with the visible object, or by the impression of the image from the visible object in the pupil of the seer, according to the two differing schools, and both are obviously impossible with respect to the Exalted God, so His vision is impossible. The answer: This is something that the philosophers have disputed, let alone the theologians, as preceded in the discussion of the faculties. And even if conceded, it is only in the witnessed realm, not the unseen. Either on the assumption that the two visions differ in essence, it is obvious, or on the assumption that they agree, it is because it is permissible for individuals of a single essence to occur in different ways.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the second suspicion against the vision of God, based on the two classical theories of physical vision: the emission of a ray from the eye to the object, or the impression of the object's image in the eye. Both are obviously impossible for God. The author responds by noting that even philosophers dispute these theories, so they are not universally accepted. Moreover, even if one concedes them, they apply only to physical sight (the witnessed realm), not to the unseen vision of God. The two types of vision either differ in essence, making the objection irrelevant, or they are of the same essence but can occur in different ways, so the physical conditions need not apply.

Correction: The author argues that the two types of vision are different; physical conditions need not apply to the divine.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The suspicion that vision requires physical processes like emanation or impression does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah, as physical and divine visions are distinct or can occur in different ways.

Refuting the suspicion of continuous vision
Sanusi presents and refutes the suspicion of continuous vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sanusi refutes the suspicion that if God were visible, He would be seen continuously, and why he invokes divine custom rather than logical necessity.

Core Idea
The non-vision of God in this world is not due to impossibility but to divine custom.

The third suspicion against the possibility of seeing God argues that if vision were possible, it would be actual and continuous. After all, the necessary conditions—sound senses and God's visibility—are always present. Al-Sanusi replies that the absence of vision is not a logical flaw but a matter of God's habitual way of acting. In this lesson, you will examine the suspicion and the beginning of its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the suspicion carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how the suspicion is structured as a conditional argument: if A, then B.
The suspicion is the third one listed by Sanusi.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objection claims that since the condition for vision (sound senses and visibility of God) is always present, vision should be continuous. Identify the implicit premise that if the condition is met, the effect must occur.
3
Analyze the author's response
Sanusi responds that the absence of vision is not based on the objector's reasoning but is a 'customary necessity'—like other regular occurrences that happen by God's habit, not by logical necessity. Reflect on what this means for the debate.
The response is cut off at the end, but the key point is made.

Source Text

Otherwise, it would be possible that there are towering mountains and vast seas in our presence that we do not see due to God not creating vision or due to the absence of a specific condition for it. We said: Its absence is not based on that, but rather it is necessary like other customary things. Then if conceded...
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the third suspicion against the vision of God: if God can be seen, why is He not seen continuously in this world and the hereafter, since the necessary conditions (sound senses and God's visibility) are always present? The objection argues that if vision were possible, it would be actual, otherwise we could have unseen objects in our presence unnoticed. The author answers that the absence of vision is not due to a logical impossibility but is a matter of divine custom ('adah) — just as many things occur regularly by God's habit, the non-vision of God in this world is a customary necessity, not a logical one. The answer is incomplete in the text, ending with 'then if conceded...'.

Benefit of Negating Perception and the Third Objection
Explains benefit of negation and third objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the benefit of negating perception of God and the third objection concerning the distinction between perception by eyes and perception by seers.

Core Idea
Negating 'perception by eyes' is praise, not a denial of all vision of God.

The verse 'لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ' is often cited in discussions of divine transcendence. Our commentator examines its benefit and addresses a third objection. This lesson unpacks the logic behind the negation and clarifies the key distinction between 'eyes' and 'seers.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three parts: benefit, objection, and qualification.
2
Understand the benefit of the negation
The commentator states that the benefit is praise (tamadduḥ) by declaring God transcendent of marks of temporality and deficiency. Reflect on why this is praiseworthy.
The negation highlights God's incomparability to created things.
3
Examine the third objection and its weakness
The objection is that what is negated is perception by eyes (al-abṣār), not perception by seers (al-mubṣirīn). The commentator finds this weak because all visible things are perceived by seers, not by eyes, making the negation trivial.
This objection is attributed to al-Ashʿarī.
4
Note the qualification and its significance
The commentator then qualifies: if 'perception by eyes' means vision by the bodily organ in a confrontational and impressionistic manner, then its negation is indeed praise, negating direction but not vision in the general sense under dispute.
This preserves the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife (ru'ya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا: أما فائدته فالتمدح تنزيهه عن سمات الحدوث، والنقصان من الحدود، والنهايات، وأما إدراك الأبصار العبارة عن رؤيته إياها، أو علمه بها، تعبيراً عن اللازم بالملزوم . وثالثاً: أن المنفي إدراك الأبصار (۱) ولا نزاع فيه، والمنازع إدراك المبصرين، ولا دلالة على نفيه، وهذا ينسب إلى الأشعري، وضعفه ظاهر لما أشرنا إليه، ولما أن جميع الأشياء كذلك . إذ المرئيات منها إنما يدركها المبصرون لا الأبصار، فلا تمدح في ذلك بلا فائدة أصلاً اللهم إلا أن يراد أن إدراك الأبصار هو الرؤية بالجارحة على طريق المواجهة والانطباع، فيكون نفيه تمدحاً، وبياناً لتنزه الباري تعالى عن الجهة ولا يستلزم نفي الرؤية بالمعنى الأعم (۳) المتنازع .
We say: As for its benefit, it is praise by declaring Him transcendent of the marks of temporality, and of deficiency from boundaries and endpoints. And as for [the phrase] 'perception of sights' being an expression for His seeing them, or His knowledge of them, it is an expression of the necessary by the necessary condition. And thirdly: that what is negated is the perception of eyes (al-abṣār) – and there is no dispute about that – while the disputant [asserts] the perception of seers (al-mubṣirīn), and there is no evidence for its negation. This is attributed to al-Ashʿarī, and its weakness is apparent from what we indicated, and because all things are like that. Indeed, visible things among them are perceived by seers, not by eyes, so there is no praise in that without any benefit at all – unless it is meant that the perception of eyes is vision by the organ in the manner of confrontation and impression, in which case its negation would be praise, and a declaration of the Exalted Creator's transcendence of direction, and it does not entail the negation of vision in the more general sense (al-aʿamm) under dispute.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines the benefit (fāʾida) of the negation of perception in the verse 'لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ' and presents a third objection against a certain interpretation. The commentator begins by stating that the benefit is praise (tamadduḥ) by declaring God transcendent of marks of temporality (ḥudūth) and deficiency from boundaries and endpoints. Then he explains that the phrase 'perception of eyes' is an expression for God's seeing or knowing them, using a figure of speech (the necessary for the necessary condition). The third objection is that what is negated is perception by eyes (al-abṣār), which is uncontested, whereas the opposing party asserts perception by seers (al-mubṣirīn), for which there is no evidence of negation. This objection is attributed to al-Ashʿarī, but the commentator considers it weak because all visible things are perceived by seers, not by eyes. Therefore, the negation would not be praiseworthy without benefit. However, the commentator then qualifies: if 'perception of eyes' is interpreted as vision by the bodily organ in a confrontational and impressionistic manner, then its negation is indeed praise and a declaration of divine transcendence of direction, and does not entail negation of vision in the general sense under dispute.

Key Terms
إدراك الأبصار
التمدح
الجهة
الرؤية بالمعنى الأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that negating perception by eyes negates all forms of vision of God.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that it negates only sensory vision by a bodily organ, not vision in the general sense (ru'ya) that is debated.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the benefit (fāʾida) of the negation of perception by eyes (إدراك الأبصار) in the verse?
A) It proves that God cannot be seen at all.
B) It is a praise (ت مدح) declaring God's transcendence of marks of temporality and deficiency.
C) It shows that God has no direction.
D) It refutes the Ashʿarī position.

Key Takeaway
The negation of 'perception by eyes' in the verse is a declaration of divine transcendence from corporeality and direction, not a denial of the possibility of seeing God in a non-corporeal sense.

The Claim of Mā'iyyah and Its Refutation
Presents the mā'iyyah claim and its refutation via genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term mā'iyyah (quiddity) is rejected in Islamic theology because it implies belonging to a genus, which leads to anthropomorphism (tashbīh).

Core Idea
Mā'iyyah implies genus; Allah is beyond genus, so the term is rejected.

Theologians discussed whether Allah can be described by a 'whatness' (mā'iyyah). This lesson examines the controversial view attributed to Ḍirār and why Abū Ḥanīfa's companions vehemently denied it. By analyzing the argument, you will see that mā'iyyah necessarily implies membership in a genus, which contradicts divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mā'iyyah
Read the Arabic passage that introduces the issue of mā'iyyah and the view of Ḍirār.
Notice that the passage begins by identifying this as a known theological discussion.
2
Understand Ḍirār's claim
Ḍirār said Allah has a mā'iyyah known only to Him and could create a sixth sense to perceive it. Consider why this would be problematic.
3
Examine the denial of the report about Abū Ḥanīfa
The passage mentions that when this view was reported from Abū Ḥanīfa, his companions strongly denied it. Why? Because mā'iyyah implies jins (genus).
The key is that 'what is it?' asks about genus.
4
Learn the reasoning against mā'iyyah
Anything that has a genus is similar to its genus and its members. Attributing a genus to Allah would be tashbīh (anthropomorphism). This is the core refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا البحث عند المتكلمين يعرف بمسألة المائية، وينسب القول بها إلى ضرار حيث قال : إن الله تعالى مائية لا يعلمها إلا هو، ولو رؤي لرؤي عليها، وفي قدرة الله تعالى أن يخلق في الخلق حاسة سادسة بها يدركون تلك المائية والخاصية ، وحين روي ذلك عن أبي حنيفة رضي الله أنكر أصحابه هذه الرواية أشد إنكار وذلك لأن المائية عبارة عن المجانسة حيث يقال ما هو بمعنى أي جنس هو من أجناس الأشياء والله تعالى منزه عن الجنس ، لأن كل ذي جنس مماثل لجنسه ولما تحته من الأنواع والأفراد، فالقول به تشبيه، وفسره بعضهم بأن الله تعالى يعلم نفسه بمشاهدة لا بدليل ولا بخبر، ونحن نعلمه بدليل وخبر ومن يعلم الشيء بالمشاهدة يعلم منه ما لا يعلمه من لا يشاهد، وليس هناك شيء هو المائية ليلزم التشبيه ، وكان أصحابنا يعدلون عن لفظ المائية إلى لفظ الخاصية
And this investigation among theologians is known as the question of mā'iyyah (quiddity). It is attributed to Ḍirār, who said: Allah has an essence (mā'iyyah) that none knows but Him; if He were seen, He would be seen upon it. And it is within Allah's power to create in creation a sixth sense by which they would perceive that mā'iyyah and specificity. When that was reported from Abū Ḥanīfa, may Allah be pleased with him, his companions denied this report vehemently, because mā'iyyah is an expression of belonging to a genus (jins)—where 'what is it?' means 'what genus from among the genera of things?'—and Allah is transcendent above genus, for everything having a genus is similar to its genus and to the species and individuals under it. Therefore, saying that is tashbīh (anthropomorphism). Some interpreted it as: Allah knows Himself by direct vision (mushāhadah) without proof or report, while we know Him by proof and report. One who knows a thing by direct vision knows from it what one who does not see does not know, yet there is no entity that is mā'iyyah so that tashbīh would be necessary. And our companions used to avoid the term mā'iyyah and instead used the term khāṣṣiyyah (specific property).
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage discusses the controversial term mā'iyyah (quiddity/ whatness) in relation to Allah. Theologians refer to this as the question of mā'iyyah. It is attributed to Ḍirār ibn 'Amr, who claimed that Allah has a mā'iyyah known only to Him, and that Allah could create a sixth sense in humans to perceive it. The report that Abū Ḥanīfa held a similar view is strongly denied by his followers, because mā'iyyah implies belonging to a genus (jins). Asking 'what is it?' means 'what genus is it from?' Allah is free from genus, since anything with a genus is similar to its genus and its individuals—this would be tashbīh (anthropomorphism). An alternative interpretation is given: that mā'iyyah refers to Allah's knowledge of Himself through direct vision without proof, while we know Him through proof; the difference in knowledge does not imply a separate quiddity. Therefore, the author's school (māturīdiyya or hanafiyya) avoided the term mā'iyyah and used khāṣṣiyyah (specific property) instead.

Key Terms
مائية
ضرار
مجانسة
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mā'iyyah is a acceptable term for Allah without implying anthropomorphism.
Correction: Mā'iyyah necessarily implies belonging to a genus, which entails similarity and thus tashbīh, so the term is rejected.

Checkpoint
Why did Abū Ḥanīfa's companions reject the report about mā'iyyah?
A) Because it contradicts the Qur'an
B) Because mā'iyyah implies belonging to a genus, which is tashbīh
C) Because they denied Allah has any attributes
D) Because they did not accept Ḍirār's authority

Key Takeaway
Mā'iyyah is rejected because it necessarily implies a genus, which would make Allah similar to creation.

?
When you hear phrases like 'Allah is with you' (ma'iyyah), do you guard against imagining a physical presence? How does the lesson's refutation of mā'iyyah help you purify your belief?
?
Ḍirār claimed Allah could create a sixth sense to perceive His quiddity. What subtle desire in your own heart might want to 'perceive' or 'measure' Allah in some way? Are you willing to accept that He is beyond all categories?

Alternative Interpretation and Preferred Terminology
Provides an alternative interpretation and the preferred terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative interpretation of mā'iyyah as referring to direct knowledge, and why theologians prefer the term khāṣṣiyyah to avoid anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Mā'iyyah can be reinterpreted but is risky; khāṣṣiyyah is safer.

While the term mā'iyyah is problematic, some scholars offered an interpretation to avoid tashbīh. This lesson explores that interpretation—that it refers to Allah's direct self-knowledge versus our proof-based knowledge—and concludes that the school avoided the term altogether, preferring khāṣṣiyyah instead.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative interpretation
Read the part of the passage that explains how some scholars interpreted mā'iyyah in a non-anthropomorphic way.
They said it means Allah knows Himself by direct vision without proof, while we know Him by proof.
2
Analyze the reasoning
The interpretation argues that difference in knowledge does not imply a separate quiddity. Think about why this avoids tashbīh.
3
Learn the preferred term
The author's companions avoided the term mā'iyyah and used khāṣṣiyyah (specific property) instead. Understand why this term is safer.
Khāṣṣiyyah does not imply genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا البحث عند المتكلمين يعرف بمسألة المائية، وينسب القول بها إلى ضرار حيث قال : إن الله تعالى مائية لا يعلمها إلا هو، ولو رؤي لرؤي عليها، وفي قدرة الله تعالى أن يخلق في الخلق حاسة سادسة بها يدركون تلك المائية والخاصية ، وحين روي ذلك عن أبي حنيفة رضي الله أنكر أصحابه هذه الرواية أشد إنكار وذلك لأن المائية عبارة عن المجانسة حيث يقال ما هو بمعنى أي جنس هو من أجناس الأشياء والله تعالى منزه عن الجنس ، لأن كل ذي جنس مماثل لجنسه ولما تحته من الأنواع والأفراد، فالقول به تشبيه، وفسره بعضهم بأن الله تعالى يعلم نفسه بمشاهدة لا بدليل ولا بخبر، ونحن نعلمه بدليل وخبر ومن يعلم الشيء بالمشاهدة يعلم منه ما لا يعلمه من لا يشاهد، وليس هناك شيء هو المائية ليلزم التشبيه ، وكان أصحابنا يعدلون عن لفظ المائية إلى لفظ الخاصية
And this investigation among theologians is known as the question of mā'iyyah (quiddity). It is attributed to Ḍirār, who said: Allah has an essence (mā'iyyah) that none knows but Him; if He were seen, He would be seen upon it. And it is within Allah's power to create in creation a sixth sense by which they would perceive that mā'iyyah and specificity. When that was reported from Abū Ḥanīfa, may Allah be pleased with him, his companions denied this report vehemently, because mā'iyyah is an expression of belonging to a genus (jins)—where 'what is it?' means 'what genus from among the genera of things?'—and Allah is transcendent above genus, for everything having a genus is similar to its genus and to the species and individuals under it. Therefore, saying that is tashbīh (anthropomorphism). Some interpreted it as: Allah knows Himself by direct vision (mushāhadah) without proof or report, while we know Him by proof and report. One who knows a thing by direct vision knows from it what one who does not see does not know, yet there is no entity that is mā'iyyah so that tashbīh would be necessary. And our companions used to avoid the term mā'iyyah and instead used the term khāṣṣiyyah (specific property).
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage discusses the controversial term mā'iyyah (quiddity/ whatness) in relation to Allah. Theologians refer to this as the question of mā'iyyah. It is attributed to Ḍirār ibn 'Amr, who claimed that Allah has a mā'iyyah known only to Him, and that Allah could create a sixth sense in humans to perceive it. The report that Abū Ḥanīfa held a similar view is strongly denied by his followers, because mā'iyyah implies belonging to a genus (jins). Asking 'what is it?' means 'what genus is it from?' Allah is free from genus, since anything with a genus is similar to its genus and its individuals—this would be tashbīh (anthropomorphism). An alternative interpretation is given: that mā'iyyah refers to Allah's knowledge of Himself through direct vision without proof, while we know Him through proof; the difference in knowledge does not imply a separate quiddity. Therefore, the author's school (māturīdiyya or hanafiyya) avoided the term mā'iyyah and used khāṣṣiyyah (specific property) instead.

The philosophers' first argument: knowledge as representation and its refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the philosophers' argument that knowledge of Allah's essence is impossible because it requires representation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that knowledge requires representation and why it fails to disprove knowledge of Allah's essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not merely impression of forms; Allah's knowledge transcends representation.

Al-Taftazani introduces the first philosophical argument against knowing Allah's essence, based on the definition of knowledge as impression of forms. He then systematically refutes it through a series of concessions and denials. This lesson unpacks both the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the structure: the philosophers' claim followed by a three-part response.
The response uses a pattern of 'we do not grant... even if granted... even if granted...'.
2
Identify the philosophers' definition of knowledge
Locate the phrase 'العلم هو ارتسام صورة المعلوم في النفس' and understand its implications: knowledge is the mental impression of the universal quiddity stripped of individuating characteristics.
3
Understand why this leads to impossibility
The philosophers argue that Allah has no universal quiddity subject to individuation, so such an impression is impossible. If it were possible, many forms would apply, violating tawhid.
4
Follow the three-tiered refutation
Al-Taftazani gives three responses: first denies the definition of knowledge; second denies its applicability to knowledge of Allah; third distinguishes between multiplicity of individuals and multiplicity of concepts.
Each response is conditional, showing that even if previous points are conceded, the argument still fails.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Consider how the refutation protects divine transcendence without denying the possibility of knowing Allah. The response does not require a positive theory of knowledge; it only blocks the philosophical objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسكت الفلاسفة في امتناع العلم بحقيته بوجهين : أحدهما : أن العلم هو ارتسام صورة المعلوم في النفس أي ماهيته الكلية المنتزعة من الوجود العيني بحذف المشخصات، بحيث إذا وجدت كانت ذلك الشيء، وليست للواجب ماهية كلية معروضة للتشخص على ما تقرر في موضعه ولو فرض ذلك لكان الواجب مقولاً على تلك الصور المأخوذة في الأذهان فيصير كثيراً، ويبطل التوحيد . وأجيب : بأنا لا نسلّم أن العلم بارتسام الصورة. ولو سلم فلا كذلك العلم بالواجب ولا علم الواجب، ولو سلّم فالمنافي للتوحيد تعدد أفراد الواجب لا الصور المأخوذة منه ، والمخل بالشخصية إمكان فرض صدق المفهوم على الكثيرين لا صدق الموجود العيني على الصور.
The philosophers clung to the impossibility of knowledge of His reality by two aspects: One: that knowledge is the impression of the form of the known in the soul, i.e., its universal quiddity abstracted from concrete existence by stripping individuating characteristics, such that when it exists it is that thing. And the Necessary Being has no universal quiddity that is subject to individuation, as established in its place. If that were assumed, the Necessary Being would be predicated of those forms taken in minds, and thus become many, and tawhid would be invalidated. Response: We do not grant that knowledge is the impression of the form. Even if granted, the knowledge of the Necessary Being and His knowledge are not like that. Even if granted, what contradicts tawhid is the multiplicity of individuals of the Necessary Being, not the forms taken from Him. And what violates His uniqueness is the possibility of supposing the concept to be true of many, not the truth of the concrete existent over the forms.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' first argument against knowing Allah's essence. They define knowledge as the impression (irtisam) of the form of the known in the soul, i.e., the universal quiddity abstracted from concrete individuals. They argue that Allah has no universal quiddity subject to individuation, so such an impression is impossible. If it were possible, the necessary being would be predicated of many forms, leading to polytheism. The response denies the definition of knowledge as impression. Even if accepted, the knowledge of Allah and His own knowledge are not of that type. Even if accepted, the multiplicity that invalidates tawhid is multiplicity of individuals of the Necessary Being, not multiplicity of conceptual forms. The violation of His uniqueness is the possibility of the concept applying to many, not the actual concrete existence applying to forms.

Key Terms
ارتسام صورة
ماهية كلية
تشخص
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument is irrefutable and proves that we cannot know Allah's essence at all.
Correction: The argument relies on a specific definition of knowledge (impression of forms) that is not accepted by the theologians. Even if that definition is conceded, the argument fails because it conflates multiplicity of concepts with multiplicity of individuals.

Checkpoint
What is the philosophers' definition of knowledge presented in this argument?
A) Knowledge is the soul's acquisition of concepts
B) Knowledge is the impression of the form of the known in the soul
C) Knowledge is the correspondence of mind and reality
D) Knowledge is the intuitive understanding of essences

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument fails because it imposes a definition of knowledge that does not apply to Allah and confuses conceptual multiplicity with ontological multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you have ever assumed that Allah is beyond your knowledge and thus felt distant from Him, how does this lesson challenge that assumption? What does the refutation of the philosophers' argument tell you about the possibility of knowing Allah? Will you now seek deeper knowledge of His attributes?
?
Do you rely on a particular definition of knowledge (e.g., intellectual grasp) that might limit how you relate to Allah? Can you think of a time when you felt you 'knew' something about Allah beyond conceptual representation? How can you cultivate that type of knowledge?
?
The philosophers assumed that knowing Allah would require a mental form that multiplies Him. Do you ever reduce Allah to a concept in your mind, thinking you have Him figured out? How can you worship a God who transcends your mental images?

The Philosophers' Second Argument: Can We Conceive Allah?
Al-Taftazani refutes the philosophers' claim that conception of Allah is impossible because it is not through intuition, definition, or description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that conception of Allah is impossible because it cannot occur through intuition, definition, or description, and the refutation that offers alternative ways of conception.

Core Idea
Conception of Allah is not limited to logic; divine intervention and direct perception are possible.

The philosophers argue that we cannot conceive Allah because all ways of conception are closed. They list three possible ways: intuition, definition, and description, and find each impossible for Allah. But is this list exhaustive? Al-Taftazani responds with a powerful refutation that opens the door to divine intervention and direct perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: the philosophers' argument followed by the response.
2
Identify the three ways of conception
Find where the philosophers list the three ways: intuition (badihah), definition (hadd), and description (rasm). Understand why each is deemed impossible for Allah.
Intuition is negated by consensus; definition requires composition; description does not yield true knowledge.
3
Examine the refutation's alternatives
Notice how the response rejects the restriction and offers alternatives: inspiration, necessary knowledge from Allah, or direct observation after death.
This shows that conception can come from divine means, not just human logic.
4
Consider the final concession
Even if the restriction is granted, the response argues that description may still lead to conception of the reality, as previously established.
This strengthens the refutation by accepting the premise but denying the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما : أن تصور الشيء إما أن يحصل بالبديهة وهو منتف في الواجب وفاقاً، وإما بالحد وهو إنما يكون للمركب من الجنس والفصل والواجب ليس كذلك، وإما بالرسم وهو لا يفيد العلم بالحقيقة والكلام فيه . وأجيب بأنا لا نسلّم انحصار طرق التصور في ذلك بل قد يحصل بالإلهام أو بخلق الله تعالى العلم الضروري بالكسبيات أو بصيرورة الأشياء مشاهدة للنفس عند مفارقتها البدن كسائر المجردات ولو سلَّم فالرسم، وإن لم يستلزم تصور الحقيقة. لكن قد يفضي إليه كما سبق .
And the second: that conception of a thing occurs either by intuition, which is negated in the case of the Necessary Being by agreement; or by definition, which applies only to what is composed of genus and differentia, and the Necessary Being is not like that; or by description, which does not yield knowledge of the reality, and the discussion is about it. Response: We do not grant that the ways of conception are restricted to these. Rather, it may occur through inspiration (ilham) or through Allah creating necessary knowledge of acquired things, or by things becoming observable to the soul when it separates from the body, like other immaterial entities. Even if granted, description, even if it does not entail conception of the reality, may lead to it, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' second argument: that conception (tasawwur) of a thing can only occur through one of three ways: intuition (badihah), definition (hadd), or description (rasm). They argue that intuition is impossible for Allah (by consensus), definition is only for composite things (and Allah is simple), and description does not give knowledge of the true reality. The response rejects the restriction of conception to these three ways. It offers alternative ways: inspiration (ilham), or Allah creating necessary knowledge of acquired things, or the soul's direct perception after death like other immaterial entities. Even if the restriction is accepted, description may still lead to conception of the reality, as mentioned earlier.

Key Terms
تصور
بديهة
حد
رسم
إلهام
مجردات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three ways of conception (intuition, definition, description) are exhaustive and mutually exclusive.
Correction: The refutation shows that conception can also occur through divine inspiration, necessary knowledge created by Allah, or direct observation by the soul, especially regarding immaterial entities.

Checkpoint
According to the response, which of the following is NOT mentioned as an alternative way to conceive of Allah?
A) Inspiration (ilham)
B) Necessary knowledge created by Allah
C) Sensory perception of Allah in this world
D) The soul's observation after separation from the body

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument fails because conception can occur through divine inspiration or direct perception, not only through logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Taftazani's argument—that description can still lead to true conception—challenge you to take the Qur'anic descriptions of Allah more seriously as a genuine path to knowing Him?
?

If that is true, how should it affect your longing for the afterlife and your relationship with Allah now? Are you living as if you will truly see Him?

Fourth Proof: If God Created Acts, He'd Be Described by Them
Fourth proof: if God creates all acts, He would be described with evil and mundane acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth rational argument against the Ash'ari position that God creates human acts, and why this argument safeguards divine transcendence.

Core Idea
If God created evil, He would be describable as evil—impossible for the Transcendent.

This lesson presents the fourth rational argument, which uses a reductio ad absurdum. It shows that if God were the creator of all human acts, then He would be described with those acts—including disbelief, injustice, and mundane actions. Since such descriptions are impossible for Allah, He cannot be the creator of our actions. The source passage is a concise statement of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the logical structure: if...then...
Pay attention to the phrase 'since the meaning of disbeliever is nothing but the doer of disbelief'.
2
Analyze the reasoning
Understand that the argument hinges on identifying the agent with the act. If God creates disbelief, He is the doer of disbelief, so the term 'disbeliever' applies.
This is a linguistic point, not a claim about God's essence.
3
Apply the principle to divine transcendence
See how this argument protects God from being described with imperfections. Since God cannot be a disbeliever, He cannot be the creator of disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : لو كان الله خالقاً لأفعال المخلوقين لصح اتصافه بها، إذ لا معنى للكافر إلا فاعل الكفر، فيكون كافراً ظالماً فاسقاً أكلاً شارباً قائماً قاعداً إلى ما لا يحصى.
Fourth: If God were the creator of the acts of creatures, it would be valid to describe Him with them, since the meaning of 'disbeliever' is nothing but the doer of disbelief. Then He would be a disbeliever, unjust, immoral, eating, drinking, standing, sitting, and so on ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth rational argument. It reasons that if God were the creator of all human acts, then He would be described by those acts. For example, the term 'disbeliever' (kafir) means 'doer of disbelief' (fa'il al-kufr). Thus, if God creates disbelief, He would be a disbeliever. Similarly, He would be unjust, immoral, eating, drinking, standing, sitting, and countless other attributes. Since it is impossible for God to be described with such imperfections, it follows that He cannot be the creator of human acts. This argument seeks to safeguard God's transcendence (tanzih) by denying that He creates acts that would attribute temporal or evil qualities to Him.

Key Terms
اتصافه بها
فاعل الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that God actually becomes a disbeliever if He creates disbelief.
Correction: The argument does not claim that God actually becomes a disbeliever, but that if He created disbelief, the term 'doer of disbelief' would apply to Him by linguistic convention, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What would happen if God created all human acts according to the fourth argument?
A) He would be praised for everything
B) He would be described with all acts, including evil and mundane ones
C) He would lose His power
D) Nothing would change

Key Takeaway
If God created human acts, He would be describable by them, including evil—so He does not create them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'God created this in me' to deflect responsibility? After reading this argument, can you still hide behind that excuse? How will you take full ownership of your actions starting today?
?
If God were to be described by every act He created, imagine the sheer weight of imperfection that would be attributed to Him. Does that thought make you more aware of the gravity of your sin—that it is not just a 'small thing' but something that, if attributed to God, would be blasphemous? What will you do to make sure your deeds honor Him?
?
The argument says that if God created disbelief, He would be called a 'disbeliever'. Now consider your own acts of disobedience: are you comfortable with the fact that you—not God—are their sole author? Will you stop blaming your environment, your upbringing, or your urges, and instead turn to repentance with genuine humility?
