The Objection of Evil Actions and Its Refutation
Objection and Ash'ari refutation regarding God's creation of evil actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God cannot create evil actions and the Ash'ari refutation based on possible good consequences and the distinction between creator and agent.

Core Idea
Creating evil may have a good end; the unjust is the one who does it, not God.

This passage addresses a key objection to the Ash'ari position that God creates all actions. The objector argues that a wise God cannot create evil. The refutation concedes rational good and evil but offers two arguments: that evil actions may have a commendable outcome, and that the agent of evil is the one who performs it, not the creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage which lists examples of ugly actions and the argument that a wise God would not create them.
Notice the examples: injustice, polytheism, etc.
2
Understand the concession
The refutation begins by conceding rational good and evil (al-ḥusn wa al-qubḥ al-'aqliyyān). This is important to note.
The Ash'ari position does not deny that actions have inherent moral qualities.
3
Grasp the first argument
The first argument is that creating an ugly act may have a commendable ultimate consequence, so it is not ugly in that respect.
This distinguishes between the act itself and its outcome.
4
Grasp the second argument
The second argument distinguishes between the one who creates the act in another (God) and the one who performs it (the servant). The unjust person is the one characterized by injustice, not the creator.
This clarifies that God is not the agent of evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أن كثيراً من أفعال العباد قبيحة كالظلم والشرك والفسق والقول باتخاذ الولد ونحو ذلك، والقبيح لا يخلقه الحكيم لعلمه بقبحه، وعلمه بغناه عن خلقه ورد بعد تسليم الحسن والقبح العقليين بأن خلق القبيح، ربما تكون له عاقبة حميدة، فلا يقبح بخلاف فعله وما يقال إنه لا معنى لفاعل القبيح لا موجده ومحدثه ليس بشيء. فإن الظالم من اتصف بالظلم لا من أوجده في محل آخر.
Second: That many of the servants' actions are ugly, such as injustice, polytheism, sin, and the statement of taking a son, and the like. And the ugly is not created by the Wise because of His knowledge of its ugliness, and His knowledge of His self-sufficiency from creating it. And it is refuted after conceding rational good and evil, by [saying] that the creation of the ugly perhaps has a commendable consequence, so it is not ugly, contrary to His action. And what is said that there is no meaning to 'agent of the ugly' — its bringer into existence and its originator is not something [evil]. For the unjust person is the one who is characterized by injustice, not the one who brings it into existence in another locus.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection against the Ash'ari view that God creates all actions, including evil ones. The objection argues that God, being wise, cannot create ugly actions because He knows their ugliness and has no need of them. The refutation begins by conceding the rational good and evil (الحسن والقبح العقليين) but argues that creating the ugly may have a commendable ultimate outcome, thus not being ugly in that respect. Furthermore, the objection that God cannot be the agent of ugly deeds is refuted by distinguishing between the one who creates the act in another (God) and the one who performs it (the servant). The unjust person is the one characterized by injustice, not God who brings that act into existence in the servant as a locus.

Key Terms
حسن وقبح عقلي
حكيم
ظلم
فاعل
محل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates evil actions, He becomes unjust.
Correction: The refutation clarifies that God is not the agent of evil; the agent is the one who performs the action. God creates the act in the servant as a locus, but the servant is the one characterized by the evil.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation of the second objection?
A) God never creates ugly actions.
B) Ugly actions may have a good ultimate end, and the agent is the one who performs them.
C) Rational good and evil are invalid.
D) God is not wise if He creates evil.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of an ugly act in a person does not make Him unjust; the person is the agent, and God may have a wise purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever blamed God for a trial or a 'bad' deed in your life, questioning why He allowed it? How does the point about a commendable consequence change your view of that event today?
?
When you commit a sin, do you tend to shift responsibility to circumstances or even God's decree? After reading that the unjust person is the one characterized by injustice, what concrete step will you take this week to own your actions?
?
If creating evil in others does not make God unjust, how does that confront your habit of judging others' sins as if they define God's goodness? What will you do the next time you feel morally superior to a fellow Muslim?

God Creates All Actions, Including Evil
Objection and answer regarding God creating evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection that God cannot create evil by distinguishing between the creation and the performance of evil.

Core Idea
Creating evil is not the same as doing evil.

The objection that a wise God would not create evil seems powerful. This passage presents the Ash'ari answer: evil acts can have praiseworthy outcomes, and the creator of evil is not necessarily evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the list of evil acts and the claim that a wise God cannot create them.
The objectors are the Mu'tazila who affirm rational good and evil.
2
Understand the reply
Notice the concession of intellectual good and evil, then the point that creating evil may have a good consequence.
This distinguishes the act of creation from the act performed.
3
Grasp the distinction
The final sentence clarifies that the unjust person is the one in whom injustice resides, not the one who brings it into existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : - أن كثيراً من أفعال العباد قبيحة كالظلم والشرك والفسق والقول باتخاذ الولد ونحو ذلك ، والقبيح لا يخلقه الحكيم لعلمه بقبحه، وعلمه بغناه عن خلقه . ورد بعد تسليم الحسن والقبح العقليين بأن خلق القبيح، ربما تكون له عاقبة حميدة ، فلا يقبح بخلاف فعله وما يقال إنه لا معنى لفاعل القبيح لا موجده ومحدثه ليس بشيء. فإن الظالم من اتصف بالظلم لا من أوجده في محل آخر.
The second: that many of the actions of servants are evil, like injustice, polytheism, immorality, and claiming that God has a son, and similar things. And the evil is not created by the Wise, because of His knowledge of its evil, and His knowledge of His self-sufficiency from creating it. It was replied, after accepting intellectual good and evil, that creating evil may have a praiseworthy consequence, so it is not evil, unlike His own act. And what is said that there is no meaning to 'doer of evil' except the one who originates it and brings it into existence is nothing. For the unjust person is the one who is described by injustice, not the one who brings it into existence in another locus.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection of the Qadariyya/Mu'tazila against the Ash'ari doctrine that God creates all human actions. The objectors argue that many human acts are evil (injustice, polytheism, immorality, claiming God has a son), and the Wise God, knowing their evil and being self-sufficient, would not create them. The answer, after conceding intellectual good and evil for the sake of argument, is that creating evil may have a praiseworthy consequence, so it is not evil in that respect, unlike God's own act (which is always good). Furthermore, the objection that the creator of evil must be evil is false: the person described as 'unjust' is the one in whom injustice inheres, not the one who causes it in another. The author thus distinguishes between being the direct doer of evil and being the originator of evil in another, which does not necessitate evil in the originator.

Key Terms
القبيح
الحكيم
الحسن والقبح العقليين
فاعل القبيح
الظالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates evil acts, then He must be evil.
Correction: God is not the one performing the evil; He creates it in another locus for wise purposes. The unjust person is the one in whom injustice resides, not the one who brings it into existence.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that God cannot create evil because He is wise?
A) By denying that such acts are truly evil.
B) By arguing that God creates evil but it is not evil in Him.
C) By noting that creating evil may lead to good outcomes, so it is not evil absolutely.
D) By claiming that God does not create evil acts at all.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of evil does not make Him evil because He is not the one performing the evil; He creates it for wise purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an evil act you have committed. How does the distinction between creating and doing change your view of God's role in that act? Are you ready to accept that God allowed it for a greater purpose beyond your understanding, even if you still bear responsibility?
?
If God creates evil for wise ends, how does that affect your trust in His justice when you face injustice from others? Can you still see Him as good while acknowledging He could have prevented it?
?
When you blame yourself for sin, do you ever resent God for creating that desire or circumstance? Reflect on the fact that He created it not for you to act on, but as a test. Are you willing to take full responsibility for your choice while acknowledging His ultimate control?

The Fourth Objection: Would God Be Described by Evil Acts?
The Mu'tazilite objection that God would be described by evil acts if He creates them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the Mu'tazilite objection that if God creates human actions, He would be described by those actions, leading to absurdities.

Core Idea
Creating an act does not make you its subject.

This lesson presents the fourth and most startling objection against the Sunni view that God creates all actions. The Mu'tazila argue that if God creates evil deeds, then He would be the doer of evil and thus described as an evil-doer. This would lead to describing God with the worst possible attributes. The author presents this objection before refuting it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice how the Mu'tazila equate 'originator' with 'doer' and then infer that the doer must be described by the act.
2
Identify the conclusion
They claim that if God creates actions, He would be an unbeliever, wrongdoer, etc.
3
Reflect on the force of the objection
Why would the Mu'tazila think this is a compelling argument? What assumption are they making about the relationship between an action and its subject?
The key assumption is that originating an action is the same as being its subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أن الله تعالى لو كان موجداً لأفعال العباد لكان فاعلاً لها، لأن معناها واحد، ولو كان فاعلاً لها لكان متصفاً بها لأنه لا معنى للكافر والظالم مثلاً إلا فاعل الكفر والظلم وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون الباري تعالى وتقدس كافراً ظالماً فاسقاً أكلاً شارباً قائماً قاعداً إلى غير ذلك من الفواحش التي لا يستطيع العاقل إجزاءها على اللسان، بل إخطارها بالبال وهذه الشبهة كنا نسمعها من حمقى العوام والسوقية من المعتزلة فنتعجب حتى وجدناها في كتبهم المعتبرة، فتحققنا أن التعصب يغطي على العقول، وعنده تعمى القلوب التي في الصدور، ولا أدري كيف ذهب عليهم أن مثل هذه الأسامي إنما تطلق على من قام به الفعل لا من أوجد الفعل أو لا يرون أن كثيراً من الصفات قد أوجدها الله تعالى في مجالها وفاقاً ولا تتصف بها إلا المحال . نعم إذا ثبت بالدليل أن الموجد هو الله تعالى لزمهم صحة هذه التسمية بناء على أصلهم الفاسد في إطلاق المتكلم على الله تعالى بخلق الكلام في بعض الأجسام، وكأن قول القائل لخصمه : مذهبك باطل حجه لكونه كلام الله تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. وهم الجهلهم يوردون مثل هذا الإلزام على أهل الحق ، ويجعلون قول السني للمعتزلي آذيتني، أو طلبتك، أو أقبل علي، وما أشبه ذلك تركاً للمذهب، ويعتقدون أن إسناد الأفعال إلى العباد مجاز عند أهل السنة.
The fourth: that if God were the originator of the actions of servants, He would be their doer, because the meaning of both is the same. And if He were their doer, He would be described by them, because there is no meaning to 'unbeliever' and 'wrongdoer' for example except the doer of unbelief and wrongdoing. Then it would necessitate that the Creator, exalted and sanctified, be an unbeliever, a wrongdoer, an immoral person, an eater, a drinker, a stander, a sitter, and other such obscenities that the rational person cannot even utter, let alone bring to mind. And this specious argument we used to hear from the foolish commoners and market people among the Mu'tazila, and we were amazed until we found it in their esteemed books. Then we realized that fanaticism covers the intellects, and with it the hearts in breasts become blind. I do not know how it escaped them that such names are only applied to the one in whom the act inheres, not the one who brings the act into existence. Or do they not see that many attributes are created by God in their loci, unanimously, and only the loci are described by them? Yes, if it were proven by evidence that the originator is God, they would be forced to accept such naming based on their corrupt principle of applying 'speaker' to God because He creates speech in some bodies. And it is as if one says to his opponent: 'Your doctrine is false because its proof is His speech' — God is exalted above that greatly. And because of their ignorance, they bring such an implication against the people of truth, and they consider the statement of a Sunni to a Mu'tazili: 'You harmed me,' or 'I seek you,' or 'Turn to me,' and the like, as a abandonment of the doctrine, and they believe that attribution of actions to servants is metaphorical among the Sunnis.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and most egregious objection: if God creates human actions, then He is the doer of those actions, and would be described by them—e.g., a creator of unbelief would Himself be an unbeliever, a creator of injustice would be unjust, etc. This would lead to describing God with all sorts of obscenities. The author expresses astonishment that such a weak argument appears in Mu'tazilite books, attributing it to fanaticism. The refutation is straightforward: a person is described by an act only when that act inheres in him, not when he merely originates it in another. God creates heat in fire, but fire is described as hot, not God. Similarly, God creates actions in servants, so the descriptions apply to the servants, not to God. The author notes that the Mu'tazila inconsistently use this reasoning: they themselves attribute 'speaker' to God because He creates speech in bodies, even though speech inheres in the body, not in God. So their own principle would require them to say God is not a speaker, which they reject. Thus their objection is flimsy and born of ignorance, often used to harass Sunnis.

Key Terms
موجد
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that originating an action necessarily makes one the subject of that action, so God would be described by human sins.
Correction: The author will later clarify that being the originator does not make one the locus of the action; only the locus is described by it.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what would be the consequence if God were the originator of human actions?
A) God would be described as just.
B) God would be described as an unbeliever and wrongdoer.
C) Humans would have no free will.
D) God would not be the creator of everything.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite objection assumes that originating an action is identical to being its subject, leading to the absurd conclusion that God would be described by evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself excusing it by saying 'God created the act in me'? How does this objection expose the flaw in that excuse and call you to own your actions fully?
?
If you truly believe that God is not described by your sinful acts, does that make you more conscious that your actions reflect your own character, not His? What will you change today to live in that awareness?
?
The Mu'tazila thought that if God creates actions, He must be their subject. Are there areas in your life where you treat God as a mere cause rather than the Lord who is transcendent over all? How can you realign your worship?

Answering the Objection: Originator vs. Locus
The author's refutation of the objection by distinguishing originator from locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key distinction between the originator of an act and the locus in which the act inheres, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite objection.

Core Idea
Names apply to the locus, not the originator.

In this lesson, the author refutes the fourth objection by pointing out a basic conceptual error: names like 'unbeliever' apply to the one in whom the act inheres, not the one who brings the act into existence. He illustrates with the example of heat in fire and notes the hypocrisy of the Mu'tazila regarding divine speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Notice the core distinction between 'man qāma bihi al-fi‘l' and 'man awjada al-fi‘l'.
2
Examine the analogy
God creates heat in fire, but fire is described as hot, not God. Similarly, God creates actions in servants.
3
Reflect on the Mu'tazila inconsistency
They apply 'speaker' to God because He creates speech, which would contradict their own principle.
4
Understand the Sunni position
Attribution of actions to servants is metaphorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What is the main flaw the author identifies in the fourth objection?

Key Takeaway
The originator of an act is not described by it; only the locus in which the act inheres is described.

?

Third Objection: Verses Ascribing Action Terms to Humans and the Answer
Author lists verses and answers via metaphorical interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash‘ari tradition interprets Qur’anic verses that ascribe creative action terms to humans, preserving divine omnipotence while acknowledging human causality.

Core Idea
Action terms in the Qur’an are metaphors for human causation, not literal creation.

The third objection against the Ash‘ari doctrine of divine creation of all actions is based on numerous Qur’anic verses that use words like 'work', 'act', 'create', and 'earn' in reference to human beings. If God alone creates, what do these verses mean? In this passage, the author lists these verses and then provides a systematic answer: they are to be interpreted metaphorically or in a specific technical sense, thereby reconciling scripture with rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of verses
The passage opens with a long enumeration of verses that use common action terms. Read them carefully and note the diversity of terms.
The terms include 'work', 'act', 'making', 'earning', 'creating', etc.
2
Understand the threat to divine omnipotence
Recognize why these verses were seen as a challenge: they seem to attribute creative power to humans, which would contradict God's exclusive creation.
3
Study the first answer: metaphorical causation
The author gives two interpretations. The first is that these ascriptions are metaphors for ordinary causation, like saying 'the amir built the city' when he only commanded.
4
Examine the exceptions and special terms
The author makes an exception for 'kash' (earning) which can be literal, and reinterprets 'khalq' (creation) as 'determination' and 'ja‘l' (making) as 'transformation'.
5
Consider the Imam's view as a stronger solution
The passage ends by noting that according to the Imam's own view (that the conjunction of power and motive is created by God), no metaphor is needed at all.
This view completely avoids the problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: الآيات الصريحة في إسناد الألفاظ الموضوعة للإيجاد إلى العباد وهي العمل كقوله تعالى (ومن عمل صالحاً فلنفسه) و(ليجزي الذين أساءوا بما عملوا) و(إن الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات) و(من عمل سيئة فلا يجزي إلا مثلها) وهذا كثير جداً. والفعل كقوله تعالى (وما تفعلوا من خير فإن الله به عليم) و(افعلوا الخير) والصنع كقوله تعالى (ولبئس ما كانوا يصنعون) والكسب كقوله تعالى (ووفيت كل نفس ما كسبت) و(كل امرئ بما كسب رهين) و(اليوم تجزى كل نفس بما كسبت) والجعل كقوله تعالى (يجعلون أصابعهم في آذانهم من الصواعق) و(وجعلوا الله شركاء الجن) والخلق كقوله تعالى (فتبارك الله أحسن الخالقين) و(وأخلق لكم من الطين) و(وإذ تخلق من الطين كهيئة الطير) والإحداث كقوله حكاية عن الخضر (حتى أحدث لك ذكراً) والابتداع كقوله تعالى (ورهبانية ابتدعوها). والجواب: أنه لما ثبت بالدلائل السالفة أن الكل بقضاء الله تعالى وقدره. وجب جعل هذه الألفاظ مجازات عن التسبب العادي أي من صار سبباً عادياً للأعمال الصالحة، وعلى هذا القياس، أو جعل هذه الإسنادات مجازات لكون العبد سبباً لهذه الأفعال كما في (بنى الأمير المدينة) هذا في غير لفظ الكسب، فإنه يصح على حقيقته. والخلق فإنه بمعنى التقدير والجعل بمعنى التصيير، وهو لا يستلزم إيجاد أمر محقق مثل جعل الله الدرهم في الكيس، وجعل لزيد شريكاً. وأما على رأي الإمام وهو أن مجموع القدرة والداعية مؤثرة في الفعل، وذلك المجموع بخلق الله تعالى من غير اختيار العبد فلا مجاز ولا إشكال ولا استقلال للعبد فلا اعتزال.
Third: The clear verses that ascribe words placed for bringing into existence to the servants: 'work' as His saying, 'Whoever does a righteous deed, it is for himself' and 'to recompense those who have done evil for what they did' and 'Indeed, those who have believed and done righteous deeds' and 'Whoever does an evil deed will not be recompensed except the like of it' – and this is very abundant. And 'act' as His saying, 'And whatever good you do, indeed God is knowing of it' and 'do good'. And 'making' as His saying, 'And how evil was what they used to make'. And 'earning' as His saying, 'And every soul will be fully compensated for what it earned' and 'Every person is held in pledge for what he earned' and 'Today every soul will be recompensed for what it earned'. And 'making' (ja‘l) as His saying, 'They put their fingers in their ears from the thunderbolts' and 'And they make the jinn partners with God'. And 'creating' (khalq) as His saying, 'So blessed is God, the best of creators' and 'And I create for you from clay' and 'when you create from clay the likeness of a bird'. And 'originating' (ihdāth) as His story of al-Khidr, 'until I relate to you a mention of it'. And 'innovating' (ibtidā‘) as His saying, 'and monasticism they innovated'. And the answer: Since it has been established by the preceding proofs that everything is by the decree and predestination of God Almighty, it is necessary to consider these words as metaphors for ordinary causation, i.e., whoever becomes an ordinary cause of righteous deeds, and so on, or to consider these ascriptions as metaphors for the servant being a cause of these actions, like 'the amir built the city'. This is except for the word 'earning' (kash), for it can be taken in its literal sense. As for 'creating' (khalq), it has the meaning of 'determination' (taqdīr), and 'making' (ja‘l) means 'transformation' (taṣyīr), and it does not entail bringing into existence a determinate thing, like God making a dirham in a purse, or making Zaid a partner. And according to the view of the Imam, that the conjunction of power and motive is effective in the action, and that conjunction is created by God Almighty without the servant’s choice, then there is no metaphor, no problem, no independence for the servant, and thus no Mu‘tazilism.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection based on Qur'anic verses that use explicit creation-related words (like work, act, making, earning, creating, originating, innovating) and ascribe them to human beings. The objection is that if God were the sole creator of all actions, these verses would be meaningless. The author answers by saying that since it has been proven by prior proofs that everything is by God's decree, these words must be interpreted metaphorically (مجازات). The author provides two possible interpretations: Either they indicate ordinary causation (التسبب العادي), meaning the person is the customary cause of the action, or they are metaphorical ascriptions similar to saying 'the amir built the city' when in reality the amir only commanded the building. The author makes an exception for the word 'كسب' (earning), which can be taken literally. Then he explains that 'خلق' (creation) can mean 'تقدير' (determination) and 'جعل' (making) can mean 'تصيير' (transformation), which do not necessarily entail bringing something into existence from nothing, giving examples like God putting a dirham in a purse or making someone a partner. Finally, the author reiterates the Imam's own view: that the conjunction of power and motive is the effective factor, created by God, and this view avoids all metaphorical interpretation, solves the problem, and negates any independence of the servant, thus avoiding Mu‘tazilism.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author completely denies any human causal efficacy, making humans mere puppets.
Correction: The author allows for metaphorical ascription and even literal interpretation of 'kash', preserving a genuine human role as customary cause while upholding divine omnipotence.

Checkpoint
According to the author's answer, which of the following action terms can be taken literally (على حقيقته)?
A) العمل (work)
B) الفعل (act)
C) الكسب (earning)
D) الخلق (creation)

Key Takeaway
The Qur’anic verses ascribing action terms to humans are interpreted metaphorically or with specific meanings to maintain God’s exclusive creation of all actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'Whoever does a righteous deed, it is for himself,' do you truly feel that the deed is from God alone? In your heart, do you take ownership of your good deeds, or do you attribute them entirely to God? What would change if you consistently viewed every action as God's creation?
?
The author says the conjunction of your power and motive is created by God without your choice. Does that thought make you feel relieved of burden, or does it unsettle you because it challenges your sense of control? How does this affect your daily gratitude and dependence on God?
?
You have just learned that your 'actions' are metaphorically yours but really God's. Think of a sin you committed. If God creates even that sin, how does that affect your repentance and your view of God's justice? Are you ready to accept that your evil deeds are also within God's decree, and still take responsibility for them?

Ali's Sermon on Belief in Qadar
Ali's sermon and poem affirming qadar and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ali affirmed belief in qadar while rejecting fatalism, as shown in his sermon and poem.

Core Idea
Belief in qadar requires action, not resignation.

In this passage, the author presents two statements from Ali that establish the correct understanding of divine decree. Ali first declares that belief in qadar is a requirement for being from his followers, then demonstrates through his own actions during war that qadar does not lead to passivity. This refutes both those who deny qadar and those who misuse it to justify inaction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two distinct statements: the sermon and the poem.
2
Identify the two statements
The passage contains two separate sayings of Ali: his declaration about belief in qadar from the pulpit, and his poem during the war in Sham. Identify each and note their contexts.
3
Analyze the poem's meaning
The poem says 'caution will not raise what has been decreed.' Yet Ali still takes action by rolling up his sleeves and ordering the banner forward. This shows that qadar is not an excuse for inaction.
Contrast with a fatalist who might sit back and do nothing.
4
Connect to refutation of fatalism
Ask yourself: How do these two statements together refute the idea that belief in qadar leads to fatalism? Ali affirms qadar but acts vigorously.
This is the core teaching of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن وقاحتهم أنهم يروجون باطلهم بنسبته إلى مثل أمير المؤمنين علي وأولاده رضي الله خطب الناس على منبر الكوفة ، فقال ليس منا من لم يؤمن بالقدر خيره وشره وأنه حين أراد حرب الشام قال شمرت عن ثوبي ودعوت قنبراً : قدم لوائي لا تؤخر حذراً لن يرفع الحذار ما قد قدرا وأنه قال لمن قال إني أملك الخير والشر والطاعة والمعصية تملكها مع الله أو تملكها بدون الله ، فإن قلت أملكها مع الله فقد ادعيت أنك شريك الله ، وإن قلت أملكها بدون الله فقد ادعيت أنك مستبد
And from their impudence is that they promote their falsehood by attributing it to the likes of the Commander of the Faithful Ali and his children, may Allah be pleased with him. He gave a sermon on the pulpit of Kufa and said: 'He is not from us who does not believe in qadar (قدر), its good and its evil.' And that when he intended to go to war in Sham, he said: 'I rolled up my sleeves and called Qanbar: Bring forward my banner, do not delay out of caution; caution will not raise what has been decreed.' And that he said to one who said 'I possess good and evil, obedience and disobedience' – 'Do you possess them with Allah or without Allah? If you say you possess them with Allah, then you claim to be a partner with Allah, and if you say you possess them without Allah, then you claim to be independent.'
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage presents additional statements from Ali to refute those who misattribute deterministic views to him. First, Ali declares that belief in qadar, both its good and evil, is a requirement for being from his followers. Second, his poem during the war with Sham shows that while he affirms qadar, he still takes action and is cautious, but acknowledges that caution cannot change what is decreed – this refutes fatalistic resignation. Third, Ali's response to a man who claims to possess good and evil challenges the man's assumption: if he claims possession with Allah, it implies partnership; if without Allah, it implies independence (and thus denial of divine sovereignty). This refutes both absolute compulsion and absolute free will.

Key Terms
القدر
الإيمان بالقدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That belief in qadar leads to fatalism and inaction.
Correction: Ali's own actions show that he took precautions and fought, but recognized that outcomes are from Allah. True belief in qadar does not negate human effort.

Checkpoint
What does Ali's poem during the war with Sham indicate about his view of qadar?
A) He believed qadar makes human effort pointless.
B) He took action while acknowledging that caution cannot change what is decreed.
C) He rejected belief in qadar.
D) He believed humans control their own destiny.

Key Takeaway
Ali's belief in qadar did not prevent him from taking action; true faith in qadar includes both belief and effort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you find yourself relying on both effort and tawakkul, or do you fall into fatalism? Examine your recent choices: where did you neglect action because 'Allah will decide anyway'? Write down one area where you need to reconcile belief in qadar with proactive effort.
?
Ali said 'ليس منا من لم يؤمن بالقدر'. If you are honest, do you truly believe in qadar in your heart, or do you sometimes doubt when things go wrong? How does your belief (or doubt) affect your daily actions?
?
Imagine you are about to make a major decision. How would Ali's example of both believing in qadar and taking precaution change the way you prepare? What is one step you can take today to act on this balance?

Ali's Refutation of Partnership and Independence
Ali's dichotomy refuting partnership and independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ali's logical refutation of those who claim to possess good and evil independently of Allah.

Core Idea
Human agency is neither partnership with Allah nor independence from Him.

In this passage, Ali challenges a man who claims to possess good and evil, obedience and disobedience. Through a simple dichotomy, Ali shows that such a claim either implies partnership with Allah (shirk) or independence from Allah (istibdad), both of which are false. This refutes both the Qadariyya (who exaggerate free will) and the Jabriyya (who deny it).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: a claim, a question, and two possibilities.
2
Understand the dichotomy
Ali presents two possibilities for the man's claim of possessing good and evil: either with Allah or without Allah. Recognize that these are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
3
Identify the two false positions
The first possibility leads to shirk (partnership with Allah). The second leads to istibdad (independence). Both are false. Understand why each is unacceptable.
This shows the middle path: human actions are created by Allah but acquired by humans.
4
Apply to theological extremes
Consider how this refutes the Qadariyya (who claim humans create actions, implying partnership) and the Jabriyya (who deny human agency, while the man's claim exaggerates agency).
This is the key insight for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If you say you possess them with Allah, then you claim to be a partner with Allah, and if you say you possess them without Allah, then you claim to be independent.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That humans have absolute control over their actions independently of Allah.
Correction: Ali shows that such a claim either implies partnership with Allah or independence from Allah, both of which are false. The correct view is that human actions are created by Allah but earned by humans.

Key Takeaway
Human agency is real but dependent on Allah; claiming otherwise leads to either shirk or denial of divine sovereignty.

?

?

?
If every action of obedience is ultimately from Allah, how does that change the way you approach your daily worship and interactions? Will you begin each action with a conscious recognition of His power?

Fifth Objection: Is Shirk's Ugliness Known by Pure Reason?
Al-Taftazani presents and answers the objection that shirk is universally known to be bad by reason alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari response to the claim that shirk's ugliness is known by pure reason, and how they argue that certainty actually comes from revealed laws and ingrained custom.

Core Idea
Certainty about the ugliness of shirk is rooted in revelation and custom, not pure reason.

The fifth objection challenges the Ash'ari position by claiming that shirk is self-evidently ugly to reason. Al-Taftazani presents this objection and then gives the Ash'ari answer, which denies that pure reason alone provides such certainty. Instead, the response traces the origin of this certainty to the consistent condemnation of shirk in all revealed laws and the reinforcement of that judgment through human customs. This passage teaches us how the Ash'aris defend the necessity of revelation for moral knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the objector's claim that shirk's ugliness is certain regardless of revelation, and that the one who knows God's essence and attributes would never associate partners with Him.
2
Examine the answer
See how Al-Taftazani responds by attributing the certainty to the establishment of religious laws and customs, not to unaided reason.
Notice the phrase 'مركوزاً في العقول' — ingrained in minds.
3
Reflect on the implication
Realize that what seems like a rational conclusion may actually be a deeply ingrained belief from religious tradition and repeated custom.
This shows the Ash'ari reliance on revelation even for moral truths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس: إنا قاطعون بأنه يقبح عند الله تعالى من العارف بذاته، وصفاته أن يشرك به، وينسب إليه الزوجة والولد وما لا يليق به من صفات النقص وسمات الحدوث بمعنى أنه يستحق الذم والعقاب في حكم الله تعالى سواء ورد الشرع أو لم یرد. والجواب : أن مبنى القطع على استقرار الشرائع على ذلك، واستمرار العادات بمثله فى الشاهد فصار قبحه مركوزاً في العقول حيث يظن أنه بمجرد حكم العقل .
The fifth: We are certain that it is ugly for God Almighty, for one who knows His essence and attributes, to associate partners with Him, and to ascribe to Him wife, child, and what is not befitting of Him of the attributes of deficiency and marks of temporal generation, meaning that he deserves blame and punishment in God's judgment, whether scripture came or not. The answer: The basis of certainty is the establishment of the religious laws on that, and the continuation of habits similar to it in the observable realm, so its ugliness became ingrained in minds, where one thinks it is merely by reason's judgment.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection against the Ash‘ari view. The objectors claim that we are absolutely certain that associating partners with God (shirk) and attributing to Him spouses, children, and other imperfections is ugly for the one who knows God's essence and attributes, and that this ugliness holds regardless of whether any scripture has come, implying it is a rational judgment. The Ash‘ari answer denies that such certainty is purely rational. Instead, it traces the certainty to the fact that all revealed laws have consistently condemned shirk, and human habits (such as reviling those who speak ill of a respected figure) have reinforced this judgment. Thus, the ugliness of shirk is deeply ingrained in minds (مركوز في العقول) due to collective religious experience, leading people to mistakenly think it is a self-evident rational truth. The passage emphasizes that the source of certainty is not unaided reason but the pervasive influence of revelation and custom.

Key Terms
شرك
العارف بذاته
صفات النقص
قبح
استقرار الشرائع
عاديات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari answer denies that shirk is ugly, or that it is known at all.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer does not deny that shirk is ugly; it denies that its ugliness is known by pure reason independent of any revealed or customary context.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fifth objection, why do people think that shirk's ugliness is known by reason alone?
A) Because reason independently recognizes the harm of polytheism
B) Because the ugliness of shirk is so obvious that no one questions it
C) Because religious laws and habits have made it so ingrained in minds that it seems rational
D) Because the objection forces them to admit it

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari answer contends that the certainty of shirk's ugliness is not from pure reason but from the accumulated testimony of revelation and ingrained custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel certain that something is wrong—like shirk—have you ever stopped to ask how much of that certainty comes from the society and religious tradition you were raised in, versus your own reason? This lesson suggests that even powerful convictions may be shaped by custom. How does that challenge your confidence in your own moral judgments? Will you today examine one belief you hold to trace its true origin?
?
The objectors claimed that shirk's ugliness is known by reason alone, but the Ash'aris responded that it is ingrained by revelation and custom. Think of a belief you hold strongly about right and wrong. Can you honestly trace its origin? Is it possible that you mistake cultural or religious reinforcement for self-evident truth? Would you be willing to test your certainty by asking yourself: 'Would I still believe this if I had grown up in a different tradition?'
?
This passage teaches that the ugliness of shirk is so ingrained that people think it is purely rational. Are there areas of your faith where you rely on 'common sense' without realizing that common sense itself is shaped by Islamic teachings? What can you do today to deepen your awareness of the sources of your convictions, so that your faith is based on conscious acceptance of revelation rather than mere habit?

The Mu'tazilite Principle of Hidden Wisdom and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents a Mu'tazilite response to divine evil and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilites attempted to resolve the problem of evil using a principle of hidden benefits, and why Al-Taftazani argues that this principle actually undermines their own position.

Core Idea
A principle that seems to defend God may actually be used against those who propose it.

The problem of evil is a classic theological challenge. Some Mu'tazilites proposed that actions appearing evil have hidden benefits, using examples from scripture. In this passage, Al-Taftazani presents their argument and then refutes it by showing that the very principle they rely on invalidates their own objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the Mu'tazilites present a great principle (aṣl jalīl) about actions appearing ugly but containing hidden wisdom and benefits.
The examples from the story of Moses and al-Khidr and human discipline are crucial.
2
Identify the principle and its examples
The Mu'tazilites claim that an act may be deemed ugly at first glance, but when hidden wisdom becomes apparent, the judgment turns to approval. They illustrate with the piercing of the ship and killing the boy by al-Khidr, and with disciplining a child.
These examples show that initial judgment can be mistaken.
3
See how they apply it to divine acts
They extend the principle to all divine actions that appear without goodness, citing the angels' wonder at Adam's creation (Quran 2:30). They argue that creating harmful things like Iblis and his progeny is thus good.
The verse 'I know what you do not know' supports hidden wisdom.
4
Understand the refutation
Al-Taftazani refutes by stating that if you reflect, this principle is against them, not for them. The reason is that if initial appearances can be wrong, then the Mu'tazilites' own objection to evil based on apparent ugliness is invalid.
The principle backfires, as it admits that judgments may be reversed.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this refutation uses the opponent's own logic. The phrase 'this principle is against you, not for you' is the key turning point.
This shows the power of internal critique in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب بعض المعتزلة بأن لنا أصلاً جليلاً تنحل به أمثال هذه الشبه، وهو أنه قد يستقبح الفعل في بادىء النظر مع أن فيه حكم ومصالح إذا ظهرت عاد الاستقباح استحساناً كما في قصة موسى مع الخضر عليهما السلام من خرق السفينة وقتل الغلام ، وكما في تعذيب الإنسان ولده أو عبده للتأديب والزجر عن بعض المنكرات . وعلى هذا ينبغي أن يحمل كل ما لا يدرك فيه جهة حسن من أفعال الباري تعالى وتقدس وإليه الإشارة بقوله تعالى : إني أعلم ما لا تعلمون حيث تعجب الملائكة من خلق آدام عليه السلام و به تبين حسن خلق المؤذيات، وإبليس وذريته ونحو ذلك. قلنا : إذا تأملتم فهذا الأصل عليكم لا لكم والله أعلم .
And some of the Mu'tazilites answered that we have a great principle by which such doubts are resolved, which is that an act may be deemed ugly at first glance while it contains wisdom and benefits which, when they become apparent, the ugly judgment turns into approval, as in the story of Moses with al-Khidr (peace be upon them) of the piercing of the ship and the killing of the boy, and as in the punishment of a man for his son or servant for discipline and deterrence from some reprehensible acts. On this basis should be interpreted every act of God Almighty and Exalted whose aspect of goodness is not perceived, and to this is the reference in His saying: 'Indeed, I know what you do not know' (Quran 2:30) when the angels were astonished at the creation of Adam (peace be upon him), and thereby the goodness of creating harmful things, Iblis and his progeny, and the like, is shown. We said: If you reflect, this principle is against you, not for you. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer sequence. Al-Taftazani first relays a Mu'tazilite response to the problem of evil: they claim a principle that actions appearing evil may have hidden benefits, citing the story of Moses and al-Khidr and human disciplining. They apply this to divine acts, arguing that all apparently evil divine acts have hidden wisdom, referencing the angels' wonder at Adam's creation. Al-Taftazani then refutes by noting that if this principle is accepted, it undermines the Mu'tazilites' own objection, since they are admitting initial judgments may be wrong. The phrase 'this principle is against you, not for you' shows the argument backfires.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha)
استقباح (istiqbāḥ)
استحسان (istiḥsān)
أصل جليل (aṣl jalīl)
مصالح (maṣāliḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans can definitively judge that certain divine acts are evil based on initial appearance.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites themselves argue that initial judgments may be overturned by hidden wisdom, so their own objection to evil is undermined.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilite response presented, what is the principle that resolves doubts about evil in divine actions?
A) That all actions are inherently good or evil
B) That an act may seem ugly but has hidden benefits
C) That God cannot do evil
D) That humans can fully comprehend divine wisdom

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite attempt to resolve the problem of evil by appealing to hidden wisdom backfires, as it undermines their own objection that evil exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a difficult event in your life that you initially saw as purely evil. Have you ever later seen wisdom in it, or are you still waiting? How does this lesson challenge your impatience with Allah's decree? Will you commit to trusting His hidden wisdom even when you cannot see it?
?
The Mu'tazilites claimed to defend God using hidden wisdom, but the argument was turned against them. Have you ever tried to defend your own actions or beliefs with a principle that actually exposed your own inconsistency? What does this teach you about pride in your own reasoning?
?
When you read the verse 'Indeed, I know what you do not know,' do you truly live as if Allah knows better than you? What is one area of your life where you are still insisting on your own judgment over His? Will you surrender it today?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

Defining Injustice and Its Application to Divine Acts
The author defines injustice and applies it to divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of injustice and why divine causing of pain is not considered unjust.

Core Idea
Injustice is undeserved harm without benefit; deserved or beneficial pain is not injustice.

The concept of injustice (ẓulm) is central to debates about divine actions. The Mu'tazila defined it precisely to defend God from the charge of injustice while allowing for pain in the world. This passage lays out that definition and applies it to examples like punishment, travel hardships, and even God's burning of an infant.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of injustice
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the definition: harm that is not deserved, does not include benefit or repulsion of harm, and is not customary.
Notice the conditional clauses that exclude specific cases.
2
Examine the excluded examples
Identify the five examples given: punishment, travel hardship, cupping, repelling an attacker, and God burning an infant. For each, understand why it is not injustice.
Each example falls under one of the criteria: deserved, beneficial, or customary.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that if pain is deserved, beneficial, or customary, it is good (ḥasan) and permissible from God without compensation. This is key to Mu'tazilite theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسروا الظلم بضرر غير مستحق لا يشتمل على نفع أو دفع ضرر معلوم أو مظنون، ولا يكون دفعاً نفسه ولا مفعولاً بطريق جري العادة، فخرج العقاب ومشقة السفر والحجامة ودفع الصائل، وإحراق الله تعالى الصبي الملقى في النار. فإن الإيلام إذا كان مستحقاً أو مشتملاً على نفع أو دفع ضر، وعادياً لا يكون ظلماً، بل يكون حسناً يجوز صدوره عن الله تعالى من غير عوض عليه.
And they interpreted injustice as harm that is not deserved, not including a benefit or the repulsion of a known or presumed harm, and it is not itself a repulsion nor an act done in the customary way. Then punishment, the hardship of travel, cupping, repelling an attacker, and God's burning of the infant thrown into the fire are excluded. For if the causing of pain is deserved or includes a benefit or repulsion of harm, and is customary, it is not injustice, rather it is good, and its occurrence from God without compensation is permissible.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage defines injustice (ẓulm) as understood by the Mu'tazila and applies it to divine actions. The definition states that injustice is a harm that is not deserved (ghayr mustaḥaqq) and does not involve any benefit or prevention of harm that is known or presumed. Additionally, it is not itself a repulsion of harm nor a customary action. Then the author lists examples that are excluded from being injustice: punishment (al-ʿiqāb) is excluded because it is deserved; the hardship of travel and cupping are excluded because they involve benefit; repelling an attacker is excluded because it prevents harm; and God's burning of an infant thrown into fire is excluded because it is not done out of injustice but perhaps for some divine wisdom. The passage concludes that if causing pain (īlām) meets these criteria (deserved, beneficial, or customary), it is not injustice but good (ḥasan) and permissible for God without compensation. This is a key Mu'tazilite principle to defend God from injustice while allowing for divine acts that cause pain.

Key Terms
ظلم
إيلام
عوض
حسن
مستحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any pain caused by God requires compensation to be just.
Correction: According to this passage, pain that is deserved, beneficial, or customary is not injustice and does not require compensation.

Checkpoint
According to the definition in the passage, which of the following is NOT considered injustice?
A) Harm that is undeserved and has no benefit or repulsion of harm
B) Harm that is deserved and includes a benefit
C) Harm that is a punishment for a crime
D) Harm that is customary and has no benefit

Key Takeaway
Injustice is strictly defined as undeserved harm without benefit or repulsion; thus many divinely caused pains are not unjust.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accused God of injustice in your heart during a trial? According to this passage, could that trial be a deserved punishment, a hidden benefit, or a customary test? How does this challenge your immediate reaction to suffering? Will you commit to pausing and asking 'Is this harm deserved, beneficial, or customary?' before you question God's justice?
?
This lesson defines injustice so narrowly that almost no divine action qualifies as unjust. Does that make you uncomfortable? Does it feel like God is being 'let off the hook'? Or does it deepen your trust that He always acts wisely, even when you cannot see the benefit? Which feeling is stronger, and what does that reveal about your relationship with Him?
?
The passage says God can cause pain without compensation when it is deserved or beneficial. Think of the most painful event in your life. If it was never compensated, could you still believe God is perfectly just? What would it take for you to reach that level of surrender? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to strengthen your reliance on His wisdom over your expectation of repayment.

Divine Transcendence

The Permissibility of Pain Under Divine Justice
Lists three modes of permissible infliction of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways pain can be permissible by God's command, permission, or enabling with delayed justice, and the divine compensation involved.

Core Idea
Pain is not inherently unjust when it serves a greater purpose under God's justice.

The author begins by addressing a common concern: how can a just God allow pain? He explains that pain can occur by God's direct command, by His permission, or by His enabling while delaying justice to the afterlife, where compensation is given. This lesson unpacks these three scenarios and their theological basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three categories: by command, by permission, and by enabling with delayed justice.
The passage uses the example of animals and oppressed persons to illustrate.
2
Identify the three scenarios
List the three ways pain is inflicted: 1) by God's command (e.g., using animals), 2) by His permission (e.g., slaughter), 3) by His enabling with postponed justice (e.g., oppression).
The third scenario includes a promise of compensation from God.
3
Reflect on divine justice
Consider why each scenario is just: command and permission imply authority, enabling with compensation ensures no injustice.
God's transcendence (تعاليه عن الظلم) is the ultimate guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الإيلام بأمر الله كما في استعمال البهائم أو بإباحته كما في ذبحها أو بتمكينه مع تأخير الانتصاف إلى دار الجزاء كما في المظلوم عوضه على الله تعالى لتعاليه عن الظلم ومنها أنه إذا استوى لذة وألم في كونهما لطفاً، فالجمهور على أنه يتعين اللذة، ويقبح الألم. لأنه إنما يحسن إذا تعين طريقاً للعوض واللطف وقال أبو هاشم: بل يتخير منهما كما بين المنفعتين لأن الإيلام بكونه عوضاً ولطفاً قد خرج عن كونه عبثاً وظلماً
And among them: That inflicting pain by God's command, as in the use of animals, or by His permission, as in their slaughter, or by His enabling while delaying justice to the abode of recompense, as in the case of the oppressed person whose compensation is upon God Almighty due to His being above injustice. And among them: That if pleasure and pain are equal in both being grace, then the majority holds that pleasure is determined, and pain is ugly, because it is only good if it is determined as a path to compensation and grace. And Abu Hashim said: Rather, one chooses from them as between two benefits, because inflicting pain, being compensation and grace, has departed from being futile and unjust.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents two related points. First, the author notes that inflicting pain can occur by God's direct command (like using animals for work), by His permission (like slaughtering animals), or by His enabling while postponing justice to the afterlife, as in the case of the oppressed who will receive compensation from God, for God is above injustice. Second, when pain and pleasure are both equally capable of being a grace (lutf), the majority (al-jumhur) holds that pleasure is determined and pain is deemed ugly (qabih). Pain is only considered good when it becomes a necessary means for compensation and grace. Abu Hashim disagrees, arguing that one may choose between them as between two benefits, since inflicting pain, now having the purpose of compensation and grace, is no longer futile or unjust.

Key Terms
الإيلام
أمر الله
العوض
دار الجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any pain in the world is unjust and contradicts God's mercy.
Correction: Pain can be permissible when it is commanded, permitted, or enabled with promised compensation, thus not unjust.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the oppressed person's compensation upon God?
A) Because God commands oppression
B) Because God is above injustice and compensates in the afterlife
C) Because pain is always a punishment
D) Because the oppressor is forgiven

Key Takeaway
Pain is permissible under God's justice when it stems from His command, permission, or enabling with promised compensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face pain or witness oppression, do you truly trust that God will compensate in the afterlife, or do you secretly doubt His justice? What would it take for your heart to rest in that promise?
?
If God commands or permits pain (as in the use or slaughter of animals), does your conscience submit to His wisdom, or do you still feel a tension? How can you align your inner response with the truth that He is above injustice?
?
Reflect on a time you suffered without seeing immediate recompense. Did you resent God or trust Him? Now that you know the third mode (tamkeen with deferred justice), how does this reshape your perspective? Will you consciously remind yourself of this 'compensation upon God' next time you feel wronged?
